# Chapter 1 — Boarding the Wyrm

The boarding line for the Skybound Wyrm opened at second bell, and Theron Mavik arrived at first bell and stood in front of a rope with eleven other people who had also arrived at first bell, all of them looking at different things to avoid acknowledging this.

He moved his thumb off the graduation pin for the fourth time this morning.

The gondola was larger than he'd expected. He'd memorized the standard passenger-cargo specifications for his licensing exam — hull length, deck plan, lift capacity, harness chain loads — but specifications were numbers, and the actual object in front of him was the length of a city block, lacquered the color of old bone, and gave off a low hum he felt through his boot soles. Three rigging chains ran up and over the forward boom, each as thick as his thigh. At the far end of the chains, three drakes were settled in their harness, wings folded, radiating enough heat that he could feel it from thirty feet away.

One of them was eating something with tremendous concentration.

"That's Mottled." A small woman with sun-freckled arms and a leather handler's apron was running a stiff-bristled brush behind the ear-ridge of one of the other drakes, unhurried, the kind of unhurried that came from long practice. "Gets anxious before a long flight. Settles herself by eating. Works through about half a crate of river fish when we're docked in the bigger ports. By altitude she's fine."

Theron looked at the eating drake more carefully. She was the second-largest, her scales the color of dried clay where they weren't mottled with a rust-orange that gave her her name, and she was working through a fish the size of his forearm with the studied indifference of someone who was aware of being watched and had decided this was not her concern.

"And that's Gravel?" He indicated the one the handler was brushing — broad-headed, darker, with the particular stillness of something that was choosing to hold still.

"Gravel, yes. I wouldn't approach him until he's been introduced to you specifically. All bite and no warning, and a very long memory for faces. He's fine once he knows you, but he does prefer to make the knowing his own idea." She said it without looking up from the brush. "The small one's Petal."

He looked at the small one. It was somewhere between six and seven feet at the shoulder and was watching him with one amber eye, the vertical slit pupil the size of his forearm. Pale gold scales along her underbelly shaded to something closer to cream at the jaw. She had the alert, unconcerned quality of a creature that knew exactly what it was.

"Petal," he said.

"She earned it." The handler — Wisp, her name badge said — grinned at the drake in the way most people grinned at old dogs, with the comfortable authority of someone who knew exactly what the other party was thinking and had long since made peace with it. She'd positioned herself between the brush strokes so Gravel could see every motion before it landed. "Petal knows these thermals by name, near enough. The Brindle Range approach, she'll shift her wing angle before I've told her to — she's been down this route seventeen times and she remembers them all."

"Do they communicate between themselves? While flying?"

Wisp considered this. "That's a question I've had for nine years." She moved the brush to the base of Gravel's neck. "They work together better than most mated pairs I've seen, but I couldn't tell you if that's language or just long familiarity. Mottled and Petal argue about altitude sometimes. You can tell by the way Mottled extends her right primary — she does it when she disagrees."

"And Gravel?"

"Gravel," Wisp said, with the gravity of long experience, "does as Gravel sees fit, and the other two have made their peace with that."

Theron looked at Mottled again. She had finished the fish and was regarding the dock with a half-lidded satisfaction that suggested she might be starting to feel better about the flight. Then she looked at him.

He looked away first.

"Boarding in twenty," Wisp said, still working at the brush. "First class?"

"Yes."

"Good route for us this run. Petal knows these thermals, and Mottled will have settled by altitude — she always does." She said it with the easy confidence of someone who'd delivered this reassurance two hundred times and found it to be consistently true. "Good view of the harness from the forward promenade, if you want it once we're up. Petal tends to fly slightly ahead and to the right on this approach."

He thanked her and moved a few feet to the right.

---

The dock filled around him in increments.

A family of four arrived with matching luggage in an ambitious shade of blue, the children immediately and separately making a case for a different vantage point while the parents held the line with practiced diplomacy. Two scholars in travelling coats arrived together, one of them carrying a shared briefcase and the other of them arguing about it in the specific, low-intensity way of people who had been having the same argument since before he could remember. A courier in working leathers was eating a meat pastry with the contentment of a man whose morning had gone correctly, taking his time, his spot in the queue settled and sufficient.

At the far end of the dock, a merchant in silk and two assistants had arranged themselves a careful distance from the queue. He caught her name in a low exchange between her and a waiting passenger: Madam Esherel, said with the slight deliberateness of someone naming someone they respected and were not entirely at ease with. She was examining the boarding ledger as though it owed her something. One of her assistants watched the drakes; the other watched Esherel.

Theron checked his own boarding card. Cabin fourteen, deck four, first class. Three days between here and his first active case assignment. His supervisor had set the card on his desk and said: "You have earned a rest. Take the Wyrm route. Come back with your head on straight."

She had said it twice. He had not needed it twice.

He was aware, standing there, that a man who had just passed his licensing examination and received a posting was not necessarily a man whose head was on straight. He was also aware that this awareness was itself slightly insufferable. He noted it. He looked at the drakes.

Mottled had started on a second fish.

---

The rope dropped at second bell.

He picked up his travel case and moved into the queue. Calden — the gondola steward, badge and pressed uniform, the deliberate unhurriedness of a man who had boarded a thousand nervous passengers — checked his name, confirmed his cabin, and handed him a key with his berth stamped in red wax.

"Deck Four, starboard, Mr. Mavik. The cabin board will show you the way." A small pause, the kind that was not really a pause. "I'll be around shortly to settle you in."

"Thank you, Calden."

"Of course, Mr. Mavik."

There was something in the way he said it — not rote, exactly, but well-worn, the phrase used so often it had become something different from automatic. Like a word that had been spoken into the right context enough times that it meant what it said. Theron filed it and moved on, which was what he did with most things: a clean, quiet receipt of the world as it presented itself, then the drawer closing, then the next thing.

He was aware this was a habit his supervisor had not described as a strength.

The boarding area past the gangway was the predictable bottleneck: a corridor built for two people, five people trying to use it simultaneously. He stepped sideways to let the family with the toddler navigate through first, then moved toward the staircase, filing the layout as it came: stairways, outer deck hatches, the window line of the first-class corridor above.

He very nearly stepped backward into a woman who was pulling her bag through the crowd by a handle that was losing its structural argument with the bag's weight.

"Sorry," he said.

She didn't answer, just angled herself and the bag around him with the efficiency of someone who had already spent too much of this morning navigating around people. Brown travelling coat, the practical kind, chosen for its pockets rather than its impression. She had the expression of a person who had agreed to a trip, arrived at the departure point, and was now conducting a quiet assessment of whether that had been the correct call.

She caught Calden's eye over the crowd and held up one finger: *just the one bag, then.* Calden nodded once, with the unhurried competence of a man who had already noted the bag and would see to it presently. She moved on.

Theron filed it and went up the stairs.

---

His cabin was halfway along the first-class corridor — small, clean, honest about its dimensions. West-facing, which meant long afternoon light and no dawn. He tested the lamp; it worked. He pressed once on the mattress to confirm it was a mattress rather than a board with ambitions. He set his case on the writing desk, hung his coat on the hook by the door, and went back out to the boarding deck.

He was getting air. That was all it was.

The tail end of the queue was still working through. The scholars had managed the gangway with their briefcase intact, apparently — only one of them was talking rapidly at the other now, which seemed like progress. Madam Esherel had found a gap and was moving through it with the authority of someone who expected gaps to appear. In the boarding area below, the family of four had spread out enough to be navigating in three directions at once.

And then the sound of the boarding area shifted.

Not loudly. It was the small recalibration of a crowd's attention — the way a room adjusts when something has entered that everyone is choosing to process in real time. Theron looked toward the dock entrance.

The man coming through was somewhere in his mid-twenties, with the jaw that came from a hundred years of portraits and a coat that communicated its price by not discussing it. Behind him, a personal steward pushed a luggage cart stacked three high. He walked at the front of the convoy with the settled certainty of someone who had never been required to hurry and had developed opinions about people who were.

His gaze moved across the dock once and efficiently. The scholars — dismissed. The family — beneath notation. Theron — assessed as young and not immediately consequential, set aside. Madam Esherel — and here his expression shifted very slightly, the way a man's does when he's encountered something he didn't expect and doesn't want to give ground on — acknowledged, not greeted.

"Lord Vannic," someone murmured, nearby. The name moved through the remaining queue in undertones, neighbor to neighbor.

He crossed toward the gangway without deviation. The courier — still finishing his pastry, not entirely out of the path — looked up and to the side and performed the quiet calculation of someone weighing inconvenience against principle, then moved.

Vannic barely registered the movement. He had already reached Calden.

At the gangway threshold, he stopped for an elderly passenger finishing the step down. Not with any performance — not the wide gesture or the produced smile — just a pause, nothing more, waiting the three seconds required and then taking the step up. He did it without looking at the man.

Madam Esherel, watching from six feet away, said something low to her assistant. The assistant nodded. Whatever had been said did not appear to require elaboration.

Then Calden stepped forward and began showing him to his cabin — speaking quietly about the window orientation, the dining arrangements, the first-class steward's hours. Vannic was listening with the attentiveness of someone who expected to get his money's worth, which was different from not listening.

Halfway across the boarding area, without breaking stride or looking at Calden, he said his name. Not the family name — the personal one, the one on the badge.

"Calden." A brief pause. "Thank you. For — all of it. The boarding business."

Calden replied that it was of course no trouble, and kept moving. The exchange had lasted perhaps four seconds. It was aimed at one person, in a tone that had no audience in mind.

Theron was still at the upper-deck rail.

He noticed he had registered it, filed it, and had already moved on — the way you moved on from something that didn't require further thought. Then he noticed the dock again. The woman in the brown coat was at the far edge of the boarding area, one hand on the strap of her bag, not moving. She wasn't looking at Vannic's back. She was looking at the middle of the crowd — the specific patch of air where a man had just thanked the steward by name, without ceremony, and said *all of it* as if that covered everything without needing to be itemized.

He didn't see her face change. She was just still for a moment, in a way that had a quality to it.

Then she shouldered her bag and moved on toward the staircase.

Theron went back upstairs.

---

He was considering his cabin key with academic patience — the lock required an angle that was not disclosed on the key — when the sound of footsteps on the upper staircase announced Lord Vannic and Calden arriving in the corridor, apparently still on the subject of window orientation.

It was a narrow corridor. There was a moment at the landing, natural and unremarkable, where one of them was required to acknowledge the other.

Vannic offered his hand first. Theron took it.

The grip was firm, brief, bare palm. And the side of his index finger — the raised ridge, built up by years of sustained casting hold, where prolonged contact with a spellwork frame wears the skin to something harder and more specific than ordinary callus. Theron's thesis advisor had one. Both of his practical illusion professors had them. You didn't get that from an occasional hobby. You got it from ten hours a day, reliably, across years.

*Noble who dabbles*, he filed. Then, amending: *practices more seriously than that implies.*

"You look young," Vannic said. It came out as observation rather than insult, which was something.

"First posting." The pin made it obvious; there was no use in false modesty about it.

Vannic's eyes went briefly to the lens embossed in bronze. "Investigator."

"Yes."

Something shifted in his face — not warming. Adjusting. "Hm." He released the handshake. "Well." And moved on down the corridor toward the upper stair.

Calden followed. Theron stood with his key.

*First posting, and no active case.* He was aware of the pin the way he'd been aware of it all morning — the slight weight of a new thing on his jacket, still unfamiliar in the way that new things were before they became ordinary. He was also aware that it would take roughly six months to stop checking whether it was still there, and that everyone who had been through the licensing examination had this same six months, and that this awareness did not make him stop checking.

He got the lock open on the fourth angle.

The corridor was quiet again — just the distant low hum of the gondola's hull and, below it, the sound of the dock at work: calls between loaders, the mechanical knock of a winch settling a crate into the hold. He stood in his own doorway for a moment before going in. First-class corridor, deck four, starboard: six cabins to his left, a narrow window at the end admitting a pale bar of morning light, a runner carpet that had been replaced recently enough to still hold its pattern. Someone had put a small table with a water carafe opposite the third door. That was Calden's thinking, probably — the kind of considered detail that didn't announce itself.

He went inside and set the key on the writing desk.

The case slate. The notes folder. His reference volume, which he had carried for three years and had memorized sufficiently that it was now ballast rather than resource, but he had not been willing to leave it behind. He set them in the right order, checked that the desk lamp could reach the writing surface, confirmed that the writing surface was level enough to matter, and then stopped himself, because he was on holiday and there was nothing to prepare.

He put the reference volume in the drawer instead.

---

The Skybound Wyrm lifted thirty minutes late, on account of a disputed crate in the cargo hold that took three dockhands and some sustained disagreement to resolve. He felt the moment in his feet first — the floor's quality changing, the deep hum of the harness chains rising to something thinner and more continuous — and then through the porthole: the dock sliding. The slow, courteous separation from solid ground.

The cabin lamp was lit. It smelled faintly of the flame oil his father kept in the dispensary cabinet at the back of the shop — not intensely, just the ghost of it, enough to be noticed. He noted it and moved on.

He sat on the padded bench beneath the porthole.

The examination record: six weeks of written assessments, three of practicals, one oral thesis defense. His thesis — *Scryproof Glamour Exploits: Theoretical Vulnerability in Long-Contact Illusion Chains* — had been called "original, if limited in practical application" by the examiner who had liked him. The one who hadn't had called it "somewhat overstated." Both verdicts appeared in the record and he had received the pin regardless, which he considered an empirical resolution.

His mother had cried. His father had said *good* in the voice that meant something longer than good — meant the scholarships and the years and all the rest of it had come down somewhere he'd hoped it would. Theron had spent three days at home pretending he wasn't enormously pleased.

He touched the pin now.

Small. A bronze oval, lens embossed at the center, the size of a coin he'd carry without thinking about. At fourteen, when an Investigator had come through his hometown to consult on a poisoning case and he'd seen one for the first time — on the woman's lapel, catching the afternoon light — he had thought: *that is the most consequential object I have ever seen.* The actual object was considerably more modest than that memory. It was also, exactly, what it was supposed to be.

*[Investigator — Field status: Unscheduled interval.]*

He couldn't tell if the System was being dry on purpose. He chose to read it as such.

The dock had gone. The city was going — rooftops, market towers, the long green stripe of the river — tilting slowly east and then dropping below the hull line, replaced by the pale flat blue of a midmorning sky that would get better once it had more time to work on itself. The harness chains were singing one clear note in the slipstream.

At the edge of the porthole, three shapes moved across the blue: wings outstretched, banking slightly starboard as they found the first thermal. Petal was on the right — recognizable by her size, which was not a navigational reference he'd expected to develop, and yet.

She banked through the air with the ease of something that had never had reason to question whether it belonged there.

Three days. No active case. Nothing to investigate, no inference chain to close. He settled his shoulder blades against the padded bench.

Petal made a banking adjustment — a small cant of the right wing, just enough to use the thermal's edge rather than fight it. He hadn't expected to be able to read her movements from this distance. He could, though. She was the one who moved like she was enjoying it.

Below the porthole, the roof of the city's market quarter was still visible, its copper weather vanes bright in the late-morning light, turning south-southwest in a wind he couldn't feel from inside the cabin. He watched until the rooftops dropped below the hull line. Then there was only sky, and the chains' continuous singing, and the three shapes holding their formation out ahead.

He had made the right choice not to become a naturalist. He would have been insufferable about drakes.

He moved his thumb off the pin, and left it there.

---

# Chapter 2 — In the Air

The drakes' rhythm came through the hull while Theron was still deciding whether to be awake — three-part pull, then held, then release — the slight irregularity consistent with what the handler had said about Mottled, who rushed the downbeat. He'd been cataloguing it for some minutes before he noticed he was doing it. His class had been active for less than a day and was apparently revising its own opinion of its usefulness.

He got up.

The cabin was small and well-considered: two portholes, a writing desk with drawer handles worn smooth by previous occupants, a wardrobe that someone with optimistic luggage would have found tight. The lamp bracket was angled exactly right for reading in bed, and the desk sat positioned for natural morning light, and someone had thought about all of this and gotten it right. He found himself approving, which was not a reaction he'd expected to have about a wardrobe.

His case slate sat on the desk where he'd left it. The System had updated while he slept.

*[Passenger manifest — 12 first class, 16 second, 44 third. Note: one first-class booking amended post-departure.]*

He read it once, set the slate in his coat pocket, and went to find coffee, because there was no body and no crime and he had promised Warden Desh, specifically and out loud at the graduation ceremony, that he would manage a holiday without treating routine administrative notes as leads. The promise had seemed more achievable on the ground.

In the corridor, Calden was mid-round, moving with the particular efficiency of gondola staff who had done this route many times — the kind of competence that looked like nothing until you tried to replicate it. He produced a folded towel from somewhere in the same motion as his nod.

"First-class breakfast is served through the forward arch, sir. Calm air this morning."

"Mottled's rushing the downbeat," Theron said.

A beat. "Wisp's been adjusting the harness rigging since first watch." The tone of a man who had answered the same question twice already this morning.

"She holds?"

"She always holds, sir." Said with the absolute certainty of someone who had made that particular promise on every previous flight and been right each time. Theron found it genuinely reassuring, which surprised him. He asked directions to the observation deck.

---

The exterior promenade ran along the gondola's starboard side — a narrow, railed walkway with a long view down to a geography that had resolved from specific to abstract sometime in the night. A river was occurring below. Hills were present in a general direction. The whole of it had the particular distance of altitude, where cities became suggestions of themselves and roads disappeared into the colour of the land.

The drakes were off the starboard bow, pulling in their spread triangle, the harness lines running back to the gondola's nose in long tapered curves. Mottled was on the left — grey-brown, slightly larger — and there it was: the downbeat coming in a fraction early, the wing folding before the full extension. Gravel and Petal adjusted to match her. The correction rippled backward through the lines and emerged at the gondola's nose as a faint, periodic creak.

The equipment hatch at the far end of the promenade opened. Wisp appeared with a tension gauge and looked at the formation, then at Theron.

"She's doing it again," Theron said.

"She's been doing it since Halverstow run last spring." Wisp moved to the railing and made a small adjustment to the gauge's reading wire. "She thinks Gravel corners too wide on the upswing. Takes it personally."

"Does he?"

"He's technically correct." Wisp didn't look away from the formation. "She's also right that his technique is lazy. I've been saying the same thing for two years. She acts like she's the first one to notice." He made a second small notation. "Petal doesn't comment. Petal is a professional."

"Do they know you talk about them like this?"

"Petal knows." He said it with complete seriousness. "Mottled has a theory that I side with Gravel. That's the opposite of the truth, but she's decided it and I've given up correcting it." He capped the gauge, and for a moment they both watched Petal hold her line — steady, unhurried, the wing extension clean through its full arc. Wisp tucked the gauge under his arm. "She's been flying this route since before Mottled was hatched. The temperament comes with the miles." He said it without affection, or with the kind of affection that had been so thoroughly absorbed into observation it no longer showed as a separate thing. "Forward arch, sir, if you want the good coffee. He keeps it at the dining-room end in the mornings."

Theron went.

---

The dining room occupied the gondola's full forward width and smelled of coffee and something warm from the galley that turned out to be pastries in a small covered basket. Three tables set for four, one for two by the window. He stood in the arch long enough to sort the room — the habit had been automatic since third year — and made his provisional assignments.

Madam Esherel at the far table, working through a merchant's folio with the concentrated displeasure of someone who'd located one error and was hunting for a second. No companion. The seal on the folio's cover was a family firm, not a registered house — the device too small to read from here. The ring on her right hand was formal silver; the traveling clothes were not. She'd packed the ring specifically.

"Vannic" in the corner, chair turned to face the wall. Nothing in front of him but his plate. Not reading, not watching the room — just the porthole and whatever was beyond it. The window light fell on his breakfast at a useful angle. Practicality, then, unless the rudeness was incidental. Possibly both. He filed it under *requires more data* and moved on.

Window table: a woman he hadn't placed from the boarding. Mid-forties, academic bearing — the set of the shoulders that came from long hours in reading-room chairs. Sensible shoes. One piece of interesting jewelry, a puzzle ring with three interlocking bands. She was reading a book with no title on the spine and had let her coffee go cold.

She looked up when he crossed the threshold. Looked at his coat. Looked at his pin.

"Investigator." Said exactly, not unkindly. "Fresh posting. Vellaine. Juridical theory, the Larent Institute."

He sat down across from her — four tables, three occupied, the alternatives being Esherel's displeasure or Vannic's back. "Theron Mavik. The pin gives me away?"

"You keep it straight." She returned to the book. "Give it a year."

He looked down. The pin was exactly level. He did not touch it.

The coffee arrived in a small copper pot and was, in fact, excellent. He poured and looked at the room properly — trained observation, left to right, floor to ceiling, everyone given a working category.

Esherel's folio was a recent printing; the binding was still tight. The error she kept returning to was near the front — page two or three, based on how often she flipped back. Business dispute, something specific, and not minor or she wouldn't have the folio out before the breakfast dishes were cleared. The formal ring and the informal clothes said she'd dressed for the journey and packed for the meeting at the other end.

Vellaine across from him: the book's lack of title meant proof copy or personal loan. Her coffee had been cold for some minutes — she hadn't reached for it once in the time he'd been watching. A researcher's focus. The puzzle ring had an academic convention knot on the outer band, probably Larent. She'd looked at his pin before his face, which was a classifier's approach — category before individual. He liked classifiers. They were easier to talk to.

"Juridical theory," he said. "Anything I'd have come across?"

She turned a page. "Probably not at twenty-one."

"What's your symposium?"

"The Halveth Compact. Three weeks of arguments about jurisdiction in inter-regional contracts, all of which could be resolved with a careful reading of the founding charter, none of which will be." She closed the book on her thumb. "Yours?"

"Holiday."

The look at that — measuring, briefly — he couldn't read precisely. Then she went back to the book.

The System noted something quiet.

*[Investigator — Insight +1. She's read your reconstruction paper. The look was recognition, not assessment.]*

He kept his face neutral. *Of course she has,* he thought, and then: *she didn't mention it.* Which was either professional courtesy or a kind of examination he hadn't been warned about, and he genuinely couldn't tell which. He found he didn't particularly mind not knowing.

He poured more coffee and let the room settle into background.

Esherel's dispute with the wine steward materialized at second watch, while Theron was on his second pastry and reviewing his own intake notes — not on any case, there wasn't a case, just the habit of having something to do with his hands. The steward had appeared with a substitute bottle; Esherel was looking at it with the careful attention of someone comparing two documents that should match and did not.

"The Tenner Twelve was specified," she said. Not raised. Just exact. "Four weeks ago. It's in the gondola manifest."

"The substitute is of comparable vintage, Madam Esherel—"

"Comparable isn't equivalent. I'd like it noted."

The substitute was a northern blend — Theron could read the label from here. Cleaner palate, sharper finish. For someone accustomed to the southern houses, the sharpness would register as wrongness even at equal quality. She wasn't being unreasonable. He said so, aloud, before he'd decided to.

Esherel looked at him for the first time. The look was thorough.

"Investigator class," he said. "First posting. I do that."

"At least you know." She turned back to the steward. "He's correct about the finish. Note it, bring the substitute. I'll manage."

The steward withdrew. Vellaine, across from him, had not looked up from her book. The corner of her mouth had moved.

"The reconstruction paper," he said.

"I have thoughts." She turned a page. "Some of them favorable."

"I'm on holiday."

"So am I. Have another pastry."

---

He went back to his cabin after breakfast and sat at the writing desk with the case slate open.

The first-class amendment was still there. He looked at it for a moment, then pulled his intake-notes page up in front of it and wrote *day one: no active queries* in the margin, which was not a thing a person on holiday needed to write but was better than the alternative.

The Wyrm shifted slightly as Mottled corrected her stroke — he felt it through the deck as a brief lateral ease, the asymmetry resolving for a few seconds before it returned — and somewhere below the galley something metal was being prepared for the midday service. The pastries had been very good. He was on the first holiday of his adult life and there was nothing wrong with being thorough.

He closed the slate on the amendment note and left the cabin.

---

The passenger lounge occupied the gondola's midpoint — lower-ceilinged than the dining room, warmer, with a row of padded settees along the port-side portholes and a scatter of corner tables. Late morning light came in at a shallow angle and made the brass trim on the porthole frames look intentional.

The woman in the brown coat had taken the corner table nearest the window. She had papers spread in front of her — the property kind, the kind that arrived in thick envelopes and needed to be read in a specific order and still managed to tell you what you hadn't wanted to know. She was reading one sheet with the expression of someone who had expected it to say exactly this and had hoped to be wrong. A pencil in her left hand. Something underlined, underlined again, then left alone.

The coat, folded on the chair beside her, had chalk on the left sleeve and what looked like paint at the cuff — not office work; something made by hand. He didn't have enough data to go further. Her own errand. No visible interest in the other passengers.

She glanced up when he crossed through — the brief automatic survey of anyone making note of arrivals — and he nodded. She registered him without particular attention and looked back at the letter.

He passed through to the reading alcove at the stern, took the window bench, and stretched his legs out. The landscape below was doing its slow thing: a city resolving from a general impression of rooftops into something specific, then falling behind and dissolving back into the colour of the land. A river was still occurring, possibly the same one.

*[No active queries. 11 hours to Halverstow Port.]*

He'd looked at the amendment note. He'd decided not to investigate it. That felt like something Warden Desh would have called progress. He put the slate face-down beside him and watched the view.

Mottled's harness creak found him even here — the Wyrm carrying it bow to stern in the walls — and the coffee in the cup he'd brought from breakfast was still warm, and for a while nothing required anything of him at all.

---

# Chapter 3 — The Manifest

The case slate had been face-down on the bench for a little over an hour when Theron picked it up again, and he was aware, picking it up, that the hour had not been a rest.

He'd told himself it was. He had read three pages of a novel about a clockwork-maker in a port city that wasn't doing anything wrong but also wasn't competing with the view, and he had eaten the apple he'd taken from the dining-room sideboard, and he had even closed his eyes for what he assumed had been longer than it had been. Then his hand had reached for the slate.

The slate was for emergencies. That was the standing rule. The slate was for emergencies and any other use was a courtesy the System extended to a class user with otherwise unoccupied attention. He turned it over and brought up the full first-class manifest with a tap.

*[Passenger manifest — first class — 12 entries.]*

He read it down the column once without categorizing — that was the discipline. Just the names. Just whose holiday this was, alongside his.

Then, on the second pass, the categorizing happened without him.

The twelfth row was the amendment.

*[Booking amended post-departure: M. Tannin, first class, cabin three. Original booking name on record under sealed administrative note. Steward's discretion.]*

He read it twice. He'd read it twice yesterday.

The amendment was a name change made after the boarding manifest was finalized but before the gondola had cleared the dock. That much was new since the morning. *Sealed administrative note* meant a clerk on the ground had recorded the original entry and locked it under Calden's office, not the System's. The kind of paperwork that happened when a passenger had a reasonable expectation of privacy. A merchant moving under a trade pseudonym. A widow not wanting to be addressed by her late husband's title. An academic incognito.

There were probably six legitimate reasons.

He could think of three illegitimate ones too, and he watched himself doing it, and he made himself stop. He pulled the intake-notes page up over the manifest and wrote, underneath yesterday's *day one: no active queries*, the line *day two: still no active queries.*

He looked at the words. Then he closed the slate. Then, and this was the part that he wanted on the record even just for himself, he stood up, walked the slate to the writing desk, set it inside the desk drawer, and closed the drawer.

The drawer was not locked. The slate was within reach. The exercise was not for the slate. The exercise was for him.

He sat back down on the bench and went back to the clockwork-maker.

The clockwork-maker, it turned out, was also failing to do anything about his problems. They had something in common.

---

By late afternoon the cabin had warmed past comfortable, and Theron went looking for air.

The exterior promenade was empty when he got there — at this hour, second watch by the slow ringing in the galley wall, most of the passengers were doing whatever passengers did in the slack period before dinner. The drakes were out on long tether, half a sky from the gondola, three small wedge shapes against a high sun.

He went to the stern, where the rigging house squatted at the back of the deck like a small, opinionated shed. The door stood open. Wisp was inside, sitting on an upended crate with a coil of harness chain in her lap, working a wax-impregnated cloth along the links one by one.

"Mr. Mavik."

"How does she know who I am from the bootsteps."

"You walk like a man who's read a book about boats." Wisp didn't look up. "It's a particular gait. The crew walk like the deck is the floor. Passengers walk like the deck is a deck." She rotated the chain a half-link and went on cleaning. "Sit, if you're sitting."

There was a second crate. He sat.

The rigging house smelled of beeswax and harness leather and the warm-iron smell of chain that had been working all morning. Inside the doorway, a row of canvas-covered tools hung on the wall in the order they were used — he could tell because the handles of the first three were a shade darker than the rest from grip-wear, and the fourth was almost untouched. He didn't ask. It was the kind of detail that belonged to Wisp.

"She's holding her downbeat now," he said.

"She's pretending. She'll go back to it after dinner when she thinks I've stopped watching." Wisp moved to the next link. "Mottled does her best work when she believes she's getting away with something. I've found it's easier to let her believe it."

"That seems like a generous theory of drake."

"It's the only theory of drake I've got that works." She held a link up to the light, turned it, set it back in the lap. "Gravel. Gravel is the opposite. Gravel does his best work when he's being watched and his worst when he isn't. I park a junior crewman by his harness pin on long runs and tell the crewman it's a safety post. It isn't. It's so Gravel performs."

"And Petal."

"Petal does her work." Wisp said it the way people said *the sun rises in the east*. "She doesn't need a theory. She has a job and she has weather and that's enough for her."

"You like Petal best."

"I like Petal *most peacefully*. I don't have favorites. Don't write that down."

"I'm not writing it down."

"You have the slate in your jacket."

He had the slate in his jacket. He'd forgotten putting it there. He must have collected it from the desk drawer on the way out without thinking. The hand had done it; the head had not been consulted.

"It's not for notes," he said. "Today."

"All right." She did not look up. Then, after a few seconds of the cloth working: "What did you study?"

"Investigation. Theory mostly."

"What's an investigator do on a holiday?"

"Mine is going to find out."

Wisp made a small noise that might have been a laugh and might have been the rag catching on a link. The chain moved through her hands one piece at a time. Outside the rigging house door, the drakes had begun their slow turn back toward the gondola; he could see Petal in profile, banking with the unconcerned ease of something that had not had its competence questioned by anyone and never would.

"How do they sleep," he said. "In flight."

"They don't. Not the way you mean." Wisp set the chain aside and stood, brushing wax off her apron. "Two of them sleep at a time, in the harness, on the long tether. One stays up. They rotate by light, mostly. Petal goes second watch and last. Mottled won't sleep unless Gravel is the one awake, which is funny because she hates him. Gravel sleeps anytime. He's not a complicated drake."

"And you?"

"I sleep when the harness tells me to." She pulled the door wider with her shoulder. "Tonight I'll be up at fourth, that's when the long thermals come off the Brindle Range and the lines want re-tensioning. Come and watch if you can't sleep. People who can't sleep are useful at fourth watch."

"I'm going to sleep."

"All right." She didn't argue. "Then dinner's in an hour, sir. Cook's done a pie. He's only done it once before, which is why he's doing it again."

He left her there with the chain.

On the walk back to the cabin, the slate stayed where he'd put it, in the inside breast pocket of his jacket. He didn't take it out. The promenade had taken on the early-evening color that meant the sun was past its working hours, and the harness chains were singing a slightly different note in the slipstream — lower, fuller, the way a string settled into a long sustain after the bow had stopped pressing.

He put a hand on the railing. The brass had gone warm in the sun and the warmth was still there.

He stood for a minute, doing nothing about it.

---

Dinner was a quieter assembly than breakfast.

The family of four had taken the long table and were working through what looked like a serious negotiation about whose turn it was to sit by the window. Esherel was in her corner again, the folio nowhere visible, which Theron read as: she had finished what she came to finish, or shelved it. The two scholars were leaning toward each other across their soup with the focused intimacy of an argument that had moved into its productive phase. Vellaine was at the small window table, her book gone and a glass of water in front of her instead, watching the dusk arrive over the Brindles like someone in no particular hurry to leave the chair.

"Vannic" came in late.

He sat at the same place he had at breakfast, corner, back to the room, plate-and-porthole, and ate steadily and without expression, and then before the dessert course had reached anyone else he was up again and gone. Calden, refilling water at the next table, did not appear surprised. The man's preferences had been registered and would be accommodated, and the dining room continued its quiet conversation around the small absence.

Vellaine looked over when Theron came in. She did not invite him; she did not have to.

He sat down across from her. She watched the dusk for another moment and then said, without turning, "Have you done anything reckless yet."

"I closed a drawer."

"That's progress."

The cook's pie was lamb and root vegetables in a crust the color of an autumn field, and it was, the cook had been correct to want to try again, very good. They ate without much conversation. Vellaine read three more pages of the untitled book between courses, and at one point looked up to point a finger at a passing carafe and have it brought to her without anyone needing to interpret the gesture. The room was, for an hour, the kind of room that did not require its occupants to perform.

When the lamps in the dining room were lit, the windows turned black and the gondola became a brighter, smaller place than it had been all day. The drakes were invisible now beyond the glass except as occasional silver flashes when the running lights caught them.

"I'm going to bed," he said, eventually.

Vellaine turned a page. "It's not even third watch."

"I know."

"Then you'll lie there and think."

"I might. I'm going to try not to."

She closed the book on her thumb again, that small bookmark she didn't seem to need a strip of paper for, and looked at him properly for what might have been the first time since the morning's first words about his pin. "You should know," she said, "that nobody ever holds their pin straight in the third week. Either it slips, or you stop touching it. Either is fine."

"Which was yours."

"That's a question you ask in the second year. Have a good night, Mr. Mavik."

He took the long way back to his cabin, past the lounge, which was empty now, the brown-coat woman gone, her papers gone with her, the corner table reset for breakfast, and up the corridor. Calden was at his station, writing something in the steward's book. He looked up.

"Good evening, sir."

"Good evening."

"Drakes are settling. The crossing should be a smooth one tonight."

"Wisp said something about fourth watch."

"She always does." Calden returned to the book. "If you want a cup of something brought up, sir, the kettle's on at first and fourth watch. Pull the bell."

"Thank you."

He let himself into his cabin and closed the door.

The lamp went on at the first turn of the wick. He hung his coat. He took the slate out of the breast pocket and laid it on the writing desk — face-down, not in the drawer this time, because the drawer business had been the afternoon's exercise and he had passed it. He undid the collar of his shirt. He sat on the edge of the bed.

He picked the slate up. He turned it over.

He looked at it for a count he didn't keep, and then he set it face-down again.

*[Investigator — Off duty. Sleep well.]*

He couldn't tell, again, whether the System was being dry on purpose. He chose, again, to read it as such. He found that he was smiling at the slate and that he didn't particularly mind being seen doing it.

He turned the lamp down.

In the dark, the harness chains' song was a softer, slower thing, settling into the long line of the night flight. He could feel Mottled's downbeat through the deck, early, faithful to her temperament, and Gravel's correction half a breath after it, and Petal underneath both of them, doing her work.

He turned over once and slept.

---

# Chapter 4 — The Locked Cabin

Theron was on his second coffee of the morning, standing at the writing desk in his shirtsleeves, when he heard Calden knock the third time.

The first two knocks he had registered without registering. They were the small percussion of the corridor coming awake, doors opening to take towels and refuse hot water, doors closing on people who had decided to sleep an hour past the dragon-shift. Calden moved through that work in a known rhythm. Theron had been on the Wyrm two days and his class had already filed the rhythm without consulting him.

The third knock was different.

It came at the same volume as the first two. That was what was wrong with it. The first two knocks had been the unhurried *good-morning-sir* of a man who expected an answer in eight seconds and would, if no answer came, knock once more before assuming a sleeping passenger. The third was that second knock. There hadn't been a fourth. Calden was standing at the door.

Theron put his cup down. He noted, with a small private wince, that he had identified the meaning of the knock before he had thought about whether he wanted to.

He pulled his coat off the hook and went into the corridor.

Calden was four doors down, on the port side, his hand still raised. Across from him Vellaine was in her own doorway in a grey travelling robe, her book closed on her thumb. Two doors past Calden, on the same side as Theron, the woman in the brown coat had her cabin door open and her hand on the latch and was not crossing the threshold. Sable. He had her name now, from the dining-room list.

"Mr. Mavik," Calden said.

"How long?"

"Six minutes since the first round. I came back. He asked to be woken for second-watch breakfast specifically — he was firm about it last night."

"Window port?"

"Sealed from inside; they all are at first watch."

"Deck hatch above the cabin?"

"Sealed. Wisp closes them all at second bell."

Theron looked at the door. The first-class cabin doors were good carpentry. Solid panel, brass plate, an interior bolt that engaged a hooked catch in the frame. The steward's master key would turn the lock proper. It would not draw the bolt.

"Service slot," he said.

"Yes, sir."

Calden produced the master from a ring on his belt with the deliberate slowness of a man who had used it perhaps three times in fifteen years of service. The lock turned. The door moved a quarter of an inch and stopped against the bolt.

Calden's hand went to his other key. It was a thin, flat thing, dull steel, the sort of object that did not appear in any guidebook to gondola travel. He slid it through the narrow brass slot at the height of the bolt and worked his wrist twice. There was the small specific sound of metal lifting metal.

The door swung inward.

Vellaine had not moved from her doorway. Sable had not crossed her threshold. Neither of them was looking into the cabin, exactly; they were looking at the doorway itself, the way you look at a window when something has changed on the other side of it without your being able to say what.

Theron went in first.

---

The cabin was Lord Vannic's cabin in every particular he had so far been able to confirm from the corridor. Same dimensions as his own, same desk-and-wardrobe arrangement, the porthole bench under the window, the lamp bracket angled for reading in bed. The lamp was out. The porthole was sealed. The deck hatch above the wardrobe was sealed, the brass ring on the inside flush against the deck plate.

Lord Vannic was on the bed.

He was lying on his back, fully dressed for the day in a dark blue dressing coat over yesterday's shirt, the collar pinned with the small enamel knot he had worn at dinner. His shoes were off and on the floor beside the bed, square to one another, the way a man set them down when he was going to sit for a moment and put them back on. His right hand was open on the bedclothes, palm half up. His eyes were closed. His mouth was very slightly open. There was nothing in the room that did not belong to a man getting dressed for a day he had expected to have.

He was the wrong colour.

Theron had seen the colour twice before, in the licensing rotations. Not pale; the word was wrong. The colour of someone whose blood had decided, some hours ago, that it was finished with its work in the surface vessels and had withdrawn somewhere quieter. The face had the stillness of a face that was not being used.

"Calden," he said. "Step out, please. Keep the corridor as it is. Nobody in, nobody past."

"Sir."

The steward went. The door closed without latching.

Theron stood for one count of four with his hand flat on the doorframe and waited for his own pulse to come back to where it ought to be. He noted that it was higher than he wanted it to be. He noted that this was the first death of his professional life and that the man on the bed was thirty hours ago a man who had thanked the steward by name. He noted that the noting was a kind of housekeeping his class did for him whether he asked for it or not.

Then he went to work.

The bedside table first, because it was nearest and he wanted to know whether the room was going to argue with him. A water carafe, half full. A glass beside it, empty, dry to the touch. Not used. The brass pocket-watch case open and tipped on its hinge; the watch inside reading the right time for the morning, which meant it had been wound at the usual hour the night before. A small ledger-pad with a list in Lord Vannic's hand, *boots, letter, V., breakfast 2nd watch*, and a graphite pencil with the point worn down on the right side, which was the wear of a right-handed writer. There was no bottle. There were no powders. There was no glass that had held anything but air.

The wardrobe next. Three coats, two shirts, a folded cravat, a pair of dress gloves in a paper sleeve. The luggage at the bottom: the boarding cart's three cases, all of them still latched. The latches had not been opened since they were set in place. The dust seal on the brass had not been broken. Whatever had happened in this cabin had not been a search.

The desk. A travel ink in its stoppered well. A short stack of correspondence, one letter half-written in the same hand as the ledger-pad, broken off at *I have given the matter the consideration you ask for and find that the answer must be the one I gave you last—*. The sentence had not been finished. The pen was on the blotter, dry, the nib cleaned. The chair was tucked in. The desk lamp was unlit.

The porthole. Sealed at first watch, the brass ring engaged, the seal undisturbed.

The deck hatch above the wardrobe. The same.

The chain trunk in the rear wall, the narrow cased channel that carried the harness chain past the cabins and to the boom. Sealed plate, four screws, screws bedded with the small ring of dust around the heads that came from screws that had not been moved in months.

The interior bolt of the door, drawn. He went over and looked at it from inside. The bolt was a heavy brass slide that engaged a hooked catch in the frame. From the inside, you slid it across with your hand. From the outside, you could not reach it. The service slot was the only access, and the service slot was three feet to the left of the bolt and required a steward's lift-key.

He stood at the door for a moment and looked at the room from the inside the way a man inside it would have. Then he looked at the bed.

He went to the bed.

He sat on his heels beside it and did not, for now, touch the body. The dressing coat was good wool, dark blue, cut for a man with broader shoulders than most. The collar was pinned with the enamel knot, pinned correctly, evenly, by a man who had practised pinning his own collar for years. The hands were not clenched. The right hand lay open on the bedclothes, the fingers where he had already placed them in his notes from the gangway. He did not stop there. He had already filed the hand.

The face had relaxed. He looked at it long enough to be sure of what he was looking at, which was longer than he wanted to look. Then he took the small notebook from his inner pocket and began to write, because the writing was the discipline and the discipline was what he had.

*Subject — Lord Petric Vannic. Cabin twelve, deck four. Discovered second watch by C. Calden, steward. Bolt engaged from interior. Service slot used to draw. Window port, deck hatch, chain trunk: all sealed, undisturbed. No bottle, no glass used, no powder, no visible wound.*

He let his hand pause and then wrote:

*Colour — withdrawn. Stiffness — not yet. Cool to touch — to be confirmed.*

He put two fingers, very lightly, to the wrist.

Cool. Not cold. Not the cold of a body that had been on a cabin bed for eight hours in an unheated room. Closer to the cool of a body that had stopped being warm three or four hours ago. Which placed the death somewhere between the small hours of the morning and an hour before Calden's first round.

He wrote: *Three to five hours. Confirm against galley fire and Wisp's last harness walk.*

The pockets. He worked the dressing coat first, then the inner pocket where a man like Lord Vannic kept the things he did not put in his case.

Inner breast: a steel pen-case, monogrammed *P.V.* in a small, expensive script. A folded handkerchief, white, fresh, the kind a man took out of a pile and forgot he had taken. A boarding card, his own, the corner worn from being slid in and out of his pocket. The small enamel signet he wore on his little finger had been removed and was on the bedside table, set down with care, the kind of removal a man did before sleep.

Outer pocket, right: a folded letter in the same hand as the desk's half-finished one, dated the previous evening, addressed to no-one yet, an opening line that read *V — I find I have to write this twice—*. He read it. He set it back.

Outer pocket, left: a folded handkerchief, used. A small steel pen-nib in a paper sleeve. And, between the two, a small wooden fish.

It was no bigger than a knuckle. Carved from a pale wood, possibly birch, the kind a man working with a cheap pocket-knife could shape in an evening. The cuts uneven where the knife had pulled with the grain, a small notch at the tail where a younger or smaller hand had slipped. The eyes were two pin-pricks, off-centre. It had been carried in a pocket for some time; the surface had the soft sheen of a thing that lived against cloth.

He turned it over once.

*Odd personal effect*, he filed. *Note for return to estate.* It belonged with the cheap pen-nib and the used handkerchief and not with the monogrammed pen-case or the enamel knot. A noble man's pocket-corner where a noble man kept the things he could not be seen using. He set it back where he had found it, between the two pieces of folded cloth, and closed the pocket.

The trouser pockets last. A short comb. Three coins, low denomination. A blue-headed pin.

He stood up.

The cabin was the cabin he had walked. The bed was the bed it had been. He let his eyes do one more pass. Wardrobe, desk, porthole, hatch, chain trunk, bolt. The room agreed with itself. Nothing had been forced. Nothing had been opened. Nobody had come through.

He stood with his hand on the brass plate of the door and waited for his pulse to come back to where it ought to be a second time. It took longer this time.

Then the System spoke.

> *[Investigator — quest assigned.]*
>
> *Solve the Death Aboard the Skybound Wyrm.*
>
> *Locked-cabin context noted. Estimated time on station: nineteen hours to Halverstow Port.*

He read it twice and was very, very mildly pleased that his class had spoken in the tone he would have spoken in himself, which was the tone of someone who did not need to be told that there was something to be done. Then he set the pleasure aside because Lord Vannic was on the bed behind him and the pleasure could keep.

He opened the door.

---

Calden was in the corridor exactly where he had been left, his back to the cabin, his hands folded in front of him with the deliberate looseness of a man who had decided not to let his hands give him away. Vellaine had not moved from her doorway. Sable was no longer in hers; the door was open behind her, but she was now standing at the rail of the corridor with her arms folded, looking at nothing in particular.

"Calden," Theron said.

"Sir."

"He has been dead three to five hours. The cabin is sealed from the inside. The interior bolt was drawn, the porthole is sealed, the deck hatch is sealed, the chain trunk has not been opened. Nothing in the room suggests a forced entry. There is no bottle, no glass used, no visible wound. The body is on the bed."

He was speaking in the corridor because in nineteen hours every passenger on this gondola would know that Lord Vannic was dead and he would rather they knew it from a man who had been in the room than from the version of the story that would otherwise be in circulation by midday.

"I'd like the corridor closed at both ends," he went on. "I'd like the cabin locked from the outside with your master and the master held by you personally, not on the ring. I'd like the captain notified that a death has occurred and that I will speak with him within the hour. I'd like the passenger lounge opened for the first-class corridor to use, with you stationed at the saloon door for the day. I'd like the manifest brought to my cabin." A breath. "And I would be grateful for another pot of coffee, when there's a moment for it."

Calden inclined his head a fraction. "I'll see to all of it, sir."

"Thank you."

The steward went. Vellaine watched him go and then looked at Theron, and her face had the expression of a woman who had taught younger versions of him in faculty rooms and was now revising her estimate of this one upward, somewhat against her will.

"Are you all right, Mr. Mavik," she said. It was not a question.

"I'd prefer to be by lunch."

"That's the right answer."

She went back into her cabin and closed the door without making a noise about it.

Sable was still at the corridor rail. She had not looked at Theron since he had come out. She was looking at the carpet at the edge of Lord Vannic's door, in a steady way that did not announce itself as looking.

"Miss Verrin," he said.

She turned her head.

"I'll need to speak to everyone on this corridor at some point today," he said. "It does not have to be now."

"All right," she said.

She held his eye a moment longer than the exchange required. Then she went back into her cabin and closed the door, and the corridor was empty.

He pulled the brass plate of cabin twelve gently to and stood with his hand on it for a count of four.

The hum of the harness chains was coming through the deck the way it had been coming through the deck since the second day, even, three-part, the slight irregularity that was Mottled rushing her downbeat. Petal was somewhere off the bow doing what Petal did. The Wyrm was thirty hours from Halverstow Port and a man inside this cabin had thanked the steward by name on the gangway and was not going to do anything by name again.

He waited for the pulse to come back a third time.

It came.

He went to find Calden.

---

# Chapter 5 — Academy Mode

The captain's chartroom was at the top of the forward stair, behind a door with a brass plate that read *Capt. M. Joriss* and below that, in a smaller hand, *please knock before entering*. Theron knocked.

"Mr. Mavik."

The voice came through the door already placing him. He let himself in.

The chartroom was the size of a generous wardrobe and ran the long way back into the gondola's nose, two chart tables along one wall, a narrow window the captain could put his elbow in if he wanted, and the smell of pipe smoke that had not been smoked in this room but was carried in on the captain's coat. Captain Joriss was perhaps fifty, with the shoulders of a man who had spent twenty years correcting other men's lines, and the steady, unsurprised face of someone who had been told the worst possible news on three previous flights and found this one was not quite that.

"Calden has given me the shape of it," he said. "I'd like it from you."

Theron gave it to him. Cabin twelve, deck four. Interior bolt drawn. Porthole, deck hatch, chain trunk all sealed. No bottle, no glass used, no powder, no visible wound. Three to five hours dead at discovery. He gave it in the order he had written it down, not the order he had observed it, because the written order was the one the captain would carry to port.

Joriss listened without interrupting. When Theron was finished he set both hands flat on the chart table and was quiet for a moment.

"You'll want the wireless held."

"Until the last hour, if you can. A general message ahead would invite a magistrate's runner aboard at port before I'd finished. I'd rather hand them a closed case than an open one."

"You think you can close it in," the captain said, and glanced at the deck-watch clock above the door, "eighteen hours."

"I think I can build the case I'd give a magistrate's office in eighteen hours."

"That's a different sentence."

"Yes, sir."

Joriss looked at him a moment longer. Whatever he had expected of a first-posting Investigator, this was either close to it or far enough off that he had stopped measuring. He nodded once.

"You'll have your wireless silence to the last hour. I'll have Calden close the first-class corridor at both ends. The cabin stays sealed under the master, off the ring. The manifest is being brought to you." He paused. "I will need to address the passengers at noon. They've already started asking my stewards questions I would prefer my stewards not be asked."

"Noon is acceptable."

"What would you like me to say."

Theron had thought about this on the stair. "That Lord Vannic has died in his cabin. That the cabin was sealed and that the cause is not immediately apparent. That an Investigator class is aboard and is working the case. That the gondola will continue its scheduled flight to Halverstow." A small breath. "That further questions should be brought to me directly. Not to your stewards."

"Plain."

"It travels best plain, sir."

The captain almost smiled. It was the kind of almost-smile that meant a man had heard one of his own working principles repeated back to him by someone he hadn't expected to know it.

"Eighteen hours, Mr. Mavik."

"Sir."

---

His cabin was warmer than he'd left it. Calden had already been by; the second pot of coffee stood on the writing desk under a cloth, and beside it, in a flat leather wallet, the passenger manifest copied out in the steward's careful hand for the first and second classes both.

He hung his coat. He poured. He sat down.

The manifest was twenty-eight names on two sheets, and he read each sheet down the column once without categorizing. That was the discipline, the same one he had used yesterday on the smaller list. On the second pass the categorizing happened without him.

Motive against Lord Petric Vannic, taken at the surface and in working categories. He wrote them at the top of a fresh page in the small notebook and underlined them: *commercial. familial. romantic. financial. political. personal.* Then he started populating.

*Esherel, Mdm.* Commercial. The folio yesterday morning at breakfast, the wine dispute, the look on the dock when "Vannic" had crossed past her without greeting. A working merchant with an active grievance and a registered firm at risk; he had not yet found the specific shape of the dispute, but he could find it. He underlined the name once and put a small *1* beside it, meaning first to interview.

*Henner family.* Improbable but recordable. A family of four traveling to a wedding, the wife's cousin's. He could see no reason for any of them to be on the manifest for Lord Vannic's sake. He put a *5* beside the row, meaning a clearing interview rather than a working one, and moved on.

*Lerren and Stoll, university press.* Soil chemistry. He set the pencil down for a second and considered the sentence *soil chemistry as motive for the murder of a central-plains lord* and put a *5* beside both names without further comment.

*Brun, courier.* Contract delivery. He looked up the license code on the slate. The contract was for the delivery of bound proofs to a printer in Halverstow and had been let three weeks before sailing. Brun's previous twelve runs were in the registry, all clean. He put a *4* beside the name, which was *plausibly nothing but verify*.

*Verrin, S.* He looked at the entry for some seconds. The booking note still read *property*. Brown coat. Pencil in left hand. Eight days' prior booking. He could think of no version of *property paperwork as motive against Lord Vannic* that did not require a chain of coincidence he was unwilling to invent in advance of evidence. He put a *4* beside the name, the same as Brun. The category *verify, not pursue*.

Sable. He thought it deliberately, the name as the dining-room list had given it. He went on.

*Vellaine, Dr.* He paused on the entry. Juridical theory. Larent Institute. The Halveth Compact symposium, a verifiable destination booked three months prior. The look in the dining room yesterday morning when she had registered his pin. The *some of them favorable* yesterday at breakfast. She had been on the gondola two days and had asked nothing about Lord Vannic, which meant either that the man had nothing to do with her or that the man had everything to do with her and she was the kind of theorist who knew when to be quiet. He put a *3* beside the name. *Verify and ask once.*

*Tannin, M.* The post-departure amendment, cabin three, original name sealed under steward's discretion. He stopped on this row for longer than the others. A clerk on the ground had recorded an original entry and locked it. The amended row had been in the manifest since before Vannic boarded. The amendment was older than the death. That did not make it innocent. He put a *2* beside the name and underneath, in small letters, *speak to Calden re: sealed admin note.*

The second-class manifest then. Sixteen names. He worked the column the same way. Three he set aside outright — a clergy party of two and an apprentice carpenter traveling for a piece of work. Two merchants he put at *3*: one whose firm had crossed Vannic's in the registry, no current dispute on record but a history; the second whose registered cargo on the lower deck was high-value enough to want to know about. A small group of four sharing a cabin block at the stern — he wrote *check whether group travels together by booking or by reassignment*. Three solo male travellers between forty and sixty he put at *3* by default — the bracket the Conservatory had taught him filled the longest list and resolved the cleanest.

He read the page once. He put a fresh sheet over it. He copied the *1*s and the *2*s onto the fresh sheet, with one line of working note beside each. Esherel, Tannin. Two names in the top working bracket.

It was a working list. Not a conclusion. Categories, not certainties. The Conservatory's *Investigation in Closed Environments*, second year, Hadrik's seminar, had been very firm on the distinction, and Theron held to it now, conscious of the holding the way a man at a banister was conscious of holding the banister.

The System had been quiet through all of this. He waited until he had set the pencil down before he looked at the slate.

> *[Investigator — Insight +1. The shortlist is the easy work. The clearing list is the rest of it.]*

He read it twice. He couldn't tell whether his class was being instructive or dry. He chose to read it as both. He had nine hours to do the rest of it before he had any business calling the list a case.

A knock at the door. Two raps, light.

"Mr. Mavik?"

Sable's voice. He stood.

She was at the threshold with the second copy of the manifest in one hand, the cover folded open against her wrist, her thumb keeping it at the third page. Not the courier's manner. A reader's.

"Calden asked whether you wanted the second-class copy now or after lunch." She glanced past him at the desk and the underlined pages on it. "I told him I'd bring it up since I was on my way back to my cabin."

She had brought it herself. Theron registered, in the part of his head that filed corridor sounds without consulting him, that she had walked past three cabin doors and a steward's station to bring the second copy of the manifest to a man she did not yet have any working relationship with. The part of his head that filed it offered no immediate interpretation. He took the copy.

"Thank you."

"Mm."

Her thumb came off the third page. He glanced down. She had stopped on the page that listed the second-class single-men bracket, which was the page he had spent the longest on.

He looked up. She was already turning to go.

"Miss Verrin."

She turned back.

"If anything has occurred to you about yesterday," he said, "about the corridor, about the rounds, that you would want a person to have. I'm a person who would want it."

She looked at him for the count of two. Then she said, "If anything occurs to me I'll bring it."

"Thank you."

"Mm."

She went. The door pulled to behind her. He stood with the second copy in his hand and did not, for a count he didn't keep, open it.

He was filing more than he was reading. He knew it, the way a man at the banister knows his grip is too tight without quite being ready to loosen it.

Then he sat back down and opened it.

---

The first-class lounge at noon was the quietest noon it had ever been.

The captain had made his address at the forward arch ten minutes before — Theron had stood at the back of the dining room and watched it land — and the news had gone through the room the way news did, in small movements of weight from chair to chair. The wife of the Henner family had taken her two children to the porthole bench and was talking to them in the steady undertone parents used for explanations they did not entirely believe themselves. Her husband had stayed at the table with his hands flat on the wood. Lerren and Stoll had stopped arguing about their briefcase and were sitting with it between them, not opening it.

Madam Esherel was at her corner table with the folio out again, and she was, for the first time since Theron had seen her, not reading it. The folio was open. Her hand was on the page. She was looking at the porthole.

He crossed the lounge to her without hurry.

"Madam Esherel."

She looked up. The look took a moment to arrive. She had been some distance away. Then it arrived all at once, and there it was, the merchant's face he had seen yesterday over the wine.

"Mr. Mavik. Sit, if you're sitting."

He sat.

"I'll be brief." He waited a beat. "I'll need a conversation with you at some point today about Lord Vannic. Not now. I'd like the proper sit-down. I wanted you to know that the conversation is on the list."

"You wanted me to know that the conversation is on the list," she repeated.

"Yes."

She held his eye. Then she made a small sound that was either a laugh or an acknowledgment that a laugh would have been the wrong shape for the moment.

"You're being very tidy about it."

"I'd like to be."

"Was it him."

The question landed before he had quite expected it. He looked at her for a moment, then said, "I'll explain what I can when I sit with you. I can't yet."

"Was it me."

"Madam Esherel."

"I'm asking because I'd like to know how the rest of my day works, Mr. Mavik. If your answer is *that's also on the list*, that is a fine answer. I'd like it now."

"That's also on the list."

She set her hand flat over the open folio page and closed it. "Then I'll be in my cabin or here until you want me. I'd be grateful if it were soon, because I have a small business that does not run itself and I would like to know within the hour whether I am answering questions or working." She paused. "I disliked the man, Mr. Mavik. I made it known. I did not, in case it is helpful in your tidying, kill him."

"Noted."

"That isn't a yes."

"It's a noted."

She nodded once. She looked, briefly, almost amused. Then the amusement went away and she opened the folio back up and went back to looking at the porthole, the folio open under her hand and unread.

He stood.

Vellaine was at the small window table he had sat at with her the previous morning. She had her book closed in front of her and was watching the room the way she watched dusk — with no particular hurry about it.

He went over. He did not sit.

"Dr. Vellaine."

"Mr. Mavik."

"I'll want a conversation with you at some point today."

"Of course."

"Was there anything you noticed last night or this morning that you would tell a person who would want it."

She thought about that for a moment. The thinking was visible; it was the kind of thinking she did at the page when she was deciding whether a line was true.

"I heard Calden's first round at first watch," she said. "I heard his second. I did not hear his third. I noticed the third only because there wasn't a fourth." She looked up at him. "I had been awake since first watch. I was at the desk. I'm working on a paper. I heard the corridor as I'd hear it any morning, until I didn't."

"Did you hear anything from cabin twelve overnight."

"No."

"Did you hear the cabin's bolt."

She considered. "I would not have noticed the bolt. I do not know whether I would have heard it. I was at the desk with the lamp on and a particular section of a difficult argument under my hand. I was not listening for the corridor."

"Thank you."

"Mm."

He left her and crossed the lounge to the corner table by the window.

Sable had her papers spread again. The pencil was in her left hand. She had not been reading when he came in; she had been looking at the porthole, the same direction as Esherel, but with a more specifically directed look. At the harness chain where it crossed the porthole's line, perhaps, or at the angle of the light along the brass.

She looked up when his shadow reached the table.

"Mr. Mavik."

"Miss Verrin." He didn't sit. "I'm going to come and ask you formally about yesterday and last night when I've finished the corridor."

"All right."

"Is now bad."

"Now is fine," she said. "Now is when I would have rather you came."

He looked at her. She did not elaborate.

"Then I'll come back," he said, "in about an hour."

"Mm."

He turned to go. He had taken two steps before he registered her last answer, *now is when I would have rather you came*, as the kind of answer a person said when they wanted the conversation done with so they could go back to the work they were doing, which was either property paperwork or something a witness wanted off her chest. He did not turn around. He did not have the second pot of categorical thinking in him yet for whichever it was. He filed the line under *come back*, and went.

---

He passed cabin twelve on the way back to his own.

Calden was at the corridor's far end on his folding stool, his book open on his knee in the deliberate way of a steward who had been told to be visible and had elected to be useful as well. He looked up at Theron, did not move otherwise, and acknowledged him with the small inclination of head that on Calden meant *I have not left this post and will not leave this post*.

Theron set his hand flat against the brass plate of cabin twelve.

The wood was the temperature of the corridor. The master had been turned in the lock and the bolt could not be drawn, not from inside or out, because the body inside had drawn it once and would not draw it again. He held his palm against the plate a moment longer than he needed to.

He had a working list. He had a captain's silence on the wireless to the last hour. He had Esherel at *1* and a sealed administrative amendment at *2* and a clearing column to walk through before either of them. He had the method his class had been built to use, and he had eighteen hours.

He took his hand off the plate.

He went to find Calden, who had a sealed administrative note to discuss with him, and the second pot of coffee in his cabin would be cold by the time he got back to it, which was fine, because he had not poured himself the first cup yet.

> *[Investigator — Quest active. Seventeen hours, fifty-one minutes to Halverstow Port.]*

He read it. He went.

---

# Chapter 6 — Esherel, in Full

Esherel's cabin was the mirror of Theron's two decks up — same length, same single porthole — but she had moved the writing desk away from the wall by a hand's breadth and turned the chair to face the door, so that the room read as an office that had been pretending to be a cabin and had given up pretending some hours ago.

She did not stand when Calden showed him in. She did not offer the chair. She gestured at it.

"Sit."

He sat. Calden closed the door behind him with the soft click of a man who had closed many doors in his career and had developed a preference for the inaudible ones.

"Madam Esherel."

"Mr. Mavik." The folio was open on her knees and she closed it without finding her place again, which was a thing he registered. "Ask."

He had a small notebook in his left hand and a pencil at the page he had reserved for her. He had reserved a full page. He suspected the page would not be enough.

"The dispute with Lord Vannic."

"Which one."

"There were several?"

"There was one, in eight separate movements." She set the folio on the desk. "I'll save us both. He contracted me for a shipment of southern table wines, two years ago. Three hundred and twelve crates. Bonded house, registered routing, the lot. He took delivery at his cellar in the third week of harvest and refused to pay the back third on grounds of damage during transit. The damage during transit was the loss of four crates to a customs error at Ferren which was the carrier's fault and which the carrier had already settled with my house at full ledger. He was informed of this in writing. He continued to refuse the back third on the same grounds. I have spent two years writing him letters that he has spent two years not replying to."

"What did the back third come to."

"Forty-one and a half thousand crowns."

He wrote it down without comment. It was not a small number. It was not a number that killed a man.

"Why did you board this gondola, specifically."
