She looked at him. The look was patient in the way a tutor's was when a student had asked a question that had already been answered by the question before it.

"Because I had heard that he had booked it, Mr. Mavik. I had heard that he had booked it three weeks before I booked it, and I booked it, and I have spent the past six days drafting in my cabin the letter I intended to put under his cabin door once we were five hours from port and he could not avoid being on the gondola with the letter for those five hours. That letter is in my folio. It is not finished. I have rewritten the third paragraph four times. The fourth paragraph is the one that wants the work."

"May I see it."

She turned the folio around and opened it. The letter ran across three pages in a clear merchant's hand. The third paragraph was crossed out and rewritten in the margin twice. The fourth paragraph was a half-page and stopped mid-sentence.

He read it through. The letter was not a threat. It was a closing of business — a clean statement of the ledger, the dates of correspondence, the names of three competitors of Vannic's whom Esherel intended to brief on the matter at the next year's southern wine fair, and a single line near the end that he stopped at:

*I would like the back third paid because it is owed; I would like the letter answered because we are both grown people; I would like neither at this point and have written this for my own use, which is also the use of the trade.*

He looked up.

"You meant to leave this under his door."

"On the last evening. I meant for him to read it and disembark with it. I meant him to know that I had stopped writing." She closed the folio. "I am, Mr. Mavik, more sorry than I expected to be that he will not read it. I'd been looking forward to him not replying to it. I'd composed the not-replying. The not-replying was the point of the letter."

He filed her face at that. She was not performing. She was, plainly, in the room with him, and she was annoyed at the man and at the ending and at the way the ending now belonged to him rather than to her.

He turned a page in the notebook.

"Where were you between first watch and discovery."

"In this cabin. Asleep until the third round, on this bed." She gestured. "I do not sleep well at altitude. I read until first watch, I slept until the gondola did the thing it does at the second pull, I read again, I slept again. I was at the breakfast room at the open. You saw me."

"I did."

"I did not leave this cabin between first watch and the breakfast room."

"Can anyone confirm that."

"Calden saw me at the third round through the door open three inches. I called him in for a sleeping draught and declined it once he was in the room, because I had remembered that I dislike them. We had a short conversation about the gondola's previous spring run, which he had also worked. He was here for perhaps four minutes."

"What time was the third round."

"You'll have to ask him."

He wrote it. The line *Calden, third round, four minutes, sleeping draught declined* sat under her name with a small underline.

"Madam Esherel." He set the pencil down. "Thank you. I'll have one more conversation with you tomorrow, probably brief."

"Tomorrow we will be landing."

"Yes."

She nodded, once, the way she had nodded yesterday at the wine substitution.

"Mr. Mavik." She had picked up the folio again, and she stopped before opening it. "You are being very thorough. I want to say that I noticed."

"Thank you."

"It is not a compliment. It is an observation." She looked at him as a merchant looked at a ledger that had been correctly kept but on the wrong pages. "I have been on the receiving end of thorough men before. The thorough is good. It is sometimes the thorough's shape that is the problem."

He held her eye for a beat.

"I'll note it."

"Note what, Mr. Mavik."

"That you said it."

She made the small sound that was either a laugh or the acknowledgment that a laugh would have been the wrong shape, again, and then she opened the folio and went back to the fourth paragraph.

He let himself out.

---

In the corridor he stood for a moment with the notebook closed in his hand.

The page he had filled was, by his lights, an excellent page. Motive: forty-one and a half thousand crowns and two years of unanswered letters. Opportunity: a cabin two decks up and a corridor she had not, by her account, walked in the relevant window. Means: not yet established. Alibi: a four-minute conversation with the steward at the third round, which placed her in the cabin at one specific point in the night, and not at the others. Demeanor under questioning: candid, dry, unflattering to herself, which his Conservatory tutor had taught him to treat as honesty until contradicted.

He underlined the word *candid* in his head and felt the small pleasure of a category that had landed cleanly.

*[Investigator — Insight +1. The candid suspect is not the cleared suspect.]*

He read the slate. He looked at it for a second longer than he needed to. Then he closed it and went to find Calden, because the line was either his class being instructive or his class being dry, and either way the next thing on the list was the steward.

---

Calden's pantry was a narrow run of cabinetry tucked between the first-class galley and the corridor's far stair — a fold-out shelf at counter height, two stoppered carafes, a tray of cups inverted on a cloth, and the small ledger book in which the steward made the marks that organized the gondola's day. He stood at the shelf with one carafe in his hand when Theron came in and made the small inclination of head that meant *yes, sir, I have a minute, sir, and you may have it.*

"Calden."

"Mr. Mavik."

"The night before discovery." He had moved to the wall opposite the shelf to leave Calden the room he needed for the carafe. "Walk me through your rounds."

"First watch, at the bell. Corridor lamp check, the cabin board, three knocks at the cabin doors that had requested a wake. Cabin twelve had not requested a wake."

"Did you go past."

"I went past. The corridor lamp at twelve was at trim, as I had set it. I did not stop."

"Second watch."

"As first, three hours later. The lamp at twelve had been turned down two notches from its trim. That is consistent with a passenger preparing for sleep. I made a note in the book." He gestured at the book on the shelf without opening it. "I did not stop at the door. There had been no call from the cabin."

"Third watch."

"I was called to cabin sixteen at the third round, before I reached cabin twelve. Madam Esherel asked for a sleeping draught and then declined. I left her cabin at approximately two minutes past the third bell. I continued the round. The lamp at twelve had been turned to dark since the second round. That is also consistent with a passenger asleep. I did not stop. I returned to my pantry and made a note in the book."

"Fourth watch."

A small pause. "I did not make a fourth watch round, sir, because I had decided the corridor was settled and I had a small administrative matter to attend to in the second-class corridor at the fourth bell. I returned to the first-class corridor at the half. The lamp at twelve was still dark. I did not stop." The pause again. "I have asked myself whether I should have stopped. I do not, at this moment, have a way of answering that question that would be useful to you, sir."

Theron made a note.

"Calden. The administrative matter."

"Sir."

"Was it the M. Tannin amendment."

A longer pause, this one professional rather than personal.

"I would prefer, sir, to answer that question with the captain's leave. I can tell you that the matter took me approximately twelve minutes and that I was in the second-class corridor for that period. I can tell you that the matter was administrative and was unrelated to cabin twelve. I cannot, without the captain's leave, tell you more than that."

"You're saying it concerned cabin three."

"I am saying, sir, that I would prefer to answer that question with the captain's leave."

Theron noted the sentence twice in the same way and underlined the second one.

"I'll speak to the captain."

"Sir."

"Calden."

"Sir."

"The sealed amendment. Without telling me what it is. Was the passenger who paid the steward's discretion fee on the dock, or before the dock."

A beat.

"On the dock, sir."

"Thank you."

"Sir."

He let Calden return to the carafe. The steward poured without looking up. The pour was steady. The pantry smelled of the same coffee Calden had set in his cabin that morning and the faint floor-wax smell of a corridor that had been kept clean by a man who treated the cleanliness as part of his profession.

Theron stood for a moment longer than the conversation required. He could feel his page accumulating. It was a satisfying feeling.

"Thank you, Calden."

"Sir."

He went.

---

The first-class lounge at second-watch's end was no longer the quietest it had ever been; the Henner family had come back through, and one of the children had been told that yes, they could still have the small lemon biscuits if they were quiet, and the child was being conspicuously quiet in the way children were when they had been promised biscuits.

Sable was at her corner table. The property papers were spread again. The pencil was in her left hand and she had her chin in her right, and she was looking at the porthole.

He stopped at the lounge arch. He did not cross to her.

The angle of her looking was the same as it had been at noon. She was not reading the harness chain — it had moved, with the gondola's slow correction, into a different segment of the porthole's frame — but she was looking at the place where the chain had been, and where it now was, and at the brass rim of the porthole where the morning light had been catching at noon and was now catching at a flatter angle.

He had Esherel on the page. He had Calden's *with the captain's leave* on the page. He had the M. Tannin amendment for the captain at second-watch end, which was now, and which was where his afternoon went next.

He filed the lounge. He filed the chin in the right hand. He filed *Verrin, S., still at corner table, still in property papers* in the part of the notebook that was the verify column, and he turned to take the stair up to the captain's chartroom.

He had taken three steps when he registered that he had filed her without looking at her. He stopped on the stair for a half-second.

Then he went on up.

---

His cabin was the temperature of the corridor. The second pot of coffee was on the writing desk under its cloth, as it had been at noon. He had not poured the noon pot either. He poured neither now.

He opened the notebook to Esherel's page. He read the page through. He added a line at the bottom: *check the carrier ledger at Ferren, three-year window, both directions.* He had no way to check the Ferren carrier ledger from the gondola. He wrote *port* in the margin and underlined it.

He opened to a fresh page. He wrote *Calden — first-class corridor rounds — confirms quiet at twelve through third watch. Fourth-watch absence twelve minutes in second-class corridor, M. Tannin amendment, captain's leave required.* He underlined *captain's leave.* He drew a small bracketed note beside it: *amendment paid on the dock, after boarding, before lift.*

He opened to the M. Tannin page. He wrote, under the *2* he had put beside the name yesterday, the four words *paid on the dock,* and stopped.

He looked at the page. The shape of the day was on it. Esherel at the top with motive and a four-minute alibi window that did not cover the night. M. Tannin at the second tier with an amendment whose timing was now slightly more interesting than it had been yesterday. The captain's chartroom on his stair, second-watch end.

He felt the small steadying pleasure of a method working. He did not know that he was feeling it. He felt it the way he felt the harness chains through the deck — as a low constant that did not require him to think about it.

He moved his thumb off the graduation pin. He had not noticed his thumb arriving at the pin.

> *[Investigator — Quest active. Sixteen hours, twenty-two minutes to Halverstow Port.]*

He read it. He capped the pencil.

He went to find the captain.

---

# Chapter 7 — Working a Scene Together

Theron came down to the steward's station to ask the captain's leave and found the leave already in progress without him.

The pantry passage on deck three was narrower than its diagram suggested. Service brass on one side, the row of bell-cords on the other, a folded chair propped under the cord-rope and a small slate with the morning's substitutions chalked on it in Calden's neat hand. Theron had meant to step in, ask the steward whether he'd had time to consider, and proceed up to the chartroom from there. Instead he stopped at the corner of the frame.

Sable was inside the doorway. Calden was facing her, holding a folded cloth that had something heavy in it, mid-sentence. Theron caught the end of the sentence.

"—Second-class corridor, yes, ma'am, but only the run from cabin nine to the steward's station and back. I'd not have crossed past nine."

"And the door to nine?"

"Closed when I went, closed when I came back."

She nodded, once, with the small finished nod of a person who had been given a fact and was setting it down where she meant to put it. Calden adjusted the cloth in his hand. Theron had the distinct sense, standing in the corner, that he had not been registered as present.

He cleared his throat.

Calden's chin lifted. "Mr. Mavik. Sir." The tone was different. Not cold — a step back. A man closing a drawer that had been open. "I was just answering Miss Verrin's question about the fourth-watch round."

"I'd like to hear it, if it's the same one I asked yesterday."

"It is, sir. Substantially." A pause. "I'd ask leave from the captain before going into the matter of it. As I said yesterday."

"Of course."

Calden's eyes flicked once to Sable. "Ma'am," he said, and stepped past Theron with the cloth, who turned to make room. The pantry passage was barely wide enough for the courtesy.

When he was gone, Sable looked at Theron and said, "Sorry. I should have asked you first."

"You didn't ask him anything I didn't ask him."

"He answered me, though."

"Yes."

She waited. Theron registered that he was meant to say the next thing, and that he did not yet have it. The brass on the bell-cords was warm-looking in the lamp-light; the slate's chalk smelled faintly of the morning. He shifted his weight off his back foot.

"I'd like to compare," he said. "If you've an hour."

"I've an hour."

---

The dining room had emptied for the captain's noon address, except for the small window table where Vellaine had left her book face-down on the cloth and gone forward with the rest. The galley boy was clearing pastries onto a tray with the careful slowness of someone whose only instruction had been *don't drop anything.* Theron took the small table. Sable took the chair across from him, set her hands flat on the linen, and said: "What do you have?"

"The four rounds," he said. "The geometry of the cabin. Esherel's grievance, in full. The amendment under M. Tannin, paid dockside before lift. Vellaine asleep through the third bell." He thought about it. "And the steward absent twelve minutes on a matter he'll discuss only with the captain's leave."

"That's what I have, too." She angled her chin slightly. "Mostly. I have the corridor."

"The corridor."

"Where Calden was in it. Not the times — I can't help you with the times. Where his body was."

He had not heard a person say a steward's *body* in a sentence that did not mean *corpse*, and he realized in the same beat that this was not of hers. He set his slate on the cloth and turned it ninety degrees so they could both read it.

"Walk me through it."

"After we eat."

The galley boy arrived with the second-watch leftovers, which on this gondola meant a pot of something soft and brown the cook had decided was now soup, half a loaf, and the dish of small lemon biscuits that had been migrating around the first-class cabins all day. Theron poured for them both. The coffee was the last of the morning's pot and had the slight burnt edge of coffee that had stood; he registered it without minding.

She picked up her spoon. He watched her tear the bread in half and offer him the larger piece without making the offer a thing. He took it.

"Tell me what you have on him," he said.

"On Vannic."

"On Vannic."

She considered her bread. "He was very tired the first night. I don't know that you'd have caught it from where you were sitting — he was facing the wall by then. He wasn't tired the second night, by which I mean he'd slept since." She took a bite, swallowed, went on. "He said *forty-one* when he meant *forty-one and a half* about Esherel's invoice. Esherel corrected him; he didn't argue. He likes — liked — the small lemon biscuits, the ones with the seed in. He took two. He doesn't eat in front of people if he can help it. The chair-facing-the-wall thing is real."

He found he was writing. He had not consciously decided to write. He set the pen down.

"Anything that didn't fit?"

She looked at her spoon. "Nothing investigatively. Small things. He reached for a word at dinner the first night — a perfectly ordinary word, *harbor* — and the vowel came out flat. Coastal flat. He said it again the right way two words later and went on. You wouldn't correct a lord at dinner. You wouldn't even register it, really. I registered it because I was sitting on his blind side and looking at the back of his head, which is where I do my registering."

"Coastal flat."

"Sea-town vowel. Pulled forward in the mouth. Two or three regions it could be." She took another bite. "He didn't do it again. I think he was tired."

He wrote *Vannic — country house, where?* in the slate's margin and underlined it once and then read what he had written and crossed it out, because the lord had been at the academy on his uncle's grant for two terms in his teens, which his slate had already noted and which Theron had already filed, and people picked up vowels from where they lived. *Small talk*, he wrote instead, in the same place. He moved on.

"What about Calden?"

She considered. "Calden likes him."

"Liked."

"Yes."

He paused. He had been about to ask why she thought so and had heard, in his own head, the sound of the question landing wrong. He took a swallow of coffee instead. The slight burnt edge was useful; it gave him a beat.

"Tell me," he said.

"Calden thanks people who thank him by name. Not formally — internally. You can see it because he goes a half-second slower the next time he comes back. He went a half-second slower on every round past Vannic's door. He's been going a half-second slower today, too, in the corridors near the cabin. He doesn't know he's doing it."

"That's something I wouldn't have caught."

"No."

He picked up the pen and set it down again. The small concrete fact she'd put on the table — the steward's half-second — was not a piece of evidence as he was trained to assemble evidence. It was not a piece on the board. It was a piece *about* the board, and he had spent the morning moving pieces.

"You said you have the corridor," he said.

"After we eat."

She had a piece of bread halfway to her mouth and the other hand around her cup, and she was eating without performing the eating, which he noticed because he was performing his eating slightly, the way he always did with new people. He stopped performing.

"What did Calden tell you in the pantry," he said, "that he wouldn't tell me yesterday?"

"He didn't, actually." She set the cup down. "He told me what door he didn't go through, in the second-class corridor. He didn't say what the errand was. He said he'd need the captain's leave for that, same as he told you. He just answered me before he said it." She lifted one shoulder, set it down. "I think he's been waiting all morning for someone he could give the part-answer to."

"Why you, and not me."

She looked at him. The look was kind without being soft.

"Because you're going to write down what he says, sir, and I'm not."

He thought about the slate on the cloth, his hand on the pen, the small precise way he was holding both. He moved his hand to his cup. The pen stayed where it was.

"Theron," he said.

"Theron."

"Not sir."

"Noted." She picked up her spoon again. "Eat your soup before it cools, *Theron*. We have a corridor to walk."

The System, somewhere quiet in the back of his attention, noted something it had clearly been holding for a minute or two.

> *[Investigator — Insight +1. She is not your assistant.]*

He thanked it silently and did not respond. He picked up his spoon.

---

The first-class corridor on deck four was the kind of quiet that announced itself. The captain's address had ended, the passengers had returned, and the corridor was empty in the deliberate way a corridor was empty when everyone behind every door was choosing not to come out. The cabin twelve door was sealed with Calden's wax and a small folded note in Theron's own hand. The lamp at the corridor's mid-point was lit; the lamp at the end nearer the chain trunk had been dimmed.

Theron stopped at the cabin twelve door, took the geometry in once for the discipline of taking it in, and turned to her. "Show me."

She did not stop at the door. She walked past it.

"Calden's station is here." She came to a stop at the recessed alcove between cabins fourteen and fifteen, four doors down on the same side, where the small wall-mounted bell-board lived and a folded chair was set into the wall. "He stands here when he's between rounds. He can see the full length of the corridor from this point, both directions. Light to his left, dark to his right." She looked toward the dim lamp at the chain trunk end. "He's not seeing into the dim end. He's *registering* it. There's a difference."

"He told you this."

"No. He stood here while we were talking yesterday afternoon and shifted twice. He shifts when he's been here a while. He didn't think about it. Stewards are bodies in spaces. The space tells you where his body is."

He moved past her to the alcove. The wall came up at his shoulder; the bell-board hung at the height of his hand. From here, the line of doors ran clean to the chain trunk hatch — twelve was the third door from the trunk, on the left. He could see the wax seal from where he stood. He could see, too, that the alcove put a slight kink in the sightline: the doors closer to the trunk required a half-step out of the alcove to read.

"You'd come out of the alcove to look at twelve," he said.

"Once a round, yes."

"And if you didn't?"

"Then twelve isn't in your sightline at the rest position." She moved past him, walked four steps down the corridor toward the trunk, and stopped at the cabin twelve door. "But Calden would. He'd come out for every round. He told you that, didn't he? Quiet through every pass."

"Yes."

"He meant it."

He looked at the alcove from where she was standing now, and at her, and at the line between them. Calden's body in the alcove. Calden's body taking the four steps. Calden's body, in some twelve-minute window in the fourth watch, *not* taking the four steps because he wasn't in the corridor at all — he was somewhere on deck three, in a second-class run that ended at cabin nine and turned around. Theron had had the times. He had not had the body.

"He was here," Theron said. "Except for the twelve minutes."

"Yes."

"And the twelve minutes is the thing the captain has to authorize him to discuss."

"Yes."

He looked at the dim lamp at the chain trunk end. The corridor was very quiet. Somewhere, two decks down, the drakes' rhythm came through the hull — three-part pull, then held, then release — the slight irregularity that Mottled had not yet been talked out of.

"I'm going to ask the captain about the twelve minutes," he said. "Today."

"Good."

"And the M. Tannin door."

"That's yours, yes." She came back up the corridor toward him. "I'll see what Calden says next. He's not going to say anything more without the captain. But he might tell me a thing he doesn't know he's telling me. He's been doing that already, today."

"Yes."

She had stopped at the edge of the alcove. He was still inside it. The recess put them at roughly the same height — closer than they had been in the dining room, less close than they had been in the pantry. He moved out of the alcove and into the corridor proper, which gave them both back a foot of distance and gave him back the door to cabin twelve at the corner of his eye.

"Sable," he said.

"Yes."

"Why did you walk past the steward's station yesterday to bring me the manifest yourself."

A pause. Not a long one. "Because you'd filed me in your verify column," she said, "and I wanted you to know I was a person you could ask."

He felt the small mortified heat at the back of his neck that he had learned, in the academy, was generally the correct heat to feel when he had filed something wrong.

"Noted," he said.

She smiled — quick, dry, not making a thing of it. "Good. Go and find the captain."

She turned and walked the corridor back toward the staircase. He stayed in front of the alcove for a half-beat after she had gone, looking at the line her body had made across the boards — alcove, four steps, door — and then at the dim lamp, and then at the wax seal.

He left to find the captain.

---

# Chapter 8 — Two Ways of Reading a Room

Captain Joriss had given him leave for the twelve minutes in three sentences and a folded note, and Theron came down out of the chartroom with the note in his coat pocket and the small clean feeling of an authorization he had not expected to be granted in the same hour he asked for it. He took the aft stair down to deck three. The afternoon had thinned. The light through the long port-side windows was sliding into the colour it took on after the meal service had passed and before the lamps were lit, which was no colour in particular and was Theron's favourite light on the gondola so far.

He found Sable in the second-class lounge bay window, two pencils, a folded paper, and an empty cup arranged in front of her with the careful spacing of someone who had been thinking and had moved items around to keep thinking.

"He gave it," Theron said, sitting.

"Conditional?"

"On Calden's consent. Which he'll give."

"Today?"

"Today."

She nodded once and shifted the cup a quarter turn so its handle pointed away from him, which was either consideration or habit, and he decided not to ask which. The bay window was warm where the late sun had been on it; outside, the long flat west had the cleared look of a sky that had decided to be a sky for the rest of the day.

He took the folded note out, read it once for the discipline of reading it once, and put it back.

"Tell me what you have," she said.

"The captain's three sentences." He counted them off on the cloth. "First — Calden goes off-station at fourth watch only for an authorized errand on his own initiative if a passenger is in distress. Second — the captain logs the report retroactively if the steward judges the matter resolved. Third — Calden is not required to disclose the passenger's name to me, only to confirm that the errand was within the authorized class of errand."

"Class of errand."

"His phrase."

"So a passenger was in distress."

"On the fourth watch. For at least the twelve minutes Calden was off-station."

She turned this over the way he had seen her turn things over — not visibly, but in the small still moment her eyes did not move while the rest of her did, the cup adjusted, the pencil rolled an eighth of a turn. He had begun to recognize the still moment as the thing it was, which struck him as a recent and useful piece of recognition.

"Cabin," she said.

"He'll say or he won't. The captain didn't authorize the cabin."

"Mm." She set the pencil flat. "I'd ask him in the order. Was a passenger in distress. Then — was the passenger the one in cabin twelve. Don't open with twelve."

He'd had it the other way around in his head ten seconds ago. He looked at her.

"You'd start with the general, then narrow."

"He'll answer the general because the captain authorized the general." She lifted the pencil and set it down a half-inch over. "He won't answer the specific. But he'll answer the general in a way that tells you the specific, if you let him."

The cost of the move he had been about to make, the elegance of the substitution, the small irritation at having needed the substitution at all.

"Right," he said. "General first."

"Then the question you actually want to ask, which is not the cabin."

"It isn't?"

"It's the time. He has the time to the minute. He has not been asked for the minute."

He sat with that. The captain had given leave on the twelve. He had been thinking, on the way down, *push him on the cabin*. He had not been thinking *push him on the minute the twelve began*. Because — he saw it as he heard her say it — the minute the twelve began was a different number than the minute the twelve ended, and he had two different overlap windows on Esherel and Vellaine depending on which end of the twelve he set the anchor against, and the captain had not authorized the cabin but the captain had authorized the duration, and the duration had a beginning, and the beginning was free.

"I had the geometry," he said. "I didn't have the geometry."

She watched him with the small patient expression of a person waiting for someone to finish doing arithmetic in their head. When he was done she said: "You had it. You were going to ask the wrong question first."

"I was."

"Now you're not."

"Thank you."

She made a small sound that was not quite a laugh. "I'd like the coffee back, please. The galley boy will do it if you smile."

He took her cup and went and asked the galley boy, who refilled it without smiling and without being smiled at, and brought it back. She had moved the pencils together while he was gone. He set the cup down where the handle had been pointing.

---

"Tell me what *you* have," he said, which was the question he had meant to open with and had not. "On him."

"On Vannic."

"On Vannic."

She put her hand around the cup and did not drink. "I'll do it the way I do it. It won't sound like anything for a few sentences. Will you wait?"

"I'll wait."

She nodded and let her eyes go to a point somewhere on the wood of the table, which he had begun to understand was where she put her attention when she was assembling what she had seen rather than what she was about to say.

"He stood for ten seconds at the boarding rail before he walked the gangway. Most people stand four or five. He took the extra five and used them looking forward, not at the gondola — through it, at the boom. He wanted to see the harness in motion before he was inside the harness. He was nervous, or he was respectful, or it was the same thing for him." She moved her thumb on the cup's handle. "He addressed Calden by name the first time Calden told him his name and not after — which is what you do when names are useful to you, not when you are a man who has staff. The lord-voice came on after the boarding deck, not before. It was a coat he put on once he had an audience. I'm not telling you anything about who he was before he put it on. I'm only telling you he put it on."

"Right."

"He ate alone where he could. He chose the chair facing the wall not because he was rude, although he was occasionally rude, but because he found the room expensive to be in. People who find rooms expensive to be in sit facing the wall when they're allowed to. I do it. You probably do it."

"I do it."

"I noticed."

He let that sit and did not enjoy it visibly, which felt like effort. She went on.

"He didn't drink much. He drank what arrived. He didn't ask for things. The Esherel feud was something he was tolerating, not running — he wasn't sharpening it on the second night when she came at him about the invoice, he was letting her take the points. He could have answered the *forty-one* correction more cleanly than he did and he didn't bother. He took his coffee black. He left the lemon biscuits alone the first day and ate them the second. He noticed the cook the second night — not the steward, the cook, who came out to talk to Esherel about the substitute bottle — and he watched the cook walk back to the galley with an expression I'd describe as professional interest, like he was filing how a man moved through a service room. I don't know what to make of that. Possibly he liked watching competent people work. Some men do."

"Some men do."

"He was kind to the airsick child."

He waited for the next item. There was a small one-second pause, the kind a list had when an item required a sentence of its own before the next item, and then she continued.

"Second night, two tables over from us, the family with the child in the blue coat. The child had gone green between the soup and the second course. The mother was halfway out of her chair, not yet committed to leaving. Vannic was facing the wall. He could see them in the porthole reflection. He didn't turn his head. He slid one of the ginger sweets off his own tray-saucer onto the steward's tray as the steward was passing and said something I didn't hear that was probably the table number. The steward took it over. The mother didn't see who it came from. The child stopped going green about three minutes after she ate it. Not how that kind of man moves through the world. They make sure the room knows it was them, or they don't do it. He did it and didn't look up."

He registered the observation. She was building a social profile of the victim — what the man had been like in the room before he became a body in a room — and the kindness was a beat in the profile the way the wall-facing was a beat and the cook was a beat. He filed it under *Sable's read on his temperament*, made a small mental adjustment to his working sense of *who Vannic was when no one was watching*, and asked the next question.

"Was the general demeanour a temperament or a performance, do you think? Across the whole evening."

She looked at him. The look was the one she did when he had said something useful and she was choosing not to make a thing of it.

"Performance on the outside. Temperament under it. He performed being a lord, badly, on top of being a man who would do that with a ginger sweet without looking up. I don't know which one was the real one. Both of them were him."

"Both of them were him."

"Yes."

He wrote two words on the slate without looking at them and turned the slate face-down on the cloth. *Wall-facing*, he had written, and *cook*. He had not written *ginger sweet*. He noticed, in the back of his attention, that he had not written it because he had filed it already.

"I'd like to walk through the timing with you," he said. "Before I ask Calden. So I know what I'm pushing for."

"Yes."

He pulled the slate back over, turned it the right way up, and they walked it through. He drew the fourth watch as a line; he marked the cabin twelve door as a tick on the line; he marked the steward's station as a longer tick; he marked Esherel's known position from her own account and Vellaine's by inference from the cabin lamp records, and Sable watched and once — only once — took the pencil out of his hand and moved a tick three minutes earlier, and looked at him to see if he objected, and gave the pencil back. He didn't object. The three minutes were on Vellaine's lamp and not on Vellaine's wakefulness, which was a distinction he had not made because he had not had Sable's habit of asking *who would have noticed the lamp going on*.

By the time they were done the slate had a workable shape and the cup had gone cold a second time. She took it away to the galley boy herself this round.

> *[Investigator — Insight +1. Two readers of the same room give you the room.]*

He thanked it silently and turned the slate face-down again. When she came back he was on his feet.

"I'll go to him now," he said. "While the captain's note is still warm in my pocket."

"Good." She set the refilled cup down for him, not for her. "Drink half of that first."

"Why."

"Because you'll be there in two minutes and the steward will notice if you arrived breathing fast. He notices that sort of thing. So do you, on someone else. Drink half."

He drank half. It was warm and slightly burnt at the edge — the afternoon pot, kept on the warmer too long — and the burn settled the small stir of haste he hadn't quite known he had. He set the cup down.

"I'll find you after," he said.

"In the corridor. I'll be at the alcove."

"At the alcove."

"With Calden's permission, this time." A quick dry look. "I've decided to start asking."

"Noted."

She smiled at *noted* the way she had smiled at it the day before, briefly and without making a thing of it, and went back to her cup. He left her in the bay window with the pencils realigned and the slate in his coat pocket and the captain's note still in the same coat pocket, two folded papers warm against his ribs.

---

The second-class corridor on deck three was the kind of quiet that had people behind it. Late afternoon. A child laughing two cabins down and being shushed; the smell of something with onion from the galley vent at the corridor's mid-point; the wood of the passage warmer underfoot than the first-class boards because the boilers ran beneath. He walked it at the pace of a man who had been authorized and wanted to arrive at the authorization without using it up before the door.

He found, as he walked, that he was thinking about Calden as Calden rather than as *the steward*. The man had a morning behind him — Theron didn't know what, exactly; the small slate by the bell-cords would have it, the substitutions, the early-bell jobs, the routine work of a route the man had run perhaps eighty times — and the morning had ended in a pantry passage with a question from a woman who had not asked it the way Theron had asked it. The man had a half-second slowing past a door because he had been thanked by name. The man had stood off-station for twelve minutes on the fourth watch because someone in his class of authorized errands had been in distress. The man would have a face when Theron arrived. The face would be tired or it would be careful or it would be both.

He was going to ask him a difficult question. He should arrive at the question by the route a person would, not by the route a manifest would.

He had the captain's order. He did not have to use it as the first sentence.

The pantry door was open. The bell-cords were dim against the lamp-light; the slate sat where it had sat that morning, with the day's substitutions chalked in Calden's hand and one new line added below in different chalk, a smaller hand, the cook's — *no more lemon biscuits, advise the saloon* — which made Theron smile in spite of himself, because Sable had eaten the last two with her bread an hour ago.

Calden was inside, with his back half to the door, writing something on the small slate in the careful left-leaning hand Theron had begun to recognize. He turned as Theron's footfall registered.

Theron raised his hand to knock on the open frame.

> *[Investigator — Field status: door open. Ask the second question first.]*

He did not knock yet. He looked at Calden — at the chalk in his hand, at his face, which was tired and was careful and was both — and held the gesture a beat. Calden looked back. Calden was waiting to be addressed.

He let the hand come down.

"Calden," he said. "The captain's given me a note. I'd like a moment, when you can."

"Now, sir," Calden said. He set the chalk in the rail of the slate without looking down. "I'll finish this after."

Theron stood at the open door, with the second question already in his mouth and the first one waiting behind it, in the order Sable had given him.

---

# Chapter 9 — A Letter That Did Not Want Sending

He came up the aft stair with the slate in his coat pocket and the small clean feeling of a conversation that had gone where he'd asked it to go and a little further. The captain's note was still warm against his ribs. He hadn't needed to produce it. Calden had answered the second question first, in the order Sable had given him, and then the first one when the second one made it natural; and Theron had walked back out of the pantry with the start-minute of the twelve and the end-minute of the twelve and a small dry pleasure he was choosing not to examine.

He found her at the alcove on deck four, as agreed.

She was leaning her shoulder on the wall at the edge of the recess, not standing inside it, which would have read as the steward's place, but at its lip, where a passenger could plausibly be considering the view of the corridor without committing to it. The pencil was behind her ear. The slate was not visible. She turned her face when his step landed on the boards three doors out.

"Well?" she said.

"Forty-six on the fourth bell to fifty-eight on the same." He kept his voice down. The corridor was the late-afternoon quiet he had walked through to get to it. "Twelve minutes. Cabin three. The matter he won't discuss without the captain's leave is cabin three, which we knew."

"And the half-second past twelve?"

"Was on the third round. Not the fourth. He didn't get a fourth at twelve because he wasn't there." He stopped at the alcove's edge, where she was. "And he asked himself, on the way back up from cabin three, whether he should have. He has been asking himself that for two days."

"Yes."

"You knew."

"I thought so. He told me without telling me. You confirmed it." She came off the wall. "Where do you want to take it next?"

"Out of the corridor."

"Reading alcove?"

"Reading alcove."

They went down the corridor together at the pace of two people who had nowhere in particular to be and would not have agreed on the pace if they'd discussed it. He noticed, in the part of his attention that had recently learned to do this, that she walked half a step ahead of him on the inside of the corridor, closer to the cabin wall, which left him the broader pass on the porthole side. He had not been given the broader pass by a person before in his memory and could not work out, for several doors, whether he liked it or whether he was being managed. Then he decided it was probably both and stopped thinking about it.

The reading alcove at the stern was empty. The last of the day was coming through the long stern window in the flat, slightly bronzed colour the gondola's port-side glass took on an hour before the lamps were lit. He took the bench seat. She took the chair across from him, at the angle he had begun to recognize as her working angle, half toward the window, half toward him, the slate's eventual reading surface tipped slightly toward her own pencil hand.

He set the slate on the small reading table.

"Calden," he said. "First."

"Yes."

He walked it through. The captain's note had unlocked the class-of-errand framing first, the duration second; Calden had given him both ends of the twelve without ever naming cabin three out loud, and had let Theron name it, and had not corrected the naming. The amendment had been *the matter* and not *the cabin three matter*; the steward's discretion had held the line he had said it would hold. Theron had asked about the half-second slowing past cabin twelve without using the words *half-second* and Calden had said, "I went past at the usual pace, sir, on the third round. I did not go past on the fourth." Which was the same answer in a different shape, and Theron had taken it and not pressed.

Sable nodded once at the end of it.

"You opened with the general."

"I opened with the general."

"And the time."

"And the time. He gave me both numbers. He had them both. He had been carrying them around in his head and was, I think, relieved to put them down somewhere."

"Mm." She moved the pencil from behind her ear to her hand and back. "He's been carrying a lot."

"He has."

He drew the corridor on the slate with the start-minute and the end-minute marked in. He drew Esherel's third-round window across the line — the four minutes Calden had spent in her cabin declining a sleeping draught — and Vellaine's lamp record adjusted by Sable's three minutes from the day before, and then the new gap that the start-minute had opened on the early end, a free three minutes he had not known he had. He looked at it.

He had two windows now. Not one.

"That's the geometry," he said.

"Yes."

He looked at it a moment longer. The early window was the one that mattered. The late window — the one that closed on Calden's return to the first-class corridor at fifty-eight past the fourth bell — was the one he had been working with for two days, and it pinned Esherel against her own four-minute alibi cleanly enough that he had been preparing to write *opportunity confirmed* under her name and underline it. The early window was a different shape. The early window was three minutes before Calden had even left the corridor, on a watch in which Esherel had been, by her own account, asleep, and by Calden's account had not yet been seen at all.

"Two windows," he said. "I had one."

"You had the one the captain's leave gave you the easy way. The other one was always there."

"Yes."

He sat with that for the small honest beat it required. He had had it in him to ask the early question; he had not had it in him to ask the early question until she had moved one tick three minutes earlier the day before and given him the pencil back. The pencil was now in his hand and the tick was where she had put it, and he found that he had no particular wish to move it.

The galley boy passed the alcove arch with a tray of cups and a folded cloth, on his round to the cabins; Theron put his hand flat over the slate as a courtesy, although the boy did not look in. The Wyrm's hum came through the bench under him as a low constant. Mottled was settled, again, on the long approach.

"All right," he said. "Esherel."

Sable looked at him.

"I'd like to talk about Esherel," he said.

"All right."

He had the shape of his argument already in the back of his teeth — the motive on the page, the opportunity, the demeanor — and was opening his mouth when Sable said:

"I don't think she sent the letter."

He paused.

"She didn't, in fact. It's still in her folio."

"I know it's still in her folio. I mean I don't think she was going to. I don't think she ever was."

He set the pencil down on the slate the careful way he set things down when he wanted to make sure they were not in his hand for the next sentence.

"All right," he said. "Walk me."

She did not look at the slate. She looked at the bronze edge of the stern window where the long light was lying along it, which he had begun to understand was the place she put her eyes when she was assembling.

"You read the letter, you said."

"I read it."

"How many rewrites in the third paragraph."

"Four."

"And the fourth paragraph?"

"Half a page. Stopped mid-sentence."

"How long has she had the folio open in the dining room."

"Six days, by her account. Since boarding."

"Six days of writing the third paragraph four times and the fourth paragraph not at all." She turned her eyes back from the window. "That is a person who is not going to send the letter."

"It's a person who hasn't finished the letter."

"It's a person who is composing the not-finishing. There is a line in the letter — you told me the line."

"*I would like neither at this point and have written this for my own use, which is also the use of the trade.*"

"That line."

"Yes."

"That is a letter someone is writing to herself, Theron. The trade is the alibi for keeping the page open. She is sitting in her cabin and her dining room and she is rehearsing the conversation she will never have, and she is making the conversation good. She has spent six days making it good. She has not slid it under his door. She was never going to. The letter is the place she is keeping it. The man was the excuse for the letter."

He thought about this.

He thought about it the careful way he was learning to think about the things she said — which was to let the sentence land first and look for what it touched, rather than to look at the sentence and decide whether he agreed with it. The sentence touched Esherel at the breakfast table on the first morning, folio open before the dishes were cleared. It touched her at the corridor outside Vannic's cabin, where she had not, in fact, ever stood. It touched the four rewrites of the third paragraph. It touched the half-page stopped at the fourth.

It also touched, in a part of his attention he was less easily able to name, the small look she had given him in her cabin — the merchant looking at a ledger that had been correctly kept but on the wrong pages.

He picked up the pencil again.

"All right," he said. "I hear you."

"I know you hear me."

"I want to take it apart on the page, though. Not against you. With you."

"Take it apart."

He turned the slate so she could see it from where she was sitting and he wrote out, in the columns he had been writing in for two days, the three pieces of the case he had on Esherel.

"Motive," he said. "Forty-one and a half thousand crowns and two years of unanswered letters. The letters are a documented pattern. The folio is the evidence."

"Yes."

"Opportunity. She booked the gondola specifically because Vannic had booked it. She placed herself within four hours of him for the duration of the flight. She has, by her own account, no alibi for the early window on the fourth watch, which the captain's leave has just given me. She has an alibi for one point in the night — the four minutes Calden spent in her cabin at the third bell — and no alibi for the rest of it."

"Yes."

"Demeanor under questioning. Candid. Dry. Unflattering to herself when she could have flattered. My Conservatory tutor's instruction was to treat that as honesty until contradicted."

"Yes."

"And the letter," he said. "Which I read in her folio. Which I hold to be motive made visible — the long, careful, accumulated motive of a person who has spent two years being refused a thing she is owed."

"And which I hold," she said, evenly, "to be a person who has spent two years building the room she lives in alone."

He stopped.

He did not put the pencil down. He let it rest on the slate at the angle it was at.

"That's a different reading of the same letter," he said.

"It is."

"It's not on the page."

"It is on the page. It's the page you read. You filed it under *motive*. I'd file it under *what she does instead of doing the thing*."

He looked at the column on the slate where he had written, two days ago, the word *candid* and underlined it. He had drawn the underline twice. He could see the pencil bear-down through the chalk. He had been pleased with the underline at the time and was, on examination, still pleased with it.

"I think," he said, "I'd rather hold her in column one until something moves her out of it."

"All right."

"You've heard me, and I've heard you, and the case as I have it now still puts her in column one."

"All right."

"It's not that I'm dismissing what you've said." He set the pencil down properly this time, parallel to the slate's edge. "It's that the page is what I work from. The page has motive, opportunity, candor, and a letter that documents the motive at length. The shape of the letter is a thing I would have to weigh against three other things on the page that are pointing the other way. I'd be working against my own method to weigh it heavier than them."

"That's fair," she said.

"Is it."

"I think so. It's your method. I asked you to hear it. You heard it."

"You're being generous."

"I'm not."

He looked at her. She looked back at him. Her face was the face it had been three minutes ago: the working angle, the small patient set at the mouth she did when she was watching him do something he was about to do anyway and had decided to let him do it.

"You think I'm wrong," he said.

"I think you're holding her where you have her because you have her there cleanly. I'd want to know what would move her, if anything would."

"A second alibi for the early window."

"Or."

"Or material that contradicted the demeanor. Or a motive larger than hers somewhere else in the manifest."

"Or."

"Or," he said, slowly, "a reading of the letter that the rest of the page sat down to agree with."

"Yes."

"Which would be a reading the page didn't currently have."

"Yes."

"I'd want to see it on the page first."

"I know."

He sat with that. The slate sat between them. The columns on it sat the way columns sat — Esherel at the top, Tannin second, Vellaine third, the early window now drawn in beside the late one, the geometry he had walked into the alcove with intact. He had thought, on the way down from the captain's note, that the chapter of the day was Calden. The chapter had widened by one room. The page had not.

"Right," he said. "On the page."

She picked up her cup, which had been empty since before he had sat down, and turned it a quarter so the handle pointed away from him. It was the same quarter-turn she had done in the bay window the day before. He had filed it then under *consideration or habit*. He had not, since, decided which it was, and he found, in the part of his attention that was not occupied with the slate, that he was still not deciding.

The light at the stern window had thinned by another notch. The galley boy passed back the other way with the tray emptier and the cloth folded smaller. Somewhere two decks below, the cook was working on whatever the saloon was going to be told, with the small assurance of certainty, was the second-watch dinner.

> *[Investigator — Insight +1. A page is a tool, not the room.]*

He read it once. He did not flinch at it and did not enjoy it, which felt, on inspection, like effort. He set the slate face-down on the reading table.

"I'll keep working the page," he said.

"Do."

"And I'll think about the letter."

"You will think about it as a thing you've considered and set aside, which is a form of thinking about it. That's all right."

"It's all right."

"It is."

"Are you teasing me."

"A little." She set the cup down. "Not unkindly. Go and write up Calden. The two windows. The cabin-three confirmation by inference. Sleep on it. You'll bring better questions in the morning than you'd bring tonight."

"And you."

"I'm going to go and have a small conversation with the second-class cook, because I have an excuse to and because he has been letting Calden eat standing up for two days in a row and someone should mention it."

"Is that an investigation, that conversation."

"It's a kindness with my eyes open." She got up. "There's a difference. Most days."

"Noted."

She smiled at *noted*, briefly, the way she always did, and then she did the thing she had done at the alcove an hour ago, which was to lean for a half-second on the back of the chair before she walked away from it — a small still moment that was not a posture, only the pause a person made when they had decided to leave and were giving the room one count before they did. Then she went.

He sat with the slate face-down a moment longer.

He turned it over. He read the columns. Esherel at the top, with the underline twice on *candid*; Tannin second, with *paid on the dock* in the margin; Vellaine third, with the three-minute lamp adjustment in Sable's hand. The new window drawn beside the old one. The motive, the opportunity, the demeanor, the letter.

He did not move the underline.

He thought about the half-page stopped mid-sentence and decided that he had thought about it. He capped the pencil. He picked up the slate.

The corridor on the way back up to his cabin was lamp-lit now, the lamps coming on in their order as the last of the day pulled away from the port windows. He passed Calden's pantry on the way and did not look in. He passed cabin twelve and made the small registration he had been making for two days of the wax seal and the folded note in his own hand. He went up the stair. He let himself into his own cabin and put the slate on the writing desk.

He moved his thumb off the graduation pin. He had not noticed his thumb arriving at the pin.

He sat down to write up Calden.

---

# Chapter 10 — The Cleanest Page

He woke at the bell and at the place his mind had been waiting on overnight, which was the underline on *candid* and the question of whether he had been pleased with it for the right reasons.

He sat at the desk with the coffee Calden had left under its cloth and read the page through. Motive. Opportunity. Demeanor. The letter, sitting where Sable had set it next to the other three and refused to make smaller than they were. The early window beside the late one, unaccounted for in Esherel's column on both sides of the gap. He had slept the way Sable had told him he would sleep, which was the kind of sleep a person had when a conversation had finished without anyone being the one who'd had to give. He found, on inspection, that he was still pleased with the underline.

He capped the pencil and put the slate in his coat pocket and went out to walk.

The first-class corridor at the early bell had the lamp-trimmed quiet of a space staff had passed through and not yet returned to. Calden was somewhere two decks down. The cabin board at the stair had the small chalk tick beside fourteen that meant *occupant up,* drawn in by Calden some minutes before Theron had risen. Calden saw a lot of risings.

He went down the corridor at the pace he walked when he was thinking around a problem rather than at it, which was slower than his usual and harder on his thumb. He passed cabin twelve and made the registration he had made eleven times now of the wax seal and the folded note. He passed the place where the corridor's slight kink put the porthole rail half a step into the floor plan. He stopped at it because the geometry had become a habit of stopping, and looked down the long line of doors.

The memory surfaced unbidden, which was a useful word for it because he hadn't bid for it.

*Boarding morning. The gangway threshold. Vannic stopped for an elderly passenger finishing the step down. Not with any performance — not the wide gesture or the produced smile — just a pause, the three seconds required, and then the step up. No one had been watching except Theron, who had been looking at the lock on his cabin key, and Esherel, whose attention had been elsewhere. Vannic had not looked at the man.*

He had filed it that morning under *manners that did not announce themselves.* He had filed it again, a week later, in the part of his attention that catalogued the lord-shaped behavior of a man with a working mage's calluses on the side of his index finger, and had decided then that the courtesy was *idle*, in the technical sense — the small unspent thing a man with too much room did instinctively, the way a tall man ducked. A lord who practices seriously, he had written in the early margin. A lord who practices seriously will also have the unspent space.

He looked at the corridor. The corridor offered no further information.

He went on.

---

The dining room at the open of the breakfast was warmer than the corridor and louder than it had been any morning of the run so far, which was the thing he noticed before he noticed what was making it loud.

The wine steward stood at Esherel's table with both hands at his sides and the careful neutral face of a man who had been asked a question he did not yet know how to answer. Esherel had not stood. The folio was open on the table at a page he recognized from yesterday by the bend of its corner. Her finger was on a line of figures halfway down.

"Crates," she said. Not raised. Exact. "Two hundred and seven, by your manifest. Two hundred and eleven, by the carrier's mark in the customhouse at Veller. The discrepancy is in the gondola's hold at this moment. I am not asking you to find it. I am asking you to confirm to me that the manifest copy in this folio is the manifest copy in your office."

"Madam Esherel—"

"It is a yes or a no."

The Henners, two tables over, had stopped talking. Vellaine had not looked up from her book. The second-class passenger up to fetch coffee, a tall man Theron had registered yesterday by his patience at the urn, had stopped at the urn with the cup not yet poured.

Esherel stood.

She did not raise her voice. She raised her body, which on Esherel was the same instrument tuned a half-step higher. "You sent the substitute Tenner on the first morning. You will recall that I asked you to note the substitution. I noted it as well. We are now four mornings past that note, and I have spent those four mornings looking through the folio at the night manifest as it travelled with me from Veller, and I am sitting at this table holding a four-crate discrepancy that I will, if I leave the table, have to write to your bonded house about with the formal phrase that means *I am no longer prepared to accept errors of this kind.* I will write that letter from Halverstow. Lord Vannic is dead in cabin twelve and I am sorry he is dead and I am sorrier that I am about to lose the gondola's wine ledger on the same run because there is a fairness to having both losses count and the fairness will not survive the gondola landing without a manifest match."

She stopped.

The room was quiet.

"Yes or no," she said.

"Madam Esherel." The steward inclined his head a half-degree. "I will check. With your leave."

"Check."

He went. The room did not, immediately, return to its noise. Esherel sat back down and opened the folio at a different page and did not, that Theron could see, read it.

He had not moved. He was at the dining-room arch with his coffee cup still in his hand from the corridor outside, which he had brought because it was warmer than the cabin's. He felt the small clean sensation of a category landing on the page.

*Demeanor under stress.*

He stepped into the room, took the chair Vellaine did not look up to acknowledge, and poured himself a second cup he did not need.

"She's having," Vellaine said, "a morning."

"She's having a four-crate morning."

"The crate is real." She turned a page. "I checked when she said *two hundred and seven* on the third syllable."

"You can read a manifest at six tables' distance."

"I can read Esherel's finger at six tables' distance." She closed the book on her thumb. "There is a thing I would like to say to you which I will say only once."

He looked at her.

"That woman is angry," Vellaine said, "and she is correct. Those are not always the same thing in the same body. I have seen the combination twice in my professional life, and I have been wrong about it once, and I will tell you that the wrongness cost me. Be careful with the cleanest column."

She opened the book again.

He drank the coffee he did not need.

---

The captain's gallery ran across the gondola's bow above the chartroom, a narrow railed walk with a window the full width of the hull and the small bench Joriss had told him at second-watch end that he was welcome to use if he didn't sit on the brass. He sat at the end of the bench that was not the brass, with the slate flat on his knee, and worked the page.

He had not written *opportunity confirmed* yet. He wrote it now, in pencil, in a careful hand, and looked at it.

The early window was three minutes in which she had been, by her own account, asleep, on a bed Calden had not seen her on at that minute. The late window was eight minutes in which she had not, by her own account, left her cabin, and which Calden had not been in the corridor to verify. The combined gap was sufficient for the corridor she would have had to walk, the door she would have had to enter, and the cabin she would have had to leave again on a half-step out to read.

He drew the corridor on the slate from memory. He drew the door of cabin twelve and the half-step pass-off that Sable had walked with him the night they'd mapped the upper-deck geometry, which had given the corridor its small clean shape. The line through the columns was uninterrupted. The early window covered the approach. The late window covered the return.

He sat with that.

Motive, on the page, at forty-one and a half thousand crowns and two years of unanswered letters and a folio of drafts. Opportunity, on the page, in two windows in series with a corridor's geometry that admitted her. Demeanor under stress, on the page, in a dining room twenty minutes earlier, in a voice that had not been raised but in a body that had stood; in a four-crate discrepancy that was real and that she had nonetheless escalated in front of three families and a second-class coffee fetcher and a juridical theorist who had read his paper.

Sable's reading sat in the column beside, in the small careful hand she did not have but which his own hand made for her sentences when he wrote them down. *Composing the not-replying. The room she lives in alone. Six days, four rewrites, half a page stopped mid-sentence.*

He looked at both columns. He became, briefly and with no productive result, aware of how much he wanted the underline to be there already.

> *[Investigator — Insight +1. The cleanest page is the page you didn't write yet.]*

He read it twice. He thought about flinching at it and chose not to.

He capped the pencil. He had not written the conclusion. He had built the page that would carry the conclusion. There was a difference, in his training, between the page and the underline at the bottom of it, and he was not yet at the underline.

He went to find Sable, who would either approve of the discipline or tease him about it. Either would be useful.

---

The stern alcove had been hers in the late afternoon for three days running, in the bronzed light he had begun to recognize as the gondola's port-side hour. She was at the window bench when he came in, with the property papers folded smaller than they had been at any previous sitting and her cup at her elbow at the angle that meant she had drunk from it recently and had not yet decided whether to drink from it again.

He took the chair at her working angle.

"How was it," she said.

"How was what."

"The four-crate morning."

"You heard?"

"The galley boy heard. The galley boy tells me things he isn't supposed to tell anyone because I asked the cook about Calden eating standing up, and the cook has decided that I am a person who hears about the second-class corridor's gossip in exchange for the information getting passed back through Calden by way of a clean corridor. There is a small economy. I am being paid in galley reports."

"She stood up."

"She stood up." Sable did not phrase it as a question.

"She did not raise her voice. She raised the body. The room heard her."

"Yes."

"The four crates are real."

"Vellaine clocked them at six tables."

"Vellaine told me to be careful with the cleanest column." He set the slate on the small reading table face-down. "I am, for the record, being careful."

"Good."

"I have not written the conclusion."

"Good."

"I have built the page that would carry it."

"Also good."

"You're not going to push back."

"On the page-building? No. The page-building is the right shape of the next two hours. Push back at the page-building and the underline at the bottom comes faster, not slower." She turned the cup a quarter so the handle pointed away from him. "Push back at the underline when the underline is on the slate. Not before."

He sat with that. The Wyrm carried its low constant through the bench. Mottled was still settled, three days into it now; the slight ease in the deck plates was the rhythm's working rhythm, no longer a correction. Wisp had been out at the bow at first watch with the gauge and was, presumably, asleep somewhere now.

"The morning has another thing in it," he said.

"What."

"Nothing useful. The kind of thing you put into the part of the page where you don't write anything down."

"All right."

"Vannic — the *I have been thinking about him in the past tense* Vannic — stepped aside in the corridor on the boarding morning for an old man finishing the step at the gangway. I had filed it at the time as a noble's idle courtesy. It came back to me in the corridor at the early bell because I was walking past the place where I'd seen it."

"Mm."

"I have refiled it under the same heading and decided it is still not on the page."

"That's fine."

She drank from the cup. She set it down. The galley boy passed the alcove arch with the tray he was now passing every afternoon at this hour, and Sable lifted a hand at him in the way that meant *not yet, thank you,* and he went on.

"He wasn't as bad as I expected," she said, half-shrug, looking at the window.

"Vannic?"

"Vannic. I don't know what I'd expected exactly. Whatever it was, he wasn't it."

"He insulted Esherel within four minutes of the gangway."

"Yes."

"He raised his voice at Calden on the first afternoon over a wardrobe latch."

"Yes."

"He's the kind of man Esherel's letter is about."

"He is."

She did not say anything else.

He held the slate's edge with his thumb on the corner where the chalk had worn the wood smoother than the rest. He was, in a part of his attention he was not at this moment giving an audit to, aware of the small still moment Sable had a way of making when she had said a thing she did not intend to expand on. He had registered the moment three times now over the run and had filed it the first two times under *consideration or habit* and was filing it the third time the same way, because he had no better column to put it in.

The line was, he thought, a thing she had said about a man whose body had been in cabin twelve for two days. People said small generous things about the recently dead. The room she lived in alone was Esherel's room. The room Sable lived in was, by all the evidence on the page so far, a room he had not been invited to look at and did not need to look at to do the work of the next two hours.

He filed *Vannic wasn't as bad as I expected* under *small talk between two people who have been thinking about a man,* and turned the slate over.

"All right," he said. "Esherel."

"All right."

"I'm going to walk it for you the way I walked it for myself this morning, and you can tell me whether I've left anything out, and then I'm going to take it up to my cabin and not write the underline."

"That is the right shape of the next two hours."

"You said."

"I'm saying it again."

He walked it. Motive. Opportunity. Demeanor under stress. The four-crate scene at the breakfast and the small clean way the body had risen in a voice that had not. The early window and the late window in series. The corridor geometry. The letter as one weight on one side of a beam that had three weights on the other. He kept Sable's reading in the column it sat in. He did not collapse it. He named it as the reading he was holding against, and he named, in plain words, the three things he was holding it against.

She listened. She did not interrupt. At one point she turned the cup another quarter and brought the handle back to where it had been, which he had begun to understand was her version of his thumb on the worn corner of the slate.

When he finished, she said, "And the underline."

"At my cabin. After dinner. Or in the morning."

"In the morning."

"Probably in the morning."

"Good. The morning page is the page that has slept."

"You said that on the night we walked the corridor."

"I'll keep saying it."

He almost smiled. He did not, but the not-smiling was the kind of not-smiling that the room got the use of.

---

He took the slate up to his cabin at the close of the bronzed hour and put it on the writing desk.

The page was the page he had built in the gallery. Esherel at the top. Tannin at the second tier. Vellaine at the third. The two windows in series. The corridor in pencil. The four-crate scene noted under *demeanor under stress* with a small clean tick. Sable's reading in the column beside, named honestly, weighed honestly, held against three other things.

He did not write the underline.

He thought about it. He sat with the pencil capped in his hand for the length of time it took the lamp oil to warm the way it warmed once the wick had been at trim for a few minutes. He decided that the morning was the right shape and that the morning page was the page that had slept. He put the pencil flat across the slate at the angle Sable put hers at, parallel to the top edge.

The cabin smelled faintly of the flame oil from his father's dispensary, the way it had on the first night. He had stopped being surprised by the smell sometime around the third evening. He was, on examination, comfortable.

> *[Investigator — Quest active. Eleven hours, four minutes to Halverstow Port.]*

He read it. He moved his thumb off the graduation pin. He had not noticed his thumb arriving at the pin.

He left the slate on the desk and went to find dinner.

---

# Chapter 11 — Three Drakes in Formation

He was on the starboard promenade at the open of late morning because the page in his pocket had begun to want air, which was not a thing the page knew how to want but was, in his experience, a thing he knew how to translate for it.

Wisp was at the bow end of the railing with the gauge.

"She's stopped," he said, by way of arriving.

"Three days." Wisp did not look up. "I want to enjoy it for at least two more before I tell her."

"You're not telling her."

"I told her this morning. She is choosing not to register it. She is showing me by holding the downbeat at the count for a third day that she has decided I never said anything to her about rushing it. We are in a small period of mutual professional fiction."

"And Petal."

"Petal," Wisp said, with the small pleased authority of someone reporting on a favourite student, "has a new line on the upswing. Watch her hand the slot to Gravel on the return. The slot is two breaths wider than it needs to be. She is teaching him to take a wider corner."

Theron watched. The three of them were off the starboard bow, drawn forward against the rigging, the slot Wisp had named opening and closing between Petal's wing tip and Gravel's shoulder on the bank. Wider than was needed. By two breaths, if you were counting breaths.

"Does he know."

"He is choosing not to register it. He is showing her that he is taking a wider corner because he has decided to. We are in a small period of mutual professional fiction at the harness end of the chain also."

"Two fictions."

"Three." Wisp made a small notation. "The third is between me and the long bolt on the port runner about whether it loosened a quarter turn since yesterday's trim. The bolt has decided it did not. I have decided to check at second watch."

Theron stood at the brass rail with his hand on it and felt the slipstream slip the cold across his knuckles. The chain ran ten feet from his head in its long curving line out to the boom, the harness's note carrying back along it the way a struck wire carried back along a length — the same constant low hum he had felt through every wall and bench on the gondola since boarding.

Underneath the main note, today, there was another one.

It was, he thought, a faintly off note. A fraction below the main. A thin secondary hum, carried along the chain.

He did not say so.

"You're listening for something," Wisp said.

"I'm listening to it."

"All right."

Wisp made another notation. The slot opened in the formation again, the same wider two breaths. Petal banked the inside and let Gravel come through it with the air she had freed for him. Gravel took the air. He did not acknowledge.

"Petal's a professional," Theron said.

"She is. Mottled has a theory I side with her. That is the opposite of the truth. I am siding with Gravel. He is not getting it. I am giving him time." Wisp capped the gauge. "Long bolt at second watch. I'll be at the boom if you want to bring the slate by."

He had not said anything about the slate. He had it in his coat pocket. He decided not to ask how Wisp had decided he had it, and went back along the promenade with the chain's two notes still in his ear.

---

The dining room at the open of the lunch service was warmer than the corridor and quieter than it had been at breakfast, which was its usual order for the second meal — the morning crowd thinned out, the afternoon crowd not yet up from their reading. Vellaine was at her window table with the book and the cold coffee. The Henners were at the far end with their daughter, who was eating a soft-boiled egg with the careful attention of a child who had been told it was a treat. The Wyrm carried its low constant through the deck plates.

Esherel was at her table with the folio closed.

He noticed it as he came in. The folio had been open at every meal since the first morning. It was closed now, set squarely at her right hand with one finger flat on its cover, the way she set things down when she had finished thinking about them for the present session. Her coffee was at her left. Her plate had bread on it that she had not yet broken.

He took the chair across from Vellaine, who did not look up.

"She's closed it," he said, quietly.

"She closed it on her way in." Vellaine turned a page. "I would not be at peace about that, in your position."

He had not asked her to be at peace about it. He did not say so. He poured himself a cup of the lunch pot, a different blend from the breakfast, less bright, more useful, and reached for one of the small breads at the centre of the table.

Esherel turned a quarter on her chair.

Not the whole way. The right shoulder. The face followed; the chair did not. She was looking at him across six feet of floor with the careful neutral attention of a merchant taking a new shipment, and the room — Vellaine's page-turn paused, the Henners' fork stopped above the egg, the silence at the urn where the second-class passenger up for the second pot had stopped pouring — adjusted around her in the small recalibration he had felt the room do twice now on this run.

"Investigator," she said.

His name was nowhere in the sentence. He set the bread down.

"Madam Esherel."

"I would like to ask you a question in front of the room, because I would like the room to have a record of the answer." Her voice was not raised. She had not stood. The chair had not moved. The hand on the folio had not moved. "Your case slate is out at every meal. Your line of sight at my table is a degree longer than at the Henners' or Dr. Vellaine's. The galley boy mentioned to the cook on the second morning that you took the seat at this table that has the longest sight onto my table, and the cook mentioned it to me as a courtesy. I would like to know whether you have a question you would like to put to me in the open, before the gondola lands, in the presence of witnesses. I would prefer to answer it here than in a port magistrate's office where the witnesses are paid for the writing."

The room held still.

He had a moment, in the back of his teeth, where the entire structure of the case-page he had built in the gallery — motive, two windows, demeanor under stress, the careful column, the parallel pencil — rose up and offered itself to him as a sentence he could speak. He could ask her now, in this room, about the early window. He could ask her about the late one. He could ask her about the letter in the folio she had just set her hand on.
