He could underline *opportunity* in front of the Henners and the second-class coffee fetcher and Dr. Vellaine of the Larent Institute.

He did not.

He took a breath, the small clean breath he had been taught to take in his oral defense when the question had been one he intended to answer, and not the question he had been preparing the answer to.

"I am working the page, Madam Esherel," he said. "I have not finished working it. When I have finished, I will speak to you. I would rather speak to you with the page finished than half-finished in front of the room."

She held the look a beat.

"That is a fair answer."

"Thank you."

"I do not require the room to record an answer I find fair." She turned a quarter back. The chair had still not moved. "Continue your lunch."

The Henners' fork resumed. The second-class passenger at the urn resumed his pour. Vellaine turned the page she had paused on. Theron broke the bread.

He drank half a cup of coffee that he could not, on inspection, taste, and waited for his pulse to return to the count he was usually carrying it at in a dining room. It took a count and a half.

"Hm," Vellaine said, without looking up. "She closed the folio because she knew she was going to do that."

"She knew at breakfast?"

"She knew at the third bell, I would guess. The galley boy is paid in pleasantness, not silence. The cook is paid in silence, occasionally."

"And you."

"I am paid for symposia, Investigator. I am here because the route was scenic."

He looked at her. She did not look back. The corner of her mouth had moved, the way it had moved on the first morning when he had read the wine substitute correctly.

He sat with the small clean sensation of a category landing on the page, the second one in three days.

*Demeanor under stress, escalating.*

He filed it. He drank the second half of the coffee. It was, on second pass, very good.

---

The captain's gallery in the early afternoon had the slow pleasure of the gondola's bow turning slightly into the long approach, the drakes still in their formation against a sky that had thinned to the pale clean blue of an afternoon that intended to behave. He took the bench at the end that was not the brass and put the slate flat on his knee.

He worked the page.

He drew Esherel's column with a finer hand than yesterday. He drew the corridor's geometry from the long memory of having walked it now twenty times. He drew the early window and the late window on a single line at the top of the slate and the corridor under it, and he drew, in the margin under cabin twelve in three small lines, the harness-chain's secondary note as a possibility he had not yet decided how to use.

*Carry-frame,* he wrote in the margin. He capped the pencil. He thought about it.

The textbook had been mostly interested in the carry-frame as a problem for long-distance courier glamours. The example had been *the unattended sustained illusion field*. He did not, on inspection, know what to do with that example yet. The sealed cabin had no wound, no powder, no bottle, and a body without a mark on it. A sustained field that did not need its caster's presence to continue running was a thing he had written in the margin of his notes on a Tuesday afternoon and had not had a use for since. He left the three small lines in the margin and did not connect them to anything. He was not at the underline yet.

He did not connect them to Esherel either. They sat in their margin and were patient.

He drew the lunch scene under *demeanor under stress* with a tick. He drew the four-crate scene under the same heading with a tick. He drew the dining-room arch in pencil and the merchant's table at six feet from the arch and the chair-quarter turn at one finger flat on the folio. He underlined *demeanor* once and looked at the underline, and decided he had been right to underline it, and was, on inspection, faintly pleased with himself for the careful hand.

> *[Investigator — Insight +1. A page can look like a face from far enough away.]*

He read it. He read it twice. He thought about flinching at it and chose, again, not to.

He capped the pencil. He set it parallel to the slate's top edge, the way Sable set hers. He looked at the column for the small honest beat he had begun to require of himself before he left the slate, the beat in which the page was a tool and not the room.

The page was the page. He had built it cleanly. He was, on examination, comfortable.

Below the gallery window, Petal handed Gravel the slot again. Gravel took it. He did not acknowledge.

---

Sable came up to the gallery at the open of the bronzed hour and stood at the door without coming in, which was her habit when she had come to find him and was prepared to be found doing nothing.

"Saloon," she said. "There is a soup and there is the lemon bread and Calden is on the second-watch rota tonight so he will not be eating standing up if I am at the door."

"Saloon."

"Bring the slate."

He brought the slate. They went down the aft stair together at the pace they walked when they had nowhere to be, which was slower than his step and slightly faster than hers, and arrived in a rhythm neither of them had settled.

The forward room on deck three was the small one — six tables, a brass urn, a porthole on the long wall and a porthole on the short — and it was warm at the open of the second-watch service in the way the small rooms on the Wyrm were warm, the smell of the bread and the soup and the lemon coming up through the floor from the galley one deck down. Calden was at the door. He nodded at Sable. He nodded at Theron. He did not nod again.

They took the corner table at the long porthole.

"Lemon bread," he said.

"Lemon bread."

The soup came. It was the kind of soup the cook did on the gondola's third day out, when the morning fish had been good — a clear broth with the small white pieces in it and the green of the herb on top — and they ate it the way two people ate a soup they did not have to talk through. The bread came after. The lemon biscuits, when they arrived on the small plate Sable had been expecting them on, arrived as four and not as six, which was the cook's small joke at Sable for the bread schedule remark the night before. She moved two onto Theron's side without comment.

"The lunch," she said.

"You heard."

"The galley boy heard. The cook heard. The cook told me, this time, not the galley boy. I am being upgraded."

"She closed the folio."

"She closed it at the third bell, Vellaine thinks."

"Vellaine said that to you?"

"Vellaine said it to a place in the room she knew I would be in by the open of the second-watch service. There is a small economy. I am being paid in observations." She broke the bread. "You answered her well."

"I did not say what was on the page."

"You said you were working the page. Which was true."

"It was true."

"True is a useful answer in a room with witnesses."

"It is."

He drank from the small cup of water at his elbow. The Wyrm carried its low constant through the bench. The harness note came up through the floor in the same way it came up everywhere on the gondola, and underneath it, when he listened for it, the secondary note was still there, a fraction below the main.

"The cook," he said, after a beat. "He told you about the bread schedule last night. He's been talking to you."

"He talks to me because I asked him about Calden eating standing up. He is the kind of man who, having opened a door, holds it open. It is restful."

"It is restful."

"You should try it sometime."

He almost smiled. He did not, but the not-smiling was the kind of not-smiling that the table got the use of.

She turned her cup a quarter so the handle pointed away from him. The small still moment after the cup-turn was a moment he had begun to file and was, on the third day of filing it, not yet ready to put a column on.

"The third night," he said.

She looked at him.

"Vannic was at his wall-facing table behind us. I had taken the seat with the saloon's long window, you were at the next table over. He was talking to the wine steward about something — not the substitute, this was after the substitute — and he said, in a part of the conversation I had not really been listening to and was nonetheless storing because the page does that —" he set the soup spoon down — "he said, *my niece carved this for me when she was six and she would be unimpressed that I have not lost it yet.*"

The bread was half to her plate. She held it there. She glanced at the porthole. She brought the bread the rest of the way down.

"That's the sentence?"

"That's the sentence."

"Mm."

He sat with the words for the small honest beat the words required, which was the beat the social texture of a man whose body was in cabin twelve had earned at any table he had been at in the run. Vannic had been at a table on the third night and had said a sentence about a small carved object and a child and the small comic indignity of the child being older now than she had been when she had carved the object. Theron had not been particularly listening. The sentence had stored itself anyway.

He picked the spoon up.

"He had a small wooden fish on him," he said. "In the coat pocket. I logged it the morning of the body."

"I remember."

He filed the sentence under *small social texture of a man whose body is in cabin twelve* and moved on, because it was not on the case-page and he was not at the underline. The cook brought a second small plate over with two more lemon biscuits on it, in the unfussy correction of a man who had counted four and meant six.

"He's correcting the bread schedule," Sable said, as the cook withdrew.

"By giving us more biscuits than the bread schedule requires."

"Same correction."

"You'll write him a thank-you note."

"I will say *noted* at him when I see him at the urn, which is the correct form."

"Noted."

He did smile, this time, briefly. She did not look at him while he did, which he had begun to understand was its own kind of looking. The cup was a quarter further on its handle than it had been a moment ago. They finished the meal. He had not, in the event, opened the slate.

---

He took the slate up to his cabin at the close of the bronzed hour and set it on the writing desk.

The page was the page he had built in the gallery. Esherel at the top, with the underline once now on *demeanor*. Tannin at the second tier. Vellaine at the third. The two windows in series. The corridor in pencil. The lunch scene noted with a tick under *demeanor under stress*. The four-crate scene with a second tick beside it. The three small lines in the margin under cabin twelve that said *carry-frame*, sitting in their margin and not yet connected to anything.

He looked at the underline on *demeanor*.

He capped the pencil and laid it parallel to the slate's top edge at the angle Sable laid hers.

> *[Investigator — Quest active. Ten hours, seven minutes to Halverstow Port.]*

He read it. He moved his thumb off the graduation pin. He had not noticed, again, his thumb arriving at the pin.

The cabin smelled faintly of his father's dispensary oil. The drakes were on their long approach. The Wyrm carried its low constant through the floor. He was, on inspection, comfortable.

He did not write the underline on opportunity. He thought about it for the length of time it took the lamp oil to warm at trim, decided that the morning was still the right shape, and put the slate where he had put it the night before, parallel to the desk's far edge.

He went to find dinner.

---

# Chapter 12 — The Page Finished

He was in the captain's gallery at the open of the second-watch coffee because the page in his pocket had decided overnight that it wanted finishing, and he had decided over the same hours not to argue with it.

The bow window had its usual knot of second-watch passengers at the brass urn. He took the bench at the end that was not the brass, and put the slate flat on his knee.

The page was the page he had built yesterday.

Esherel at the top. Tannin and Vellaine at the tiers below her. The two windows in series, drawn on a single line. The corridor in pencil. The lunch scene with its tick. The four-crate scene with its tick. The three small lines in the margin under cabin twelve that said *carry-frame*, sitting in their margin the way he had left them.

He had not written the underline on *opportunity*.

He worked the late window, which was the column he had not finished. Calden had given him the three-minute gap on the seventh day, the small clean window in the back end of the fourth watch when the steward had been on the deck-three corridor and the deck-four corridor had been unwatched. He drew the gap on the line and put a tick under it. He drew Esherel's table position at the dining-room arch, the corridor sightline from her table, the half-step kink he and Sable had walked on the seventh day.

He had built the page on the sightline from the table.

He had not, on inspection, built it on the sightline from her cabin door.

He capped the pencil and looked at the column for a count, then for another count. It was not a thing he needed. It was a thing he wanted. The page would close cleanly with the second sightline, or it would close cleanly without it; he was aware of which of those was the careful answer, and he was also aware, in the small honest part of his attention he had begun to require of himself before he made a decision, that he wanted the second sightline because the page deserved it.

He decided he would check at the open of the lunch service, when she was at her table.

He uncapped the pencil and drew a small *cabin* under the corridor in pencil. He capped the pencil again and laid it parallel to the slate's top edge. The page was patient. He left it on the bench beside him and watched the bow window for a while, where the drakes were on their long approach against a sky that had thinned to the pale clean blue of an afternoon that intended to behave.

---

He went down to deck three at the second-bell call to lunch.

The corridor was empty in the small clean way the deck-three corridor was empty at the open of the lunch service — Calden up at the dining-room arch, the second-watch passengers up at the urns, the cook one deck down sending the first plates up. He walked the corridor at the pace he had walked it twenty times now and stopped at the threshold of Esherel's cabin door.

He did not turn the handle.

He stood at the threshold and looked down the corridor at cabin twelve. The sightline carried the half-step kink. From the doorframe at exactly the angle a person stood at when she was closing her own door behind her, the cabin twelve door took itself out of the line at the count of a half-step. He filed it. He took the second step that put the cabin twelve door back into the line, the same step he and Sable had taken on the seventh day. He filed that.

A small clean sound came up through the deck plates from the galley two decks down, the small metal sound of the lemon plates being set on the cook's bench.

He went up the stair to the dining room with a tick to add to the column under *opportunity (late)*.

---

The dining room at the open of the lunch service was warm. Vellaine was at her window table with the book and the cold coffee, the same configuration she had been at every meal since the first morning. The Henners were at the far table. Their daughter had taken her egg today with less ceremony than yesterday and was working on a piece of toast with the concentration of a child who had decided toast was a more useful project.

Esherel was at her table.

The folio was closed. It had been closed at the third bell, he could see the way her hand was set on it. Her coffee was at her left. The bread on her plate had been broken cleanly in half.

He took the chair across from Vellaine, who did not look up.

"Lunch pot," he said.

"Less bright. More useful." Vellaine turned a page. "It is my favourite of the three. The Wyrm does the pots in a serious order."

"I had been wondering."

"I keep a small list, Investigator. I am here for the route, not for the symposium, as I have explained."

"You are here for the lunch pot."

"I am here for the lunch pot." She turned another page. "Are you finished with the page?"

He had not, he realized, told her he was working a page. The room had told her. The room had been telling her since the first morning, possibly. He noticed this without alarm and set it under one of the small categories he kept now for the way information moved on the gondola — categories he had not had at boarding and would not have written down at the academy, where information moved through forms and registries and not through the cook to the galley boy to Sable to a quarter-turn on a chair.

"One tick from finishing it," he said.

"Then you should speak to her with the page finished."

"I intend to. This evening, after the supper."

"Where."

"The saloon. The long porthole, if she will sit."

Vellaine closed the book on her thumb. She did not look up at him. She looked at the porthole at her elbow, where the pale clean blue had carried on with its careful business since the first morning, and after a count she said:

"I will be at the long porthole this evening."

"You do not have to."

"I do not have to." She opened the book again. "I would prefer to. A room with witnesses is a different room than one without, and you did well in the room yesterday, but a saloon at the close of the bronzed hour is a smaller room. I will be at the porthole. You will not have to see me. I will see you."

He sat with this for a count.

"Thank you, Dr. Vellaine."

"Mm. Have the bread."

He had the bread.

Esherel finished her own bread and her coffee in the careful order in which she had finished them at every meal of the run. She rose. She put the folio under her arm. She went out through the forward arch without turning the room. Vellaine turned a page. The Henners' daughter had reduced the toast to a useful crescent. The Wyrm carried its low constant through the bench. The coffee, on second pass, was very good.

---

He went back to the gallery after lunch and sat on the bench at the end that was not the brass.

The page was where he had left it. He picked it up and looked at it. He drew the second sightline from Esherel's cabin door under the corridor line, and put a tick under it. He looked at the column under *opportunity (late)*. The two ticks were the two ticks. The early window. The late window. The corridor sightline from the table. The corridor sightline from the door. Demeanor under stress, two ticks. The folio closed at the third bell, today, as a third small tick under the same heading if he chose to use it; he chose not to. He was not in the column for completeness. He was in it for what was carried.

He uncapped the pencil. He held it for a small clean breath. He wrote *opportunity (late)* under Esherel's column at the bottom of the page, in the careful hand he had begun to favour, and he drew a single line under it.

The underline took the slate seriously. The slate took the underline seriously back.

He capped the pencil. He laid it parallel to the slate's top edge at the angle Sable laid hers. He looked at the two underlines on the page, *demeanor* and *opportunity*, and they sat on the slate the way the two drakes on the outside of the formation sat against the rigging on the bow, drawn forward to a shared line.

The column had closed.

> *[Investigator — Insight +1. A column closes when the careful hand stops adding to it.]*

He read it twice. He thought about flinching, the small habitual flinch he had not yet broken himself of when the System said something dry at him, and chose, again, not to. The dry was, on examination, friendly. It had been friendly for some days now. He had not entirely decided what to do with that.

He did not write the decision about the saloon on the page. He filed it under his own attention, where he had been filing other things since the third morning and had begun to trust the filing.

Below the gallery window, the drakes were in their formation. Petal had not yet handed Gravel the slot on the return. The harness's note came up through the bench in its long constant. He listened for the secondary note he had named yesterday and was not, on inspection, surprised that it was still there, a fraction below the main. He left it in the cabin-twelve margin and did not connect it to anything. He was not at that page.

He was at this one.

He put the slate under his arm and went to find Sable.

He did not have to. She was at the gallery door at the open of the bronzed hour, standing at the frame the way she did when she had come to find him and was prepared to be found doing nothing.

"Saloon," she said.

"Saloon."

"There is the soup with the green oil on it tonight. The cook did the morning fish well and is showing off."

"I had heard."

"From whom."

"From the floor of the corridor on my way up. The lemon plates were going on the bench when I came up from deck three."

"Ah." She turned and walked. "Then we are both being informed by the gondola."

"It is restful."

"I had told you."

He went after her with the slate under his arm. She did not slow for him; he did not hurry. They arrived at the aft stair in the rhythm they had begun to walk in, slower than his step and slightly faster than hers, and went down it.

---

The saloon at the open of the second-watch service smelled of the bread and the new soup and the lemon. Calden was at the door. He nodded at Sable. He nodded at Theron. He did not nod again.

They took the corner table at the long porthole.

"Six," Sable said, when the small plate came.

"Six."

"He has corrected his correction."

"He has."

"The cook is a careful man. He is overcorrecting because the first correction was over the bread schedule and the bread schedule was, on examination, not in error. He is restoring the correct count and adding a count to me as an apology for last night."

"You are being paid in biscuits."

"It is the running rate." She moved two onto his side without comment.

The soup was the thicker kind with the small white pieces, and the green oil sat on top in the small clean way the cook did it on a good fishing day. They ate it the way two people ate a soup on the third day they had eaten in this saloon, which was unhurriedly. The bread came. The biscuits sat in their small plate to be earned.

"The bolt on the port runner," he said.

"Held."

"You asked Wisp."

"I asked the bolt. Wisp confirmed."

"The bolt's account."

"The bolt's account is that it did not loosen. Wisp has decided to believe the bolt for one more watch. They are in a small period of mutual professional fiction."

"All three drakes and a bolt."

"It is a productive gondola."

The biscuits did not move for a while. They were content to be the biscuits.

She turned her cup a quarter so the handle pointed away from him, the same small move she had made the night before. He had begun to notice he was tracking it. He chose not to mind that he was. The Wyrm carried its low constant through the bench, and the harness's note came up through the floor, and underneath the note when he listened for it the secondary note was still there.

He set the spoon down.

"After supper," he said.

She did not pretend not to understand. She held the look.

"After supper, then."

"The long porthole in the saloon. Vellaine will be at the next table. She offered."

"She did, did she."

"I had not asked."

"Of course you had not asked. Dr. Vellaine does not require asking for a thing she has decided to do." Sable turned the cup another quarter, then put the handle back where it had been. "And me."

"I had not asked you either."

"I had not waited to be asked."

"I am — grateful."

"Mm."

"I would have asked."

"You would have. I have been at the corner of the long porthole on the saloon's second day, on the fourth day, and on the seventh. I am at the corner of the long porthole the way Vellaine is at the window table in the dining room. It is a small place that I have decided is mine for the run. I will be there this evening as I have been there on three other evenings, with the small additional reason that you have decided to do the talking part of the work this evening at the table beside mine."

"You make it sound restful."

"It will not be restful." She broke a biscuit in half and ate one half. "Have the soup."

He had the soup. The talk did not move into the case again, because she did not move it there and he did not. They talked, after a stretch, briefly, about the cottage on the coast — the small inheritance, the great-aunt who had been a name on a card at Sable's mother's house and not much else, the paperwork at the other end that had its own slow texture. He did not press. He had not pressed on it at any meal in the run; he had begun to understand that the not-pressing was a thing she was, in the unfussy way of a person who did not say what she was grateful for, grateful for. She moved the biscuit plate a half-inch toward him.

"The view," she said, when the soup was gone and the bread was gone and the biscuits were down to two on the plate. "From the long porthole at the open of the bronzed hour."

"I had heard it was good."

"It is good." She did not look at him while she said it. "You should sit by it before the supper proper, so the view is in the room before the room becomes the talking part."

"Good advice."

"I have been at the table on three evenings, as I said."

"You have an investment."

"I have an investment."

He almost smiled. He did not, but the not-smiling was, again, the kind the table got the use of.

---

He took the slate up to his cabin at the close of the bronzed hour.

He set it on the writing desk parallel to the desk's far edge, in the angle he had set it on the past two nights. He looked at the page once. The column was closed. The two underlines were the two underlines. The carry-frame note in the cabin-twelve margin sat in its margin and was patient and unconnected. He did not return to it. He did not open the slate.

> *[Investigator — Quest active. Nine hours, twenty minutes to Halverstow Port.]*

He read it. He moved his thumb off the graduation pin. He had not noticed it arrive at the pin; he had begun, in the past two days, to notice this less often, which he had begun, in the past two days, to consider its own small competence.

The cabin smelled faintly of his father's dispensary oil.

He took his good shirt off the hook in the wardrobe and changed into it for the supper. He had not put it on for a supper at the academy more than twice in three years; he was, on inspection, willing to be a little careful tonight. He buttoned the collar in the small mirror over the writing desk and ran his thumb along the seam at the cuff where his mother had set the stitches the week he had left home, the careful uneven hand of a woman who had not been a tailor and had refused to let him buy a shirt for the journey.

He stood at the desk for a count.

The page had closed cleanly. The conversation was the conversation he had been working toward for ten days, and he was, in the small honest part of his attention, ready for it. He was not rehearsing the sentences. He had decided two days ago that he would let the room have the conversation, the way he had let the room have the answer at lunch yesterday, and he had not changed his mind about it since.

He moved the slate a quarter-inch on the desk to align it with the desk's edge.

He put the lamp at trim.

Below the cabin window the drakes were on the long approach against a sky that had already gone to bronze and was beginning, at the western edge of itself, to take the first warm note of evening. Petal handed Gravel the slot on the return. The slot was two breaths wider than it needed to be. Gravel took the air. He did not acknowledge.

Theron pulled the cabin door closed softly behind him.

The corridor was still warm with the bronzed light. The Wyrm carried its low constant through the floor. He went down to the supper.

---

# Chapter 13 — Petor Vann

Sable was already at the bottom of the aft stairwell when Theron came down, and she had a small square of paper in her hand folded twice at right angles.

"Third-class register," she said. "Calden had the third-class copy in a different box." She held the paper out without explanation.

Theron took it. The fold opened on a passenger name entered in the booking officer's round, hurried hand: *Petor Vann, cabin three-T, paid passage Mirren Dock to Halverstow Port, second-class berth declined, third-class confirmed.*

"Petor Vann," he said.

"Mm."

He turned it over. The reverse was blank. "The register was Calden's, you said — when did he—"

"When I asked him about the amendment. He went to his station box, came back with this one." She took the paper back, folded it the same way she'd had it. "He'd been holding on to it."

Theron put a hand on the stairwell railing. Below them the third-class corridor ran level and close, its lamp brackets lower than on the upper decks, the brass less polished. The dragon's pull was steadier down here, closer to the belly of the gondola. You felt it through the soles of your feet more than through your legs.

"And Calden knew," Theron said.

"Calden knew someone had paid cash for a third-class berth the same week the first-class booking under M. Tannin came in." Sable tucked the paper inside her coat. "I don't think he put it together. I think he was holding it because it didn't fit, and he was waiting for someone to tell him what it meant."

"Three-T," he said.

"Third class, starboard side."

He had never been in the third-class corridor. He knew it from the manifest, from the geometry of the gondola's structural drawings on the captain's gallery wall. It had twelve berths rather than four, a shared observation window at the far end, and no individual porthole allocation. The passengers booked there were the kind the manifest categorized as *economy, single travel* and that Theron had ranked in the fourth tier: too far from the first-class corridor to have reached cabin twelve, alibi by architecture.

"Lead," he said.

---

The third-class starboard passage was painted a yellower white than the upper decks, and it smelled of boot leather and last night's soup from the galley two compartments over. The lamp at the far end needed trimming. The gondola's hang had a slight asymmetry here, a lean in the deck plates, barely measurable but present under the boot.

Sable walked ahead of him, not quickly. She stopped at the third door on the right and knocked twice with her knuckle — not the flat of her hand.

From inside, silence. Then the sound of something being moved.

Theron had the manifest page in his head: cabin three-T, one male passenger, economy. He had the paper Sable had folded in her pocket, and the whole first-class column arranged in his mind like a set of slides in a lecture lantern. None of it had prepared him for the door opening.

The man who opened it was thirty years old at the outside, with the wrinkled look of someone who hadn't slept well in three days and was still wearing yesterday's waistcoat. He was medium height, medium brown hair, and he had the face of a person who had been hoping this particular knock would not come.

Theron looked at him.

The man looked at Theron.

"Lord Vannic," Theron said.

The name had come out of him with no drama, no deduction, no moment of working backward through evidence — it had arrived the way recognition always arrives, complete and sudden, the face fitting itself over memory while his lungs were still catching up. Lord Petric Vannic, alive. The dead man in cabin twelve was not Vannic. Theron could feel the entire column of his case rearranging itself like a stack of papers that had been held at one corner and released.

Sable said nothing. She was watching the door.

The man who was not Petor Vann made a sound that was not quite a word and stepped backward. He did not shut the door.

---

The cabin was barely wide enough for the three of them. It had one bunk, a small table screwed to the bulkhead, and a peg on the wall with a coat Theron recognized: pale grey, a half-cape at the shoulder, too fine for third class by two price brackets at minimum. The coat had been Vannic's. The man now wearing a wrinkled waistcoat and no coat had, apparently, been wearing that one when he boarded.

"Sit down," Theron said. It came out more gently than he intended.

Vannic sat on the bunk. He had large hands, and he pressed them flat against his knees in the way of someone whose hands have nothing else to do.

"I don't know what—" he started.

"The dead man in cabin twelve is not you," Theron said. "That much I've established on my own in the last thirty seconds. Who he is, and who hired him to perform the role, I will need you to explain. Start with his name."

Vannic put his face in his hands for a moment. Not dramatic — tired. When he looked up, he said, "Joren. He said I could call him Joren."

*[Investigator — Quest updated. The dead man has a name. Cabin twelve is not a locked room. It is Joren's room.]*

Theron read it twice without moving. He was aware of Sable settling against the bulkhead behind him with her arms crossed, and he was aware that she was not going to say anything yet, and he was glad of it.

"Joren," he repeated. "Last name?"

"He didn't give one. He — we arranged it through a factor, not directly. The factor said his name was Joren and that he was reliable. He was." Vannic's hands pressed harder against his knees. "He was extremely professional. He said he'd done impersonations before, that the illusion work was straightforward, that as long as I stayed out of the first-class corridor for the duration of the flight—"

"Why the gondola?" Theron asked.

Vannic looked up.

"A closed transit with a fixed passenger list and a three-day window — that's an unusual choice for a disappearance. Why not go overland?"

Vannic's mouth pressed together. "The engagement announcement goes into the Mirren Gazette on the twenty-second. If I'm not in Mirren on the twenty-second, I am officially — the contract specifies that a failure to appear at the formal introduction constitutes a withdrawal, which means—"

"Which means if you're not there, the contract dissolves," Theron said.

"On the twenty-third," Vannic said. "I need to not be findable until the twenty-third. After that, the Halveth Compact symposium is — Halverstow is very far from Mirren. I thought, if there was a record of Lord Vannic aboard the Wyrm, an official manifest entry, then no one would think to look for me in Halverstow. That I'd simply have travelled on. Taken the air. Shown my face." He looked at his hands. "I didn't account for — I didn't think anyone would die."

Theron had no immediate response to this, which was unusual. He filed the engagement-contract timeline in the space where Esherel's motive had been and noted, distantly, that the column he'd spent three days building was not so much wrong as it was about a man who was currently sitting in front of him with both hands on his knees.

"The forced betrothal," Sable said, from behind Theron. "Tell him the terms."

Vannic looked at her as if he'd momentarily forgotten she was there. Then he looked at Theron. "Duke Esseven's daughter. She is forty-four. I am twenty-eight. The match was arranged by my father's estate board while my father was ill, and I was not consulted, and I — I know how it sounds." He stopped.

"It sounds like a reason," Theron said.

Vannic seemed not to have expected that. He blinked.

"What I need to understand," Theron continued, "is the arrangement. You hired Joren through a factor. He boarded the Wyrm in your place. You boarded separately as Petor Vann. Who knew?"

"The factor. Joren, obviously. And—" Vannic stopped. A muscle in his jaw moved. "He was supposed to stay in the cabin. He was supposed to be reclusive and difficult and eat alone and leave on arrival. He wasn't supposed to—" He stopped again.

"He was supposed to not die," Theron said.

"Yes."

Theron thought about the dead man's coat, which was Vannic's coat, and which Joren had apparently worn with Vannic's face and Vannic's manner and, as Sable had reported at the dining-room table, an occasional slip of a coastal accent when he was tired. He thought about the carved wooden fish in the outer pocket — small, worn smooth, the kind of thing a person carries and forgets they are carrying.

"The factor's name," he said.

"Berric. Berric Toll. He operates out of the Mirren west harbour." Vannic gave the address in a flat voice that suggested he had made his decision about cooperation somewhere around the moment he opened the door. "He can confirm everything. I have his correspondence."

"I'll want the correspondence."

Vannic nodded. He reached under the bunk and produced a small oilcloth packet, the careful preservation of a man who was nervous about a great number of things.

Theron took it without opening it.

"One more question," Sable said. She said it to Vannic with the straightforward courtesy she brought to most things, not unkind. "Joren. You only met him the once, through the factor?"

"Twice," Vannic said. "Once at the factor's office, to be measured and matched and photographed for the glamour work. Once at Mirren Dock, to confirm the boarding." He looked at her directly. "He shook my hand and told me to enjoy Halverstow and not to worry about the paperwork end. I thought he was being professional. He probably was."

Sable made a small sound that was not quite agreement and not quite grief and didn't require a response.

Theron looked at the oilcloth packet in his hand, and then at Vannic, and said: "You will stay in this cabin until the gondola lands. You will not correspond with anyone on this vessel. You will not contact the factor before I have spoken to the captain." He paused. "You will eat breakfast. There's a tray service for third class at seventh bell."

Vannic stared at him. "Is that — are you giving me instructions about breakfast?"

"The case runs better if everyone on this gondola is fed," Theron said. "It is a practical consideration."

---

They came back up the aft stairwell in the direction they'd descended, and Theron stopped on the landing where the second-class passage met the service corridor, because the oilcloth packet was in his hand and the column in his mind had completely changed shape and he needed to stand in one place for a moment before he did the next thing.

Sable stopped beside him. She did not say anything. The lamp above the landing ticked as the gondola shifted on a thermal.

"Joren," Theron said. The name sat differently in his mouth than *the dead man* had. He was aware of this. He did not remark on it.

"Yes," Sable said.

"The case is not what I thought it was." He said this without inflection, by way of inventory. The corpse in cabin twelve was an illusionist named Joren who had taken contract work impersonating a lord who was alive and hiding two decks below from a marriage he had not chosen. Esherel's forty-one thousand crowns were still real. Calden's twelve-minute absence was still real. The locked geometry of cabin twelve was still real. The dead man's method of death remained unknown. All of that was still on the page. The page had simply acquired a new first line.

"Esherel didn't kill him," Sable said.

"No."

"Because she didn't know he wasn't the lord."

"No." Theron considered this. "Which means whoever killed him did know. If you don't know who Joren actually is, killing him is coincidence at best, collateral at worst. Knowing he's Joren — knowing the man under the glamour — that's a target."

Sable tilted her head. "Someone killed Joren knowing Joren was Joren."

"And wanted the body to be found as Vannic. Or didn't care which it was found as." He pressed his thumbnail against the edge of the oilcloth packet. "The factor. Or someone who knew the factor. Or someone in Joren's working history who knew his face under the glamour."

"Third class," Sable said.

"Yes. I've been looking at the wrong manifest." He looked up. The lamp ticked again. "I owe Esherel a conversation."

"You owe her an apology," Sable said. Not unkindly.

"I owe her accurate information delivered in a professional manner," Theron said. "The apology is implicit."

The corner of Sable's mouth moved. "Start with the word *sorry*. It takes four seconds."

Theron considered this. "I'll start with *I have new information that I think you'll find significant*."

"Mm."

"That is also accurate."

"It is," she said. "It's also going to make her laugh at you, and then she'll be in a better mood for the rest of it, so I suppose it works either way."

*[Investigator — Insight +1. Two manifests, two worlds, one locked room. The question is not who had access. The question is who had reason.]*

Theron read it once, tucked the notation away, and went up the stairs carrying Vannic's oilcloth packet toward the captain's gallery, with the name Joren now occupying the space in the case that had previously belonged to the dead man, and the dead man having acquired, in the space of twenty minutes, a face and two handshakes and a factor and a coastal town he had presumably come from.

The lamp at the top of the landing was well-trimmed and bright. The one at the bottom had needed work. He had not noticed until now.

---

# Chapter 14 — The Apology That Wasn't

Theron stood at Esherel's door with his knuckles raised and the oilcloth packet tucked under his left arm, and did not knock for three seconds while he composed his opening sentence.

*I have new information that I think you'll find significant.*

Six words. He had discarded three alternatives on the stairs, all of which were either more truthful or less defensible. Six words was the shape that fit.

He knocked.

Esherel answered at once, as if she had been standing on the other side of the door already, or had not slept, or both. She was in her day dress but her hair was down, which he registered and immediately set aside as not his business, and she looked at him and at the oilcloth packet and then at his face.

"Well," she said. "You've been busy."

"I have new information that I think you'll find significant," Theron said.

There was a pause. Then Esherel made a sound that was — laughter was the wrong word, it was too generous, but it was close, a compressed burst through the nose, a brief unwilling thing. Her hand dropped from the door handle. "Come in, then. Sit somewhere."

---

She poured him coffee from a pot that had gone slightly cold, which was either a courtesy or an armistice or both, and she poured herself the end of it and remained standing, which Theron took as a sign she didn't plan to be sitting still for long once he'd said what he'd come to say.

"The dead man in cabin twelve is not Lord Vannic," he said.

Esherel's cup came down on the writing desk without quite hitting it deliberately. "I'm sorry?"

"Lord Petric Vannic is alive. He has been traveling on this gondola for the duration of the flight under a false name, in third class, hiding from a forced betrothal. He hired an illusionist to perform the role of Lord Vannic for the run. The illusionist is the dead man."

Esherel looked at him. Her expression went through several things. He watched motive collapse in real time, which was not quite satisfaction — it was more like watching a ledger that has been miscategorized for days suddenly sort itself. The column he'd put her in was wrong, and she knew she'd been in it, and she was working out what to do with that.

"The man who wouldn't answer my letters," she said, "was not the man who took my money."

"Vannic took your money. The illusionist was hired twelve weeks before the flight."

"So I have been spending three days on this gondola sitting across the dining room from a dead man who owed me nothing." She turned to the porthole and stood there a moment. The sky outside was the pale grey of late morning, the kind that sits on everything without quite touching it. "I suppose I should feel something different about that."

"The contract dispute is unchanged," Theron said. "Vannic is the debtor. He is aboard. He is also, at present, in the custody of this investigation, so any conversation you need to have with him will have to wait until Halverstow."

Esherel turned back. She gave him a look he couldn't immediately classify — not anger, not relief, something sharper than either. "You spent three days watching me."

"Yes."

"Rank one," she said.

He did not ask how she knew the term. "Yes."

She settled into the straight-backed chair across from him and set her coffee down. "And now you're here because the illusionist's column is empty and mine is cleared, and you need everything I saw."

"Yes," Theron said. "You watched him across three days with a great deal of focused attention, and you are quick-minded, and I am going to ask you to tell me everything you noticed that you didn't think to mention because you assumed it wasn't useful."

"Why would I assume anything wasn't useful to an Investigator?"

"Because most people assume the investigator will ask for what he needs."

"And he doesn't?"

"He doesn't always know what he needs until he hears it."

Esherel looked at him with an expression that softened about a quarter turn from where it had been. She picked up her coffee. "All right. The man who sat across the room from me for three days." She let out a breath, not quite a sigh, a thinking breath. "He wasn't rude."

Theron opened his notebook.

"I want to be clear," she said, "that I was furious, and I had reason to be, and I was looking for evidence to justify being furious. I was building a case the same way you were, probably. And the man I was building it against — the man who had the money and wouldn't write back — would have been rude. It is the universal feature of that type: they hide behind courtesy when they can and rudeness when they can't." She paused. "This man wasn't rude."

"Can you be specific?"

"He wasn't performing politeness, either. That was the thing that irritated me. If he'd been performing it I could have worked with that. He was just — present. He listened when people spoke. He looked at the room." She made a small gesture. "At the staff."

Theron wrote: *attended to room and staff, not performing.*

"What else?"

"He was careful about taking up space. Not shy. Careful. He would step back before someone needed him to. He watched the door when someone was struggling with luggage." She frowned slightly. "I noticed him do that on the second morning and I was annoyed with him about it, because it was inconvenient to my accounting of the man."

"The accounting you were building."

"Yes." She drank the last of her cold coffee. "I had decided he was a bully. The money suggested it and the letters suggested it and I had the letter in my folio. Then there was this man being meticulous about doorways." She set the cup down. "What was he, really? The illusionist."

"A professional. He'd done impersonation work before."

"A working mage."

"Yes."

She was quiet for a moment. "Then the carefulness makes sense. Working mages learn early to map a room. They have to know where everyone is." She said this to herself as much as to Theron, filing it away in whatever internal ledger she kept. "I couldn't square him with the letters. I spent three days unable to square him with the letters. At dinner I kept watching his hands."

Theron went still. Then he wrote it down, carefully.

"His hands?" he said.

"He held a wine glass the way you hold tools, not stemware. Flat palm, not the pinch." She demonstrated briefly with the coffee cup. "I thought it was an affectation. Some kind of — statement. Aristocratic eccentricity." She looked at Theron's notebook. "That's significant, isn't it."

"It's consistent with what I know," he said, which was true without being anything it shouldn't be.

Esherel leaned back. "You should have come to me earlier."

"You were rank one."

"I know I was rank one. I could still have told you he was watching the room like someone who needed to know the exits." She said it without heat. "I noticed it because I do the same thing at trade dinners. It's what you do when you're not sure of your position."

Theron looked up from the notebook. "When did you notice that?"

"Second night. Before the first-class lounge closed." She tilted her head. "Does it help?"

"It establishes that the illusionist was aware he was performing. Actively, throughout the flight." He wrote: *actively maintaining orientation — second night, confirmed.* "Did you speak to him at any point?"

"Once." She looked slightly surprised at herself. "Third morning, the observation deck. He was there before I was, standing at the brass rail, and I came out meaning to walk directly to the other end, and he turned and there was — there was a moment where I thought he was going to speak to me about the contract." She paused. "He didn't. He nodded, very correctly, and then went back to looking at the clouds. I stood at the other end for twenty minutes."

"What did you think of him in that moment?"

She considered it. "That he was managing something." She looked at the porthole. "The way you manage something that costs effort. Not the contract. Something else. He didn't look like a man who'd been ignoring my letters."

Theron wrote: *managing visible strain, third morning, observation deck.* He thought about a man three days into a performance who could not break character on a commercial transit and who had, apparently, been very good at not breaking it until the body was found.

"That's everything that was specific," Esherel said. "I could give you impressions."

"Impressions are useful."

She was quiet for a moment, in the way of someone weighing something they hadn't meant to say. "He seemed tired. Not from the flight. From something that required a great deal of ongoing attention." She picked up the empty coffee cup again, turned it once in her hands, set it down. "I don't know that I ever had reason to notice a working mage being tired at the end of a long hold. I'm noticing it now, in retrospect, because you've told me he was one."

"Retrospect is useful," Theron said. "Particularly when the original observation was precise."

Esherel gave him a look that was, very briefly, close to warm. "You're better at this than I thought you were going to be."

"Than you thought when?"

"When you told me, in the lounge, that I was on the list."

"You asked directly. A direct question deserves a direct answer."

"Yes." She stood, which he took as the end of the interview and gathered his notebook accordingly. "The contract," she said, when he was at the door.

"When Halverstow is in sight I'll arrange a time for you to speak with Vannic. Not before."

"He owes me forty-one and a half thousand crowns."

"He does. That conversation will be considerably more productive with an Investigator in the room." He paused. "If you want one."

Esherel looked at him steadily. "I want one," she said.

---

Sable was in the steward's alcove off the second-class passage with the third-class manifest spread across the fold-down table, the passenger copy and what looked like a handwritten supplement from Calden. The alcove was barely four feet wide, and a shelf of folded napkins blocked the upper left corner of the light, and she'd moved the lamp to compensate.

"Well?" she said, without looking up.

"She wasn't in my column," Theron said. "Or rather — she was, but the observations she gave me are more useful than anything my column contained." He pulled the second chair from under the table and sat. "She watched him through all three days. She caught the wine-glass grip. She observed him orienting to the room."

"Hmm." Sable weighted the right side of the manifest with a ring-stand she'd apparently borrowed from the galley. "Third class has fourteen passengers beyond Vannic. I've been sorting on two axes: who could have known Joren's name, and who had any professional path that crosses illusion work."

"Show me."

She pushed the manifest toward him. She had drawn a soft grid on the corner of a separate sheet — one of Calden's receipt papers, turned over — with four loose categories marked in her small, round hand: *trade contacts, working mage adjacent, factor network, unknown.*

"I don't have enough to fill these properly," she said. "What I have is shape."

Theron looked at the fourteen names. Most were first-class manifest rejects he recognized — economy travelers, a textile buyer whose address was in the Halveth inland, three students booked together under a shared-passage notation. One name had an annotation in different ink: *Maret Fell, cabin seven-T, Mirren east harbour, no stated profession.*

"This one," he said.

"Mirren."

"Berric Toll operates out of Mirren west harbour. That's a different end of the dock." He paused. "But same port."

"Same port," Sable said. She had a pencil and was not using it yet. "I didn't want to draw lines before we had anything to draw them to. But the address is the only one on the list that shares a port with Toll."

Theron looked at the manifest again. Third-class cabin seven-T was two doors from cabin three-T, where Vannic had been sleeping under a false name. The gondola's third-class corridor was close quarters. It would not require any particular maneuvering to move between cabins at night without crossing the second-class passage.

"Who else on the list do we need to account for?" he said.

"The textile buyer has a letter of reference in Calden's records. The students were booked together and there are meal records for all three at the same table for every sitting. Calden's confident about them." She put her finger on three names, held it there. "These four I have nothing on. Paid in cash, no annotations, no professional reference."

"That's not unusual in third class."

"No." She took her finger away. "But it means we start from the beginning on them. Occupation, travel reason, any prior connection to Mirren or Halverstow or the kind of contract work that would put them in the same professional network as a working illusionist."

Theron looked at the manifest. Fourteen names. Four blanks. One address in a shared port.

"We'll need Calden," he said. "He'll know which ones have spoken to staff. Third-class passengers talk to the steward — there's less to do, fewer places to sit."

"I'll ask him," Sable said. "He'll be more forthcoming than he was at the beginning." She began writing names onto the receipt paper in a second column.

"You're better at asking him than I am," Theron said.

She looked up, briefly. "Yes." She said it without ceremony and went back to the list.

Theron picked up the oilcloth packet from where he'd set it on the shelf beside the napkins. He had been carrying it since cabin three-T without opening it, and there had been reasons — the interview with Joriss, the corridor, Esherel — but there had also been a certain deliberateness to not opening it while he didn't yet know what questions to bring to it.

He now had the beginnings of the questions. He untied the cord.

---

The packet held four letters and a contract sheet, all written on the same weight of paper in a hand Theron didn't recognize. The letters were dated — the earliest was six weeks before the flight, the latest three days before Mirren Dock — and they were addressed to no one by name, only to *my lord* in the practiced deference of a factor who understood that discretion was part of the fee.

He read them in order.

The first two established the arrangement's logistics: the glamour parameters, the payment schedule, Joren's references, the boarding protocol, the contact silence clause. Standard factor language, careful and compressed. The third confirmed the final payment and gave Joren's boarding time at the dock.

The fourth was shorter.

He read it twice.

It was a schedule addendum. The factor had included, as an afterthought in the closing paragraph, a note that *the practitioner would be available for a second engagement of similar scope starting the twenty-eighth, as he had indicated he would be in the Halverstow region for three to four weeks on prior personal business.* There was a contact method — a postal address in a town south of Halverstow, a name: *Pessel, care of the Copper Finch*.

Theron set the letter on the table.

Joren had a destination after Halverstow. He'd told the factor. The factor had put it in writing.

Whatever personal business Joren had been carrying toward Halverstow — the three to four weeks, the contact address, the name he'd given a postal house in a southern coastal town — it was sitting in the letter, and it had been sitting in the oilcloth packet under Vannic's bunk for three days, and nobody had opened it until now.

He looked at the postal address again.

*Pessel, care of the Copper Finch, Sannholt, south coast Halveth.*

Sannholt was three hours south of Halverstow by the coast road.

Theron put the letter face-down on the stack, aligned the edges, and sat for a moment with his hands flat on the table.

*[Investigator — Insight +1. He was going somewhere after. The somewhere is still there, waiting for him.]*

"Sable," he said.

She looked up from the list.

"Joren had an address after Halverstow. Three weeks of prior personal business in a town called Sannholt, south coast. The factor put it in the last letter."

Sable was still for a moment. Then she reached across and held out her hand for the letter. He gave it to her. She read the closing paragraph without touching her pencil to the page.

"Pessel," she said. "That's not a surname. That's probably a contact alias. Or a family name, but common enough on the south coast that it's not traceable without more."

"Joren's own origin," Theron said. "If he's from that coast."

"Maybe." She put the letter back on the stack, face-down the way he'd left it. "Or someone he knew who lived there."

The pencil was still in her other hand, and she set it on the manifest without writing anything, which was the only sign that the letter had landed in any way she had not expected.

"Start with the fourteen," Theron said, after a moment. "We'll bring Sannholt to the captain after we've worked the list."

Sable picked up the pencil again. "Calden first, then the blanks." She drew a circle around *Maret Fell, cabin seven-T*.

The gondola shifted on a thermal, a long and gradual thing like a held breath releasing, and the stack of letters on the table moved a half-inch before Theron put his hand on them.

Outside, through the alcove's narrow port window, the Halveth coastline was beginning to show at the edge of the sky.

---

# Chapter 15 — The Third-Class Corridor

Calden was in the steward's alcove reshuffling a row of passenger files when Theron came down the aft stairs with the manifest, and Sable came down two steps behind with the second sheet and a pencil she hadn't stopped carrying since the alcove.

The steward looked up. He had the kind of expression that meant he had been waiting for this conversation, had organized the relevant facts into something presentable, and would like credit for that without being asked for it directly.

"The four I haven't heard from," Theron said, setting the manifest on the table fold-down.

Calden straightened the passenger files by a quarter-inch. "Three of the four made contact at some point. That's what I've been sorting."

"Tell me."

Calden's briefing was methodical and careful, in the way of a man who had worked out in advance exactly how much he was permitted to say. Passenger one, in cabin two-T, was a woman returning to Halverstow from a trade visit. She had asked about baggage claim at disembarkation, mentioned her employer by name, a textile house in the upper market district that Calden could confirm existed. Passenger two, cabin five-T, had requested a sleeping draught on the second night, which was in Calden's records, and had told Calden at that point that he was traveling for a family funeral, gave the name of the deceased, which Calden had not verified but had no reason to doubt. Both, Calden felt, were soft alibis by profession and timing. Neither had left their cabins after the third watch.

"Soft but credible," Theron said.

"They're good witnesses to themselves," Sable said, from beside the folded-napkin shelf. "That's not the same thing, but it's not nothing."

Theron had noted it down before she finished the sentence.

"The third," Calden said, "is the Fell woman. Cabin seven-T. She spoke to me on the first morning, asked about the observation window in the shared passage. Whether it was accessible in the evening." He paused. "Which is a question I can't fairly mark up or down, since half the third-class passengers ask it."

"But you noted it," Sable said.

"I noted it because she was the only one on this run who asked specifically about the evening."

Theron looked at the manifest. Maret Fell, cabin seven-T, Mirren east harbour, no stated profession. He circled the name again, not because it needed circling, but because the motion helped him think. "What's the fourth blank?"

"Cabin nine-T." Calden's tone shifted slightly, or didn't shift, which was itself a shift. "Halsa Renn. She hasn't spoken to me or any member of staff beyond boarding acknowledgment. No requests, no complaints, no meals taken outside cabin on record, though she was present at the shared observation window on the second evening."

"Alone?"

"As far as I saw. Briefly." He paused again. "She's quiet. Not conspicuously quiet. I mention it only because you asked."

"Thank you," Theron said. He wrote *9-T, Renn, no contact* on the margin and left it there.

*[Investigator — four passengers inventoried. Two credible soft alibis. One positive ask about the window at evening. One absence of contact.]*

Sable glanced at the note beside the manifest. She did not add anything to it.

"We'll start at the far end and work back," Theron said. "Calden, thank you. If any of the four come to the steward's station before we've reached them, please don't mention we're working the corridor."

Calden looked very slightly pained, as if the suggestion that he might do this otherwise was itself an imposition. "Of course not, sir."

---

Third class ran narrower than the second-class passage above it. The carpet had been replaced somewhere in the gondola's service history by a scrubbed wood floor that flexed slightly on the dragon-harness tremors, and the wall-lamps were single-wick rather than double, which meant that late afternoon in the corridor had the quality of a room where someone had just left after occupying it: warm but not bright, full of small sounds.

Theron knocked on cabin two-T. The textile buyer was forthcoming and uneventful: a compact woman with a trade schedule in her coat pocket and the kind of firm handshake that said she had been introduced to investigators before and knew what they needed and would provide it efficiently. She had her receipts in order. She had heard nothing unusual on the third night. She had been awake at first watch reading contracts and asleep before second. She had not, to her knowledge, interacted with any other third-class passenger beyond nodding in the shared passage.

"The Fell woman," Theron said, toward the end. "Do you know her by sight?"

She thought about it. "Brown coat. Keeps to herself." She frowned slightly. "She doesn't look like she's on holiday."

"What does she look like?"

"Someone doing a job." She shrugged, without apology. "I don't mean that badly. It's just how she sits in the corridor. Occupied."

Occupied. Theron wrote it down.

Cabin five-T: a middle-aged man with the permanently fatigued look of someone in the middle of a long grief. He confirmed the funeral, gave the name of his brother, named the village outside Halverstow without prompting. He had taken his sleeping draught at second watch and had no memory of anything after. He showed Theron the empty vial still tucked in the pocket of his coat because he had meant to return it to Calden and kept forgetting. The small kindness of showing it unprompted, the wanting to do the right thing even in the right-of-mind administration of it, left no room for anything else.

"I'm sorry about your brother," Sable said, from the doorway.

The man looked at her. His expression was the kind that doesn't have a quick answer and doesn't try to find one. "Thank you," he said.

They stepped out.

---

The shared observation window in the third-class passage was a broad brass-framed port set just above waist height on the starboard side, angled slightly forward. It was the longest window in this section of the gondola, long enough that three people could stand at it comfortably or two could stand at it closely, and through it the sky in the late afternoon was a deep and layered blue, the kind that sits above the cloud-line and doesn't announce itself.

Theron stood at it for two seconds while the passage was empty.

Halveth. Still two hours out, or close enough.

He knocked on cabin seven-T.

Maret Fell opened the door with the particular manner of someone who had been expecting a knock and had decided in advance how to answer it. She was in her early forties, angular, with the kind of composed attention that would have read as polite in a shorter conversation and read as something more precise in a longer one.

"The investigation," she said. Not a question.

"Theron Mavik. I'm working the flight manifest. This is my working partner, Sable Verrin. May we have a few minutes?"

"Of course." She stepped back. The cabin was orderly: bag closed, no loose papers, the traveling coat on the hook rather than on the bed. A professional habit, or the habit of someone who expected to be looked at.

Sable took the narrow chair by the wall without being asked. Theron stood at the end of the bunk.

"Your address in the manifest is Mirren east harbour," he said. "Trade work?"

"Shipping assessment." She offered it without hesitation, the way you offer a word you've said many times. "I consult for a house in the inland districts. I've been in Mirren three months on a contract."

"What brings you to Halverstow?"

"The end of the contract. I'm taking a week before the next one." A slight pause. "I usually take a week."

"Do you know the Mirren docks well? The full harbour?"

She looked at him steadily. "Well enough for the assessment work. I know the main commercial stretch and the factor district." She said it evenly. "The west harbour less so."

"Have you had reason to deal with a factor named Berric Toll?"

Her expression stayed where it was. Not a flicker, not a breath held. The expression of someone genuinely thinking, not performing thinking. "I know the name. He's in the upper-factor tier. I have not dealt with him personally."

Theron watched her. She was watching him back, without challenge, with the specific attentiveness of someone who understood that an interview had a structure and was content to meet it at each step.

"Were you at the shared observation window on the second evening?" he said.

"Yes." She didn't ask how he knew. "I wanted air and the deck hatch was closed. I was there for perhaps twenty minutes."

"Did you see anyone in the corridor during that time?"

"One other passenger, briefly." She looked at the port window in her own cabin, a smaller one, and then back at him. "Quiet woman from the far end. She looked at the view and left before I did."

Theron noted it. "Was there any conversation aboard between you and any other passenger? Work, travel, the Mirren docks?"

"No." Flat, not unfriendly. "I tend not to socialize on transit."

He asked two more questions about her movements on the third night. She had been in her cabin after supper, had heard a gondola creak she couldn't place sometime past first watch, had not opened her door. Her answers were clean and specific and added up to a picture of someone with nothing to hide and no reason to offer more than was asked. She gave him the name of the inland house she worked for, the name of her contact there, unprompted.

It was also the profile of someone with the right background and the patience to know that the cleanest accounting tends to pass earliest.

He wrote it down without drawing a line.

"Thank you," he said. "If you think of anything from the third night that seemed unusual, please speak to Calden. Any sound, any light under a door, anything that didn't fit."

"I will." She held the door for them with the same composed attention she'd opened it with.

In the corridor, Sable fell into step beside him. She said nothing for a beat, which he had learned by this point was not absence of opinion.

"Well?" he said.

"Clean," she said. "Correct clean or practiced clean, I can't tell you."

"That's the question."

"Yes."

---

Cabin nine-T was at the end of the corridor. Theron knocked twice, brief and even.

The woman who opened the door was younger than he'd expected from the travel register. Late twenties, perhaps thirty. Dark-haired, plainly dressed in a way that was clean rather than worn, with the kind of direct stillness in her expression that read as either patience or deliberate calm.

"Halsa Renn?" Theron said.

"Yes."

"Theron Mavik. I'm working the manifest, speaking to all third-class passengers as part of the investigation. This won't take long."

She nodded. The door opened a little wider, but she didn't step back. Theron noted the habit: answering in the doorway, not retreating into the space.

"Where are you traveling to in Halverstow?"

"Through to Sannholt. I have family there." Her voice was level, unhurried.

"You'll take the coast road?"

"The morning post-coach. Yes."

He asked about her work. She said she did small-contract labour, seasonal, had spent the last three months on the inland plains, was taking the flight as the faster route home before the roads turned in autumn. She had paid cash, which she acknowledged plainly, because she always paid cash on transit. She had no professional reference he could check, which she also acknowledged plainly, as a fact rather than an apology.

"Third night," Theron said. "After supper. Where were you?"

"In my cabin." She met his eyes without extra effort. "I sleep early on transit. The hum helps."

"The dragon hum?"

"Yes." The faintest shape of something in her expression that he filed as recognition of an unremarkable thing, the way you look when someone names a preference you've had for years. "I was asleep by second watch."

"Did you hear anything unusual after the second?"

She considered it, genuinely. "A creak from toward the second-class passage. Could have been the frame. It's not unusual."

"Was that before or after second watch?"

"After. Not much."

He asked whether she'd spoken to any other passengers during the flight. She said no, except for an exchange at the observation window on the second evening. A woman in her forties, brown coat, brief.

Theron wrote it down. Two accounts of the same window, the same evening, neither inconsistent with the other.

"Is there anything you noticed during the flight that seemed out of place to you? Anything that didn't fit?" He asked it the same way he had asked Maret Fell, as a closing question rather than a pointed one.

She thought for a moment. "The steward was quieter on the third morning than the first two." She said it with mild curiosity, not suspicion. "He has a particular walk. Professional but easy. It changed."

"You noticed that."

"I notice how people move when something's changed for them." She said it the same way she said everything: simple, without theater. "I didn't know what it was."

Theron looked at the notation in his margin. Traveling through to Sannholt. Family. Cash. Early sleeper. Observant.

"Thank you," he said. "We're nearly at Halverstow. Safe travels to the coast."

"Thank you," she said. She had already started to close the door before he turned away, and she closed it quietly, without hurry.

Sable waited until they were past the observation window before she said anything.

"She notices how people move," she said. Neutral tone. Not an accusation.

"She does," Theron said. He was looking at the four names on his sheet. Fourth-tier. Traveling through, south coast, family, cash, no apparent connection to Mirren west harbour or Toll or any of the professional paths that linked to Joren's work. Nothing to push on. He folded the sheet.

The corridor creak of the gondola shifted under them, a long slow accommodation: the frame was working harder, the angle of the gondola's nose beginning to change.

Theron looked up at the port window at the far end of the passage.

---

They came out of the third-class corridor into the aft stairwell landing, and the landing's narrow window faced south-west, and through it the light had a different quality than it had had an hour ago. Amber-edged. Lower. The sky had shifted from afternoon blue to the first thickening of early evening, and where the cloud-line broke away toward the horizon, the land was there.

Halveth coast. He could see the dark run of the headland, and below it, the long pale strip of the shore, and below that — just at the edge, at the line where sky and sea stopped competing — the clustered lights of a harbor, beginning to show.

Halverstow Port.

He stood at the window for a moment. Behind him Sable stopped on the step.

"Fell," she said. "How do you want to play it at dock?"

"Carefully." He watched the harbor lights. "She gave clean answers and the right name and a professional background that holds at arm's length. I want to push the Toll connection with someone who has access to the west harbour registers before I move any further." He paused. "The captain can authorize a shore contact in advance of landing."

"I'll ask Calden to ask him," Sable said. "He'll move it faster than a formal request."

Theron looked at the manifest sheet in his hand. Joren had had a destination after Halverstow. Three weeks in Sannholt, a name at a postal house, a contact called Pessel. He did not know whether Pessel was family or colleague or something else, but the address was real and the timing was specific and whoever had arranged to meet Joren there would, by now, have been waiting past the expected arrival by two days.

He folded the manifest and put it in his coat.

The gondola shifted again, this time decisively — a long and conscious reduction in angle, the whole frame acknowledging the descent with a low harmonized note from the harness chain. Through the window the harbor grew by a fraction, the lights sharpening as the distance closed.

Halverstow was coming up.

The lights ran along the waterfront in a broken chain, amber and white and one red marker at the harbor mouth, and below the headland the dark water was beginning to show the first pale creases of the port approach.

He had Maret Fell on the leading thread and three weeks of Joren's plans sitting in a letter, and the gondola was going down.

---

# Chapter 16 — He Had a Name

The Halverstow harbormaster's tug came out to meet them at the half-mile mark, and Theron stood at the aft rail and watched the ropes go across.

He had the small cloth bag in his coat pocket. Three things in it: one handkerchief, used, folded because he had folded it; one steel pen-nib, bent slightly at the tip; one carved wooden fish, which he had not touched since logging it in the cabin and logging it again at the stern alcove, and which he was now aware of with the constant low awareness of something occupying a pocket that had nothing else in it.

The drakes dropped out of harness forty feet above the docking mast. He watched them bank south — Petal first, Gravel close, Mottled lagging by the beat she had already apologized for, or Wisp had apologized on her behalf. Three shapes going small against the harbor sky. The gondola settled on the mooring lines with the slow deliberate sigh of a large object agreeing to rest.

He stayed at the rail.

Below, the cargo bay opened. The first-class passengers were already in the corridor — he had passed Esherel on the way aft, her luggage neatly stacked, the folio in its case for the first time in three days. Vellaine had stopped to hand him a card: *Larent Institute, if the case asks for theoretical consultation.* He had put it in his breast pocket without looking away from her face, and she had nodded the small nod that meant she found him worth the card and had decided not to say so. The Henners were bringing their daughter down the gangway now. The scholar pair. The second-class passengers after.

One man was still in third class, in cabin three-T, waiting to be told when he could disembark as himself.

Captain Joriss had the wireless open to the port magistrate. Theron could hear the click of it through the chartroom window, three decks up. He had delivered his brief report at the first-watch bell: facts only, full name of the deceased unknown pending further inquiry, matter of identity-illusion involved, passenger in third class to be held aboard pending formal interview. Joriss had listened with the expression of a man who had heard stranger things from Halverstow approaches and had decided not to say so either. Good man.

The cloth bag sat in his pocket.

He went inside to find Sable.

---

She was in the captain's day-cabin, which Joriss had opened to them as the magistrate's arrival was expected at the second bell. Two chairs and the narrow writing desk and the smell of beeswax and old charts. The porthole looked out onto the harbor — still water, the long line of fishing boats, a coal barge working the far slip. She had taken one chair; the other was waiting.

He came in and shut the door.

She looked at him steadily across the desk. He had the sense she had been waiting long enough to decide how this was going to begin.

"Joren Aldis," she said.

He heard it. The full name landed on the desk between them, unhurried.

"He had a surname," Theron said.

"He had a surname. It's in the letters. Second page, the Berric Toll correspondence — Toll uses the full name when he signs off the contract particulars. I read it at the alcove when you were reading the schedule addendum."

He had not read that far. He sat down. He put the cloth bag on the desk without deciding to.

"Joren Aldis," he said.

"Yes."

The name was different now that he had said it. That was the specific mechanics of it. Theron Mavik. Sable Verrin. Joren Aldis. There was an order to these things.

She leaned forward, elbows on the desk, hands loose. "Can I walk through it."

"Walk through it."

"All of it."

"Yes."

She looked at her hands for a beat. Then she looked at him, which was how she set herself, and began.

"The kindnesses," she said. "There were three I logged. The steward on the boarding morning — you would have heard it from the corridor; I saw it from the doorway. *Thank you. For the — all of it. The boarding business.* Not a performance. Aimed at one person. Calden went a half-second slower past cabin twelve every round after that because he had been thanked by name and it mattered."

He nodded. He had heard it.

"The second one was the second night at supper. The airsick child — there was a family in the wall-facing section, their daughter had been sick on the approach, she was in the seat nearest the galley passage.
