He took a ginger sweet from the condiment dish on his tray — the one the cook sets out for the wine courses, you don't usually eat them plain — and slid it onto the steward's tray without looking up, and Calden knew what to do with it, and the child's mother didn't see where it came from. He went on eating."

He had not seen it. He had been facing the opposite direction.

"The third was the gangway threshold," she said. "The elderly passenger on the step. He waited. No gesture, no production. Just the three seconds."

"I saw it."

"You told me you'd refiled it under idle courtesy."

"I know what I told you."

A beat. The harbor was quiet outside; a rope somewhere creaked in the gentle rock of the mooring.

"Those weren't lapses," Sable said. "He wasn't forgetting to be Vannic. He was forgetting to try." She was not telling him he was wrong to have missed it. She was not doing the thing that looked like that. She was setting down a weight, and the weight was simply heavier than it had looked. "Vannic didn't have a default toward kindness. Joren Aldis did. Every time the disguise held, he'd put the effort in. Every time he let go of the effort, he was himself. Three times. That's the shape of it."

Theron sat with it.

"The accent," she said.

"The harbor vowel."

"Sea-town vowel, flat and forward. He reached for it the first night at dinner when he was tired, said it wrong, caught himself. I didn't say anything. You file a thing like that away and don't mention it because correcting a lord at dinner is rude. I had it in the back of the room and didn't pull it." She set her right hand on the desk, the way she set things down when they had finished being held. "Coastal town. Two or three stretches it could be. Now it belongs to where Joren was from, not to where Vannic's estate is. The vowel was his."

He wrote nothing. He had no slate. He was aware of the absence of the slate in the way he had been aware, at the beginning, of the weight of the pin. He was not sure whether to name the absence out loud and decided not to.

"The callus," she said, and looked at him. "That one is yours."

"Index finger, right hand," he said. "Working-mage ridge. Not a hobby mark — the kind built over years, ten hours a day. I registered it at the handshake on the boarding morning. I filed it as a noble who practices more seriously than idle suggests."

"And he did practice seriously."

"Yes. Because he was working mage, not a noble who dabbled." He heard himself say it plainly and did not look away from it. "I had the right fact and the wrong filing. The ridge was accurate. The explanation was wrong. The man who built that callus spent years on a mage's work. Joren Aldis's hands."

"Joren Aldis's hands."

He waited.

"The fourth," she said. "You were at the table. The third dinner, the saloon on deck three." She paused for long enough that he heard the silence not as hesitation but as respect for the distance between them at that table. "He spoke about his niece. He said she had carved a small object for him when she was six, and that she would be unimpressed that he hadn't lost it."

"I heard the sentence."

"I heard the voice." She turned her left hand over, looked at it. "The voice changed. You heard the words and they went into the social texture of a man whose body was in the cabin, and you moved on. What I heard was a shift — here and then gone, the way a room shifts in temperature when someone opens a window and closes it again. That tone doesn't come from a performance. A man performing a character at a dinner does not slip sideways into that tone by accident. He slipped into it because he wasn't trying, for four seconds, and what came out was Joren."

She stopped.

"She's eight now," she said, quietly. "If she was six at the carving." Not interiority; a fact, stated plainly, for the record. "She's somewhere this morning. Her mother is somewhere."

The room was very still.

He held the stillness. He was aware of the window, the harbor, the coal barge making its slow progress across the slip. He was aware of the cloth bag on the desk. He was aware of the specific texture of the morning three days ago in the stern alcove, the bronzed light, Sable's cup turned a quarter, the way she had said the line without looking at him.

*He wasn't as bad as I expected.*

It came back to him not as her voice but as his own register of it, in the particular flat tone the part of his mind that stored things without examining them used. He had heard it. He had filed it under *small talk between two people who have been thinking about a man.* He had turned the slate over.

He had not asked why.

She had told him who Joren was — not in the language of an accusation, not as a clue she was pressing into his hand, but in the simple way she said true things when she knew she was the only person in the room who would say them. She had said *he wasn't as bad as I expected* and he had heard it and filed it and moved on to *Esherel*, and the line had waited there in its column, correctly filed, never asked about, and here they were.

He sat with it.

The time it took him to sit with it was the time it took the coal barge to clear the end of the slip through the porthole glass. He watched it. He did not say anything about the coal barge. He did not say anything about the line.

Sable watched him sit with it and said nothing, because she did not need to.

After a while, he reached for the cloth bag.

He did not open it for a moment. He held the shape of it — the handkerchief and the nib against the palm of his hand, the fish underneath them, which he could feel through the cloth by its specific weight and its specific lack of sharp edges. A small thing. Longer than it was wide, the tail end rounded, the fin line worn almost smooth where it met the body.

He untied the bag and set the handkerchief and the nib on the desk and took out the fish.

It sat in his palm.

It was pale wood — birch, maybe, or ash. The length of his middle two fingers together. The carving was careful but not precise: the fin rose at an angle that was not quite level, the tail had one side fuller than the other, the mouth was a confident groove that ran slightly deep on the left edge. The kind of confident imprecision that came from a hand that knew what it intended but was not yet old enough to have closed all the gaps between intention and execution.

An eight-year-old had made this. Six years ago. A child with a knife or a chisel and the determination to make a thing for a person she wanted to keep.

The grain of the wood was warm from the cloth.

He had logged it at the body. Outer coat pocket, between a handkerchief and a pen-nib, logged as *odd personal effect, note for return to estate*. He had held it for the thirty seconds it took to log it and put it in the bag and moved on to the geometry of the porthole latch. He had not thought about it again until the stern alcove when he had handed the bag to Calden and taken it back and put it in his own pocket, and it had been in his pocket since.

Joren Aldis had gone into the run with this in his coat pocket. Not in the luggage. On his body, on the person it reached him through the fabric of the outer coat when he reached the pocket for something, which he must have done a hundred times on the run without taking the fish out. The coat pocket with the handkerchief and the nib. The pocket you put things in when you wanted them to be findable but not on view. The pocket where a person keeps the thing they carry because it is theirs.

Sable was looking at it.

He set it on the desk between them, where she could look at it.

The fish sat there. Worn smooth at the body where hands had held it. Careful and imprecise in its carving, the fin at its small angle, the mouth with its deep left groove. An eight-year-old's handiwork, traveling.

*[Investigator — Quest updated: Solve the Death of Joren Aldis.]*

He read it. He looked at it for a longer moment than he usually looked at a System line.

Joren Aldis. Joren Aldis. The name was in the log now. The name was where the case was.

He picked up the fish again, gently, and held it.

Outside, the harbor rocked the gondola on the lines. The coal barge was gone. The fishing boats knocked softly at their moorings, and somewhere two decks up the magistrate's bell would ring at the second hour, and the work would begin again, and the name on the page would stay there.

He kept the fish in his hand.

---

# Chapter 17 — The Magistrate and the Debt

The magistrate's aide knocked at the second bell. Theron had the cloth bag in his left coat pocket and his notebook in his right, and he answered the door.

The magistrate came in behind the aide. Compact, somewhere in her fifties, grey at both temples, a port authority sash worn flat and undecorated. She looked at the room, at Theron, at the desk with its two chairs, and made a small precise nod.

"Mavik. Investigator class, Conservatory graduation — three weeks ago." She had already read the wire. "You're the one who found the body."

"Yes. Theron Mavik." He pulled the second chair out for her. "The steward found the cabin. I examined the room."

"Halved it." She sat. Her aide took up the near wall with a ledger board and a stub of chalk. "Give me the names, the facts, and the outstanding questions. Don't give me a narrative."

Theron did not give her a narrative. "The deceased is Joren Aldis. Working illusionist, no formal credentials on record, contracted by Lord Petric Vannic of estate-of-record under the name of his factor, one Berric Toll of Mirren west harbour. The contract was an identity performance: Aldis was to travel the Halverstow run as Lord Vannic; Vannic traveled separately under a false name in third class, cabin three-T, to evade an estate obligation. Aldis was found in the first-class cabin twelve at second watch, third day of the run. Time of death three to five hours prior. Cabin sealed from the interior; no wound present; cause of death not yet confirmed."

The aide's chalk moved. The magistrate's expression did not.

"Primary suspect pool," Theron continued. "Third class. One passenger — Maret Fell, cabin seven-T, east Mirren — shares a port with the factor, and her account is clean and specific in a manner consistent with preparation. The thread is not yet closed. There's a Sannholt connection in the Toll correspondence as well; not yet actioned."

"Vannic." The magistrate said the name like a line item.

"Cooperative. He'll give you the full account; he's been expecting it. He hired Aldis for the performance and met him twice — once at the factor's office in Mirren, once at dock before boarding. He has no motive I've identified for Aldis's death, and his presence in third class under a false name is adequately explained by the estate obligation."

She looked at him for a moment. "Are you keeping anything back."

"No."

"You're sure."

"I'm an Investigator. I have one active suspect lead and one outstanding location thread. Both are documented. I'll give you the case notes when this is finished."

A flat, considering pause. Then she made a short mark on her own notebook, the kind of mark that meant *acceptable, proceed*. "I'll see Vannic now."

Theron went to the door and asked the aide posted outside to bring him. When Vannic came through the cabin door, he looked exactly as he had in third class the morning they had found him: overheated, apologetic, wearing yesterday's second-best waistcoat. He had his hat in both hands.

"Magistrate," he said. "I — this is entirely my —"

"Sit down," the magistrate said. "Tell me about Berric Toll."

Vannic sat. He told her about Berric Toll. He did it in the agonized, digressive fashion of a man who had been rehearsing a story and kept catching himself adding disclaimers, and the magistrate let him go until she had what she needed and then said "stop" and he stopped and she asked the three questions that mattered and he answered them. The aide's chalk went. The magistrate wrote two lines. Theron, standing near the porthole, watched and said nothing.

At the end she said: "You hired a man under false papers to perform your legal identity on a transit gondola and that man died under circumstances still under investigation. The estate obligation question is not my office. Failure to report an identity substitution is, technically, a harbor infraction, but I am going to note the circumstances and move on. You will remain in port and available for further questions. Yes?"

"Yes," Vannic said, in the voice of a man grateful to have a yes left in him.

"Go."

He went. The door closed. The magistrate looked at Theron.

"The Fell lead," she said. "If it breaks, you have my office at the Gray Quay. I want a written report by the evening bell, Aldis on the front page, the Sannholt thread on the second."

"You'll have it."

She rose, tucked her notebook under her arm, and looked at him one more time. Not assessing. Just noting. "You're three weeks out of the Conservatory."

"Yes."

"The body was sealed in a cabin with seventeen other passengers for two days and you held the wireless silence."

"The captain held the wireless silence."

"Yes, and you asked him to." She nodded the same small precise nod. "I'll read the report."

She left.

Theron stood with his notebook and looked at the porthole and the still harbor outside it for a moment. Then he wrote *magistrate interviewed — Vannic cleared, Fell thread noted, Sannholt on file* and closed the notebook.

---

Esherel was waiting in the first-class corridor when he came out, her luggage still stacked against the bulkhead. She had not disembarked.

She looked at him and then at the spot six inches past him and said, "I'll take my introduction now."

"He's still aboard," Theron said.

"I know. I've been waiting."

He brought her to the day-cabin. Vannic was standing near the far chair, having apparently come back when the magistrate left, because Theron had not told him to go far and he had not gone far. When he saw Esherel he straightened up and took a breath, which was not the worst sign.

Theron positioned himself at the writing desk and said nothing. He had told Esherel he would be present as a witness. He was present.

Esherel set her folio on the desk, opened it to the first page, and looked at Vannic. "Forty-one thousand and five hundred crowns," she said. "Two years of unpaid back third on the southern wine contract, which was extended at your estate's request on the understanding that the Customs-error loss was under review." She turned one page. "The review concluded eighteen months ago. I have the letter."

"I — yes," Vannic said. "I know about the letter."

"The interest has continued to accumulate." She turned another page. "This is the full accounting. I'm going to leave it with you. I'd like you to read it before the end of the week."

Vannic looked at the folio the way a man looked at a summons he had already been served and was hoping might dissolve. He didn't take it from her; she set it on the desk.

"You had him hire an illusionist," she said. "To fly on a gondola in your place. While you hid in third class."

"To be fair, I didn't — I was avoiding —"

"Duke Esseven's daughter. Yes, I know. Calden told me. He told me rather quickly when I asked, which tells me either he doesn't like you or he simply treats everyone equitably." She closed the outer folio cover. "I am not interested in the betrothal. I am interested in the forty-one thousand five hundred crowns and the additional nine months of interest, which you will find itemized on the third page."

Vannic opened his mouth, looked at the folio, and shut it again.

"You've had the accounting for two years," she said. "If there's a dispute, name the specific line."

He looked at Theron. Theron looked back at him in a manner that communicated clearly that he had been introduced as a witness, not a resource.

Vannic picked up the folio.

He read the first page slowly and turned to the second and read that. He was not a fast reader. Esherel waited with the particular patience of a woman who had been waiting eighteen months for a specific page to be opened. Theron found himself watching her hands: the folio case under her elbow, her fingers still, the kind of stillness that came from having run out of things to do with restlessness and finding it inefficient.

"The interest calculation," Vannic finally said, "uses the standard rate."

"It uses the contract rate," Esherel said. "Which is slightly lower. Page three, footnote."

He found the footnote. He read it. He set the folio on the desk.

"I can't pay it from here."

"I know that."

"I mean — the estate, the board has —"

"I am aware of how estate boards function," she said. "This is a letter of acknowledgment." She produced it from the outer folio pocket and set it beside the accounting sheets. "It says: debt acknowledged, amount agreed, schedule to follow by the first of next month. It doesn't commit you to a payment date. It commits you to a number."

Vannic looked at the letter of acknowledgment. It was one page, brief, with a line for his signature and a line for his title and a line for the date, and nothing else.

"That's all," Esherel said. "Today."

He picked up the pen from the writing desk.

He looked at it for a moment. Theron had the sense that this was the first time, in the entire run of things, that Vannic had a clear lane ahead of him — one action, specific, no betrothal, no dead illusionist, no identity documents to maintain. Just a pen and a number he knew was correct.

He signed it.

Esherel took it back, read the signature, folded it into her folio. Her expression did not change except in a very small way that might have been relief and might have been the particular satisfaction of a woman putting a two-year account in order.

"Thank you," she said.

Vannic made a sound that was not quite a word. He was still holding the pen.

---

Theron left them sorting through the schedule discussion. It would take as long as it took; Esherel had both the patience and the documentation to outlast any objection Vannic could field. He went down to the gangway.

The Halverstow waterfront was fifteen feet below the gondola's lower deck. He could see the quayside factors' offices from the rail, the cargo ledgers going in and out, the day-labourers on the rope gangs, a harbor warden checking manifests at the second berth. Somewhere past the grain stores, the Gray Quay stretched east along the port. The magistrate's office would be out there. Maret Fell was somewhere out there too, if she had already disembarked.

Sable was at the foot of the gangway.

She was talking to a man in a dock-pilot's coat, older, grey-headed, with the particular solidity of someone who had worked the same fifty feet of water for twenty years. She had a small slip of paper; he was reading it with the expression of someone who preferred information condensed. Then he said something and she nodded, and he said something else and she glanced up at Theron on the deck above.

He came down the gangway. The dock boards were salt-bleached, with a smell of tar and low tide and caulk.

"This is Master Olen," Sable said, without preamble. "He works the pilot's association. He knows the Mirren run."

Theron looked at the pilot. "Berric Toll."

The pilot made a face that was not quite a confirmation and not quite its opposite. The kind of face people made when they had an opinion about a name and had learned that opinions in public about factors were bad for repeat work. "I know *of* him."

"Maret Fell. East Mirren harbour, shipping assessment."

The pilot's face shifted slightly. Less ambivalence; more consideration. "I've seen the name on the load assessments. She's done three or four runs through the east yard in the last — eight months, nine. Independent work. No standing contract that I know of."

"She's on this gondola. Traveling from Mirren to Halverstow."

"Plenty do."

"What does shipping assessment in the east yard normally look like?" Theron asked. "As work."

The pilot shrugged, unhurried. "Small-cargo verification mostly. Spot checks, load tolerances, sometimes customs-assist when the yard's short. You know somebody's load, you can find something wrong with somebody else's if you need leverage. Or it's entirely clean. Hard to say without the specific jobs." He handed the slip of paper back to Sable. "What's she done."

"We don't know yet," Theron said.

The pilot nodded. This was also, evidently, how the east yard worked. "If she's coming through Halverstow on the regular, the Gray Quay factors' board will have her name. They log all the independent assessors coming off the Mirren line. Saves time when they need one in a hurry."

Theron wrote *Gray Quay factors' board — Fell name check* in his notebook.

"The east yard," he said. "Would it overlap with the west? Berric Toll is west harbour, Mirren."

"Not naturally." The pilot considered. "The yards don't share much. Independent assessors work where the money is. If she was getting work from the west side, she'd have a reason for it. Or someone made it worth crossing."

He left it at that, in the manner of a man who had given what was freely givable and was now returning to the day's work.

Theron watched him go back along the quay. The harbor light was flat and direct, the late morning clear enough to read the names on the grain-store walls from where he stood.

Sable said, "He came off the early-morning run. Calden arranged the introduction; Joriss has the contact for the dock association on file."

"I know." He looked at the note. "She's on the factors' board, she's been in the east yard nine months, she knows Toll's name but hasn't dealt with him personally." He turned the page. "That last one came from her directly."

"Yes."

"Which is either true, or it's the kind of thing you say when you'd rather have the question stop."

Sable said nothing, which was how she agreed with something she didn't want to agree with too quickly.

He put the notebook away and looked at the magistrate's office buildings, gray-fronted beyond the cargo sheds. "The Gray Quay board," he said. "That's the next step."

"This afternoon?"

"After the written report." He looked out at the harbor — fishing boats, flat water, a pair of gulls working the far ropes. Somewhere in the hold of the gondola there was still a sealed first-class cabin with a locked-room geometry he hadn't finished explaining, and a name that was in the case log now, correctly placed.

*[Investigator — Status: Fell thread active. One address in Sannholt, one board list to check. The case is not tidier than it was. It is more accurate.]*

He read it and folded his notebook.

"Gray Quay this afternoon," he said. "Bring what you have on the Sannholt contact."

Sable tucked the empty slip of paper into her coat. "The Pessel name."

"Yes."

She nodded, once, and turned back toward the gangway.

He stood a moment longer at the quayside rail, looking at the harbor. A boat was coming in to the near berth with the particular low-riding heaviness of a full catch, and the dockers were already moving. Someone on it called out a name and someone on the quay called back, and for a moment the sound of it carried clearly across the water.

Then it didn't.

He went up the gangway to write the report.

---

# Chapter 18 — The Factors' Board

The Gray Quay office kept its ledgers in a narrow room behind the main hall, stacked on oak shelves that ran floor to ceiling along two walls. The clerk who led Theron in was young enough that he'd grown up with the shelving and moved through it without looking, pulling a bound volume of the current year's assessor logs before Theron had finished explaining what he wanted.

"Independent assessors, Mirren line," the clerk said. "You want the full year or from a specific date?"

"Nine months back."

The clerk left to deal with a cargo dispute in the outer hall.

Theron opened it.

The entries were alphabetical within month, columns for name, port of origin, job reference, and factor of record if any. Fell appeared first in the early autumn: two load assessments for a grain-charter company he didn't recognize, east Mirren origin, no factor of record. A third in the winter, same column. Then two in the spring, closer together, with a different job reference that was new: customs-assist on perishable cargo, and a notation in the margin — *refer: TL-9* — in a different hand, written smaller than the others, with a different pen.

He looked at TL-9 for a moment.

The T could be Toll.

He put his finger over the L-9 and thought about that. Then he turned to the back of the volume, where the key for factor reference codes was printed inside the back cover. TL-9 was not listed there. He looked at the columns again. There were references to TS-4, TM-11, TA-2. None of them were listed individually in the key; the key was for the standard dock-authority factors, of which there were twelve, all named. The TL-9 notation was not among the twelve.

Theron wrote *TL-9 — not in key, margin notation only, spring entries, customs-assist perishable cargo* and went back through the autumn and winter entries to check whether the same notation appeared anywhere earlier.

It did not.

*[Investigator — Insight +1. The notation is new. So is the relationship.]*

He looked at the two spring entries again. They were dated three weeks apart. Between them: nothing for Fell in the ledger. The second customs-assist job had a finish date stamped in, and the finish date was six days before the Halverstow run departed from Mirren harbor.

He wrote that down too.

The clerk came back, a tea tray now balancing on one arm, and set a cup at the edge of the reading table without being asked. Harbor clerks were efficient that way; they knew when someone was in the room for more than a quick check.

"The reference code TL-9," Theron said. "Is that a factor of record, or a job notation?"

The clerk looked at it. Something flickered in his expression — not recognition, exactly. More like the expression of someone who has seen a thing before and not asked, because the floor around it was not clearly his to walk. "That's not one of ours," he said. "We have twelve standard codes. That would have been added by whoever was verifying the job."

"Which would be?"

"The assessor, or the factor who arranged the work, if they used a secondary reference they wanted tracked."

"Can you look up who submitted this entry?"

The clerk reached for a different shelf. He found the submission ledger for the same month, ran his finger down the right column. "Both spring entries, Fell Maret. Self-submitted, east Mirren address, assessed by dock-authority standing appointee on arrival." He looked up. "The notation would have come in with the self-submission."

So she had written it herself.

Theron drank the tea. It was from the harbor-side blend that all the Gray Quay offices used, slightly over-steeped, with the faint mineral edge of water that came through old pipes. A woman with three months of east-yard work and a clean account had added a notation code that matched no standard reference, on two jobs in the spring, after a gap, and the second job ended six days before she boarded a gondola out of Mirren harbor.

"Are there other assessors with TL-9 in the logs?" he asked.

The clerk thought about that. "I can't do a full search in the time you're here. That's a month of turning pages."

"Would you be willing to flag the volume for the magistrate's office?"

A pause, the length of someone calculating which of their obligations outweighed the others. "If the magistrate's office asks formally," the clerk said, "yes."

Theron wrote *request formal flag — TL-9 reference, full year's log — magistrate's office* and underlined it. "Thank you," he said. "And the tea."

The clerk managed a brief, professional nod. He was already watching the outer hall again, where two men had arrived with a counter-claim form, which was worse than a cargo dispute.

---

Theron came out of the ledger room and stopped.

Sable was in the main hall's front window, standing at the edge of the broad counter where a harbor notary was logging in documents. She had not been there when he'd gone in. She was talking to a man in a cotton work coat, older, wearing the canvas cap that most of the long-serving dock laborers favored in Halverstow, with a band sewn at the brim from a gondola-line company. Not one of the pilots. A deckhand or a loader, by the look of the hands.

She saw Theron and gave the small chin-tilt that meant *one moment*.

He stood at the hall window and looked out at the quay. The afternoon was well into itself; the cargo traffic had thinned. Someone had chalked a berth number on the gray post-stone outside, and a third-year courier was arguing with a porter about who had the right to re-chalk it.

Sable came across the hall. "His name is Pell," she said. "He loads for the Halverstow-Sannholt post-coach line. Came over when he saw me looking at the route board."

"Looking for what?"

"The coach schedule. The Sannholt run."

He turned from the window. She had a folded slip in one hand, the same size as the one she'd given Master Olen that morning. "How do we contact Pessel at the Copper Finch?"

"Standard letter. The coach carries mail through to Sannholt twice a week — Tuesdays and Fridays. Pell says there's a factor's clearing house at the south Halverstow station that handles forwarding." She held out the slip. On it she had written a station address, a box number, and the name of the clearing-house register. "If we put a letter in with tomorrow's run, it reaches Sannholt in three to four days. Pessel would have it before the end of the week."

Theron looked at the address. "That's the Sannholt thread," he said. "First formal step."

"Pell says the clearing house also handles return. If Pessel writes back, it comes to the same box."

He folded the slip and put it in his notebook. "You'll draft the letter?"

"You'll draft the letter. I'll read it."

He could not argue with this, which was probably why she said it that way. He made a note under the TL-9 notation: *Sannholt letter — clearing house box, Tuesday run, draft tonight*.

*[Investigator — Status: Two open threads, two forwarding steps. The case is moving. You are writing a letter.]*

Theron looked at the System line for a moment, decided it had earned the observation, and closed the notebook.

---

Outside the Gray Quay office, the quayside opened up into the broader harbor walk. The light was the particular flat yellow of a Halverstow afternoon in the middle of the working week: not the bright angle of morning, but the lower, settling kind that put long shadows across the dock-stone and gave the water a dull pewter sheen. The harbor smell was different now than it had been at the gangway that morning: less tide, more tar and rope-salt, with the faint sweetness of unloaded grain from the east stores. A pair of dockers were moving a hand-trolley with a stubborn wheel along the near edge of the stone. From further up the quay came the percussion of a steam-crane doing intermittent work, each lift and drop spaced with enough silence between that it didn't become noise.

"She wrote in the TL-9 reference herself," Theron said. "Self-submitted."

Sable had her hands in her coat pockets. "Which means she knew what it stood for."

"And chose to record it, or had to because the work couldn't be submitted clean without it."

"What's TL-9?"

"Not a standard code. It's not in the key." He turned the page to his notes. "The clerk couldn't cross-reference without a month of page-turning; I've asked him to flag it for the magistrate. But it only appears on the two spring entries, both customs-assist. After a gap from the autumn and winter work."

"Which is clean."

"Which is clean and has no notation. The notation appears on the jobs that are closer to the departure date."

Sable walked a few paces without speaking. The dockers moved past them with the trolley, the stubborn wheel scraping in its set groove.

"So she does regular east-yard work," she said at last. "Clean for most of it. Then something changes in the spring — she picks up two jobs with a reference she writes in herself and doesn't explain, and the second one finishes six days before she boards out of Mirren."

"Yes."

"We don't know if TL-9 is Toll."

"We don't know." He put the notebook away. "But the T fits. And Toll has a west-yard presence, and she's been in the east yard, and Master Olen said crossing them would need a reason." He looked back at the magistrate's office buildings, gray-fronted in the afternoon. "The magistrate's flag will answer it, when it comes."

Sable came level with him. "That's a day. Possibly two."

"Yes." He looked at the harbor. A barge was coming around the breakwater arm, deep-loaded and patient, and the harbor master's launch was out to meet it. "The Fell thread is real. We just can't walk it further today."

The barge moved on its steady course. The launch paced it.

Theron was watching the water and not quite watching it, the way you watched something when you were thinking about something else. The TL-9 notation sat in his notes between two known facts and one gap. The notation was new: spring only, absent from the autumn and winter entries. Newness in a working pattern was a change, and changes needed causes. Either the nature of the work had changed, or the relationship behind it had. The six-day gap between Fell's second customs-assist finish date and the gondola's departure from Mirren harbor was the kind of gap that could be coincidence, or the kind that was a preparation window. He did not write that down. It was not yet a claim; it was a shape.

He had the TL-9 notation. He had the six-day gap. He had the factors' board flagged and the Sannholt letter drafted in his head. He had the correct name in the case log, and the case log was where it should be.

"The post-coach runs Tuesday and Friday," he said, more to the water than to Sable. "That's a two-day window for Pessel to receive the letter, three to four days for a reply to come back through the same box."

"If Pessel writes promptly."

"If Pessel writes at all." He turned from the harbor. The steam-crane up the quay did another lift, the chain going taut with a sound like a note held too long. "I'll write the letter tonight. You can tell me what's missing."

Sable made no particular response to this, which was its own kind of answer.

---

The day had gone long. By the time they came back along the harbor walk toward the gondola, the late afternoon light was settling in over the waterfront, and the Wyrm's mooring ropes cast long shadows across the dock boards. The overnight berth crew had come on; the day-labourers were packing up. At the cargo shed corner, someone had left an empty tea crate turned upside down with a lantern balanced on it, not yet lit.

Sable stopped at the bottom of the gangway.

She looked at the harbor, at the water moving under the quay boards, at the direction of the Halverstow postal district. Not at Theron.

"Someone has to tell his family properly," she said. "In person. Before any letter from the port office gets there."

He heard it. He stood at the gangway foot and did not say anything.

She turned and went up the gangway.

Theron waited one moment. The harbor water moved under the boards. The unlit lantern sat on its crate, patient.

Then he followed her up.

---

# Chapter 19 — The Reply from Berric Toll

Theron was two pages into a fair copy of the magistrate's supplemental request when the messenger arrived.

He had requisitioned a small table in the harbourmaster's waiting room that morning — not an office, just a table with two chairs and a lamp that pulled sideways on its gooseneck — and had been there long enough that the staff no longer looked up when he crossed the main desk. The letter to Pessel at the Copper Finch had gone in Tuesday's post-coach run the evening before. The TL-9 flag was in at the magistrate's office. Both threads were now moving without him, which meant the morning belonged to paperwork, and Theron, despite everything the Academy had said about field work, was better at paperwork than almost anything else.

The messenger was twelve, the kind of twelve that had done this many times, and set the sealed packet on the table beside Theron's inkwell without ceremony. The wax impression on the seal was the magistrate's stamp, which meant it had come through their office rather than directly from the factors' board. Theron looked at the time of the gooseneck lamp — the oil reservoir had a calibrated scale on the side, and he had started it at second watch — and calculated that the flag had turned over in less than two days.

He thanked the boy, who left, and broke the seal.

*[Investigator — Insight +1. Faster than expected. Someone in the magistrate's office recognized the notation.]*

The packet held three sheets. The first was a cover from the clerk, formal and brief: the TL-9 flag had been cross-referenced at the magistrate's request through the harbor customs register, the bonded factor ledger for the current year, and the Mirren district trade correspondence file. The results were attached. The clerk had underlined the result in the second sheet himself, which was unusual enough that Theron looked at the underline first.

*TL-9 — private factor shorthand, registered informally to Berric Toll, west harbour, Mirren, as of the 14th of the third month of this year.*

He read it twice.

Then he set the second sheet beside the first and read the third. It was shorter: a supplemental note from the magistrate's own hand, not the clerk's. *The registration is informal — Toll submitted it to the bonded factor registry as a shorthand for his current network of sub-contracts, but it was never formally assigned. An informal code, used by Toll's subcontractors to self-identify work arranged through his network. The bonded registry accepted it as a notation of record, not a license. Advise whether the Fell matter requires a warrant inquiry.*

Theron put the three sheets together and aligned them by their top edge.

So Maret Fell had been doing work for Berric Toll's network. Had been, at minimum, self-identifying as a subcontractor of Toll's when she logged her spring jobs into the Gray Quay board — either because she was required to, or because she wanted the notation on record. Either way, the connection was no longer a guess about one letter in a code. It was confirmed, if not yet explained.

He wrote in his notebook: *TL-9 confirmed — Toll private shorthand, informally registered, third month of this year. Fell used it on two spring customs-assist jobs. Second job finished six days before departure from Mirren.* He added a box around that last sentence. Then he sat back and thought about the magistrate's question at the bottom of the third sheet.

A warrant inquiry meant naming Fell as a person of formal interest. That was a step he didn't have the standing to compel from here. The magistrate could, if Theron asked and the case held enough weight. But a warrant also meant Fell knew she was named, wherever she was, and the Fell thread was currently running on the assumption that she didn't.

He wrote: *Do not recommend warrant at this stage. Request: can the magistrate's office locate Fell in Halverstow — without formal contact? Determine whether she is still in the city.* He folded the note into the front of his notebook for later.

---

Lord Petric Vannic was still in port.

Theron knew this because the magistrate's hold-for-questions letter was still valid and the harbor master had confirmed that morning that no private passage had been booked under Vannic's name or any variant of Petor Vann. He knew, too, that Vannic had not used the first two days of his enforced stay to make himself useful, which was perhaps consistent with Vannic's overall character, and that the man was currently lodged at the Halverstow Arms on the harbor-facing side, which cost approximately three times what it should.

What Theron did not know was what Vannic had not told him on the gondola.

He found him at noon, in the Arms' street-facing parlour room, eating what might charitably be called a late breakfast. Vannic looked up when Theron came in, and the expression that crossed his face was a sequence — relief that it wasn't anyone official, wariness that it was Theron, and then a performance of ease that sat slightly wrong, like a collar adjusted in a mirror just before the mirror was taken away.

"Mavik," he said. "I wasn't expecting you."

"No," Theron agreed, and sat.

The parlour was mostly empty at this hour. A second table had a man reading a shipping gazette, boots off, feet resting on his own empty chair. The Arms' parlour was that kind of room — comfortable enough to stay in, public enough not to need explaining.

Vannic's plate held a soft-boiled egg, a small pot of something preserved, and a bread roll that he had been tearing apart rather than eating. His coffee was already cold; the steam had been gone from it for some time. He picked up the roll fragment nearest to him and put it down again.

"Theron," Vannic said, with the tone of someone about to say something he'd been practicing, "I want you to know that I understand I'm in a difficult position."

"You're in a straightforward position," Theron said. "You hired a man and he died on the arrangement you set up. The straightforward position is that anything useful you know, you tell me."

Vannic looked at the cold coffee. A muscle in his jaw moved. "I've told you everything about the arrangement."

"You've told me the arrangement's shape. Toll, the factor's office, two meetings, the correspondence packet." Theron set his notebook on the table, but didn't open it. "What you haven't told me is anything about Joren. About him specifically."

A pause. Theron counted to five in his head, which was the interval his practicum supervisor had once told him held more diagnostic weight than any question.

At four, Vannic said: "He was careful."

Theron waited.

"I don't mean careful like he was nervous. I mean careful like — a craftsman." Vannic picked up the coffee and looked at it and set it down. "When we met at the factor's office, he was already there when I arrived. He'd looked up the records. My manners, my typical port, the way Vannic —" he stopped, made a small, uncomfortable gesture that acknowledged the strangeness of the third person — "the way *I* dress, what I eat at public functions. Not just the face. He had a whole file."

"From whom?"

"I assumed Toll. He didn't say." Vannic broke off another piece of roll without eating it. "He asked me questions. What my posture was like, whether I had any habits that were specifically mine and not just class habits. I'd expected someone who would look at me and cast something. He actually took notes."

Theron wrote: *Joren — prepared notes on Vannic's specific mannerisms, distinct from class habits. Consulted records before the factor's office meeting.* "What kind of questions?"

"Small ones. Whether I gestured when I was bored. Whether I held my glass differently when I was tired. Whether there was a way I arranged my desk items when I was anxious." Vannic finally ate a piece of bread roll. "He said he'd spent two years doing a magistrate's council secretary. Said you had to hold the performance in the small things, because anyone could learn to walk like a lord but only a person knew how to sit like one."

"A magistrate's council secretary." Theron looked up from his notebook. "In which district?"

"He didn't specify. He mentioned it in passing — by way of explaining his method." Vannic rubbed the back of his neck. "He also said something else. At the dock, when we were doing the last check. He said —" Another pause, different in quality from the first. This one had something in it. "He said, *if anyone asks about Lord Vannic during the passage, tell them I'm difficult to find*." Vannic looked at the table. "I thought he meant me. If anyone in third class somehow connected me to him."

Theron was very still. "But he said it to you about himself."

"I think so. Now. I didn't think about it then." Vannic's voice had shifted — the performed ease was gone, and what was under it was not pleasant to look at, and not because Vannic was dramatic about it. It was the face of a man realizing that a joke he'd laughed at earlier had not been a joke. "He said it like — like it was a standard precaution. Like someone had already asked about him before, and it had not been academic."

*[Investigator — Insight +2. The warning was active intelligence, not professional habit. Someone was already in Toll's network when Joren took the contract.]*

Theron looked at the System line and thought about the six days. Fell's second customs-assist job ending six days before departure. The observation window question on the first morning of the flight. Twenty minutes at the shared window on the second evening.

He wrote: *Joren's warning re: Lord Vannic — suggests prior awareness of surveillance or contact. Possibly knew he was being tracked through the Toll network.* Then underneath, in a separate line: *Did Fell and Joren know each other? Or did Fell only know the contract?*

He did not write the next question out, because it was the one that would need Fell in a room to answer, and she was not currently in a room.

"Is there anything else?" he said. "About Joren directly. Any other conversation, any other thing you noticed about him."

Vannic considered. He looked, for a moment, like he was actually thinking rather than managing what he was saying, which Theron had not seen from him before and noted accordingly. "He was — kind," Vannic said at last. "Not to me specifically. I just noticed it. At the dock, when we were going over the last details, one of the handlers dropped a stack of crate-checks and they scattered everywhere. Joren helped him pick them up. In character — in the voice, doing the lord thing — but he helped."

Vannic picked up the cold coffee and drank some of it, then made a small, private expression about its temperature that he did not appear to notice he was making. "I thought it was a choice about maintaining the performance. But he did it before he'd put on the voice for anyone. It was just what he did."

Theron did not write that down. He already knew that about Joren. He let the pause run for a moment, then closed the notebook.

"Thank you," he said. He stood. "Don't leave Halverstow before the magistrate's office releases you."

"I know," Vannic said. He was looking at the cold coffee. He seemed, for the moment, no longer bothered by the instruction.

---

Sable was at the harbor agent's table in the harbourmaster's hall when Theron came back from the Arms, making notes from a route schedule the agent had spread out for her. She looked up, did the brief visual check that Theron had come to understand meant *anything urgent*, and returned to the schedule when he shook his head.

He waited.

She thanked the agent, rolled the schedule, handed it back, and came across to where he was standing by the hall window with the notebook open on his palm.

"Vannic gave me something," he said. "Joren warned him on the dock. If anyone asks about Lord Vannic during the passage, tell them I'm difficult to find."

Sable looked at the harbor through the window. A moment passed. "He knew someone was looking."

"He suspected. Or he'd been warned. Either way, the precaution was specific." He showed her the notebook. "The TL-9 result came this morning. Confirmed. Toll's network notation, informally registered this spring. Fell was self-identifying herself as a Toll subcontractor on those customs-assist jobs."

Sable read the relevant lines, turned the page, read the next entry. She did not read quickly; she read the way she looked at things, which was at a slight angle, as though the center of a page was less useful than its edges. When she was done she gave the notebook back. "So Fell was working for Toll, Joren was hired through Toll, and Joren knew someone was tracking him through Toll's network."

"It's a triangle," Theron said. "Three points. We have the shape of it."

"We don't have how they connect."

"We don't." He looked at the harbor, at the deep-water berths where the Wyrm was still moored. "The magistrate has the flag. I've asked them to locate Fell in the city — quietly — before we move further."

Sable looked at him. The look had a question in it that she didn't say, which was the version she used when the answer was already in the room.

He found it. "If she's still here, we talk to her. Directly, with what we know. The confirmed Toll connection gives me something to put on the table — she self-submitted that notation, she knew what it was. She can choose to tell us what TL-9 was for on those jobs, or she can say nothing and the magistrate files a warrant."

"And if she's left the city."

"Then the magistrate's warrant is the next step anyway." He put the notebook in his coat. "Either way, the Sannholt letter is in. If Pessel knows something about Joren's contacts in the last months, it comes back to us through the clearing house."

Sable nodded. She looked at the route schedule she'd been studying, still thinking about it, which meant she was still thinking about the thing next to it. "How long is Vannic in port?" she asked.

"Until the magistrate releases him."

"And he's at the Arms."

"Third floor, harbor-facing, which he presumably chose for the view."

The corner of her mouth moved. "Does he know Fell's name?"

Theron stopped. He had not asked that. It was a clean question and he had not asked it, which was the gap between his method and hers that had not closed over the course of the case. "I don't know," he said. "I'll go back."

"Don't," Sable said. "Write him a note. Ask one question. Don't sit with him again today — he's in the mode where he wants to confess things, and if you give him the room he'll confess things that haven't happened yet."

Theron looked at her.

"One note," she said. "Yes or no. Did Toll name any of the network to him when they arranged the contract."

He wrote it in the notebook and tore out the page. "You're better at this than I am," he said, which was something he had not said out loud before, in this register, without qualifying it.

Sable took the note and folded it. "You're better at knowing what the question is. I'm better at knowing when to ask it." She put the note in her pocket. "I'll have it sent to the Arms."

*[Investigator — Status: Fell/Toll thread confirmed. Joren's awareness noted. Next step: location of Fell. Thread is narrowing.]*

Theron read the System line. The case log had not been this ordered since the early morning runs on the gondola, when everything had been wrong in an organized way. Now the organization was in the right direction.

"The Sannholt thread," he said. "If the reply from Pessel comes back before the magistrate locates Fell."

"Then we have two threads arriving at once." Sable was already walking toward the side hall where the outgoing messages were handled. "Write the summary for the magistrate tonight. Clean copy, facts only. Give them Vannic's dock conversation as supplemental."

He was making a note of that when she stopped at the hall entrance and looked back. "Theron. Joren's name should be in the header."

He had already written *Case of Joren Aldis* at the top of the page. He showed her.

She turned and went through to the outgoing messages. He watched the doorway for a moment, then looked at the summary in his notebook, then at the harbor through the window glass where the deep-water berths held the familiar silhouette of the Wyrm against the late-afternoon quay.

The case had the right name in it. The thread had the right shape. Tomorrow or the day after, Fell would be found or would not be found, and either answer moved the work forward.

He pulled the notebook into his coat and went to write the clean copy.

---

# Chapter 20 — The Peacock in the Rain

Theron was sharpening a pencil when the reply came.

He had borrowed a knife from the harbourmaster's writing table and was working around the cedar barrel in slow even passes when the boy appeared at the door with a folded note and the flat look of someone who had been told not to wait for an answer. Theron thanked him, took the note, and went back to the pencil while he read.

The writing was Vannic's: heavily pressured, slightly tilted, the hand of someone who had been educated for signatures rather than correspondence. It said, in its entirety: *Yes. One name. Roven. Come if you need more — I don't want to put it in writing.*

He turned the pencil point against the lamp glass to check the tip, blew the shaving from it, and read the note again.

*Roven.*

A subcontractor's name. Someone inside the Toll network that Toll himself had seen fit to name to Vannic when the contract was arranged — which meant either Roven was notable enough to mention, or Roven was the one who would handle the arrangement if anything went wrong.

He put on his coat.

---

The Halverstow Arms was doing a steady noon business in the ground floor taproom — dock brokers, a pair of customs clerks at the far end of the bench, a woman at the corner table who was eating fried fish and reading a letter and ignoring everyone with the practiced efficiency of a person who ate alone by preference. Theron went up the stairs to the harbor-facing rooms without being stopped.

Vannic opened on the second knock. He was fully dressed, which was presumably an improvement over some previous condition, and had the careful stillness of a man who had been waiting and was now pretending not to have been.

"Mavik," he said, and stepped back from the door.

The room was large — harbor-facing, as reported, with a window looking out over the deep-water berths where the Wyrm's lines were still visible from the right angle. The bed was made. The writing desk had a stack of correspondence on it, half-sorted, with one letter set apart from the others under a paperweight shaped like a small brass anchor. A second chair had been pulled to the window at some point and never returned.

Vannic sat on the edge of the desk. He gestured at the window chair by way of invitation and apparently noticed as he did it that it was at the wrong angle, and made no move to correct this, and then appeared to notice that he'd noticed. He looked at his hands instead.

Theron sat in the window chair at the wrong angle.

"One name," he said. "Roven."

"Yes." Vannic looked at his own hands. "When Toll presented the contract — in the office, just us and Toll and the clerk who notarized it — he said the arrangement had been handled through someone he called Roven. That this was the person who had found Joren and vouched for him. He said it by way of reassurance. That the man working my passage had been verified by someone in the network."

"He gave you Roven's full name?"

"Just Roven. I assumed it was a surname. He didn't offer a first name and I didn't ask." Vannic's jaw tightened a fraction. "I know I should have. I know a great deal of things I should have asked, in hindsight."

Outside the window, a cargo barge was clearing the Wyrm's berth on the slow outgoing tide, its crew moving in the loose, unhurried way of men doing a routine they had done enough times to stop thinking about it. Theron watched it for a moment.

"Toll named Roven specifically," he said, thinking aloud — not to Vannic, but not hiding it from him either. "Not just 'we have people who verify.' He named this particular person."

"He said Roven had worked with Joren before. Several times. That the pairing was reliable."

Theron turned back from the window.

"He said that precisely? That Roven and Joren had worked together before?"

Vannic's expression shifted — the particular shift of someone recalling something more clearly than they'd wanted to. "He said — I remember because it was the kind of thing you hear at an auction preview, when a dealer is explaining provenance — he said, 'Roven and your man have run work together. The relationship is established.' Then he moved to the fee schedule." Vannic spread his hands slightly. "I thought it was professional reassurance. Like telling a client the surgeon and the anaesthetist have worked together before."

*[Investigator — Insight +1. Roven is not just a network contact. Roven knew Joren's working style. The relationship was already established.]*

Theron read the System line and thought about the Fell thread and the shape of the triangle and the one point of the triangle he couldn't yet see clearly. "Toll also named Joren to you. By name, or by description?"

"By name. Joren. Just the one name." Vannic rubbed the back of his neck. "I asked, when the contract was drawn up, whether I'd have a way to verify the arrangement was working. Whether anyone would be watching to ensure the performance held. Toll said Roven would be aware of the passage. Those were his words. 'Roven will be aware of the passage.'"

The room was quiet for a moment. From below, the taproom sounds drifted up — the clatter of a tray, a brief burst of laughter from the dock brokers' end.

*Roven will be aware of the passage.*

"Was it stated as oversight?" Theron said. "Or as protection?"

Vannic blinked. The question had apparently arrived from a direction he hadn't anticipated. "I — I assumed oversight. Of Joren. Of the performance."

"But Toll didn't say which direction the awareness ran."

Another pause. Vannic looked at the floor, at the worn center stripe of the carpet where someone had walked back and forth between door and window long enough to thin it. "No," he said at last. "He didn't." He looked up. "You think Roven was there to watch the passage for something other than — quality control."

"I think," Theron said carefully, "that Joren warned you on the dock that if anyone asked about Lord Vannic, you should say he was difficult to find. And Toll told you Roven would be aware of the passage. And Roven had worked with Joren before." He stood. "Those three facts are in the same shape. I don't know what shape that is yet."

Vannic was quiet. He had the look now of a man rearranging furniture in a room and discovering that every configuration left something blocking the door.

"What happens to the Vannic estate," he said, not quite a question, "when this resolves. When you find whoever — when this ends."

"That's outside my class assignment." Theron picked up his hat. "The magistrate handles the estate implications. You should have your factor write to them."

"My factor," Vannic said, "is Berric Toll."

Theron stopped.

Vannic made the gesture again — the small, uncomfortable spread of hands that acknowledged the shape of his own situation with minimal drama. "He's been my factor for three years. The gondola arrangement was a separate thing — I went to him precisely because the arrangement was separate. But yes. He manages the Vannic accounts."

"Get a different factor," Theron said. "Today, if you can. Ask the magistrate's office for a referral — they maintain a list of bonded agents outside active case connections. Do not send correspondence to Toll while this investigation is running."

Vannic nodded slowly. He was not performing anything now. He just looked tired, and somewhat chastened, and also like a man who had eaten half a bread roll this morning and not replaced it.

"The Sannholt matter," Theron said, pausing at the door. "Joren's contact in Sannholt. If you had any dealings with anyone on the south coast through Toll — any factor's referrals, any names that came through that account — I need those."

"I don't — I haven't done business in Sannholt." Vannic frowned. "Is that where it connects?"

"Possibly. I don't know yet." Theron opened the door, then stopped with it half open. Something was still sitting at the edge of the afternoon's accounting, and he turned back. "Vannic. When Toll said 'Roven and your man have run work together' — did he say it as though Roven was senior to Joren in the network? Or as equals?"

Vannic thought about it. He was doing this more carefully now than he had been two days ago, and Theron noted it without mentioning it. "Senior," Vannic said at last. "Definitely. The way Toll described it — Roven was the one who made the evaluation. Not the other way."

Theron filed that. "Thank you," he said, and went.

---

Sable was where he'd left her, at the far end of the harbourmaster's main hall, having a conversation with the same harbor agent as the day before that was apparently still going. She was looking at a chart this time rather than a route schedule. When Theron came in she did not look up, but she angled the chart slightly so he could see it from where he'd stopped — a reflex, he had come to understand, that meant *I'm not done but you can start.*

He waited. The agent finished the point he was making, tapped a distance marking on the chart, and Sable thanked him and took her notes and folded them.

"Roven," Theron said when the agent had gone. "A name in Toll's network. Senior to Joren. Toll told Vannic when the contract was arranged that Roven had worked with Joren before and would be 'aware of the passage.'"

Sable looked at the folded notes in her hand. "Aware. Not managing."

"Precisely."

"So Toll told his own client that a third party would be watching the arrangement." She set the notes in her satchel. "Why would Toll do that?"

"I've been asking that." He sat in one of the hall chairs. "If it was quality control — reassurance that the performance would hold — you don't name the watcher. You say we have protocols. You name someone when you want the client to know the name."

"Or when you want the client to feel safe. To feel like the network is watching in the direction of Joren, not the client."

Theron looked at her. "Vannic could have bolted. At any point. If he panicked about the contract, canceled the passage —"

"The arrangement collapses, the job fails, Roven's relationship with Joren becomes a liability." Sable had the considering expression she wore when she was reading something no one else had written. "But if Vannic believes Roven is watching Joren, he stays calm. The passage runs."

"And if Roven was actually watching the passage from inside it —"

"Then Roven knew exactly where Joren was," she said. "For the duration."

The hall had the particular late-afternoon quiet of a working building where the morning's urgency had passed and the evening's hadn't arrived yet. A clerk at the front desk was sorting incoming packages by size with the focused satisfaction of someone who preferred tasks with clear endpoints.

"Fell," Theron said. "The magistrate's office hasn't placed her in the city yet. If she's already gone —"

"She's still here." Sable said it without inflection, which meant she had information she hadn't presented yet. She reached into her satchel and produced a folded note, smaller than the one the morning messenger had brought.

He took it. It was from the magistrate's constable — four lines, plain hand, very clear: *Fell, M., observed yesterday noon at the harbor-agent registry on Dockwater Lane. Registered as a transit assessor for a cargo verification scheduled three days from now. She is booked at the Compass and Beam inn, south harbor quarter.*

He read it twice, the way he always read things that changed the shape of what he was doing. "She's still working."

"She's still here and she's taken a new job." Sable's tone was not quite dry but it was close. "Either she doesn't know how close the thread is, or she does know and she's decided staying visible is safer than moving."

"The second," Theron said. "If she knew the thread was loose, she'd be gone. She's stayed because she thinks she's clean on the gondola record."

"Which she is, by the factors' board." Sable paused. "She's not clean on TL-9."

"No." He stood. "I should write to the magistrate. Request a formal introduction — not a warrant, an interview. With the TL-9 notation on the table." He was already working through the shape of it: what to lead with, how much to show Fell before asking the thing he actually needed, which was how Roven connected to a dead man in a sealed cabin and a six-day gap before departure from Mirren. "Tomorrow morning, at the magistrate's office. Neutral ground."

"Take the full TL-9 documentation," Sable said. "Not a summary. The actual notation in the factors' board ledger — ask the clerk to make a copy. If Fell sees her own handwriting identified as Toll's shorthand, the conversation starts in a different place."

*[Investigator — Insight +1. Evidence first, theory second. The notation is indisputable. Start there.]*

He made a note. The System had caught up to Sable three seconds after she'd said it, which was about its usual interval.

"The name Roven," Sable said. "You're going to put it to Fell."

"Not first." He was writing. "First we establish the Toll connection and let her choose her answer. If she chooses to be cooperative, I ask about Roven then." He looked up. "If she knows that name — if she goes still when she hears it — that's a different conversation."

Sable was looking at the harbor chart she'd left on the hall table, not reading it now, just looking at the shape of it. "Roven," she said quietly, by way of thinking the word rather than asking a question. "Senior to Joren in the network. Toll names him to Vannic as a guarantor. He's 'aware of the passage.'"

"Three points," Theron said.

"Four." She turned from the chart. "You, Joren, Toll, and the person Toll pointed at as the one holding the arrangement together. Roven is in the middle of this. Not on the edge of it."

He looked at what he'd written. The triangle he'd drawn in yesterday's notebook wasn't a triangle anymore; it was a shape with a fourth point, and the fourth point had a line running from Toll to Joren to the sealed cabin on the Skybound Wyrm.

"Fell tomorrow," he said. "And the Sannholt reply."

"The coach clears in three to four days. If Pessel writes back immediately —" She did the arithmetic. "Possibly Friday. Possibly Saturday."

"Possibly Friday." He closed the notebook. The case had the shape now of something that was almost ready to hold its own weight — not resolved, but coherent, the way a building under construction reaches the point where you can see what it's going to be, even through the scaffolding. "We need Fell's account and Roven's name in the same room."

"Start with Fell," Sable said. "The rest follows, or it doesn't." She gathered her satchel. "Write the magistrate's note now. If you send it before fifth watch they'll have it before morning."

He was already writing.

The harbor sounds came in through the high windows — the long pull of the tide against the quayside, the chain-ring of a mooring line, somewhere a drake calling over the water in its low carrying tone, nothing urgent, just the noise of a working port at the end of a working day. Theron finished the note, sanded it, folded it, and pushed back his chair.

He had Roven's name. He had Fell's location. He had the shape of the fourth point and a meeting in the morning.

It was enough for today.

---

# Chapter 21 — The Fell Interview

The clerk's ink-brush was making a noise.

Theron had been sitting at the side table in the magistrate's outer office for five minutes waiting for Fell to be brought through, and for four of those minutes the clerk had been copying something into a register, and the brush was dragging on the paper in the particular way of a brush that had been re-inked too recently. It set his teeth on edge. He moved his case slate an inch to the right and stopped noticing it.

The TL-9 notation was already on the table. Not his summary. The clerk-made copy from the factors' board, exact to the original, with Fell's own self-submitted hand visible in the margin column. Sable had suggested the copy; he had taken it. He was thinking now, as he looked at it, that she had been right about where the conversation would start when Fell recognized her own annotation.

The anteroom door opened. Fell came in ahead of the constable, and if she was surprised to find two people waiting she didn't show it.

She wore the same coat as the factors' board day, or one like it — dark wool, well-kept, buttoning to the collar. She looked at the table, at the slate, at Sable's notebook, and at Theron, in that order, with the calm professional inventory of someone accustomed to walking into rooms where not everyone was glad to see her. She sat when the constable indicated the chair.

"Maret Fell," Theron said. "Thank you for coming."

"I was asked," she said. Not *summoned*, not *required*. Asked. The word doing its small work.

---

The first ten minutes established the facts both parties already knew they would establish: her name, her professional history, her listing in the Gray Quay factors' board. Clean autumn and winter work in the east Mirren assessment yards. Two spring customs-assist jobs. The kind of interview she had apparently done before, if not this specific one, and she took each question in its correct shape and answered it in the same shape, not more.

Theron let her finish each answer before he moved.

"The two spring customs-assist jobs," he said, and pushed the copy of the factors' board notation across the table.

Fell looked at it. She took the same length of time to read it that a person with no reason to be concerned about it would take. Then she looked at Theron.

"TL-9," he said. "Not a standard dock-authority code. Not in the Gray Quay key. Your notation. In your hand."

"The first job used a customs broker who ran his own internal reference codes," Fell said. "I picked up the notation from him and applied it to the second job for cross-reference. A sloppy habit." She moved the paper an inch back toward him. "If it's created a filing difficulty —"

"The broker's name."

A pause. Brief, calibrated. Not the pause of someone caught, but of someone deciding something.

"Berric Toll." Her hands, which had been flat on the table, moved to stack themselves, one over the other, in her lap. "West harbour, Mirren."

"How did you come to work customs-assist for Berric Toll?"

"A referral through the yard. East Mirren assessment, as I said. There was a job in spring — a cargo manifest discrepancy, customs-side — that came through two offices. I was the faster turnaround. Toll's office contracted for a second job directly after."

Theron looked at her. She met it without difficulty.

"You listed yourself as Toll's subcontractor on both jobs."

"I used his reference code," she said. "As a broker's shorthand. I wouldn't describe myself as a subcontractor."

*[Investigator — Insight +1. She's distinguishing the administrative fact from the professional relationship. The distinction may be genuine or may be load-bearing.]*

"Did you know a man named Joren Aldis?"

It lasted perhaps two seconds, and then she said, "I worked through Toll's office on two jobs. I didn't know his other contacts."

"That isn't the question I asked."

Fell looked at the copy of the notation. Then she looked at Theron, and there was something behind the professional guard now that he hadn't seen in the cabin-seven interview on the Wyrm — something that was deciding, the same way she'd decided before she named Toll, but taking longer. "I knew the name," she said at last. "By reputation. Through the yard circuit. A working illusionist who did the kind of contract work that took him off the coast occasionally."

"What kind of contract work?"

"The kind that didn't stay in the yards." Her tone was flat. Not hostile. Flat. The flatness of a person choosing neutral ground and standing on it.

"Did Toll tell you who else he had working the passage?"

"Toll didn't brief me on the passage. I did customs-assist in spring. The gondola run was separate."

"Did you see Joren Aldis on the Skybound Wyrm?"

A second pause. Different from the first: not deciding, more like landing somewhere she'd been expecting to land. "I saw a man in first class. I was in third. I didn't know who the first-class passengers were."

"But you knew you were on the same passage as someone from Toll's network."

Fell's jaw moved slightly. The first departure from the controlled register. "No one told me that."

"Did you work out it was likely?"

The room was quiet for a moment. Through the office wall came the sound of the outer register: the clerk's brush, the scratch and drag of it.

"I had the TL-9 reference from the spring jobs," she said, choosing her words with the care of someone navigating a credible story through a narrow channel. "I knew the passage had gone through Toll's office. Not in what capacity. I didn't ask."

*She knew.* Theron kept the thought off his face. What she'd said was almost true, and the almost was everything.

He set his pencil down.

"Roven," he said.

She went still.

Not the calibrated stillness of the Joren question. That had been a controlled response. This was the stillness of someone who had just heard a word in a place it should not have been, and was processing the fact of its presence before the rest of the thinking could proceed. Her hands, in her lap, stopped. Her gaze, which had been moving between him and the table, stopped.

Then she looked at Sable.

Sable had not moved, had said nothing since the interview began, and met the look with the same steady attention she'd given the room since they sat down.

Fell looked back at Theron.

"I don't know that name," she said.

"That's interesting," he said, "because you've just given me a very different answer to this question than you gave to every other one."

The silence that followed was not empty. Fell was in it, doing something, measuring something, and Theron let it run. There was a technique his instructor at the Conservatory had called *giving the space to the other person*, and it had felt artificial in training and felt entirely natural now. He waited.

"Roven," Fell said at last, and her voice had changed register. Not warmer. Careful in a different way. "Is a name in the network. Senior, I was given to understand, to most of the contacts working that side of the coast. I have never dealt with Roven directly." She paused. "I don't know where Roven is, if that's the next question."

"It was going to be."

"Then I've saved you it." She looked at the table. "What I know about Roven is what you learn in the yards when you work near a network without working inside it. The name attached to a category of job. Serious commission work, not one-off assessments. I was never offered that category."

"Why not?"

"I didn't want it offered." She met his eyes. "I was doing legitimate work, Mr. Mavik. The TL-9 notation was sloppy record-keeping from a spring contract. I have a cargo-verification job scheduled in three days. That is the whole of my business in this city."

Theron looked at her for a moment. The professional register was back. She had gathered it around herself like the well-kept coat, and it fit. Everything she'd said was defensible. Most of it, he thought, was even true.

"One more question," he said. "The two customs-assist jobs in spring. The second one ended six days before the gondola run. Were the manifests for those jobs connected to the Wyrm's Mirren departure?"

A small pause. "One was adjacent. Not directly."

"Adjacent how?"

"The cargo line that ran through the same dock window." She said it steadily. "Not the passenger manifest. The freight side."

"I'll need the job reference numbers."

She gave them without hesitation. Whatever she was protecting, it wasn't the job numbers.

---

Outside, the magistrate's front step had a low stone wall running along its north side where the garden had once been and was now gravel. Sable was sitting on it. Theron came out and did not sit, because his legs had gone slightly wrong from forty minutes in the office chair and he preferred to stand. The harbor was three blocks south; you could smell it from here, salt and coal smoke and the particular sweetness of a bakery operating somewhere on the adjacent street.

"She knew," Sable said.

"She knew Joren's name. She knew the passage had a Toll connection. She almost certainly knew the shape of what she was on the same gondola as." He turned to look at the door he'd just come through, then at the gravel, then at his notes. "She didn't know Joren was the first-class passenger."

"You believe that."

"Yes." He turned the question over. "She saw a first-class man she didn't know. Joren was using the full glamour — she'd have seen Vannic. Not someone she recognized from the yards." He looked at his notes. "She's protecting the cargo-side connection, not the passenger side. The spring job adjacent to the Wyrm's freight manifest. That's where she draws the line."

Sable was looking at the gravel. "Roven."

"She knows that name. She gave me more on Roven than she intended to. Senior, serious-commission work, never made direct contact." He stopped. "She's afraid of that name."

"Not afraid of you finding Roven. Afraid of Roven knowing she talked."

It was the same thought he'd been reaching for, and she'd gotten there with less friction.

"She gave me the cargo job numbers," he said. "Those will trace."

"Back to Toll. Back to the freight side of the Wyrm's departure." Sable stood, brushing gravel dust from her coat. "She's telling you something with those numbers. She gave them too readily."

He looked at the note again. "She wants the cargo thread pulled and not the Roven thread."

"She wants to be cooperative without giving you anything that brings Roven into a room."

The bakery smell was stronger now. Someone had opened a door somewhere. Theron looked at his notes, then at the harbor-facing street. Fell had given him one genuinely useful piece: the cargo-side connection and its job numbers, which would trace through Toll's operation and might open the freight manifest from the Wyrm's Mirren departure. And she had protected one thing, carefully, behind a solid wall of plausible deniability: whatever direct knowledge she had of Roven's involvement in the passage.

The thread was not resolved. He hadn't expected it to be.

A boy came around the corner of the magistrate's building at a half-run, which was the city speed between walking and actually running that messengers used when the message was time-sensitive but not an emergency. He slowed, looked at them, and held out a folded note.

"Mavik?" he said.

"Yes."

The note was from the clearing house at the south Halverstow station. The handwriting was a clerk's, neat and quick and impersonal. Theron read it once, then again.

*Pessel, care of the Copper Finch, Sannholt — confirmed as registered box-holder, this clearing house and both southern transit houses. Box is active, payments current. Your letter was accepted Friday run and should arrive in Sannholt by tomorrow noon at latest. Reply within four to five days of delivery.*

He passed the note to Sable.

She read it. "Active box. Real address."

"Real address," he said. "Pessel exists. Or someone has maintained that name long enough to pay for a registered box."

Sable folded the note and handed it back. The morning light was at the angle now where the harbourmaster's hall windows would be catching it, sending the brass-colored light across the floor that meant mid-morning had arrived and the work traffic of the port had shifted from loading to administering. Theron put the note in his coat pocket beside the Fell interview notes.
