The Sannholt reply was coming. Friday if Pessel wrote immediately, Saturday if they waited a day. By the end of the week, the address would either reply or it wouldn't, and the case would have a third thread confirmed or a thread ended.

He looked at his notes again: Fell's cargo job numbers, the Roven description, and the clearing house confirmation sitting beside each other on the page.

"Sannholt by tomorrow noon," he said, more to himself than to her.

"Then we wait," Sable said, "and follow the cargo numbers while we do."

He tucked the notebook under his arm and went down the magistrate's steps into the street.

---

# Chapter 22 — Vannic's Full Accounting

Theron had spread the Fell interview notes across the small writing table by the window before he remembered there was no window. The room at the Arms was one of the interior ones. Cheaper by the week, Vannic had explained when they'd first visited, as though the admission embarrassed him and he wanted credit for the candor.

He restacked the notes along the edge of the table and knocked twice on the door to the room at the end of the corridor.

---

Vannic was eating breakfast at eleven in the morning, which Theron filed under *consistent character*, and had not shaved, which he filed under *difficult week*. He stood when they came in and then appeared to decide this was excessive and sat back down. The plate in front of him held what had been poached eggs and was now the evidence of poached eggs.

"The note said this morning," Vannic said.

"It's still morning," Theron said.

Sable took the chair by the window. Theron sat across from Vannic and opened his notebook.

"I want to run through the contract process again," Theron said. "From the beginning. Toll's office — when you first contacted him."

Vannic's expression shifted in the way of someone who had been hoping the subject was already done. "I went over this."

"You went over parts of it. I need the full picture." Theron set his pencil on the notebook. "The payment terms specifically, and what Toll told you about how the operation worked — who was responsible for what."

Vannic looked at his egg plate. A passing impulse, quickly suppressed. "I hired Toll through the estate management — he'd been my factor for three years at that point. I went to his office in Mirren west harbour personally, in the sixth month. The usual estate business first, then the other matter."

"Meaning the impersonation."

"Meaning that." Vannic shifted in his chair. "He took it in stride, which told me this wasn't his first such commission."

Theron noted: *not first commission*.

"He named a fee," Vannic continued. "I won't tell you the number."

"You will, actually."

A pause. "Three thousand crowns. Half at signing, half on completion of the passage."

"Paid to Toll directly?"

"Paid to Toll's account, yes. What he paid the — what he paid Joren I was not told."

*Joren.* The name came out with the kind of deliberate, controlled care that meant Vannic had been practicing using it.

"What other terms were in the contract?"

"Discretion was explicit — a standard clause, nothing unusual. And there was a clause about oversight." Vannic looked at his hands. "Toll said there would be a person on the passage who was responsible for ensuring the arrangement ran without difficulty. He described it as standard practice for a commission of this kind."

Theron looked up from his notes. "Did Toll name this person?"

"Not at signing." Vannic met his eyes, briefly. "He said the name after I pressed him. At the end of the meeting, when I was already at the door. As a — reassurance, I took it. He said Roven would be keeping watch."

"*Keeping watch.* Those are the words he used?"

"He said, 'Roven will have an eye on the passage.' I thought he meant —" Vannic gestured with his fork, which was still in his hand for no reason either of them acknowledged. "I thought he meant generally. Administrative oversight. I didn't think he meant *on the gondola*."

"When did you start thinking it might mean on the gondola?"

"When you told me Joren was dead on the gondola." The fork went back to the table. "Before that, I confess, the nature of Roven's oversight was not my primary concern."

*[Investigator — Insight +1. He genuinely didn't know Roven was aboard. The surprise in Ch. 20 was real.]*

"Did Toll say anything about what Roven's role was in the network? What kind of work he did normally?"

Vannic considered this with more care than he usually gave questions. "He said Roven was the person who had found the illusionist — had, as he put it, *vouched for the quality of the work*. And that they'd worked together before on several occasions and the arrangement had always been satisfactory."

"Toll described Roven as the person who sourced Joren."

"More than that." Vannic frowned. "He said Roven was responsible for the — the talent side. Meaning the people. Toll handled the brokering and the contractual structure. Roven supplied the names."

Theron wrote: *Toll = broker/contracts. Roven = talent source. Joren was Roven's placement.* He underlined the last line, then thought better of it and set the pencil down.

Sable had said nothing. She was looking at the window, which let in the street noise from below: someone arguing about a cart, a child, the intermittent clop of a horse being led. Her notebook was open on her knee.

"How many names?" Theron asked.

Vannic looked at him.

"You said Roven supplied names. Did Toll tell you how many people were in that part of the network? How many illusionists Roven was placing?"

"He said — he implied it was more than one. A roster, he called it. People with specific skills, specific track records." Vannic picked up his knife, put it down again. "He said Joren was the best he had for this particular commission because Joren had worked this class of client before. Meaning quality of performance. He was reassuring me."

"What else did Toll say about Roven? Any other details — where Roven worked, how to contact him?"

"No. Toll said quite specifically that Roven's contact was Toll's concern and not mine." A careful pause. "He said this after I asked a similar question. He was not hostile about it, but he was clear. Roven was not my contact."

"And on the payment side — was there anyone else named? Any other parties to the arrangement?"

"Not by name." Vannic thought. "Toll said there were people on the cargo side of the passage who were Toll's regular accounts, and that the gondola run had been partly selected for that reason. That was the only reference to anyone else involved."

Theron noted: *cargo side — Toll regular accounts, gondola selected partly because of it.*

He looked at the note. Then at Vannic. "When you say the gondola run was partly selected for that reason — whose decision was it? Did you choose the Wyrm, or did Toll?"

Vannic had the grace to look uncomfortable. "Toll proposed the Halverstow route. I had a version of my own in mind, but his was — he made a case for it, and his case was reasonable."

Theron picked up his pencil. "Toll chose the passage."

"He proposed it," Vannic said, with what was clearly a distinction that mattered to him and no one else.

"He chose the passage," Theron said, and wrote it down.

He closed the notebook at the bottom of the second page. Vannic had given him the full accounting: the payment structure, the Roven description, the cargo-side note. Theron's question about additional network names had produced one more name, offered under light pressure: *Dosseren*, a clearing broker in Mirren west harbour who had processed the contract's fee payment at Toll's direction. Not a new thread, Theron thought, but confirmation of the financial shape.

"Is there anything else you haven't told me," Theron said, "that you've been deciding whether to tell me?"

Vannic looked at him steadily. "I was trying to decide about the cargo-side comment since you first asked about Fell."

"What about it?"

"Toll mentioned a name. When he was talking about the regular accounts on the cargo side." Vannic folded his hands on the table, a conscious gesture. "He said Fell was reliable. That she had done two jobs for him in spring and was known to him."

Theron looked at his notes. Fell. Toll calling Fell reliable to a client at the contract signing, not concealed, just not flagged as significant, filed away until pressed.

"You didn't mention this in earlier interviews."

"You asked about the people Toll told me about in the network. Fell's name wasn't in the network. She was a reference to competent regular work. I didn't connect it."

"You're connecting it now."

"You have Fell's name on your list. I have eyes." Vannic looked at the egg plate. "I'm trying to be useful. You've made it fairly clear what happens if I'm not."

*[Investigator — Quest progress: Toll-Roven-Fell triangle fully confirmed. Payment chain traceable. The gondola was not incidental.]*

Theron looked at his notebook for a moment. Then he said, "One more thing. Did Toll name anyone called Dosseren to you as a principal contact, or is that the only reference?"

"Only the reference," Vannic said. "He handled the fee. That's all I know of him."

Theron nodded, made a final note, and closed the notebook.

---

There was a small sitting room at the end of the Arms corridor that was empty at this hour: two armchairs angled toward a cold grate, a table with a crack running across its near edge. They took it because the corridor wasn't the place, and the street wasn't the place, and Sable said this was fine with a gesture that indicated she had made worse work-spaces serve.

"Roven placed Joren," Theron said. "Not as a one-off. As Roven's usual work — sourcing talent, vouching them into Toll's commissions. Joren had a track record with Roven. That means Roven knew who Joren really was."

"He knew what Joren looked like," Sable said. "Knew his real work. Not the face he wore on the Wyrm."

"Toll chose the Wyrm because of the cargo side. Because Fell was already in the manifest — as a regular account, not as a coincidence." He set his notebook on the cracked table and looked at it. "Joren didn't end up on a passage with a Toll contact by accident. The whole thing was threaded together."

"From Roven's end or from Toll's?"

"Both, I think." He turned the notebook over. "Toll picked the route for business reasons — the cargo accounts. Roven had Joren as a ready placement for the impersonation. The two ends of the arrangement fit together because Toll was managing the whole commercial picture." He looked at the grate. "The question is whether Roven knew Joren was going to die."

Sable didn't answer that. It wasn't a question for the sitting room at the Arms.

"Fell's cargo job numbers," he said. "When those trace through Toll's operation, they should confirm the freight-side connection to the Wyrm's Mirren departure. That tells us Fell's spring work wasn't incidental — she was being set up as a regular account for the passage."

"By Toll. Or Roven."

"More likely Toll." He looked at the notebook, turned a page. "Roven handles talent. Fell's work isn't talent work — it's cargo-verification, customs-assist. That's Toll's side of the arrangement." He paused. "Which means Roven and Toll had a clean division of their functions. They weren't interchangeable."

Sable said, "Joren was Roven's asset. Toll brokered the commission, but Joren was Roven's."

"And Roven was on the passage," Theron said quietly. "Aware of it. Watching it."

The sitting room was cold. Someone had let the fire go out mid-morning and not bothered to reset it. Through the floor came the distant rhythm of the Arms kitchen, a steady percussion that had been running since they sat down.

He picked up his pencil, set it beside the notebook, and let the full shape of it sit there for a moment. The Wyrm's Mirren departure was not a coincidence. Joren's presence on that specific passage was not incidental. Roven, who knew what Joren was, had been on the same gondola. Framed as oversight. Framed as reassurance.

The answer to why Joren had died was somewhere in that.

There was a knock at the sitting-room door. The Arms boy put his head in. "Post for Mavik," he said, and held out a sealed envelope.

Theron took it.

The seal was a small wax impression he didn't recognize. The direction on the outside was his name at the Arms, in a hand he didn't recognize either: careful, deliberate, the hand of someone who had been taught to write and had stopped practicing regularly somewhere along the way. The postmark was Sannholt.

He broke the seal.

---

*Mr. Mavik —*

*Your letter arrived yesterday. I am glad someone has written, though the circumstances are not what I hoped for when I first began to watch for Joren's post.*

*Joren Aldis was expected in Sannholt. He had written to say he would be here by the third week of the month at the latest, and I have been holding the box for him since. He is now past his expected date by eleven days, which I had put down to a delay on the passage, or a delay at port, as these things happen. Your letter tells me it was not a delay.*

*I cannot write the rest of what I have to say here. It is not post-coach business. What I can tell you is that Joren was coming to Sannholt for a reason that had nothing to do with the season or the coast roads, and that I have been waiting for him to arrive for two years, not three weeks. He wrote to me in spring to say the time was nearly right. I have a great deal to tell him and apparently can no longer do that.*

*If someone intends to come to Sannholt — and I think someone should — I am at the Copper Finch most evenings after the fourth bell, and all day on the days the post-coach runs.*

*— Pessel*

He read it twice. Then he set it down.

Sable read it and handed it back without comment.

"Two years," he said. "Pessel has been waiting for Joren for two years. Whatever Joren's business in Sannholt was, it wasn't personal in the way I was reading it — a friend, a contact, something convenient. Pessel says the time was *nearly right*."

"That phrase could mean anything."

"It could." He folded the letter carefully, running his thumbnail along the fold. "The address has been maintaining a registered box, paying fees, holding post for a man for two years. That is a specific kind of waiting." He put the letter in his notebook. "And Pessel won't say what it is. Not in writing."

The kitchen percussion downstairs continued its steady rhythm. He looked at his notes, at the Roven summary, at the Fell cargo numbers, at Pessel's letter folded into the back page.

Sable said, "I'll take the next post-coach."

He looked at her.

"Friday run," she said. "It's Wednesday. That's two days. I'll be in Sannholt by the weekend." She had her notebook open, not looking at it. "I need to tell Pessel in person before any magistrate's letter gets there, and Pessel clearly has something to say that won't travel by post. That's two good reasons and I only need one."

"I know," he said.

"You don't need to come." She said it without edge. Purely practical. "You need to stay here and follow the cargo numbers when they trace. Someone has to be in Halverstow when that comes back."

She was right. He turned this over for a moment, which was all the time it took to confirm she was right.

"The clearing house has the Friday run departing at the seventh bell," he said. "I'll send a note to the Arms booking office."

"I'll send it," she said. "I know where it is."

He nodded.

She closed her notebook and set it on the cracked table beside his. Through the window above the grate, the light had gone flat with the angle of early afternoon. Somewhere on the street below, the argument about the cart had either resolved or moved. The Arms kitchen went on with its steady work.

"Whatever Joren was going to do in Sannholt," Theron said, "he'd been planning it for two years. It was important enough to plan around."

"And someone didn't want him to arrive."

He looked at the folded letter in his notebook.

"We don't know that yet," he said.

Sable said nothing.

He picked up his pencil. "I'll have the cargo numbers with the magistrate's clerk this afternoon. When those trace, we'll know whether the freight connection runs directly back to Toll's regular accounts or whether there's another layer." He made a note at the bottom of the open page. "And you'll be in Sannholt Friday night, and we'll know what Pessel knows."

"And we'll know what Joren was going to do," she said.

"And we'll know that." He looked at the note. "Or at least more of it."

She stood, picked up her notebook, and tucked it under her arm. He followed her out of the cold sitting room and into the corridor, where the Arms afternoon was going about its business, carrying trays and correspondence and none of their concerns.

---

# Chapter 23 — Everyone Walks Out

The magistrate's clerk had two speeds: one for documents she considered appropriate to her office and one for everything else. She ran a finger down the reference column at the appropriate speed, which was no speed at all, while Theron stood at the counter and watched.

"TL-9," he said, in case she had forgotten.

"I know what I'm looking for," she said, without looking up.

He waited. The clerk's office smelled of sealing wax and something camphor-adjacent that was probably the lining of the ledger cabinet. Through the window to his left, the harbor's morning light came in at a low angle and put a long rectangle of gold across the flagstone floor, which moved imperceptibly while he waited. A second clerk at the back of the room was copying from one register into another with the serene patience of someone who had long ago made her peace with the world.

The finger stopped. "There," she said, and turned the ledger to face him.

---

He walked back to the Arms with the clerk's notation in his case book: two spring customs-assist jobs, reference TL-9, both connecting through a Mirren west-harbour account registered to a cargo storage firm on the harbor's outer ring. The firm's name was Vantrel Secured Storage, registered agent listed as one H. Marbeth. Not Toll. Not Roven. A name Theron had not seen in any previous interview, any letter, any yard log.

He stopped at the corner of the harbor road and wrote it out in full. *Vantrel Secured Storage, outer ring, west harbour. Registered agent: H. Marbeth.*

Another layer. He put the case book in his coat pocket and kept walking.

---

The problem he had been sitting with since yesterday had not gone away for being slept on.

He brought it out properly at the small table in the Arms dining room, over coffee that arrived without being ordered, which was one of the benefits of staying somewhere long enough. His notes from the early stages of the case lay beside the cup: the categorization he had made in the first hour, the suspect columns, the framework he had built in the captain's gallery with his method organized and precise and, as it turned out, organized and precise around the wrong question.

He had asked: who had motive against Lord Vannic?

The question was correct. It was the correct question for the case he had been given. A man found dead in a locked cabin, the man identified to all present as Lord Petric Vannic: the question is who among his known circle had reason to want Lord Vannic dead. He had built that case with care, and the case held, and it was the wrong case entirely.

The real question, the one that would have led him to Roven, to Toll, to a working illusionist named Joren Aldis and the professional network that had put him in that specific cabin on that specific passage, was: who had reason to stop Joren Aldis?

He turned the page and wrote that at the top of a clean sheet. Let it sit. Then he looked back at his first-class suspect columns.

Esherel: forty-one thousand five hundred crowns of motive, clear opportunity window, demeanor that had looked like calculated presentation. He had nearly committed to a conclusion that would have been wrong in every particular. Not because his method was flawed. Because his method was working on a case that didn't exist.

Vellaine: a juridical theorist, a witness with a lamp-timing discrepancy, a woman who had told him to watch his cleanest column. She'd known the column looked clean because she'd seen cases built on clean columns before. Not a critique of his conclusion. An observation about his frame.

The second-class field: three merchants with dull alibis, a wool-trade agent, a woman traveling to see her sister. He had built brackets around all of them because they were on the same gondola as the name Vannic and might have had reasons he couldn't yet see. None of them had known anything about Joren Aldis. None of them had been near that network.

The method was correct. He had run the method correctly. What the method had needed was a different set of inputs, and he hadn't had them until cabin three-T.

He wrote, in the margin of the original categorization page: *case one and case two. Wrong shape for case two; correct procedure throughout.*

Then he closed the early notes and set them at the far edge of the table.

*[Investigator — Annotation flagged: case file Ch. 1–12 reclassified as wrong-case documentation. Not error. Frame shift.]*

He looked at that for a moment. Then he finished his coffee.

---

The cargo numbers had traced to Vantrel Secured Storage and stopped. He spent the late morning writing to the magistrate's office to request the full year's registered-agent records for the firm. They would need a search warrant to go further, and the warrant would take time he didn't yet have. He noted *Marbeth* as a thread for Act 3 and left it there.

By mid-afternoon, the case had the shape of something in progress. Roven placed Joren. Toll chose the passage. Fell was on the cargo side, connected but not at the center of it. Dosseren processed the money. Marbeth, or the firm called Vantrel, or whoever sat behind both, was another wall that needed time to come through. Sable was leaving in the morning for a Sannholt conversation that would not travel by post.

He sat with all of it in the dining room when the lunch hour cleared out, the way he sat with cases when the work was in the stage of becoming clear rather than the stage of being done. Progress was real. The shape of what had happened was comprehensible now in outline. What was still missing was the part that turned outline into evidence.

---

Esherel came down the front steps of the Arms just as he was going up.

She pulled on her glove — one finger, then the next, with the precision of someone who had once worn gloves to dinners that required precision — and said, without preamble, "You've been at the magistrate's office this morning."

"I have."

"The cargo trace." It wasn't a question.

"Partial result," he said. "Useful, not conclusive."

She looked at him steadily. "Are you going to get there?"

"I think so." He paused on the step. "Not quickly."

Esherel put the second glove on and smoothed it at the wrist. "Joren's family," she said. "Does anyone know — has anyone contacted them?"

"Sable is going to Sannholt tomorrow. There's a contact there who knew him." He stopped. "The family would be elsewhere — he came from the coast, from a fishing town somewhere in Joren's home region. We don't have the specific address yet."

Esherel's expression shifted slightly. Not softer, exactly — more settled. "I wanted to ask because I intend to say something, when there's a proper address and a proper moment. I was clear-eyed about what he was. A professional deceiver. That doesn't mean his people shouldn't hear from someone who was in the room."

Theron didn't say anything for a moment. "That's —" He stopped himself. The word he had been about to use was *generous*, and it was wrong, or at least incomplete. "That would mean something," he said.

She made a small sound that was not a dismissal. "I have a letter to the solicitor's to see about, and you have whatever you have." She went down the last step and then paused. "Come and find me if the address turns up. I'll be at the Arms through the end of the week."

He watched her go across the harbor square.

Then he went up the steps and inside.

---

He was arranging the afternoon's notes into the case book when someone knocked at the parlor door.

It was Esherel again — he thought, half a second before the door opened and the thought corrected itself. The step was different.

Madam Esherel's hair was steel-grey and entirely assembled; the woman in the doorway had light-brown hair wound up with the slight carelessness of someone who had done it themselves this morning without a mirror. She was neither young nor old. Her coat was the practical heavy wool of the harbor quarters, not the harbor-walk coat of someone passing through.

"Mr. Mavik," she said. "I was told you were here. I'm sorry to come without sending word first."

He didn't recognize her. Then, the fraction of a second later, he did: the shape of the face, the directness, the slight formal stiffness around the edges of her voice that said *this matters and I am aware it is slightly awkward that it matters*.

"Madam Esherel," he said, and caught himself. The woman in the doorway was not Esherel. He knew perfectly well who Esherel was. He had just watched Esherel cross the harbor square with her gloves on.

The confusion lasted one more beat — and then he had it. The voice. Not Esherel's voice. Similar precision, different register.

"I'm sorry," he said. "Please come in. You're —"

"Norra Esherel," she said. "Fellin's —" She paused. "Madam Esherel's niece. I came down from the city when she sent word. She said — she thought you might be willing to speak to me. About the investigation."

He gestured to the chair across from him. "Please sit."

She sat with the careful economy of someone managing her own dignity without a great deal of practice at it. Her hands went to her lap, then to the chair arms, then back to her lap.

He waited.

"She told me about the man," Norra Esherel said. "The illusionist. Joren Aldis." She said the name with a slight pause around it, the pause of someone putting careful weight on a word they'd recently learned. "She told me what he was like on the gondola — from her perspective. What she noticed." She looked at the case book. "She also told me she gave you three observations. But she didn't tell me everything she observed."

Theron looked at her. "How do you mean?"

"Because she didn't think it mattered," Norra Esherel said. "At the time." Her hands settled finally on her lap and stayed there. "She told me when she got in — not the first night, the second night, after she'd had time to think about it. She saw something that she'd — she'd filed it away. As nothing. As a private moment that she hadn't understood and hadn't wanted to intrude on."

"What did she see?"

The young woman looked at the case book for a moment, not as though she expected it to answer, but as though looking at an object somewhere below eye level helped her put the words in the right order. "On the second evening of the passage," she said. "She was coming back along the first-class corridor. Late — past the second watch. She nearly ran into him. He was standing in the corridor on his own, half-turned toward the wall, and he had something in his hand." A pause. "He'd taken it from his coat pocket. Something small. He was just — looking at it. Quietly. She said he looked like a man in a conversation with himself that he'd had many times before."

Theron set his pencil down.

"She almost said something," Norra Esherel continued. "She didn't, because it was private. He hadn't seen her — or if he had, he hadn't acknowledged it. He put whatever it was back in his pocket after a moment and walked on toward the dining room. She thought it was a letter, or a small token of some kind. Something kept rather than used."

The corridor. Second watch. Joren in the first-class passage that connected his cabin to the dining room, and something small taken from an outer pocket, looked at, put away. The outer coat pocket. The right one.

"She knows now," Norra Esherel said quietly, "that it was the fish. The carved one." Her voice was level. "She was told about that — about what was in his pocket. And she wanted — she asked me to come because she didn't want to come herself and make it — she said she didn't want to make it about her feelings, which aren't relevant, and she was afraid she would." She looked at Theron. "So she sent me to tell you."

"Why was it important to tell me?"

Norra Esherel's hands tightened slightly, then released. "Because someone is going to go and tell his family. And Aunt Fellin thought — they should know. That he had it with him. That he reached for it." A slight pause. "That at the end, or near it, there was a private moment where he looked at something a child had made for him and carried for two years. And that it was quiet. She said — she said he didn't look sad. He just looked like a man who knew what mattered."

Theron looked at his case book.

The carved fish had been in his notes since the first examination of the body. Outer coat pocket, between a used handkerchief and a steel pen-nib. Filed as *odd personal effect, note for return to estate*. Re-filed after the reveal, after the recall passage, after Sable had named what the fish was and who had made it. He had written Joren's name across the top of the case file when the quest updated, and had not flinched at it, because that was what you did once you knew the name.

He had not known about the corridor. He had not known about the second-watch moment, the small private looking, the thing put back into a pocket and carried on.

He closed the case book.

"Thank you," he said. "Please thank Madam Esherel." He looked at Norra Esherel steadily. "The family — when we have an address. She'll be contacted directly, with her permission."

"She'd like that," Norra Esherel said.

She stood. He stood. She went to the door with the same careful economy she'd come in with, and paused there, her hand on the frame. "She said one more thing," she said. "I almost forgot. She said — he didn't look like a man afraid of being caught. In the corridor. He just looked like himself."

Then she went.

---

Theron sat for a while. Not long — the afternoon light still had time in it, and the Marbeth note wanted a follow-up letter to the harbor registry before end of business. But long enough to let the corridor image settle into the case: the first-class passage on the second evening, Joren Aldis standing half-turned to the wall, the fish in his palm, looking at it the way a man looks at something that has been the reason for a very long stretch of difficult work. Put it away. Walk on toward the dining room.

He picked up his pencil. At the top of a fresh page he wrote: *What Esherel saw — corridor, second watch, second evening. Small carved object from outer coat pocket. Returned to pocket. Private.*

Below that: *Not afraid. Himself.*

He underlined neither.

*[Investigator — Witness addition logged: Esherel observation, second-evening corridor. Corroborates known-facts entry on the carved fish. New detail: pre-death, private moment, deliberate.]*

He looked at the line. Then he set the pencil down, picked up his coat, and went to find the harbor registry before it closed.

The fish had been there in his pocket the whole passage. He had reached for it in a quiet corridor and looked at it, and then he had gone into the dining room and eaten supper with a fork in his hand and a dead lord's voice in his throat, and none of the first-class passengers had known what he was, or what he was carrying, or what two years of careful waiting felt like when the time was finally nearly right.

Theron went out into the afternoon. The harbor road was busy with the end of the working day, the carter's children running the edges of the cobbles, the smell of the day's tide coming in off the water. He walked quickly. He had letters to write before evening.

---

# Chapter 24 — Everyone Walks Back In

Theron spread the third-class passenger list across the table in his room at the Arms and weighted the top corner with his case book.

Fourteen passengers, minus Vannic. Twelve of those accounted for, at least in outline: the two with soft alibis from the steward's briefing, the textile buyer, the woman with the funeral record, the six who had cleared on profession and reference, and Fell, who had not cleared so much as complicated. That left two columns that, until yesterday, he had considered closed. He turned to the first.

*Maret Fell.* Mirren east harbour, shipping assessment. On the gondola for cargo-side reasons that overlapped with the Wyrm's freight manifest in ways she had been willing to give him, cheerfully, without being asked to give more. He had his read; Sable had her counter-read. The warrant was in the magistrate's hand. He left the column where it was and moved on.

The second column stopped him.

He had written it out at the time with the unhurried confidence of someone filing a complete entry: *Halsa Renn. Cabin nine-T. Traveling through to Sannholt, family business. Cash. Seasonal work. No apparent connection to Mirren west harbour, to Berric Toll, to Joren Aldis's professional network.* He had closed the column and turned the page.

The question had been: who had motive against Lord Vannic?

He knew what the question should have been.

He picked up the pencil and wrote it in the margin. *Who had motive to stop Joren Aldis? Who had prior knowledge of the swap? Who, among the people I filed as no motive, had a connection to the network I hadn't found yet?*

He looked at what he had written on Renn.

*Traveling through to Sannholt.* He had filed this as confirmation of no connection — the through-traveler, south coast, local errand. Now he read it differently. The Sannholt address in Joren's correspondence. Pessel at the Copper Finch, waiting two years. A contact whose business with Joren was close enough that she had declined to put it in writing. Sannholt was not a large town. Someone traveling through to Sannholt on personal business was — he stopped himself. That was not evidence. That was geography.

*No apparent connection to Mirren west harbour, Toll, or Joren's network.* Apparent. He had written *apparent*, which was honest. He had also filed it as functional closure, which was not.

*Seasonal small-contract labour. Cash. No professional reference.* He had read this as low-tier at the time because his frame was Vannic's world: contracts, nobles, factors, cargo accounts. A seasonal worker on cash terms had no obvious entry point into that hierarchy. But Joren's world was not Vannic's world. Joren's world was illusionists and network brokers and a ten-year career taking off-coast contract work to feed someone else's children. The entry point into Joren's world was different.

*Halsa Renn observed that Calden's walk was quieter on the third morning. She noticed it but did not know what it meant.* He had written this in his notes and filed it under *third-class corridor, incidental observations* without following the thread. He read it again now.

Calden's walk was quieter because a man who had thanked him by name was dead. It was the kind of specific, attended observation that came from someone who watched how people moved through spaces. He had written down that Sable had said the same thing about Renn herself, the observation delivered in the corridor after the interview: *she notices how people move*. He had folded the page and walked on.

He put the pencil down.

The re-examination didn't give him an answer. What it gave him was a correction: he had asked whether Renn had a connection to a network he'd understood at the time as Vannic's world, and she hadn't. But that wasn't the right network. He had been filing suspects against the wrong map.

He wrote across the bottom of the Renn entry, in the small hand he used for things that were neither conclusions nor dismissals: *Revisit. Wrong frame at time of interview. Sannholt thread may have contact.*

Then he turned the page.

*[Investigator — Case file annotation: four fourth-tier dismissals reclassified as wrong-frame, not cleared. Method correct; input set incomplete. No new leads. Process continues.]*

He looked at that for a moment, then turned back to Fell's column and began the follow-up notes for the warrant.

---

He ate breakfast in the Arms dining room at the table by the window, which he had occupied long enough by now that the young woman who laid the cloth put the coffee down first and the bread second without asking. The harbor was starting its day in the street below: a dray horse objecting to something at the corner, two factors with their cases walking quickly in opposite directions, a boy carrying a stack of slates almost certainly heavier than he was allowed.

Sable's bag was in the corridor when he came down. He registered the brown traveling coat over the chair back when he crossed to his table, which was how he knew she'd been up earlier and had not slept through the coach window.

She came in twenty minutes later with a folder tucked under one arm. She got her own coffee from the sideboard without asking and sat at the far end of his table, which had happened every morning for a week, the table being large enough that it was easier than finding another.

"Calden found me last night," she said.

He looked at her over his cup.

She set the folder on the table between them and opened it. Inside was a handwritten register, several pages in a close, practiced hand, not the Arms's stationery: smaller, the kind of notebook a man uses for work rather than for correspondence. "He made notes during the passage," she said. "Third-class corridor. Which passengers spoke to staff, which ones he read as unusual, what he couldn't place at the time."

Theron set his coffee down. "He gave you his working notes."

"He said he'd been waiting for someone doing the follow-through to come past." She turned to the first page. "He left them with me because I was the one leaving in the morning. Figured they'd be useful in Sannholt."

The register was Calden's. The notation was the same economical hand from the corridor passes Theron had studied in the first week. Third-class passengers organized by cabin number, with marginal annotations in a different pencil: timing observations, behavioral notes, a row of small marks Theron didn't immediately read.

"What are the marks?" he said.

"Ones who made no contact with staff beyond boarding acknowledgment," she said. "Which he codes separately from the ones who kept to their cabins but thanked him for the sheets." She pointed to a row. "There are three in that category. One of them is Maret Fell."

He looked at the page. Fell was there, annotated in Calden's hand: *professional, polite, asked once about observation-window hours, no further contact, no cabin conversation.* The observation-window question was already in his notes. The "no further contact" had a small additional mark beside it in pencil, different from the notation key. He looked at Sable.

"He put that there last night," she said. "When he remembered something he hadn't put in the original. He said one of the quiet-cabin ones, the no-contact passengers, had a lamp on past third watch on the third night. He'd noted it at the time as insomnia or reading and hadn't flagged it because it wasn't unusual enough. He wasn't certain which cabin."

Theron went still for a moment. "He doesn't know?"

"He said he thought it was nine. He wasn't certain."

Cabin nine-T. He kept his face where it was.

"He filed what he remembered," Sable said, in the even tone she used for facts that did not require emphasis. "He wasn't certain. It's in there."

He looked at the annotation. Calden had written it in the uncertain hand of a man putting down a memory he wasn't fully confident in: *third night, late lamp, nine possibly, not certain.* Then he turned back to the first page and began reading through the full set of cabin observations, methodically, from the front.

*[Investigator — New material: steward's working notes, third-class corridor. Third night, cabin nine-T, lamp past third watch — uncertain provenance. Flag for verification.]*

"That's useful," he said.

"I thought so." She picked up her coffee and watched the harbor through the window.

---

The coach departure was confirmed for seventh bell. He had asked at the clearing house counter the previous afternoon, and the clerk had confirmed it with the flat certainty of a woman who had given the same answer several hundred times. He relayed this to Sable now, across the remains of breakfast, more for the record than because she didn't know.

She nodded and reached for the notebook she kept in her inside coat pocket, the small one, not the one she used for correspondence. She opened it on the table and read through what she had.

"Pessel," she said. "The letter accepted Friday. Reply within four to five days, which is already in and waiting. Copper Finch, Sannholt." She turned a page. "Two-year wait. Joren wrote in spring that the time was nearly right. Won't explain by post."

"Approach her on her terms," he said. "Not as the investigation — as the person who's been waiting."

Sable looked at him briefly. "Yes."

He turned to his own notes. "I have the cargo trace in the magistrate's hands: Vantrel, Marbeth, the warrant pending. That's the thread I'm running here." He tapped the third-class list. "And the re-examination."

"Any movement on the re-examination?"

"Corrections," he said. "I was working four of the dismissals against the wrong map. I've got a revisit flag on Renn."

She looked at the page he was on. He watched her read the notation, the marginal note he'd written that morning: *wrong frame at time of interview, Sannholt thread may have contact.* She didn't comment on it. She turned back to her own notebook.

"If Pessel knew Joren well enough to wait two years," she said, "she may know who else he was in contact with in that final period. Before the Vannic commission."

"That's what I'm hoping you'll find out."

"I know." She closed the notebook. "And if she's protective —"

"She will be."

"Then I'll be slow about it." She said this the way she said most things: as a statement of method rather than a reassurance. He nodded.

She knew the shape of the case as well as he did. Listing the open threads would be recapping for no audience.

"The carved fish," he said instead. "If there's a conversation about Joren's family — anything about where they are, whether they've been told —"

"I'll find out." She closed the folder with Calden's notes and tucked it under her arm. "And if there's a family address, I'll send it on before you need to ask."

He didn't say anything to that. There was nothing particular to say.

She stood, and he stood, and they went out into the hall where her bag was packed and sitting against the wall with the particular tidiness of someone who traveled often enough to have a system for it. He walked her to the Arms door and held it for her against the harbor wind.

On the step she paused and adjusted the folder under her arm. She looked at him — not the way she looked at the case, but the way she looked at things she was about to leave and wouldn't see for a while.

"Keep the Marbeth thread moving," she said. "Don't let the warrant sit."

"I won't."

"And eat something that isn't coffee and case notes."

He looked at her. "I had bread."

"Half a piece," she said.

He couldn't immediately contradict this, because it was true. She went down the steps, and he stood in the doorway until the harbor road turned and she was out of sight behind the factor's building.

Then he went inside and back up to his room, where the third-class passenger list was still spread across the table and Calden's notes were already making him want to pull the timing slate.

---

He worked until mid-afternoon, when the light through the window started its angle shift and the Arms dining room below produced the sound of the early supper setup. He had built a new working column: the re-examination, the four wrong-frame dismissals, the Renn flag and what he still needed to verify. He had written to the harbor registry about Vantrel Secured Storage's annual records, a second letter reinforcing the warrant request. He had noted Calden's lamp observation in its proper place, uncertain provenance marked.

The case had the shape of something waiting for its next two pieces: Pessel's account from Sable, and Marbeth's records from the magistrate. Both were moving. Neither had arrived.

He made a clean copy of the afternoon's notes into the case book and closed it. Then he checked the coach schedule on the folded slip he'd tucked into the inside cover three days ago and confirmed what he already knew: seventh bell, Friday, Halverstow south station. Sable would be on it. The Sannholt road was four hours in good weather, and the weather had been good all week.

He set the case book at the desk's far edge, where he could see it without looking at it, and poured the last of the afternoon's cold coffee into his cup and drank it standing.

He had fourteen passengers, minus one. Twelve of those with some kind of accounting. Two columns still working. One of them waiting on a warrant. The other flagged for revisit with an uncertain steward's note and a wrong-frame correction and a journey to Sannholt that was already in progress.

He wrote one more line in the margin of the Renn column, under the revisit flag: *Sannholt thread active as of today.*

Then he put the pencil down, picked up his coat, and went to find the harbor registry before end of business.

The coach was confirmed for seventh bell.

---

# Chapter 25 — The Coach Departs

The magistrate's clerk came at half past fifth bell, before Theron had finished his first cup of coffee.

The man was apologetic about the hour, which was its own kind of information: clerks who came at half past fifth were either carrying good news or had been up since third watch putting a search result together. He set a folded document on the table and accepted the chair Theron offered him but sat on the edge of it, which meant he was on his way somewhere else after this.

"Vantrel Secured Storage," the clerk said. "West harbour Mirren. You asked for the full-year registered-agent records." He tapped the document. "The warrant came through this morning from the harbor registry. Full disclosure, first of the year through now."

Theron set his cup down and unfolded the pages.

The records were organized in the harbor registry's standard format: columns for agent name, term dates, capacity, the notary's mark. There were four entries. The first three were consistent with the kind of storage firm that ran cargo through legitimate channels — commercial-rate agents, short terms, clean entries, each one vouched by an independent party whose name appeared nowhere else in the document. Routine work, routine paper. The fourth entry was different.

Registered agent, third month through seventh month of the current year: *H. Marbeth, independent, west harbour Mirren, vouched by Vantrel Secured Storage's own registered operator, Fenn Kolach.*

No address for Marbeth beyond the harbor district. No further detail. The term dates ran through the seventh month — which put Marbeth's active period overlapping exactly with Fell's two spring customs-assist jobs, the ones that had carried the TL-9 notation.

A name he hadn't encountered anywhere in the case file until Fell's job numbers had pointed him here.

*[Investigator — Registered agent H. Marbeth, west harbour Mirren. Term: third through seventh month, current year. Active during Fell's TL-9 customs-assist period. Next step: locate Marbeth through Fenn Kolach, Vantrel's registered operator.]*

He read through the rest of the document twice. Nothing else new. The Kolach name was clean in the harbor registry — a long-standing commercial operator, nothing irregular in the standing record, the kind of entry that had been renewed so many times it had acquired the texture of furniture. But Kolach had personally vouched for Marbeth, which meant the two men were connected well enough for Kolach to put his operator's license behind the introduction. That was not nothing.

"The Kolach vouching entry," Theron said.

The clerk nodded. "Unusual. Normally the registered operator isn't the vouching party for his own firm's agents — you'd get a third party. The magistrate's office flagged it when they ran the disclosure check."

"Has Kolach vouched for any other agents this year?"

The clerk opened his own folder and ran his finger down a column. "Two. Both through independent parties. This is the only one he carried himself."

Which made it particular to Marbeth. Whether that was professional loyalty, social obligation, or a more calculated kind of arrangement was a question for Kolach's own account — the document said what it said, and the document's job was not to explain itself. Kolach would do that.

"Is Kolach in Mirren?"

"Per the registry, he holds a west harbour address. We haven't verified current residence."

A letter would take three days each way. An investigation warrant to the Mirren magistrate's office could run parallel — one thread on the official record, one on the informal, Kolach pulled from both directions at once before he had time to calculate what each request meant. That was the sequence.

"I'll need a letter of introduction from the Halverstow magistrate's office," Theron said. "For Mirren counterparts. Authorization to request a formal interview with Fenn Kolach."

The clerk wrote something in his own folder. "I'll have it drafted by afternoon."

Theron set the records at the top of his working stack and walked the clerk to the door. He stood in the doorway for a moment after the man had gone, the harbor smell of fish-brine and coal smoke lifting off the street at this hour before the day had quite heated up. It was not Kolach he wanted to speak to, eventually — it was Marbeth. But Kolach was the door to that room, and the door was the door.

He went back to the table and picked up his coffee. Cold now.

It was twenty minutes past sixth bell by the time he put the cup down. The coach was at seventh.

---

He had the fish.

He had carried it since the fourth day of the investigation — lifted it from Joren's outer coat pocket during the cabin examination, logged it in the case notes as *odd personal effect, small carved wooden fish, return to estate* — and had not found an estate to return it to. The address in the Toll correspondence had given him the Sannholt thread, the Pessel name, the Copper Finch contact. It had not given him a family direction. That had waited.

The fish itself had moved, at some point in the second week, from his evidence pouch to his coat pocket. He had not made a decision about it. He had put it somewhere he could reach it and not thought about it further, and somewhere in the sequence of days and harbor walks and magistrate's offices it had settled into the left pocket the way things settle when you stop asking where they belong.

He had taken it out once, in the captain's day-cabin at the dock with Sable sitting across from him, and turned it over while she named the details in order — the callus, the accent, the kindnesses, the niece. She had not named the fish. It had been in his palm while she spoke and he had put it away without saying anything about it, and neither had she, which was the right choice. The recall was about Joren Aldis as a person. The fish was about where the person was going afterward.

He had felt the shape of it through the coat lining on the docks, on the Gray Quay steps, walking to the Arms with a cold harbor wind doing its best work.

The niece had carved it at age six. She was eight now. He had not let himself do the arithmetic on what the family was waiting for.

He put it in his left coat pocket, where it would be easy to reach. Then he put on his jacket, picked up his notebook, and went out.

---

The south station clearing house smelled of horses and coal smoke and the particular compressed urgency of an early departure — something about a yard before seventh bell that had more in common with a dock than a street, the sense of a timetable that did not negotiate. Two coaches stood in the yard, one already loaded and standing, one still being worked on: a driver checking the harness buckles methodically, running his thumb along each strap in sequence, while a boy handed up luggage from a cart. A woman in a gray traveling coat stood near the booking window with two bags at her feet, watching the boy work with the look of someone calculating whether he would get to her trunk before the boarding call. Farther along, two men in merchants' coats were arguing about something in the comfortable way of people who had argued about it before and expected to argue about it again.

The yard man moved between the coaches with a list, checking things off.

Theron found Sable near the far end of the coach, not at the window. She had her bag at her feet and was reading a page from her notebook, which he had seen her do before a departure — checking what she had, not what she'd forgotten. Her traveling coat was the darker one, good wool, built for weather. She looked up when she heard him.

"Warrant came through," he said.

"Marbeth?"

"H. Marbeth, registered agent, Vantrel Secured Storage, west harbour Mirren. Active through seventh month — overlaps Fell's TL-9 jobs. Vouched directly by Vantrel's own registered operator, Fenn Kolach. That's the irregular piece." He kept it compact; she was boarding in ten minutes and the full document would follow by letter. "I'm requesting a letter of introduction for the Mirren magistrate's office to interview Kolach. The address is in the harbor registry."

She folded the notebook page and put it away. "How long to get Kolach in front of someone?"

"Ten days if the Mirren counterpart is cooperative. Longer if they aren't."

"Send me a line when you have his statement." She meant it as a fact, not a request. He would, and she knew it.

The driver finished with the harness and climbed to the box, settling in with the air of a man who had done this at this hour for long enough that he had no more opinions about it. The yard man called the boarding. The woman at the window picked up her bags. The two merchants concluded their argument and moved toward the door. Theron registered Calden at the far edge of the yard, near the factor's shed — the gondola was still docked in the harbor, and a steward's departure paperwork ran long — but Calden was watching the coach, not the passengers, with the particular stillness of a man waiting to see something depart safely. Not a professional stillness. Something else. He did not come forward, which was the right thing to do.

Esherel was not here. He had not expected her to be.

The boy loaded the last case onto the boot and stepped back. The yard man checked it off his list, made a mark, looked up at the driver. The coach door opened from inside — one of the merchants holding it for the woman with the bags, which she acknowledged with a brief nod and went through.

Theron reached into his left coat pocket.

The fish sat in his palm. He looked at it — birch or ash, the fin at its imprecise angle, the wood worn smooth to the texture of something that had been held a long time. The niece's work, the way a six-year-old's hands worked: not quite right, better than it had any reason to be, shaped with the confidence of someone who had not yet learned to doubt the shape. Eight years old now. The fish was meant to go somewhere. Not an evidence locker. Not a coat pocket on a Halverstow street while the harbor went about its business. He had been carrying it long enough. It had somewhere to go, and Sable was the right person to carry it there.

He held it out to her.

Sable looked at it, then at him.

He didn't say anything. There was nothing particular to say.

She took it. She put it in the breast pocket of her traveling coat, the inside one, the same motion she used for the notebook. The coach door was open behind her, the driver settled, the other passengers already in.

She picked up her bag.

"Don't let the Marbeth letter sit," she said.

"I won't."

She went up the step and into the coach. The door latched.

The yard man stepped back. The driver released the brake and spoke to the horses — the same short phrase they would have heard every morning from him, at this yard, at this hour — and the horses moved. The coach rolled across the yard stones toward the gate, iron wheels loud on the cobbles, the sound bouncing off the factor's sheds and the booking office wall, and then it went through the gate and out onto the south road. Theron watched it go. Past the gate, the road softened to packed earth, and the wheel sound dropped to something lower, the horses moving into their working stride, and then the coach went around the chandler's building and the horses and the sound and the particular line of it went with it.

Calden was gone. He had not heard the steward leave.

---

Theron stood in the yard for a moment. The second coach was still being loaded. The woman who had been arguing with the booking clerk was at the window again, carrying a different document this time, her tone unchanged — patient, specific, not going anywhere. The boy went past with an empty cart and disappeared into the stable end.

He took out his notebook.

The Marbeth entry was at the top of the current page: *H. Marbeth, Vantrel, west harbour Mirren. Kolach vouching. Letter of introduction: afternoon.* He added a second line below it — *Follow-up: Mirren magistrate, formal interview request, Fenn Kolach, Vantrel Secured Storage* — and below that, in the smaller hand he used for threads that were open but not yet moving: *Renn: wrong frame confirmed; Sannholt thread active; Sable in transit.*

He read the page. The shape of it was right. The Kolach thread was the active work; the Sannholt thread was Sable's to run until her first letter came back; the two would converge somewhere in the middle weeks, and where they converged would tell him which way to press. He had made similar diagrams before, in Conservatory, in the second-year case practice where they gave you partial records and asked you to name the next step. The diagrams from case practice were always cleaner than this. This one had a man's name in the header and a carved fish traveling south on the morning coach.

That was the shape of it. Two threads. One moving south on the coach road. One waiting on a letter that would come back from Mirren in ten days or fourteen.

He closed the notebook and walked back through the yard gate onto the Halverstow street, where the harbor was going about its business in the middle-morning light and the Arms was three blocks north and the magistrate's clerk had a letter of introduction to draft by afternoon.

The case had the right names in it now. That was something. The rest was work.

---

# Chapter 26 — A Name on the Wrong Register

The clerk came back on Saturday morning, which was sooner than the ten days Theron had budgeted and approximately four days sooner than he had hoped to be ready for.

He was at the dining-room table at the Arms with a fresh pot and the third-class manifest spread next to his notebook, working the Renn revisit against the corridor-pass times he had taken from Calden's notes. The knock was a clerk's knock, two short and one slightly heavier, and he registered it as a Halverstow magistrate's office knock before he had finished setting the cup down.

"Wire summary from Mirren," the clerk said, when Theron opened the door. He was the same man who had brought the Vantrel warrant the day before, and he was carrying his hat under one arm and a folded telegram-sheet in the other. "Came through at first bell. The Mirren counterpart moved on the introduction letter on receipt. Kolach was in front of a magistrate by yesterday afternoon."

"Sit down," Theron said.

The clerk sat. He did not put his hat on the table; he kept it on his knee. The sheet he set down was a working transcription, not the original. The magistrate's office at this end ran a clean-copy service for incoming wires, and the original was already in the case file at the courthouse.

Theron unfolded the sheet.

The summary was three paragraphs in the close hand of someone trained for it. He read the first paragraph standing, then sat down to read it again.

Kolach had not denied anything. The vouching irregularity for H. Marbeth had been on the Mirren books for some months as a flag awaiting cause; under the introduction letter and the threat of formal proceedings, Kolach had explained the arrangement in fifteen minutes. He ran a side service through Vantrel: discreet credential placement for clients who paid cash and could not, or chose not to, provide professional references. The clients were typically working mages whose contract work was not the kind that generated the references the harbor registries required. He charged a placement fee and a renewal fee. He had been doing it for eleven years. Marbeth's papers had been placed through this channel.

The third paragraph was the part Theron read twice.

Kolach had been required to produce the channel's placement log for the past two years. The Mirren magistrate's office had attached the relevant page from the current year. Twenty-three placements in the period under examination. The wire had transcribed all twenty-three.

Theron set the cup aside and read the list.

Names he didn't recognize, mostly. Two factor's names he knew from cargo correspondence around Toll's office. One name he half-knew from an apprentice register at the Conservatory, which was its own line of work for later. He went down the column with his finger.

The seventeenth entry stopped him.

*Renn, Halsa. Booking credential placement, third class, Skybound Wyrm, twenty-third departure of the current year.*

He read it. He read it again. He set the sheet down flat against the table.

The clerk was watching him with the polite attention of a man who had carried a result he did not himself understand. Theron schooled his face into nothing in particular and looked up.

"I'll need the original of this page sent through," he said. "Sealed copy, registry to registry. And I'll need the Mirren counterpart's full statement on Kolach's testimony — verbatim transcript, not the summary."

"Already requested by the magistrate at this end," the clerk said. "He thought you'd want it. Ten days for the sealed copy, four to six for the transcript by post."

"Thank you."

"Anything else?"

Theron looked at the list once more, found the line again, confirmed it had not rearranged itself. "Not at this hour. I'll be in this afternoon to add a column to the working file."

The clerk picked up his hat. At the door he paused. "Mavik."

"Yes."

"The name on that list. I don't know who she is. But the Mirren counterpart noted at the end of the wire that Kolach had said the seventeenth placement was personally referred to him."

"Referred by whom?"

"It's in the transcript," the clerk said. "The wire wouldn't carry the full name through cleanly. I'd wait for the post."

He let himself out.

Theron sat with the sheet for a long minute, the dining room quiet around him, the harbor outside doing its Saturday business at half speed. Then he turned the page of his case book to a clean spread, dated it, and wrote the line at the top: *Renn, Halsa — booking credential placed through Kolach channel. Personal referral. Source pending.*

He underlined *personal referral* once, lightly, and went back to the table.

---

The third-class manifest was still where he'd left it.

He had filed Halsa Renn at the time of the Wyrm interview as fourth-tier: travel-through, south coast, family business, cash, no professional reference. He had marked the cash booking as low-tier, because his frame was Vannic's world, and a cash passenger with no reference in that world was someone with nothing to hide and no leverage to use.

He had been wrong about the frame. He had written that down in the working column.

He had not, until now, understood what *no professional reference* had been doing in the cash booking process. He had read it as absence. It was substitution. A cash booking on a Wyrm route required a reference; a reference could be provided through the standard professional channels; or it could be provided through a placement service whose existence was not advertised because its clientele preferred it that way. Kolach had been running that placement service for eleven years. He had placed Halsa Renn's booking credential for the Wyrm's twenty-third departure of the current year.

The departure that had carried Joren Aldis under a forged name. The departure on which Joren Aldis had died.

He put the pencil down because he wanted to be sure he was not writing something he hadn't yet earned.

Then he picked it up and wrote, in the working column under the Renn line: *Booking credential channel — same channel through which Toll's contract paperwork moved (working assumption, to be verified against Vantrel records).* He added: *Halsa Renn's reference was not absent. It was placed.*

He sat back.

The Sannholt destination he had filed as innocuous geography. The cabin-nine-T late lamp Calden had flagged with uncertain provenance. The observation Sable had made about how Renn watched corridors. The detail in the Wyrm interview about hearing a creak from toward the second-class passage on the third night, the night of the death, which he had taken at face value as a witness's small contribution, because that was how he had categorized the witness.

Halsa Renn had walked through three weeks of his case file as background. A through-traveler, cash, family business, low-tier dismissal. She had moved through his frame the way a steward moves through a room: present, polite, unremarkable to anyone whose attention was elsewhere.

She had been in Joren's professional network. That was what the placement channel said. Not in the noble-rival pool. Not in Vannic's world. In Joren's.

*[Investigator — New column opened: Renn, Halsa. Booking credential through Kolach channel, personal referral. Joren's professional network. Status: working suspect, evidentiary basis incomplete.]*

He read the System line once. He didn't touch it. He turned the page and began drafting the cross-reference list he would take to the magistrate that afternoon.

---

Sable's first letter arrived at the Arms shortly after second bell.

The boy from the post-coach station carried it in the inside pouch he reserved for letters that had been paid extra to move on the morning's return run from Sannholt. He found Theron in the dining room and handed it over with the formal half-bow the post boys at this station all used, which Theron was now familiar enough with to nod through without comment.

He gave the boy a small coin for the carry and broke the seal at the table.

Sable's hand was the same on paper as it was in her notebook, small and economical and slightly leftward, and the letter was three folded pages.

*Theron —*

*The Sannholt coach was as advertised. Three other passengers, none of them my business. The road was good. I am at the Whitepost Inn on the south side of the market square, room six, until I find out whether I want to be somewhere else.*

*Pessel agreed to speak in the morning. I sent word from the coach office on arrival; she replied within the hour at the inn. The reply was three sentences. She named the hour, she named the place — her own front room, not the Copper Finch — and she asked one question, which was whether I had been on the Wyrm with him. I wrote back that I had been. That seems to have been the answer she needed.*

*I have not seen her yet. What I can tell you about her so far is that her hand on paper is steady, that she writes her own letters and does not use a clerk, and that she did not ask me to bring documents. She is not approaching this as an inquiry. She is approaching it as a conversation she has been waiting to have for two years.*

*I asked at the inn for her address and got it without difficulty. The landlady knew her by name and by what she did for a living, which is what she had said it was in the harbor registry: small-contract paperwork, marriage settlements and inheritance papers and the occasional probate consultation. She is what she said she was. That is worth knowing as a starting point.*

He turned the page.

*The detail I am writing this letter for: when I asked the landlady — casually, in the way you ask landladies things — whether Pessel had many friends in the town, she said not many but loyal ones, and then she said something I think you should have before tomorrow's meeting. She said Pessel used to host a young woman in the back room of the Copper Finch perhaps three or four times a year. Coastal, she said. Worked the same kind of small magical trades. Quiet. Apprenticed once to a man who did the same work, the landlady thought, though the apprenticeship was years ago by the time she had started noticing the visits.*

*I did not ask the woman's name. The landlady did not offer one. I am writing this because I think the connection — Pessel, an apprentice, coastal, small magical trades — is the kind of detail your column-work would want before I sit with Pessel and ask her about Joren tomorrow. If the name turns out to matter, it will come up. I will let it come up the way she wants to bring it, not the way I want to take it.*

*Still have the fish. I have not taken it out. I will not bring it to the meeting tomorrow. I do not know yet whether tomorrow is the day for it.*

*— S.*

He read the letter through once at the table, and then he read the middle page again, slowly, line by line, and then he folded it along its existing creases and put it inside his case book.

He sat with the cup for a moment.

The shape of it was visible now. Pessel was Joren's long-running connection in Sannholt; an apprentice had moved through that connection; the apprentice was coastal, female, in the same trade. Halsa Renn was in cabin nine-T on the Wyrm, traveling through to Sannholt under a booking credential placed through Joren's professional network, and had observed the corridor with the attentiveness of someone who watched how people moved through spaces. Sable would have a name from Pessel by tomorrow afternoon, or she wouldn't, and either way the Halverstow side and the Sannholt side were converging on the same suspect column.

He did not write *Halsa Renn was Joren's apprentice* in the case book. He had not earned that line. He wrote, in the new column: *Sannholt source confirms coastal female apprentice in Joren's professional circle, name not yet given, visits to Pessel three to four times yearly. Cross-reference pending Pessel statement.*

He underlined nothing.

---

The dining room had filled around him while he wasn't watching. A factor at the next table was arguing politely with his wife about whether they should leave for Mirren on Monday or wait for the wire from her brother. A traveling cleric was eating a slow breakfast at the far window. The light through the window had moved the angle it had been at when the clerk arrived, and the harbor smelled, faintly, of the tar they were applying to one of the slips a block over.

He drew a fresh sheet from his case book and set it square in front of him.

*Sable —*

*The Kolach interview came back from Mirren faster than the budget. The wire arrived at first bell, the original transcript follows by post in four to six days. Kolach broke at the introduction letter. He had been running a credential placement service through Vantrel for eleven years; the Marbeth papers came through that channel; so did twenty-two other names in the past year. One of those names is Halsa Renn — booking credential, third class, our Wyrm departure.*

*She was in his network. Not Vannic's. Joren's.*

*Your apprentice detail is the same name in a second column. I do not have the personal-referral source yet — that is in the transcript, not in the wire — but Pessel may give it to you in the morning before Mirren gives it to me by post. Take it on her terms. I am not asking you to test the connection. I am asking you to know it is there while you listen.*

*I have not added her to the working column as a suspect. I have added her as a name that should now be in front of both of us.*

*The fish is right where it is. Tomorrow may or may not be the day. You will know.*

*— T.*

He read it through, made one small correction (*I am not asking you to test the connection* was clearer than the longer phrasing he had begun with), and sealed it for the afternoon post-coach back to Sannholt.

Then he turned back to the case book and wrote the new column out properly, in the order he wanted it in front of him for the next morning's work.

*Renn, Halsa. Cabin nine-T, Wyrm 23rd departure. Booking credential through Kolach channel — personal referral, source pending. Sannholt destination. Late lamp, cabin nine-T, third night (Calden, uncertain). Corridor observation behavior (Sable). Sannholt source — coastal female apprentice in Joren's circle, Pessel connection, name pending. Status: in front of us.*

He set the pencil down.

*[Investigator — Working column: Renn, Halsa. Status updated: in front of us.]*

He read the System line and almost smiled, because the System occasionally did this. It quoted his own phrasing back at him as though it were holding the page steady for him. Then he closed the case book.

The Saturday harbor outside was busy in its ordinary way. The wire transcript would arrive in four to six days. Sable would sit with Pessel in the morning. The post-coach to Sannholt left at fourth bell.

He picked up his coat and went to find the magistrate's afternoon clerk.

The case had a name in it that had been there all along. That was something. The rest was work.

---

# Chapter 27 — Unpicking a Profile

The Arms was quieter on Sunday than on the working mornings, and Theron took the corner table by the window because the corner table by the window had been his on every morning since he had arrived and the dining room had decided, without consulting him, that it was now where he sat. The pot came without asking. The toast came without asking. The host nodded at his case book and went back to the kitchen.

He opened the case book to yesterday's column page and read it through.

The column was four lines long. *Cabin nine-T. Booking credential through Kolach. Sannholt destination. Status: in front of us.* He had stopped there yesterday afternoon because he had wanted to stop there. He picked up the pencil now to keep going.

He wrote *Profile* across the top of the next clean spread and underlined it once.

Then he sat with the pencil over the page for a while.

The trouble was that he knew how to write a profile. He had been writing profiles since his second-year practicum, and the format on the page was a habit of the wrist. Background. Motive shape. Method affinity. Network. Behavioural signature. He could fill the boxes for any noble in the manifest's first-class column inside an hour, and had: six of them, in the first three days of the case, when he had still been working the Vannic frame.

The trouble was that the format was a frame too.

He set the pencil down and looked at the column from yesterday.

The noble-rivalry profile was a specific shape. It assumed a person who had stood to gain (money, position, marriage, suppression of a scandal) in a public economy where motive was visible because reputation was the currency. The profile filtered for that economy. Halsa Renn had moved through his case file for three weeks because nothing about her fit the filter, and he had read the absence of fit as absence of relevance.

He picked the pencil up.

*Profile — Renn, Halsa,* he wrote, slowly, because he did not want to drift back into the old shape while pretending to be writing a new one. Then he stopped and wrote, underneath: *Question one — what is she doing on the gondola.*

Sannholt destination, said cash, said family business. He had taken those answers at face value when he had taken her interview, because in the noble-rivalry frame a cash third-class through-passenger heading to a southern coastal town on family business was the lowest-tier reading there was. He had been right that the answers were not for him. He had been wrong that they were not answers.

The answers were the cover. Cash because the booking credential had been placed through Kolach, and the credential was the reference, and the cash was the part that made the reference unnecessary on the manifest's face. Family business because a stated reason for travel that nobody would ask about was the cleanest cover available. South coast because Sannholt was where her actual business was, and her actual business, whatever it was, was not the kind that named itself in a harbor registry.

He wrote: *Cover designed to pass a Wyrm registry, not to pass an investigator.*

He set the pencil down and read the line and picked the pencil up again.

That was the part. The cover had not been designed to pass an investigator because the cover had not anticipated one. A Wyrm departure registry-checked a third-class booking by reference, payment, and stated destination, and Halsa Renn's cover had answered the three questions a registry asked. The registry's questions were not his questions. His questions only mattered because the cabin twelve door had been bolted from the inside and a man had been found dead inside it, and the body of an illusionist named Joren Aldis had pulled an investigator onto a Wyrm route that had not been planning to carry one.

He wrote: *She did not expect me. She expected the manifest.*

That was not a flattering line about himself. It was also accurate. He left it.

He went on.

*Question two — what does she do for a living.* He wrote that and stopped. He did not know. The Wyrm interview had given him *travel-through, south coast, family,* and the booking credential through Kolach told him she was inside the same professional channel as Joren, which was a channel made for working mages whose contract paperwork did not generate the standard references. That was not a profession. That was a category of profession.

He wrote: *Working mage, contract-side, no formal credentials registered. Inside Joren's channel.*

It was thin. It was as much as he had. He went on.

*Question three — relationship to Joren Aldis.* Sable's letter from yesterday had given him the apprenticeship shape: Pessel had hosted a coastal young woman three or four times a year, formerly apprenticed to a man in the same magical trades. The landlady had said *apprenticed*. The landlady had not said to whom. He wrote: *Apprenticeship line — pending Pessel confirmation. Source: Sable / Whitepost landlady.*

Then he wrote, because it was the next thing on the page he had to be honest about: *Motive — unknown. Working assumption: personal, not professional. Basis: she boarded a flight Joren was on, under a placed credential, with cover designed for registries, and the death method (working assumption pending) was suited to a closed cabin and an illusionist target.*

He underlined *unknown* once, lightly, and set the pencil down.

He drank coffee.
