The noble-rivalry profile would have given him a motive in line one. *Wants the dukedom. Wants the dowry. Wants the silence about the affair.* He could write those sentences in his sleep. He could not write a sentence here because he did not know what Joren had done to a working mage who had apprenticed to him, eight or ten or fifteen years ago, in a coastal fishing town Theron had never been to. The motive lived inside a life he had not had a category for.

He looked at the column.

He had missed her because his training pattern had no slot for her. That was the finding. He wrote it at the bottom of the page in a single line: *Profile he had been trained to write: noble-rivalry economy, public motive, reputational currency. Profile required: contract-side network, private motive, personal currency. The first profile excluded the entire shape of the second.*

He read the line.

He read it again.

He did not feel particularly clever about it. He felt the way he had felt after the recall passage in Sable's cabin. The feeling of having been carrying a wrong shape for a long time and having it set down. The setting-down was a relief. The carrying had cost him three weeks.

He turned the page and wrote the morning's date and the place and *Working profile, R. Renn, draft one* at the top, and copied the lines across in the order he wanted them in front of him for the magistrate tomorrow. He did not include the line about himself. That one stayed on the page in the case book, where he could look at it again later and not forget what it had cost.

The pot was empty. He signalled the host for a fresh one and went on.

---

By second bell the post-coach from Sannholt was due, and Theron had read his working profile through four times and stopped revising it because he was past the point where revision improved it.

The boy from the station found him in the dining room with the second letter, which had come up on the morning return run and which carried the Whitepost Inn's wax. Theron gave the boy the same small coin and broke the seal at the table.

The letter was four pages this time.

*Theron —*

*I am back at the inn. The meeting was an hour and a quarter. I am writing this while it is in front of me because the shape of what she said will compress by tomorrow and I want you to have the uncompressed version.*

*Pessel met me at the door of her front room at the named bell. The room is small and clean and faces north. There were two chairs by the window and a tea-set already on the side table, which she had set out before I arrived. She is in her early fifties, grey, very still in the shoulders, the kind of stillness that comes from a profession that requires you not to react to other people's documents. She made the tea. She handed me a cup. She sat down. Then she said:*

*"Tell me whether he died well."*

*That was the first thing she said, after the cup. I told her what I could. I told her that the death was inside a sealed cabin, that there was no visible wound, that you had said, when I asked, that whatever had been done to him had been done by someone who knew how to do it cleanly. She listened without interrupting. When I was finished she nodded once and drank her tea and said: "Thank you. It was the question I needed answered before I asked the others."*

*Then she told me about him.*

*She has known Joren, known him as Joren and not as the working name he used in Mirren, for eleven years. He came through Sannholt three or four times a year. He sat in this front room. He brought her papers to look at, the small contract-side paperwork his work generated, marriage settlements that were not exactly marriages, inheritance papers that were not exactly inheritances. She made them clean enough for the registries he had to file with. She did not ask him what the work was. She had a sense. She is not naive.*

She turned the page. The paragraph below was newer ink, written after a pause.

*She told me his Sannholt errand. I am putting this here exactly as she said it, because I do not want to interpret it for you. She said: "He had been putting money aside for the child for eleven years. The arrangement was that when he had enough to come home, he would come home. He told me, the spring before last, that he was close. The Wyrm route was the last large piece of work. He intended to come back south, settle the money on the child's mother, and stay. The child does not yet know he existed. The child's mother knows. I held the money."*

*She held the money, Theron. She has been holding it for two years. She had been waiting for him to come home and tell the mother what he had been doing for eleven years, and to be in the child's life as something other than the man the mother never named. She said this without any tone in it. She said it the way someone says the address of a house they have walked past many times.*

*I did not write any of this down in front of her. I am writing it now.*

He turned the page.

*Then she said, without my asking, that he had trained someone, once. A young woman, coastal, the same fishing-town stretch he came from. She used to come through Sannholt as well, three or four times a year, sometimes overlapping with him and sometimes not. Pessel said she stopped coming about two years ago. Pessel said she did not know whether the apprentice had known Joren was putting money aside. Pessel said the apprentice had known about the work, because she had done the same work, because that was what an apprentice meant in their trade.*

*Pessel did not say the apprentice's name.*

*I did not ask it.*

*She told me this with the same evenness she had told me about the money. She did not link the two. She told me the apprentice had stopped coming two years ago, and the money had been held for two years, and she did not say whether the two facts were connected in her mind. I think they may be, but I do not know, and I am writing what she said.*

*I asked her one question. I asked her whether Joren had been afraid, on his last visit, of anyone in particular. She thought about it. Then she said: "He was not afraid of a person. He was tired of the work, and he was running out of time on the arrangement, and he wanted very badly to come home. That is not the same as being afraid. He was a man who had taken work he should not have taken, more than once, and he had decided some time ago that he would carry that himself and not bring it into this room. If he had been afraid of someone specifically, he would not have told me. He had a courtesy about what he brought through this door."*

*That was the meeting. She thanked me for coming. She asked, at the door, whether I would come back if there was more to say. I said yes. She thanked me again. The whole of it took an hour and a quarter, and I do not think she lied to me once.*

*The fish is in my coat. She did not ask about Joren's effects. I did not offer.*

*— S.*

He sat with the letter for a long minute.

The Sunday afternoon was doing its quiet thing through the dining-room window. Two factors at the next table were debating the price of slate. Someone in the kitchen had begun on a bread that smelled of caraway. He let the letter sit in front of him until the smell had filled the room, and then he refolded it carefully along its existing creases and laid it inside the case book.

He turned to the working profile he had drafted that morning.

He picked up the pencil.

He wrote, under *Question three — relationship to Joren Aldis*: *Apprentice line confirmed. Pessel: Joren trained a young woman, coastal, same fishing-town region, came through Sannholt three or four times a year for an extended period. Stopped approximately two years ago. Name not given by Pessel; not requested.*

He stopped. He read the column.

He added a fourth line, under the others: *Question four — what changed two years ago.*

He did not answer it. He underlined the question once and set the pencil down.

The line about the money he did not put in the working column. That was not yet the column's business. He turned to a blank page at the back of the case book, the place he kept the things he had been told that he had not yet earned the right to use, and wrote: *Joren had been putting money aside for the child for eleven years. He intended the Wyrm route to be his last large job. The child's mother knows. The child does not. Pessel held the money.*

He read that. He read it again. He let it sit.

*[Investigator — Working column updated: Renn, Halsa. Apprentice line confirmed via Pessel (Sable). Question four open: what changed two years ago.]*

The System line had not asked his permission. He read it and let it be.

---

He drew a fresh sheet for the reply.

*Sable —*

*I have the letter. Read it twice. Do not compress; you wrote it the right way.*

*The apprentice line confirms the column. I have not added the name; Pessel did not give it and you were right not to ask. The wire transcript will give us the personal-referral source from Kolach's end in three or four more days, and that will close the line from the Halverstow side. We will have the name from one side or the other. Probably both.*

*The thing she told you about the money. I have put that in the back of the book, separately. It is not the column's business yet. It may not become the column's business. It is hers and the child's mother's, and we are not the people who carry it. I am writing it down because you wrote it down, and so that one of us has it on the right page if it ever needs to be on a page.*

*The two-year gap is now a question on the working column. I do not know what it means. I am not going to guess at it from this side. If Pessel offers more when you go back, take it on her terms again.*

*The morning here was a profile draft. I will tell you about it when we are in the same room. The short of it is that my training pattern was the wrong filter for the person we are looking at, and the wrong filter excluded her cleanly for three weeks. I had not understood, before this morning, how clean an exclusion that was. It is a finding about my work. It is also a finding about her.*

*The fish stays where it is.*

*— T.*

He sealed it, addressed it for the afternoon coach, and handed it to the host on his way past the kitchen.

Then he went back to the corner table, refilled the cup, and read the working profile through once more.

He did not make any changes to it.

The bread came out of the oven somewhere behind the kitchen door, and the smell moved through the dining room the way good smells do on Sundays, in no particular hurry. He let it.

Outside the window, the Halverstow harbour was doing its Sunday work at half speed. The tar smell from yesterday was gone. Somewhere down the quay, a child was being given instruction about a rope, in the patient tones of a parent who was going to give the same instruction four more times before the morning was out.

He turned the case book back to the column page and looked at the four questions in their order.

Three of them he could begin to answer.

The fourth was still open, and he was content to leave it open until something else arrived to begin the answer for him. The post-coach to Sannholt left at fourth bell. The wire transcript was due Wednesday or Thursday. The working profile was on a page, in the order he wanted it in front of him, and the morning's finding about himself was on a separate page where it would not be forgotten.

He drank the coffee while it was still hot.

It was, he reflected, the most honest morning's work he had done on the case to date. He was aware that this was a low bar, and he was aware that the awareness did not make the morning any less worth keeping.

He closed the case book.

---

# Chapter 28 — The Wrong Kind of Certainty

The magistrate's small office was at the back of the courthouse, on the side that took the morning light through one square window and gave it to a desk that had been the same desk for thirty years. Theron arrived at second bell because that was when the magistrate had said to arrive, and the clerk in the front room nodded him through without asking.

Magistrate Davren was already at the side table with two cups poured.

"Sit, Mavik."

Theron sat. He set the case book down between them, square to the edge of the table, and opened it to the column page. He had not made any changes since Sunday afternoon. He had read it through twice this morning to be sure.

"Walk me through it," Davren said.

Theron walked him through it. He kept the order he had practised: cabin nine-T, the Kolach placement, the Sannholt destination, Calden's uncertain lamp observation, the corridor-watching behaviour Sable had logged from the original Wyrm interview. He named the apprentice line from Pessel and was careful to say *confirmed via Sable* rather than *confirmed by Pessel directly,* because the distinction mattered for the magistrate's notes and would matter again if any of this went into a brief.

He did not raise his voice. He did not say *the suspect.* He said *the column,* and *the working line,* and once, *the person we are looking at.*

Davren listened. He did not write anything down. He was a man who had learned to listen first and write afterward, and the listening had a quality to it — not patient, exactly. Available.

When Theron had finished, Davren drank coffee.

Then he asked the first question.

"Did she enter cabin twelve on the third night?"

"No," Theron said. "Calden's corridor sheet has her in cabin nine-T from second watch to first bell. Two of the steward's pass-by notes have her cabin lit at first watch and dark at third. Nothing in the corridor record has her moving."

"Did anyone else enter cabin twelve on the third night?"

"No. The door was bolted from inside at the time the body was discovered. The bolt was the original Wyrm fitting, not tampered. The cabin's only other access is the porthole, which was latched, and the porthole would not pass an adult."

Davren nodded once. He set the cup down.

"Then I have a column I can keep in front of me, Mavik, and I do not yet have a case I can bring forward."

"I know," Theron said.

"The Kolach placement is good work. The Sannholt apprentice line is good work. The corridor behaviour is observation, not evidence. The lamp is steward's uncertainty. If you put this in front of a coastal magistrate at her destination, and she asked you on what grounds she should hold the woman for examination, you would have to tell her: on grounds that the woman's booking was placed through a channel the dead man also used, that she was in his old professional circle, and that she watched a corridor with attention. Those are not arrest grounds. They are interview grounds at best, and only if you can produce the channel-source link in court, which means the Mirren transcript, which is not yet in your possession."

"It's due Wednesday or Thursday."

"Good. Bring it to me when it lands." He paused. "What I do not see here, Mavik, is the method. You have a working suspect. You have a closed cabin and a man dead inside it without a wound. What I do not have on this page is any account of how the woman in cabin nine-T caused a death in cabin twelve without entering it."

Theron had been waiting for that one. He had hoped not to need to hear it said aloud, and he had known he would.

"I don't have the method," he said.

"No," Davren said. "You don't."

He let the silence be exactly long enough.

"You have done a good piece of work on the who," he said, after. "Take it the rest of the way. Find me how. When you find me how, we go to the coastal magistrate together."

"Yes."

"Wednesday or Thursday for the transcript. I'll keep the column open at this end." He closed the case book and slid it back. "Drink your coffee, Mavik. The morning's still young."

Theron drank his coffee. It was good coffee, the courthouse blend, brewed thicker than the Arms ran it. He left twenty minutes later with the case book under his arm and Davren's two questions clean in his head, which was the way Davren had wanted them to be.

The walk back to the Arms was short. Halverstow's harbour was at full Monday speed. Two skiffs were unloading at the near slip and the rope-master was shouting good-naturedly at an apprentice who had fed the line through the wrong block.

Theron did not stop to watch. He went up the steps of the Arms and back to his corner table, and sat down to the column with one new line at the bottom of the page.

*Method — unknown.*

He underlined it once.

---

The partial transcript came up on the late-morning post-coach.

He had not been expecting it before Wednesday. The boy from the station found him at the table with the cup half empty and handed over a single folded sheet sealed in the Mirren magistrate's office wax, which was the small green wax used for partial issues, not the heavy black used for full transcripts.

The note inside the wax was the magistrate's, not the clerk's. *Mavik — the personal-referral source from Kolach's testimony you asked us to expedite. Full transcript follows by post as agreed. — Velden, Mirren.*

The sheet itself was two sentences in the Mirren clerk's hand.

*Kolach, sworn statement, paragraph nine: the seventeenth placement of the current year, Renn, Halsa, was personally referred to my service by Joren Aldis at the time of her first placement, eleven years prior. The current-year credential is the renewal of the original placement made under his vouching.*

Theron read it.

He read it again.

He set the sheet down flat against the table and put his cup beside it.

The dining-room sounds did their ordinary thing for a moment. A factor at the next table laughed at something his clerk had said. Someone on the stairs was carrying a tray. The smell from the kitchen was the same caraway loaf Sunday's had been, or the host had baked another, or it was a different smell altogether and he was not noticing properly.

He picked the pencil up.

He did not write the thing he wanted to write, which was *Joren placed her in the trade himself.* That was the sentence the page wanted from him, and the page was wrong. The placement was true. The sentence carried freight he could not yet warrant. He knew that eleven years ago a young woman had needed a credential placement to work in a contract-side trade with no formal channel for new entrants, and that Joren had vouched for her with a man who ran a discreet placement service, and that the original vouching had been renewed in the current year for the Wyrm departure on which Joren had died.

That was what he wrote, in the working column, in the order he had practised: *Personal-referral source confirmed. Kolach paragraph nine, Mirren transcript partial issue. Source: Joren Aldis, eleven years prior. Current-year placement is a renewal of the original.*

He stopped.

He read the line.

He did not underline anything.

He sat back. The cup was lukewarm now. He drank it anyway, because the alternative was to ring for a fresh pot and have to say *thank you* to the host, and his face was doing whatever it was doing about Joren having put her into the trade, and he did not particularly want the host to see his face for the next few minutes.

He turned to the back of the case book, to the page where he kept the things he had been told that he had not yet earned the right to use, the same page where Sunday's note about the money for the child sat in its careful single-line summary. He wrote, under it: *Joren personally referred Halsa Renn into the Kolach placement channel eleven years prior. Current-year credential is the renewal of that original vouching. Awareness on Joren's part of any grievance against him at time of original placement: unknown; current working assumption is that no such grievance existed yet, or had not yet reached him.*

That sentence took him longer to write than he wanted to admit.

He read it. He read it again. He let it sit.

Then he turned back to the column and looked at the four questions in their order, and at the new line at the bottom of the page.

*Method — unknown.*

He drank the cold coffee.

---

The afternoon brought the second post.

There was no letter from Sable. The boy carried up a thin courier note from the Whitepost Inn, no seal, just the inn's stamp, and the note was four lines.

*Theron — leaving Sannholt on the evening coach. One more thing to look at this afternoon; will write properly tonight from the road, the letter to follow the morning coach. In Halverstow by Wednesday's last bell at the latest, perhaps Tuesday's. — S.*

He read it twice and put it in the case book.

He did not know what the *one more thing* was. She had not said, because she had not had time to write, and because she had a habit, by now, of not writing what she had not yet looked at. He would have done the same. He approved of it without making a particular point of approving of it; the approval was the kind of small habitual recognition he had stopped registering as a thing he did about her some weeks ago.

The dining room around him had filled, then half-emptied. The light on the harbour had moved through its slow Monday afternoon arc and was working on the brass fittings of the slips opposite. The skiffs from the morning unloading had gone. A larger vessel was working into the near berth, and the rope-master from earlier had a new audience for the same patient instruction.

He drew the working profile out, and the partial transcript, and the column page, and laid them in front of him in their order.

He had the *who.*

He read the column down. Halsa Renn, cabin nine-T, Kolach placement personally referred by Joren eleven years prior, Sannholt destination, apprentice line confirmed via Pessel, corridor-observation behaviour, late lamp uncertain, Q4 (what changed two years ago) open. The column was not yet a brief. It was, however, no longer four lines of inference. It was a person.

He could see her in it. Not as the killer. As the person who had moved through three weeks of his case file as background and was now in front of him, in the column, with eleven years of context attached to the name. He could see what she had been doing on the gondola: travelling under a credential she had every administrative right to carry, on a route her destination explained, with cash because that was how her trade booked itself, and watching a corridor because that was the kind of attention her working life required of her. None of it was guilt. All of it was *being on the gondola for a reason that was not Sannholt.*

She had been three doors down the corridor from cabin twelve on the third night. The cabin had been bolted from inside. The man inside had died without a wound.

He did not know how.

He turned to a fresh page in the case book and dated it. He wrote *Method* across the top and underlined it once. Then he sat with the pencil over the page for some time.

He had not been ready to think about method since the assumption had collapsed when Joren's real name came clear — three weeks of working one interpretation, and then the dock, and then none of it. The assumption he had been carrying through those weeks had brought its own probable methods with it: poison, hired hand, a quarrel that escalated. When it had broken, the methods had broken with it, and he had been working the *who* ever since. He had not been wrong to do that. Davren had said the morning's work was good work. It was good work.

It was also, now, finished. The *who* was the column. The column was in front of him. The *how* was a blank page.

He wrote, slowly: *A passenger in cabin nine-T did not enter cabin twelve on the third night. The door was bolted from inside. There was no visible wound. The body was Joren Aldis. The working suspect is an illusionist trained by Joren Aldis, who knew his working methods better than anyone else on the gondola.*

He stopped.

He read the last line.

The last line was new. He had written it without planning to. It had come out at the end of the paragraph because it had been sitting at the back of the morning's thinking since Pessel's apprentice line had landed in Sunday's letter, and at the front of the afternoon's thinking since the Mirren partial had landed in the morning's post. *An illusionist trained by Joren Aldis.* Halsa Renn had been Joren's apprentice. Apprenticeship in a working magical trade was specific. An apprentice learned, over years, the working repertoire of the master. The master's habits. The master's specialities. The kinds of work the master refused, and the kinds of work the master took, and the small private exploits the master had figured out across a career that had not generated any formal scholarship to record them.

Whatever method had been used in cabin twelve was a method that could be used by a passenger in cabin nine-T at thirty feet of corridor distance, against a sealed door, without entering, against a target who was an illusionist himself and would have recognised any standard approach.

He stopped writing because he had reached the edge of what he could put on the page tonight without going past what he had yet earned. He had the shape of the *how* in the negative. He did not have the *how* in the positive.

He set the pencil down.

*[Investigator — Working column updated: Renn, Halsa. Personal-referral source confirmed (Joren Aldis, eleven years prior). Method: unknown. Note: working suspect is uniquely positioned to know subject's professional repertoire.]*

He read the System line.

He did not particularly want the System to be helpful tonight. He wanted to be the one who got there. He read the line through twice anyway, because the System was not in the business of doing his work for him; it was in the business of agreeing, in its laconic way, that the work was now where it was. The line was an acknowledgement, not a hint. He took it as the acknowledgement it was.

He turned the case book back to the column page and looked at the four questions in their order, and then at *Method — unknown* under the column, and then at the new sheet behind it with the negative shape of the *how* on it.

He had been certain about the *who* since Davren's first question this morning. He had not articulated the certainty in those words because the column did not permit them yet, and would not until the method closed. The certainty was unprofessional in the technical sense (the column was not a case) and it was also true. He had not had this kind of certainty about any name on the interpretation he had been carrying since the dock, and looking back, he never would have. He had been working a frame that produced confidence, not certainty. The thing that had come into the case in the last seventy-two hours was different.

The trouble with certainty about the *who* was that it did not, by itself, produce the *how.* And without the *how,* the certainty was a private possession of his, and Davren's, and not a thing the world could be told.

He closed the case book on the method page.

He drank the rest of the coffee, which had been cold for an hour.

Outside, the Halverstow harbour was settling into its evening half-speed. The vessel in the near berth had finished its unloading. The rope-master was gone. The smell from the kitchen had moved on from caraway and was now something heartier, a stew or a pie, the kind of smell the Arms put out on a Monday because the Monday trade liked to eat at the bar and walk home before dark.

He would have the host bring him whatever it was. He would eat at his table. He would go to bed early, because the *how* was not going to come tonight, and tomorrow was a clean morning, and the wire transcript was due Wednesday, and Sable would be in Halverstow by Wednesday last bell at the latest.

The method was not coming tonight.

He was, however, certain. He let the certainty be private. He kept the case book closed.

The host came past to ask whether he wanted the pie or the stew. Theron asked which the kitchen was prouder of. The host said the stew, but the pie was the better bet on a Monday, because the stew had been simmering since lunch and the pie was an hour out of the oven. Theron asked for the pie.

It came on a thick plate with a single bay leaf laid on the crust for decoration, the way the Arms' kitchen had been doing it for as long as the Arms' kitchen had been baking pies, and the crust was very good indeed.

He ate it slowly.

The case book sat by his elbow, closed, with the column page underneath the method page underneath the back-of-book page where the careful sentence about the original placement sat in its single line. The whole of it was a quiet weight on the table. He was content to let it be a weight he did not open again this evening.

The *who* was settled.

The *how* was the next morning's problem, and the morning after that, and however many mornings after that it took.

He finished the pie. He paid for it. He went up the stair.

---

# Chapter 29 — The Exam-Fodder Theory

The morning post-coach came in at first bell instead of second, which the host mentioned in passing as he set the pot down. Tuesday's coach ran early when the harbour wind ran true, and the harbour wind had run true since before dawn. Theron thanked him for the pot and the information, in that order.

The letter arrived twenty minutes later, in the small brown wrapper Sable used for road letters, with the Whitepost Inn's stamp on the seal and a smudge of ink on the corner where she had refolded the sheet without waiting for it to dry.

He opened it. He set it flat against the table where he could read it without holding it up.

*Theron,* she wrote.

*One further errand done. The Whitepost landlady's hint about the boarding-house was good: Copper Finch, two streets north of Pessel, back room let by the week. The proprietor is a woman named Hesh and she does not give a stranger anything she has not decided to give. I gave her your name and Davren's and she gave me three answers, no more, and was careful with each one.*

*One. A young coastal woman did stay in the back room three or four times a year, for the last nine of the eleven Pessel has been here.*

*Two. She stopped coming about two years ago without quarrel — Hesh's word — and without notice.*

*Three. She always travelled south from Sannholt afterward, never north.*

*Hesh added one line without my asking. She said the woman did work that needed quiet rooms. I wrote it down in her words. She did not elaborate and I did not press.*

*I am on the road south. I will be in Halverstow by Tuesday's last bell, possibly earlier if the second coach holds its schedule. — S.*

He read it through once.

He read it through a second time on the line about the quiet rooms.

He picked up the pencil, turned to the column page, and wrote, under the apprentice-line entry: *Copper Finch, Sannholt — back room, three to four visits/year for nine years, stopped two years ago, southbound after each visit. Proprietor Hesh, unprompted: "did work that needed quiet rooms."*

He set the pencil down.

The dining room was working its morning. A factor at the second table was reading a manifest aloud to a clerk who was not writing anything down. The kitchen door swung. Somebody on the stair was carrying a child who did not want to be carried.

*Did work that needed quiet rooms.*

He drank coffee.

The sentence was not the kind of sentence that did its work fast. It was the kind that sat in the column and got picked up later, when something else came near it and the two of them gave off a small heat together. He had a great many sentences like that in the case book at this point. He was not, as a rule, in a hurry to read them.

But this one was sitting next to a blank method page.

He read the line again.

Then he stood up.

His cabin slate was in his coat pocket, where it had been since he had unpacked at the Arms on Friday. The thing he wanted now was not the slate. The thing he wanted now was a small unlovely notebook he had not opened since he had left the Conservatory three weeks before he had boarded the Wyrm. He went upstairs to get it.

---

The notebook had been part of the graduation packet — every newly licensed Investigator was issued one, and most of them put it in a drawer and forgot it. It was a slate-bound thing, the size of a hand, containing the abstracts of the licensee's final-year thesis and any examined practicum work, copied in the Conservatory clerk's hand and bound for licensing reference. The point of the thing was that any working magistrate could, in principle, demand a young Investigator's working background on the spot, and the young Investigator could open the notebook and put it in front of the magistrate.

In practice no magistrate had ever asked for one. Theron's was at the bottom of his case-bag.

He brought it down to the table. He set it beside the case book and beside the partial Mirren transcript and beside Sable's letter, and the four of them made the shape his table had been making for three days now: a working surface, no decoration, the host moving the cups around them with the practised inattention of a man who knew when not to look at a guest's papers.

Theron opened the notebook.

The first page was the licensing summary, which he skipped. The second was the practicum log, which he skipped. The third was the thesis abstract.

*Scryproof Glamour Exploits: Theoretical Vulnerability in Long-Contact Illusion Chains. Th. Mavik, final year, Conservatory.*

He read his own abstract.

He had not read it in nine months. It was, in the cool way of clerk's transcription, almost not his — the sentences were the sentences he had written, but the rhythm in the clerk's hand was different from the rhythm he heard in his head, and the abstract sat on the page like a piece of someone else's work he had been asked to evaluate.

*The thesis examines a class of long-contact illusion-chain exploits in which a sustained glamour, once seated within a compatible magical signature, persists after the casting party's withdrawal and registers to standard scry-detection as ambient field rather than as cast magic. The exploit's defining requirement is sustained contact between caster and target during the seating phase. Its second requirement is structural compatibility between caster's and target's signatures. The exploit's practical applications are limited by the difficulty of arranging the seating phase under non-laboratory conditions; no historical practical instance is recorded. The thesis examines the theoretical vulnerability and recommends scry-detection refinements to the Conservatory's current field standards.*

He read it twice.

He read the line on sustained contact a third time.

The examiner who had liked the thesis had written, in the margin of his examined copy, *Original, if limited in practical application.* The examiner who had not liked it had written, in the margin of the same copy, *Somewhat overstated.* He remembered both of those marginal notes the way he remembered his mother's voice on the morning she had cried and his father's saying *good* over the kitchen table. The notes had become part of the pin, in his head. He had not, since the pin had been put on his lapel, gone back to read the thesis itself.

He sat with the notebook open in front of him.

The dining room was still working its morning. The child had stopped objecting to being carried. The Arms' pot was, as always, very good.

He went on reading. The abstract was a page long. He read the entire page. Then he turned to the back of the notebook, where the examiner's marginal notes had been copied separately, and he read those too.

The examiners had asked him, at his defence, why he had spent a year of his final work on a class of exploit for which no historical instance was on record. He remembered answering that the absence of recorded instance was itself the structural interest: a class of exploit that did not generate historical instance was, by definition, a class of exploit whose successful uses did not register as exploits at all. They had nodded at the answer. The examiner who had liked the thesis had liked it more after the answer. The examiner who had not liked it had not liked it less.

He closed the notebook on his thumb and looked at the case book.

The Mirren transcript lay folded next to it. The method page was the page after the column page, and it had been blank since Monday morning.

He opened the case book to the method page.

---

He did not write *the method is the scryproof glamour exploit.*

He had written that kind of sentence before, in his second-year practicum, and the practicum supervisor had read it and underlined the word *is* and written in the margin *too early.* He had carried the marginal note since. He carried it now.

He wrote, instead, on the method page, in the unindented hand he reserved for working theory not yet earned: *Working theory only.*

He wrote, under it: *Long-contact glamour-chain exploit (Mavik thesis, final year, Conservatory). Defining requirement: sustained contact between caster and target during the seating phase.*

He stopped.

He sat with the pencil over the page.

The seating phase. He had written, nine months ago, *non-laboratory conditions* in the dry voice the thesis had asked for. He had not written, anywhere in the thesis, what a non-laboratory seating phase would look like. He had not been writing a case. He had been writing a theoretical paper. He had assumed, with the comfortable assumption of a student under examination, that any real seating phase would have to be arranged deliberately, in private, by parties who had reason to arrange it.

He did not believe that any more.

He wrote, slowly: *Seating phase satisfied by a working apprenticeship. Eleven years. Casting alongside the master across a working relationship that required contact-range channelling.*

He read it back. The pencil hovered. He did not underline.

He wrote, under it: *Signature compatibility satisfied between two illusionists.*

He read that line back, and stopped, and read it back a second time, because the sentence on the page was not the sentence he had imagined he would ever write. *Signature compatibility between two illusionists* was a phrase he had used in his abstract to indicate the laboratory pair-test scenario, in which two licensed casters of the same school agreed to a controlled seating under supervisor observation. It had not occurred to him, while writing the thesis, that the same words described a master and an apprentice working in a coastal town for years at a time.

It had not occurred to him because he had not been writing about people. He had been writing about a structural exploit, in a theoretical paper, for an examination he had passed.

He wrote, in the same careful hand: *Once seated, the exploit does not require the caster to be near the target at the moment of activation. Activation requires only a trigger keyed to the seating signature. Trigger known to caster; not known to target.*

He set the pencil down.

He read the four lines together.

The four lines did not say that Halsa Renn had killed Joren Aldis in cabin twelve from cabin nine-T with a glamour seated during her apprenticeship.

The four lines said that, if Halsa Renn had killed Joren Aldis in cabin twelve from cabin nine-T, the long-contact glamour-chain exploit from the final year of his own thesis would have been a method she had access to, and that the access was structurally consistent with the apprentice-line confirmation from Pessel and with the *did work that needed quiet rooms* note from Hesh and with the eleven-year placement from the Mirren transcript. The four lines were a negative shape. They described what a method could be, if there were a method.

That was not the same as the method.

He let it be a negative shape. He did not let it be more. He had been at the table for three hours and the host had come past and refilled the pot twice and the four lines on the method page were the most he had been able to write in three weeks about how a man had died in a cabin nobody had entered, and he was not, on a Tuesday morning, going to let himself name them more than they were.

He did write, in plain ink, under the four lines: *To be tested against cabin twelve geometry and against the bolt and porthole on Sable's return.*

He underlined *tested* once, lightly. He underlined nothing else.

---

The host brought him soup at midday, on the assumption that a man at a working table since first bell would not have thought to order it himself. Theron looked up, registered the soup, registered the spoon, registered the host's mild expression, and thanked him.

"It's the white-bean," the host said. "Tuesday's. The kitchen does it with the parsley early on, before the long flavour comes in. You catch it now or you catch it at four."

"Now," Theron said.

"Good answer." The host moved on.

He ate the soup. It was, in fact, the kind of soup that did better at the early window. He thought about the four lines on the method page while he ate it, and the soup did not lose by the thinking. The two did their work in parallel and were companionable about it.

He set the bowl aside and went back to the notebook.

The thing he had not yet done was the thing he should have done in the first hour, and he had not done it because he had not wanted to. He turned, in the slate-bound notebook, to the section of his examined defence script that the Conservatory clerk had copied verbatim for the licensing record. The script was the transcript of the oral defence — his words and the examiners', clean, no editorial. He had not opened the section since the day he had collected the pin.

He read the examiner's question about the absence of historical instance.

He read his own answer.

He read the examiner's follow-up, which was the question he had remembered better than the first one and the answer to which had won him the pin.

The follow-up had been: *Mr. Mavik, in the absence of any recorded case, on what evidentiary basis should the Conservatory recommend altering its field scry-detection standards?*

He had answered: *The absence of recorded case is the evidentiary basis. A class of exploit that does not register to current scry-detection will not generate cases under current scry-detection. The Conservatory is being asked to alter the detection because the detection itself is what is filtering out the evidence.*

He had been pleased with the answer at the time. He had thought, as he sat down after the defence, that he had said the thing he had come to say. The examiner who had liked the thesis had written, on the inside cover of the examined copy after the defence, *A good year's work. Well argued.*

He had not, on the day, considered the corollary.

He considered it now, at his table at the Arms, on a Tuesday afternoon, with the white-bean bowl pushed to one side.

The corollary was that *a class of exploit that does not register to current scry-detection will not generate cases under current scry-detection* meant, also, that any case it did generate would arrive looking like something else. A sealed cabin. A man dead inside it without a wound. No method on the page.

That was the corollary he had not stated at his defence, because at his defence he had been twenty-one years old and writing a theoretical paper and the corollary belonged to a working investigator, not to a candidate. He was a working investigator now. He had been one for three weeks.

He closed the notebook.

He sat with the case book open on the method page and the working-theory lines unindented on it.

He did not feel pleased.

He had been told, at the Conservatory, that there would be a moment in the first year of a working career when an examined piece of his own past work would arrive in the present and be useful. The Warden had said it, dryly, on the day of the licensing ceremony, as a thing to expect and not as a thing to wait for. *The pin is for what you have already learned. The work is for what you will be made to learn it for.*

He had taken the line for the kind of ceremony-day sentence the Warden was given to producing on ceremony days. He had been wrong. The Warden, as a rule, did not produce sentences for ceremony days. The Warden produced sentences for use, and the day had been incidental.

He read his four lines on the method page one more time.

He left them as they were. He did not add a fifth. The fifth line was *to be tested,* and *to be tested* was already on the page, and the testing was a thing he was not doing this afternoon because Sable was on a coach south and would be at the Arms by last bell and the testing wanted both of them at the table for it.

He picked up the pencil. He turned to the back-of-book page, where the Sunday note about the money for the child sat in its single line, and where the careful sentence about the original placement sat under it. He wrote, at the bottom of that page, in the smallest hand he had: *The thesis was not exam fodder.*

He read it.

He drew a single line through it and wrote, beside the struck-out line: *The thesis was exam fodder. It was also correct.*

That was the sentence he had needed to write. He did not need to write any more.

*[Investigator — Method page updated. Working theory logged. Status: theory only; pending field test against cabin geometry.]*

The System was being its laconic self. He read the line through once and gave it the small dry nod that was the only acknowledgement the System ever wanted from him. He did not particularly enjoy the System's neutrality this afternoon. He approved of it anyway.

He closed the case book on the method page with the four working-theory lines still unindented in it.

Outside, the Halverstow harbour was doing its Tuesday afternoon. The tide was on the turn, and the rope-master from yesterday had a new apprentice and the same patient instruction, and the brass on the slip fittings was throwing back the afternoon sun at a friendly angle. The Arms' kitchen smelled, now, of bread, which meant the host had started the second loaves of the day for the evening trade.

The coach to Halverstow from the north was due at last bell.

The case book sat on the table. The slate-bound notebook sat beside it, closed, the abstract three pages in and the defence script ten pages further. The method page was, for the first time since Friday, no longer blank.

It was not solved.

He let it be unsolved. He drank what was left of the coffee, which was cold, and waited for the bell.

---

# Chapter 30 — The Theory Holds

Last bell came in over the harbour with its usual slight argument from the church on the western slope, which rang fractionally early on Tuesdays and had done so for as long as the host had been at the Arms. Theron heard the difference and the resolution from his corner table. He set the pencil down and closed the case book on the method page and turned the cup so the handle pointed away from the door.

He had been sitting at the table since the soup. The soup had been four hours ago.

The host came past with the lamp-stick on his way to the front room. "She'll be on this one if she's on any."

"Thank you."

"I held the bean soup. There's a half-loaf put by." Said without ceremony, the way the host said most things. He went on to light the lamps.

Theron waited.

The coach came in at the side door with the small commotion of a coach arriving — bag down, fare paid, the driver thanking somebody who had ridden up top in the cold. He heard her voice before he saw her, asking after a room and being answered before she had finished the question.

Sable came into the dining room with her bag in her left hand and the brown travelling coat over her arm, already half off her shoulders, her hair pulled back hard against the road. She saw him at the corner table. She did not smile. She nodded once, the small efficient nod of a person who had been on a coach since dawn and had got off it.

"You sat."

"I sat."

She put the bag down by the chair and dropped into it. The host was already coming over with the bread and the bowl and a clean cup, and Sable accepted all three in order without breaking stride. She ate two spoons of the soup, paused, ate a third, then set the spoon down for long enough to lift the cup.

"Tell me about the room first."

"Top of the stair. Next to mine. The host put a warming-pan in at fourth bell."

"Then I can stop thinking about it." She picked the spoon back up. "Don't open the book."

"I wasn't going to."

She kept eating. He watched her do it without watching her do it, which was a small recent skill he had been developing without comment. The host moved past with the pot. Somebody in the kitchen called to somebody else about the second oven going cold. A man two tables over was paying his bill in the slow voice of someone who had been at the Arms since before dark.

She finished the soup. She tore the half-loaf into two pieces and put one of them back on the plate.

"Now," she said.

---

He turned the case book around so it faced her and opened it to the method page.

He did not say the four lines aloud. He let her read them. She read them once at her own pace, and then again with the cup at her mouth, and then she set the cup down and put her finger on the second line.

"Sustained contact during a seating phase." She looked up. "Define seating phase."

"It's the period during which a glamour is laid into a host signature." He kept his voice low; the room was thin. "The technical word is *seating* because the spell isn't worn — it's set in. Once it's set, it stays without further casting on the caster's part. Until then it requires the caster and the target to be in continuous contact-range channelling. Not touching. Within working distance. For long enough that the two signatures register one another at a structural level."

"How long is long enough?"

"It varies by signature. Theoretical floor is hours of continuous work. Conservative practical estimate is — " he stopped, because he had been going to give her the laboratory number, which was eighty hours, "— I don't know what it is between a master and an apprentice. The thesis didn't speculate. The thesis assumed laboratory pair-tests."

"But an apprenticeship would do it."

"An apprenticeship of any length would do it many times over."

She read the second line again. She turned the page back to the abstract and read the abstract, slowly, her lips moving once on a phrase, the way she read documents when she was not going to take them in any other way. He drank coffee. He did not interrupt.

She finished the abstract.

"The thing you wrote on the third line." She put her finger on it. "Activation does not require the caster near the target."

"Once it's seated. The seating builds the structural pair. After that, the caster carries the trigger in her own signature. The target carries the seated glamour in his. The trigger reaches the glamour through the structural pair, not through proximity."

"Distance limit?"

"In theory none. In practice the field falls off over very long ranges, but thirty feet of corridor is not very long ranges."

She looked at him.

She did not say *thirty feet of corridor.* She did not need to. The number had been in the case book for a long time. It had not, until tonight, attached to anything.

She read the fourth line.

"Trigger known to caster, not to target." She set the finger down. "He didn't know it was there."

"No."

"He couldn't have noticed it. Couldn't have looked for it."

"No."

"Because it wasn't being cast on him in the moment. It had been seated years ago and it was waiting."

"That's the shape of it."

She closed the case book on her own finger, kept it there, and looked at the wall for a moment. The lamp on the corner sconce was doing its small fluttering thing against the draught from the front-room door. He watched her not see it.

"All right," she said. "Show me the cabin."

---

He did not have the original Wyrm cabin schematic at the Arms — that was in the magistrate's evidence file, two streets over. He had something almost as useful, which was his own intake-note sketch from the first morning of the case, with the door, the porthole, the bolt diagram, and the corridor pass marked in his hand. He pulled it out of the back of the case book and laid it flat between them. Sable produced her own notebook from the inside pocket of her coat and turned it to the corridor sheet without being asked.

The sketch was not pretty. It was a working sketch. The cabin was a rectangle. The door was a thicker line on the corridor side. The bolt was a small notch on the inside of the door. The porthole was a circle on the outer wall with a latch mark on the inside. The corridor was a single straight line with the cabin numbers along it.

He pointed to the door.

"Bolted from inside at the time of discovery. The bolt was the Wyrm fitting, original, not tampered. The frame is steel-cored. The bolt seats into the frame. There is no external override on a Wyrm first-class cabin bolt. The crew used the master-key on the latch to open the door for the discovery, and the bolt had to be drawn from inside before that could be done, which it wasn't, so they had to cut the bolt."

"And the porthole."

"Latched on the inside. The latch was not tampered. The frame would not pass an adult. We measured."

"And the corridor."

"Two pass-by entries from Calden during the third watch. One at one bell of the third watch, one at three. He noted no movement at the cabin twelve door either time. He noted the lamp in cabin nine-T as on at one bell — that's your sheet — and on or possibly off at three." He moved his finger along the corridor line on the sketch. "Cabin twelve. Cabin eleven, empty. Cabin ten, the Stallens. Cabin nine, also empty by then. Cabin nine-T, Renn."

Sable looked at her own corridor sheet. She did not move it next to the cabin sketch. She kept the two side by side and ran her finger along the corridor in her own notebook, naming the same cabins under her breath in the same order, the way a person checked a list against a list.

She stopped at nine-T.

"She did not pass cabin twelve."

"No."

"She did not need to."

"No."

"Because she had seated the glamour in him eleven years ago, and she was sitting in nine-T with the trigger in her hand."

She did not put a question mark on it. He did not answer it. The sentence was on the page in front of them and the page in front of them was a sketch of a cabin and a corridor, and the sentence belonged to the sketch in a way it had not belonged to any sentence on any page he had drawn since the bolted door had been cut on the third morning.

He breathed out, once, slowly.

"That's the geometry," he said.

"That's the geometry."

He let himself sit with the sentence for the length of one short pull from the cup. Then he turned the page in the case book and showed her the second thing.

---

The scry log was a single folded sheet with the Wyrm's standing-detection record for the third night. He had it because the Wyrm captain had given it to him on the first morning of the case as a matter of course, the way Wyrm captains gave standing-detection records to investigators boarding a death they had not been expecting to host. He had read the sheet at the time. He had filed it under *no cast magic in cabin twelve during the third night* and moved on.

He unfolded it now. He turned it toward Sable.

She read it. She knew how to read a scry log because Calden had walked her through one the previous week, in the slow patient way Calden walked her through things. She found the cabin twelve row. She found the third-watch column. She read the three letters in the cell.

"*N.R.*"

"No registration."

"Meaning no cast magic was detected in cabin twelve during the third watch."

"On the standing-detection rig the Wyrm carries, yes."

"Which is the rig that registers cast magic."

"That's its job."

She set the sheet down. She looked at him.

"The exploit." She picked her words carefully, because the next sentence was the one that mattered. "It doesn't register as cast magic."

"It registers as ambient field. If it registers at all."

"On standing-detection."

"On standing-detection." He turned the abstract over so the relevant paragraph was facing up between them and he put his finger on the sentence. "*A sustained glamour, once seated within a compatible magical signature, persists after the casting party's withdrawal and registers to standard scry-detection as ambient field rather than as cast magic.*"

"So the scry log saying *no registration* — "

"Is what the scry log would have said whether the exploit was being used or not."

"It doesn't clear cabin twelve."

"It doesn't speak to cabin twelve at all. It speaks to cast magic, in cabin twelve, on standing-detection. None of those things are the exploit."

She read the paragraph in the abstract. She read it twice. She turned to the page in his case book where he had been keeping the negative findings — *no wound, no poison vessel, no entry, no exit, no cast magic on scry* — and she put her finger on the last entry.

"You can move this one off the negative page."

"I know."

"It's not a negative finding any more. It's a positive consistency."

"I know."

She looked at him for a moment, the steady look that was not pity and was not satisfaction either. It was the look a person gave another person when the other person had just done a piece of work that mattered and was not going to be allowed to enjoy it because the work was not finished.

She picked up his pencil. She did not write in his case book — she never wrote in his case book — but she turned to her own notebook and wrote one sentence in it, in her small clear hand, and turned the notebook so he could read it.

*Only an illusionist, or someone trained alongside one, would know to look for a method that does not show up on a scry.*

He read it.

He read it again.

Then he picked up his pencil and turned to the method page and wrote, under the four working-theory lines, in the same unindented hand: *Only an illusionist, or someone trained alongside an illusionist for sustained periods, would know that the absence on the standing-detection record is consistent with the method, rather than exculpatory. The method assumes its own invisibility on the rig that was watching for it.*

He read the line back.

He did not underline anything.

Sable was eating the second piece of bread now. She had cut it into smaller pieces on her plate while he had been writing. She did not look at the page while he wrote. He had noticed, over the last three weeks, that she did not watch him write. She let him write. The line on her own notebook page she pushed across the table when he set the pencil down.

"For your column," she said. "You can copy it across in your own hand."

"It's your sentence."

"It's the working theory's sentence. I borrowed it from the page."

He did not argue. He copied her line across, in his own hand, under the fifth line, in plain ink. He left the version in her notebook where it was. Then he set the pencil down and turned the case book around so they could both look at the method page right-way-up.

Five lines. The cabin sketch. The scry log. The abstract turned face-up under all of it.

---

The Arms was almost down for the night. The kitchen door had stopped swinging some minutes ago. The man who had been paying his bill had gone. The host was putting the chairs up on the far tables, two at a time, with the particular economy of a man who had been putting up chairs for thirty years.

Theron read the five lines through.

The theory explained the cabin. The theory explained the scry. The theory required three things of the person who had used it: training next to an illusionist long enough to satisfy the seating phase; signature compatibility with that illusionist; access to the trigger after the apprenticeship had ended.

Halsa Renn satisfied the first. Pessel had given them eleven years of apprenticeship. The Mirren transcript had given them an original placement made under Joren's vouching. Sable's three answers from Hesh had given them a back room in Sannholt let three or four times a year for nine of those eleven years and *did work that needed quiet rooms.*

Halsa Renn satisfied the second, by inference. An apprentice in a working magical trade developed signature compatibility with her master as a matter of the training itself. You could not avoid it. The compatibility was the apprenticeship's working product. Theron had not known this when he had written the thesis. He knew it now because Sable had said *because that was what an apprentice meant in their trade,* and Pessel had said it through her.

Halsa Renn satisfied the third because the trigger was hers. The trigger was always the caster's. Joren had not known it existed. He had carried the seated glamour in his signature for years and not looked for it, because *trigger known to caster, not to target* was the defining property of the exploit. Halsa had carried the trigger out of the apprenticeship with her and used it when the cabin geometry let her use it.

The theory held.

The theory held against the cabin. The theory held against the scry. The theory held against the eleven-year apprentice line and the credential placement and the cash booking and the corridor measurement. Every page in the case book that he had filed as a negative or as an awkward shape or as a fact without a frame now sat inside the frame the theory provided.

He picked the pencil up.

He turned to the back-of-book page where the careful sentence about the original placement sat.

He wrote, under it, in plain ink: *Exclusivity to Renn — not yet established. Joren may have trained other apprentices over a thirty-four-year working life. The theory holds against the case as known; the theory does not yet specify that Renn is the only person on the Wyrm capable of having performed it.*

He underlined nothing. He set the pencil down.

Sable read the line over his shoulder. She did not move from her chair to read it — she had simply turned her head — and he registered without comment that she had been close enough to do that.

"All right," she said.

"All right."

"Tomorrow."

"Tomorrow."

She picked her cup up. The cup was nearly empty. She finished it.

"It's a theory," she said.

"It's a theory."

"Not a thing for Davren yet."

"No. It is a thing for Davren when it has *only Renn* on the end of it."

"Then that's tomorrow."

She stood up. The travelling coat went back over her arm. She took her bag with her own hand, did not let him take it, and started for the stair. At the foot of the stair she stopped and turned.

"Theron."

"Yes."

"The thesis." She thought about the rest of the sentence. She let it go without finishing it. "Tomorrow."

She went up. The host came past with the lamp-stick and gave him the small nod that meant *I'll close the front room when you're done.*

He sat at the table for a few more minutes with the case book open on the method page and the cabin sketch beside it and the scry log on top, and the abstract underneath all of them with his thumb in the place where the paragraph about ambient field was, and read the five lines through once more. The fifth was in his hand and in Sable's hand both. He liked that about it. He did not say so to anyone.

*[Investigator — Method page updated. Working theory consistent with cabin geometry and standing-detection record. Status: theory holds against case as known; exclusivity to working suspect not yet established.]*

He read the System line through once. He gave it the small dry nod the System ever wanted from him. He did not particularly enjoy that the System had said *holds.* He approved of the qualification on *exclusivity.* The System had read the back-of-book page with him, which it sometimes did, and was being scrupulous about what the night's work had earned.

He closed the case book on the method page with the five working-theory lines unindented in it.

The kitchen lamp went out behind the door.

He carried the cup to the bar, thanked the host, and went up the stair.

---

# Chapter 31 — The Person-Shaped Hole

Wednesday morning started in a thin grey light from the harbour side of the dining room and the kind of pot the host put down at first bell when the second loaves were still ten minutes off. Theron had been at the corner table since the lamp went on. The case book was open at the method page. The five working-theory lines stood where they had stood last night.

He had not yet written anything new.

Sable came down at the half-bell. She had her hair pulled back the way she pulled it back when she had slept enough to bother, which was a smaller margin than not pulling it back at all. She had the brown coat over her arm and put it on the chair before she sat. The host arrived with the second cup before she had finished sitting.

"Bread in a minute."

"Thank you."

The host went on. Sable drew her own notebook out of the inner pocket of the coat and set it beside Theron's case book without opening either. She drank a quarter of the cup first. Then she looked at him over the rim.

"You haven't written."

"No."

"You're waiting."

"I was waiting for you. Now I'm not."

"Then write."

He did not write yet. He turned the case book around so the method page was right-side up for her, the way he had turned it last night, and put his finger on the second line: *sustained contact during a seating phase*.

"I want to say something about this line." He kept his voice in the register the corner of the room allowed. "Last night I said an apprenticeship of any length would satisfy it many times over. That's true. It satisfies the *contact-time* requirement. It satisfies the *signature* requirement, by inference. It does not satisfy a third thing I did not write down."

She set the cup down. "Go on."

"The seating phase, as a method of laying a glamour, is one thing. The seating phase, as a *trigger architecture* — meaning, set up so that the seating itself prepares a later remote activation — is another thing. They use the same words. The work is different. The first is in any practical illusion textbook. The second is in three papers, one of which is mine, none of which are in the trade."

"So an apprentice would learn the first."

"An apprentice would learn the first as part of any working trade. The second she would only learn from a master who knew it and chose to show it to her."

She did not nod. She turned a page in her own notebook and ran her finger down the column and stopped at a line he could not read upside-down from where he was sitting.

"Joren knew it."

"Joren knew it enough to use it. He would have had to teach it for an apprentice to learn it. There is no other path."

"And he would not have taught it to a passing apprentice."

"He would not have taught it to a competent apprentice. He would have taught it to a particular one."

She read her own line again. She set the finger on it and lifted the cup with the other hand and drank without looking up.

"All right," she said.

The host brought the bread.

---

She did not open her notebook to him.

She read from it instead, two sentences, in the slightly careful voice she used when she was reading her own field notes aloud and she wanted both of them to hear what the field notes said and not what either of them wanted the field notes to say.

"From Pessel, Sunday afternoon, second sitting. *She could do the work after about three years, but Joren kept her seven more.*"

He waited.

"Same conversation, fifteen minutes later. *He sent her south alone for the work that needed her hand more than his.*"

She set the notebook down so the page faced him. The two sentences were in her small clear hand, one above the other, with a thin pencil line between them. Underneath the second one she had written, in a hand that was even smaller, *training arrangement / trust.* That note had a thin pencil line through it. The line was not from this morning. The pencil was paler than the pencil she had this morning. She had struck it out at some point between Sunday and now and had not told him she had struck it out.

He read both sentences twice.

"Three years to do the work," he said. "Seven more to do — "

"Not the work."

"No."

"Something he kept her for that the work didn't require."

"And then she went south, for work he didn't go with her on, that the work-she-could-do-alone needed her hand more than his." He set his finger on the second sentence. "Pessel said *more than* and not *instead of*. Which is the way Pessel said most things — she chose the smaller word. So in some part of it, his hand was still in it. The work was still partly his, but the seating was hers."

"Yes." She drew the notebook back. "That's the line I read three times on the coach yesterday and didn't tell you about because I didn't know what it was for."

"It was for this." He did not say it as triumph. He said it the way he had said *that's the geometry* last night, in the flat register of a working sentence sitting on the page where it belonged. "The apprenticeship was longer than competence required. That's the seating phase she's describing without knowing she's describing it. He kept her near him for seven years past sufficiency because he was teaching her something the work itself didn't ask for."

Sable said nothing for the length of a slow pull from the cup.

She said it the way she came to conclusions, not as arrival but as settling: the sentence already present, set down without ceremony.

"Then she came south alone for work he could no longer do himself."

"Or did not want to do himself. We don't know which."

"No. We don't."

She turned the page back to the column where the Pessel notes lived and closed the notebook on her finger and looked at him with the steady look from last night, the one that was not pity and not satisfaction. He had seen her give it twice now. He was beginning to recognise the muscles in her face that produced it. There were not many.

"Write your list," she said.

---

He turned to a fresh page in the case book.

He did not write *Halsa Renn* across the top. He did not write a date. He wrote, in the unindented hand he used for working theory not yet earned: *Person who could have performed the method.*

He wrote, under it:

*(a) Sustained-contact apprenticeship with the victim, of seating-phase duration. Not less than the laboratory floor. In practice many years.*

He read it back. He moved on.

*(b) Signature compatibility with the victim. Produced by (a) as a matter of the apprenticeship's working product.*

He read that back. He moved on.

*(c) Instruction in the trigger architecture, not only in the lay-down of glamour. Given by the master, not learned from books. Not standard apprenticeship content.*

He held the pencil over the page. He wrote:

*(d) Retention of the trigger after the apprenticeship ended. The trigger does not transfer back unless the master demands it back. The master did not demand it back, or did not know to.*

Sable read the line as he wrote it.

"He didn't know to."

"He didn't know it had been completed. The seating phase doesn't announce itself to the host. The trigger does not exist for him. He carried the glamour and not the awareness of the glamour."

"Then the apprentice walked out with the trigger and the master did not know what she walked out with."

"That's what (d) says."

"That's not common."

"It is not common." He set the pencil down for a moment. "It is the kind of thing that happens when the master has not done the work himself and the apprentice has. When she has worked alone on the technique past where he was working. The competence ran ahead of the relationship at some point. The relationship did not catch up."

She did not say *prodigy*. She did not say *unusual*. She did not say anything. She turned her cup so the handle pointed away from the table edge and sat back fractionally in the chair. He saw the small adjustment and noted it without comment.

He wrote:

*(e) Voluntary, repeated proximity from victim to caster late in the apprenticeship, of the kind that allows the seating to be completed without the victim's awareness. Required from the victim's side. Trust-shaped, not coercion-shaped.*

He read that back twice.

He did not amend it.

He wrote:

*(f) A working life after the apprenticeship that did not register on the standing-detection rigs the trade ordinarily checks. Required so that the method could be carried into the field without prior detection elsewhere. Consistent with the Copper Finch note: "did work that needed quiet rooms."*

He set the pencil down.

Six lines.

He read the page through.

The six lines did not name a person. The six lines said what the person would have had to be. He read each line and felt the case book lie under his hand the way it had been lying since Friday. Heavier on one side, where the column page was. Lighter under the method page. Lighter still under this new page, because the new page was a shape and the shape was already mostly filled in from work he and Sable had done in other rooms in other weeks.

The first line: Pessel, eleven years, Sunday afternoon. The second line: Pessel again, *because that was what an apprentice meant in their trade.* The third line: the thesis abstract and the working-theory four lines from yesterday morning and Pessel's *he sent her south alone for the work that needed her hand more than his* read against them. The fourth line: trigger known to caster, not to target — the defining property of the exploit. The fifth line: Joren-Aldis-eleven-years-of-quiet, which the case book held in three places. The sixth line: Hesh at the Copper Finch, *did work that needed quiet rooms,* and nine years of southbound visits without registration anywhere the trade would look.

He did not write the name at the bottom of the page.

He did not need to.

He sat with the six lines for a slow minute. The host was carrying a pot to the second table. The factor from yesterday had not come in. The harbour was making its Wednesday-morning sound through the window, a hand quieter than Tuesday because the wind was off the land.

Sable was looking at the page. He was looking at her looking at the page.

She did not name the person.

"It's a shape," she said.

"It's a shape."

"And there's one person on the manifest who fits the shape."

"Yes."

"That's not the same as no one else could fit it."

"No. It's not."

"But the shape doesn't argue for anyone else."

"No. It doesn't."

She did not push past that line. She did what she had been doing for three days now, which was to keep the working sentence to its working width and not let it grow. He had a quiet appreciation of her for it that he was not going to say out loud and that he suspected she would not have accepted if he had.

She picked up her cup and finished it.

---

He turned to the back-of-book page.

The page held the careful sentence about the original placement from Saturday and, under it, last night's line in his own hand: *Exclusivity to Renn — not yet established. Joren may have trained other apprentices over a thirty-four-year working life. The theory holds against the case as known; the theory does not yet specify that Renn is the only person on the Wyrm capable of having performed it.*

He read the line. He did not strike it out. He did not want to strike it out — striking it out would have been the cheap pleasure of yesterday's certainty over today's, and he had been at a graduation table four months ago and had a sense of what cheap pleasures cost a year later.

He picked up the pencil.

Under the line, in the smallest shadow-note hand he had — the hand for things he wanted on the page and not in the centre of the page — he wrote: *Requirements list converges to one person on the manifest. Exclusivity not proven. Structural fit is.*

He set the pencil down.

He read the two lines together. Last night's and this morning's, the open question and the morning's narrow answer. They sat on the page the way the lines had been sitting all week: not as conclusions, as the small steady accumulation of what the working pages allowed him to say.

He did not feel triumphant.

He felt the specific quiet that came after a piece of structural work had finished placing itself on a page. He had felt it once before, in his thesis defence, at the moment when the second examiner had asked the follow-up question and he had heard the answer in his own head before he said it and had known, on the way to saying it, that the answer was the answer. The feeling now was the same feeling. The room was different.

He turned to the new page, the requirements list, and read the six lines through one more time.

The shape on the page had a person in it. The person was not on the page. The person was, however, on the passenger manifest at the magistrate's office two streets over, in cabin nine-T, marked Sannholt-bound, cash, no professional reference, a creak near the second-class passage after second watch on the third night and a quieter walk on the third morning. The person had eleven years of Pessel's testimony behind her and a back room at the Copper Finch with three or four southbound visits a year for nine years and a placement made eleven years ago through a network now unwound.

The person fit the shape.

He closed the case book on the requirements page, with his thumb in to hold it, and let it sit closed for a moment under his hand.

Sable was watching the page-edge of his thumb. She had been quiet for the length of his reading.

"The work today," she said. "Was the work today."

"Yes."

"Tomorrow is the exclusivity."

"Tomorrow is the exclusivity."

"How do you do that?"

He had not, until she asked, thought of the answer in plain words. He thought now.

"I show that no other person on the manifest could stand in the shape on the page. I take each of them — the Mirren passenger list, all of them, every cabin — and I lay the requirements list against the manifest entry and I see which lines fail and where. The work is mostly subtraction. Most of them will fail at line (a) before I have to check the others. A few will need two or three lines to clear them out. One will not be cleared out by any line."

"And the one that isn't cleared out — "

"Is not proven. It is what is left after the subtraction. Which is a different thing than proven, and a thing I will name as that thing when I give the page to Davren."

"All right." She stood up. She put the cup on the bread plate and picked up her own notebook and tucked it back into the inner pocket of the coat. She did not take the coat. She left it on the chair. "I have to write to Pessel."

"What about?"

"About the seven years."

She did not elaborate. He did not press. He had a sense, by now, of what Sable wrote letters about and the order she wrote them in, and *the seven years* was a phrase that would sit with her on the way to her room as cleanly as it had sat on the table between them.

"Bring it to me before you post it?"

"Yes."

"Thank you."

She did not say *of course* the way Calden said it and the host said it and the people on the gondola corridors had said it. She said *yes,* and started for the stair, and stopped at the foot of the stair and turned half around.

"Theron."

"Yes."

"The list is good."

"Thank you."

She went up. He did not watch her go up. He turned back to the case book and opened it on the requirements page and read it through a third time, because that was the count he had been keeping for the morning's pages and he had not let himself get to three with it yet.
