*[Investigator — Working page added: Method-of-perpetrator structural requirements. Six lines. Status: structural fit narrowed to one named candidate on manifest; exclusivity check pending against full passenger list.]*

The System line came in over the page in its laconic way. He read it through once. He noticed that the System had said *one named candidate* and not *Halsa Renn* — the System had the same caution he had about putting a name at the bottom of a structural page before the subtraction was done — and he gave the System the small dry nod that was all it ever wanted from him.

He sat at the table for a while longer.

The harbour wind shifted once on the window. The host carried the second pot through. A pair of road-coats came in and were settled at a table by the front-room door. The case book sat under his hand on the requirements page.

He had been told, at the Conservatory, that a working investigator would have a day in the first year when a piece of his own past work would lie on the table beside the case and the two of them would have nothing left to say to each other but yes. The Warden had said it on the ceremony day. He had not believed it that day, because most ceremony-day sentences were ceremony-day sentences and not working sentences.

The Warden had been right.

He closed the case book on the requirements page with his thumb still in the place where he had been reading.

He took his thumb out. He let the page lie.

The host came past with the third pot and set it down without saying anything, and Theron drank what was in the cup and did not pour another, and the morning's work, for the morning, was done.

---

# Chapter 32 — The Name at the Foot of the Page

The Thursday post-coach came in at second bell with the small commotion the host's side door always made of an arriving coach. Theron heard the wheels on the cobble and the driver thanking somebody and the inn door opening twice in close succession. He kept his hand on the case book and did not turn around.

Sable was at the table opposite him. She had been there since first bell. The bread was on a board between them, half a loaf left, and the second pot had come and was three-quarters down.

"That's Sannholt," she said.

"Yes."

She did not get up. The host was at the side door already and would bring the letter through when it came through, and there was no point standing in his way. She turned a page in her notebook to a blank face and set the pencil beside it the way she set pencils beside blank notebook faces when she was about to write something and had not yet decided what.

The host came across the dining room with a single small envelope between his thumb and forefinger.

"From your Sannholt friend," he said. "Driver said second-fastest reply he's carried this season."

"Thank you."

"Pot?"

"Please."

The host went on. Sable broke the seal with her thumbnail and slid the folded sheet out. She set it on the table flat. She read the salutation upside-down to him first.

"Pessel's hand," she said.

"Yes."

She turned it the right way for herself and read it through. He drank coffee. He did not lean forward. Watching her read a letter did not make the letter arrive any sooner.

She read it a second time. Then she set her finger on a line in the middle of the page.

"Here."

She turned the sheet around for him. Pessel's hand was small and sloped slightly to the left and used a particular comma he had got used to looking for as a signature. He read the line her finger was on.

*The seven years past sufficiency were a deliberate teaching. He kept her because there was a thing he meant to show her that he showed nobody else. He called it the late-stage work, when he called it anything. Quiet method, mostly. He did not write it down.*

He read it.

He read it twice.

"Quiet method," he said.

"Mostly."

"That's the trigger architecture without the words for it."

"Pessel doesn't have the words for it. She has the shape and what he called it. The words are yours."

He set the page back down between them. Pessel's letter ran another six lines past that one, and he read those too. She wrote that she had thought about Sable's question on the walk back from the bakery and again on the way to bed and a third time over breakfast, and that she had nothing to add to the line she had set down at the top, except that he had taught no one else the late-stage work, she would have known, and that the apprentice had carried it out of the house with her when she went south, the way a journeyman carried out a master's last gift. *He did not get it back from her*, Pessel wrote, in the smaller hand she used for things she had thought about for longer than she liked to admit. *I never asked whether he wanted to.*

He read the closing sentence.

He pushed the page back so it lay between them on the open notebook face and weighed it down with the back of his pencil.

"That's the master side," he said.

"That's the master side."

"He taught it to her. He taught it to her only. The trigger left the house with her."

"And he didn't take it back."

"And he didn't take it back."

She did not put a question mark on it. He did not answer it. The sentence belonged to the letter and the letter belonged to the table, and the table had been getting longer sentences put on it for a week now.

The host arrived with the new pot. He set it down without ceremony and took the old one with him, and the smell of the new pot was a different smell from the smell of the old pot in the way the second loaves of the day smelled different from the first loaves of the day. Sable refilled her cup and did not refill his because his was still half.

She closed Pessel's letter along its old fold and set it on the corner of the table where they kept letters they were going to want again.

"All right," she said.

---

The Wyrm passenger manifest was a long folded sheet from the magistrate's office that Davren had let them take a fair copy of on Wednesday afternoon. It was three columns: cabin number, name, place of disembarkation. There were forty-one entries. Theron had read it through twice already, on Wednesday evening and again before Sable had come down this morning, and he had not, until now, sat with it beside the requirements page.

He laid it flat between them.

He turned the case book to the requirements page and set it parallel.

"Read me the list," he said.

"All of it?"

"Cabin by cabin. I'll mark the page where each one fails."

She put her finger on the first row. Cabin one, the Mirren factor and his clerk, Halverstow-bound. She read it aloud in the slightly careful voice she used for documents, name and cabin and disembarkation in the order they were on the page, and waited.

He looked at the requirements page.

"(a). Fails."

"How?"

"He's a Mirren factor with eleven years in the same office. No coastal apprenticeship. No illusion training. The work he could do for Joren would have been credential-only and would not satisfy contact-time."

"Mark it."

He drew a small flat line through the cabin-one row on his copy of the manifest. He did not write *fails* against it. He had no use for the word on the page. The line was enough.

"Cabin two."

"The Stallens. Mother and adult son. Gladwater-bound."

"(a). Fails for her. She's a textile factor, sixty-one, no working magical practice. The son was at the Conservatory for one term and dropped to his father's trade. He could pass first-year glamour but not seating, and the time isn't there."

"Mark it."

A small flat line, through both Stallen entries.

"Cabin three."

"Dr. Vellaine."

He paused on Vellaine for a moment, because she was the only entry on the manifest that did not fail at line (a) in a way that did not need explanation. He had cleared her in his head twice already, but the work was to clear her on the page.

"(a) clears. She is a working scholar of illusion with a thirty-one-year career. She knew Joren by professional acquaintance; they shared a panel at the Erethon symposium in the year I was nine. (b), signature compatibility, fails. Vellaine and Joren never trained together. Compatibility is the apprenticeship's working product, not the panel's. They were colleagues. They were not paired. (c) fails for the same reason. She would have known the theory of the trigger architecture; nobody taught it to her in practice because she had no practice with him to teach it in."

"Mark it."

The flat line, on the cabin-three row. He had been a little reluctant to draw it. He drew it anyway, because the work was to draw it.

"Cabin four. The Rennison sisters."

"(a). Fails for both. They are road-coats, third trip together this season, both work in textile dye. There is no illusion in their hands at all. Calden said one of them asked him on the third night what the bell schedule meant by *first watch*, and Calden told the story to your steward friend, who told you. People who do not know what *first watch* means are not running long-form magical apprenticeships."

"Mark it."

She read on.

Cabin five. Cabin six. Cabin seven. He marked the line each time. Some failed at (a) before he had finished hearing the name; some required (b) or (c). The factor in cabin five was an iron merchant from a town on the upper river; he failed at (a). The widow in cabin six had been an apothecary's wife and had filed her own grief letters through the Halverstow office and had no contact-range magical practice on her record; (a). The captain's cousin in cabin seven had been a courier his whole adult life and could not lay glamour at all; (a).

"Cabin eight. Madam Esherel."

He sat with her for two breaths.

Esherel had been his cleanest red herring in the first week of the case. He had narrowed in on her in the Wyrm corridors and had been wrong about her and had let her become a source about Joren in the second week instead. He held her name in his head now without the feeling either time had put on it.

"(a) clears. Esherel did three years as a stage assistant for a touring illusionist in her twenties; she was open about it when I asked her last Sunday. Three years is laboratory-floor contact-time but probably below seating-phase under non-laboratory conditions. (b) fails. Her apprenticeship was not with Joren. Their relationship is mercantile and recent; their signatures are not paired. (c) fails for the same reason. Nobody taught her the trigger architecture during a three-year stage-assistant arrangement two decades before Joren's working method existed in usable form."

"Mark it."

He marked it.

She read the second-class rows. He worked through them. The road-coat couple in twelve-A failed at (a); the courier in twelve-B failed at (a); the second-class purser failed at (a); the academic couple in fourteen-A and fourteen-B failed at (b) and (c) respectively, by separate routes. The third-class rows were faster. Most of them failed at (a) on the first half of the line, and most were marked before Sable had finished reading the row.

She came to cabin nine-T.

She did not read the name. She read the cabin number and stopped. The pencil was in his hand. He did not move it to the page.

"Renn," she said.

"I know."

"(a)."

"Clears. Eleven-year apprenticeship with Joren, confirmed by Pessel on Sunday and reconfirmed by this morning's letter."

"(b)."

"Clears. Apprenticeship's working product. Signature compatibility between two illusionists who worked alongside each other for eleven years."

"(c)."

"Clears. Pessel this morning: *he taught her the late-stage work, the quiet method, only her, did not write it down*. The trigger architecture is the quiet method. He taught it deliberately and to her alone."

"(d)."

"Clears. Trigger retention. The trigger left the house with her, Pessel's letter says, and he did not take it back."

"(e)."

"Clears. Late-stage proximity from victim to caster. The seating phase completed during the back half of the apprenticeship. We have nine years of southbound visits from Hesh, and the Pessel apprentice-line, and Joren personally vouching her placement eleven years ago through Vantrel."

"(f)."

"Clears. Working life undetected on standing-detection rigs. Nine years of quiet rooms at the Copper Finch, Sannholt-bound trips, no professional reference on the Wyrm manifest, cash booking, the Mirren transcript's pattern of *picked up the work, never wrote it down*."

He stopped.

The page in front of him was a manifest with forty flat lines drawn through it and one row unmarked.

Sable was looking at the page. He was looking at the page.

She set the pencil she had been holding down beside the line of the unmarked row and did not draw any line through it.

"That's the subtraction," she said.

"That's the subtraction."

"It doesn't prove she did it."

"No. It proves no one else on the Wyrm could have."

"Different thing."

"Different thing. And the thing Davren needs."

He read the manifest through one more time, top to bottom, and his eye stopped at the unmarked row the way an eye stopped at a window where a lamp was burning when every other window on the street was dark. The lamp did not name the person inside the room. The lamp said the room was the one that was lit.

---

He turned the case book.

He opened the requirements page to its second face. He had left it blank yesterday morning at the foot of the six working lines, on the chance that he would need the space and on the second chance that he would not need it and the blank would mean something different. He had not, on Wednesday, known which it would be.

He knew now.

He set the case book square in front of him. The six lines stood at the top of the page in the unindented hand he used for working theory not yet earned. The page below them was as he had left it, no marks, no stray pencil-shadow at the margin. The blank ran the length of the page from the bottom of line (f) to the page's foot.

He picked up the pencil.

He held it for the length of one slow breath.

He had been thinking about this moment, in the back of his head, since Wednesday afternoon, since the line on the back-of-book page had read *converges to one person on the manifest* and he had not let himself write more than that. He had not, at the time, known what the moment would feel like. He had thought, in the way a person thought about a working sentence he had not yet written, that it would feel like the second examiner's follow-up question in his thesis defence: the moment of hearing the answer in his own head before he said it.

It did not feel like that.

It felt the way the lamp on the unmarked row had looked: simple, particular, no larger than itself.

He set the pencil to the page.

He wrote, in the unindented hand he used for working theory now earned, two words at the foot of the page.

*Halsa Renn.*

He read what he had written.

He did not underline it. He did not date it. He did not put any sentence under it explaining what the name meant or what it did not yet mean. The two words sat on the page below six requirement lines, and the requirement lines, read down to the name, made the name the only English the page was capable of producing. He had built the page so that this would be true. The pencil had done what the page had asked.

He set the pencil down beside the case book.

He had a strange small impulse to close the book. He did not close it. He let the page lie open between them.

Sable had not moved while he was writing. She had not looked away from the page. He registered the not-looking-away the way he registered most of what she did, in a part of his head that did not say it.

She said it.

"You wrote it," she said.

"I wrote it."

"It's true."

"It's true. Which is a different thing from proved, and the page knows the difference."

"The page knows the difference."

"And so do we."

She did not say anything for the length of a breath. She did not pick up her own pencil. She did not turn her notebook. She read the two words at the foot of the page with the steady look that was not pity and not satisfaction, and he had seen her give it now four times since Sunday, and he was beginning to suspect it was the look she gave a piece of work she was willing to stand beside without needing it to be hers.

"All right," she said.

"All right."

> *[Investigator — Working page closed. Method-of-perpetrator structural requirements: six lines, exclusivity established by exhaustive subtraction against the Wyrm passenger manifest. Named candidate logged: Renn, H. Status: structural and exclusive fit. Working theory now of a quality to be carried to a magistrate.]*

He read the System line through once.

It came in with the same dryness it had come in with on Wednesday, after *one named candidate, exclusivity check pending*. This time the qualification had moved. *Structural and exclusive fit.* He registered, without taking pleasure in it, that the System had used the word *carried*, which was the word he had been about to use himself and had not yet said out loud. The System had read the page with him again. He gave it the small dry nod that was the only answer it had ever wanted.

He looked at the name at the foot of the page.

He let himself look at it for a slow count he did not count.

He had not, on a Thursday morning in November, in a dining room two streets from a magistrate's office, in a town he had not heard of three weeks ago, expected to be the person who put that name on that page. He had expected, three weeks ago, to be on holiday. He thought briefly of the graduation pin under his coat, and of the Warden's sentence about a working investigator's first year, and of the small careful difference between *the theory holds* and *the theory holds against everyone else on the manifest*. One of those sentences had been Tuesday's. The other was today's.

He did not write any of that on the page.

The page already had what the page needed.

---

He closed the case book on the requirements page, with his thumb in for a moment to hold it, and then without his thumb in, so that the case book lay shut on the table between them with the name at the foot of a page nobody else could see.

Sable was drinking the last of her cup. The host was at the front-room door, putting the morning's chairs back the way a host put chairs back at the change of bell.

"Tomorrow is Davren," she said.

"Tomorrow is Davren."

"You'll bring him the case book."

"I'll bring him the case book. I'll bring him the requirements page and the manifest and the abstract and the scry log and the Mirren transcript and Pessel's two letters. He'll read the page that says her name. He'll read the lines above it. He'll ask what I'd like him to do with it."

"And what would you like him to do with it?"

He thought.

He had not, until she asked, thought of the answer in the form of a sentence. He had thought of it in the form of a shape: a room, two doors, the magistrate at one end of a table and Halsa Renn at the other, and Theron and Sable at neither end. He had not let himself dwell on the shape because the shape was Friday's, and Friday was the day after the day he was sitting in.

He thought now in plain words.

"I'd like him to bring her in," he said. "Not by force. By appointment. He sends a clerk to her boarding-house with a magistrate's request. She comes to his office at a time of his arranging. The case book is on his table. I am at his shoulder. You are at mine. He puts the page to her and asks her to read it. She reads it. What she does after that is her piece of the morning and not mine."

"You'll let her read it."

"I'll let her read it. The page is the page. She'll know what it says before she's read three lines of it. The reading is the courtesy."

"All right."

"All right."

Sable set the cup down. She did not stand up. She stayed at the table opposite him and looked at the closed case book, and at the manifest with its forty flat lines and its one unmarked row, and at Pessel's letter at the corner of the table where they kept letters they were going to want again.

"It's a quiet morning," she said.

"It is."

"Quieter than I'd have thought."

"I'd have thought louder."

"And it isn't."

"And it isn't."

He let himself sit with the quiet for the length of one slow pull from the cup. The host went past with the lamp-stick to the front-room sconces, which he lit at the change of bell on weekdays in November when the harbour wind ran off the land. The brass of the sconces took the small flame and held it without commentary, which was about right for the room and for the morning and for the table between Theron and Sable and the closed case book on it.

He did not open the case book again.

He did not need to. The page was the page. The name was at the foot of it. The work for tomorrow was known.

He drank what was left of his cup, and Sable drank what was left of hers, and the second pot sat warm between them and was not, this morning, asked to do any more than that.

---

# Chapter 33 — Passengers Assembled

Davren's office was on the second floor above the wool warehouse, with a stairwell that smelled of lanolin in the cold months and the same smell fainter in the warm ones. Theron came up it at half past first bell on Friday with the case book in a leather strap under his arm and the requirements page set as the third sheet down, where Davren would meet it after the manifest and the abstract. Sable was a flight below him, climbing at her own pace. She had said at the foot of the stair that she would come up and stand at the wall and not say anything, and that the case was his to put on the table.

He had said: all right.

The clerk at the top was a young man in a grey-edged coat who had been writing a date on a docket and stopped when he saw the strap.

"Mr. Mavik."

"He's expecting me."

"He is. Go through."

Theron went through. Davren was at the long table by the side window, not the desk, which was the table he used when he expected to be reading rather than receiving. He had a pot of his own and two cups already out. He did not stand. He set down what he had been reading and looked at the strap.

"Sit."

Theron sat. Sable came in behind him without speaking and went to the chair against the wall and sat in it with her notebook closed on her knee. Davren marked her with a small look that was civility and not invitation, and then turned the look back to the table.

"Show me."

Theron undid the strap. He laid the case book down right-side up for Davren. He turned the pages he had decided last night to turn — the abstract, the scry log, the Mirren transcript, the two Pessel letters laid side by side in the order they had arrived, the manifest with its forty flat lines, and last the requirements page with the six lines at the top and the name at the foot of it.

Davren read the abstract first. He read it once. He drank coffee. He read it a second time and turned the page.

He read the scry log without comment, which Theron took as the answer he had been waiting for on the scry log. He read the Mirren transcript with one finger moving down the column. He read both Pessel letters and laid them back down in the order Theron had set them. He read the manifest at the speed a magistrate read a manifest, which was twice as fast as a layman read a manifest and four times as slow as a magistrate's clerk did, and he stopped twice — once at cabin three, where he made a small sound in the back of his throat that Theron did not interpret, and once at the foot of the third-class block, where the unmarked row stood plain.

He turned the requirements page.

He read the six lines.

He read the name at the foot of the page.

He sat with the page for the length of a slow breath. Then he turned it ninety degrees so the foot of the page was toward him and set his finger flat below the two words and did not, for the moment, lift it again.

"Walk me through (c)," he said.

Theron walked him through (c). He kept it short. He used the words Pessel had used in the letter where Pessel had had them, and his own where Pessel had not. Davren listened without interrupting. He did not move his finger off the page.

"And (d)."

Theron walked him through (d). The trigger left the house with her. The master did not take it back. The sentence belonged to Pessel; Davren let the borrowed weight sit.

"And (f)."

He walked him through (f). The nine years at the Copper Finch. The cash. The pattern of *picked up the work, never wrote it down*. Davren took his finger off the page at the end of (f) and put it back on, half an inch to the left, on the bare strip of paper between the last requirement line and the name.

"It's a working theory," he said.

"It is."

"It's a working theory of a quality I haven't been handed in nine years. The last one was the Veldris matter, which you would not have heard of, and the one before that was before I was magistrate here."

"I know what the page is."

"You know what the page is not."

"I know what the page is not. It is not an arrest warrant. It would not be sufficient for one. Structural exclusivity is not opportunity proven and not act witnessed. It is the work that has to be done before the work that ends in a warrant. I am not asking for a warrant."

Davren looked at him. He set the page down and did not speak immediately. Theron let the silence sit and did not fill it with the second sentence he had prepared.

"What are you asking for."

"A magistral travel-order. The morning service to Sannholt lifts tomorrow at first bell. She is on it. She booked a return three weeks ago at the Halverstow office, paid in cash, single cabin. The order would attach me to the flight as your deputy for the route, with the authority to take a statement from her *en route*, and a clerk of the Sannholt office to receive her at landing for further questioning under their writ. She is not under arrest. She is not free to disembark before Sannholt. She is told she is under magistral accompaniment for the purpose of a statement, and not why."

"Not why."

"Not why. The name at the foot of the page does not travel with the order. The order names Joren Aldis as the open case and Halsa Renn as a person whose statement the investigation requires. The page stays here, in your case-room, until she has read it."

"Until she has read it."

"Until you put it to her at Sannholt and she reads it. That is the courtesy I want. The page is the page. It was built so that the reading of it is its own kind of answering."

Davren did not say anything for a moment. He drank coffee. He looked at the manifest, and at the requirements page, and then at the wall above the side window where the magistral seal hung framed in a small dark frame with its candle-wax button intact.

"You'll bring the case book on the flight."

"I'll bring it. Not the requirements page. The abstract, the scry log, the transcripts, the manifest. I want the statement to be a statement, not a reading."

"And if she asks why the magistral order."

"I'll say what the order says. I'll say no more. I'll be on the flight as your deputy, not as a man on his own work, and the cabin will know it."

"Sable goes with you."

It was not a question. Sable had not spoken from the wall and did not speak now. Davren had looked at her once on the way in and was looking at Theron and not at her, and the sentence belonged to the table.

"She goes with me."

"Then she's named on the order. I am not putting a private party on a magistral flight in a deputy's cabin without naming her."

"Name her."

Davren turned and called through the half-open door for the clerk. The clerk came in with the docket-book and a blank order form. Davren dictated the order in the short clean sentences of a man who had dictated three thousand of them and had stopped putting feeling into the verbs a decade ago. *In the matter of the open investigation into the death of Joren Aldis*, he said. *Magistral accompaniment for the southbound morning service of the seventeenth*. *Deputy: Mavik, Theron, Investigator, licensed by the conservatory of the second city, sealed in this office on date stated. Attached: Verrin, Sable, Witness, in her own capacity, by the deputy's nomination.* He named Halsa under her booking-name and her cabin number and the route. He did not name the requirements page or the working theory or the name at the foot of either.

The clerk set the order on the table for the seal. Davren reached into his coat pocket for his ring and put the wax to the candle and set the ring to the wax and held it the way a man held a seal-ring to wax — not pressing, just resting, the wax doing its own work under the heat. He lifted the ring and set the order down between Theron and the case book.

"Two copies," he said.

"Two copies."

"The original goes with you. The duplicate sits here." He tapped the docket. "If she asks at the dock to see the writ, you let her see the original and she lets you keep it. If she does not ask, you do not produce it until she does. The flight crew has its own copy from the magistral office through the harbour clerk; she does not see that one. The clerk of the Sannholt office will be on the morning post-coach this afternoon and will be at the landing dock when you arrive."

"Understood."

"She'll be told by your clerk-of-mine this evening. He'll go to her boarding-house at sixth bell, when she'll have come back from the Copper Finch if her week is the week we think it is. He'll hand her a sealed note from this office. The note says what the order says. It does not say anything else."

"Yes."

"You will not be at the door when he goes. You will not be at the dock when she boards. You will be on the flight in your cabin when she is shown to hers, and Sable will be where Sable chooses to be, and the cabin numbers will be such that you do not pass her in the corridor before second bell. I will not have it look like an ambush, on the dock or in the air. The order is not an ambush. The order is a courtesy with a magistrate's hand on the door."

"Yes."

Davren took the original off the table and folded it once and slid it into the leather strap above the case book without being asked. He capped his pen. He set his hand on the requirements page one more time and did not turn it.

"This is good work," he said.

He did not look at Theron when he said it. He looked at the page. Theron did not say anything. There was no English available for the sentence that Davren had just put down, and he had learned this week that the absence of English was often the right answer to a sentence of that shape.

Sable had stood up at some point in the last sentence. He had not heard the chair move. She was at the table now, beside Theron's shoulder, not above the page but near it.

"Tomorrow at first bell," she said.

"First bell," said Davren.

He folded the requirements page back into the case book.

He did not close the case book. That was Theron's to do.

Theron closed it.

---

The southbound morning service lifted from the Halverstow harbour-dock at first bell on the seventeenth of November, in the kind of grey light that had not yet decided whether it would be rain or only the threat of it. The dock was a quarter the size of the city dock at the southern terminus and had no rope to drop. The handler was a long-armed older woman whose name badge read *Roe*, and she had two drakes in the harness rather than three, and the gondola was a third the length of the Wyrm and lacquered the colour of wet slate. The hum it made through the boot soles was the same hum the Wyrm had made. The bone was the same bone.

Theron arrived at the dock with the leather strap under his arm and the order in the inner pocket of his coat and the case book tied tight inside the strap. Sable was already there. She had a cup of coffee in her gloved hand that she had bought from the dock-side stall and was finishing without enthusiasm. She held the spare cup out as he came up.

"It's not as good as the host's."

"I didn't think it would be."

"It's warm. That's all it was."

He took the cup. It was warm. That was all it was. He drank half of it standing beside her and watched the dock.

Halsa was not at the dock. He had not expected her to be at the dock, and Davren had instructed against it, and the order had been built so that she would board through the second gangway at the far end, where the dock-clerk in the grey-edged coat was standing with the manifest copy from the office and a small leather case at his feet. Theron watched the second gangway without watching it. He saw the clerk shake hands with the gondola purser and hand him a folded sheet and accept the seal-mark in return. He saw a small grey bag set down beside the clerk's case. He did not see the woman who had set it there. He looked back at his own gangway and finished the cup.

"All right," Sable said.

They boarded. Their cabins were on the same passage, near the forward end. Theron's was number two, Sable's was four. Roe was at the boarding door and checked their names off without flourish and gave them keys with their berths stamped in red wax. Theron put his key in his pocket and held the strap with both hands going up the stair.

In the corridor at the head of the stair he stopped, because his cabin door was three steps in front of him and because the case book was heavier in the strap than it had been at the foot of the stair, and because he had not yet, that morning, thought of the corridor as a corridor. He had thought of it as a manifest. The manifest had said *cabin six, Renn*. He looked at the door of cabin six. It was painted the same wet-slate green as the rest of the doors. There was no light at the threshold.

Sable was at his elbow.

"Theron."

"I'm not going down there."

"I know. I'm telling you that I know."

He let his shoulder go off the strap by half an inch. He went into cabin two. Sable went into cabin four. The doors closed at almost the same beat, and the lift began under his feet before he had put the strap down on the writing desk.

He felt it the way he had felt it on the Wyrm — the deep hum rising, the floor's quality changing, the slow courteous separation from the dock — and he sat on the padded bench beneath the porthole and watched Halverstow tilt and drop below the hull line. The river was a thin grey line. The wool warehouses were small dark blocks at the harbour edge. The two drakes pulled out into the slipstream in a tighter formation than the Wyrm's three; Roe had said one of them was new to the route and would settle by the second hour, in the same tone of voice Wisp had used about Mottled, which Theron had stopped being surprised by some time ago.

> *[Investigator — Manifest of the southbound morning service of the seventeenth: deputy-of-record under magistral accompaniment. Subject named on order: Renn, H., cabin six. Order in force from first bell to second bell on landing. Investigator is travelling under writ.]*

He read it once.

It came in dryer than usual, which he had not, last night, predicted. The System was using *travelling under writ* like a man putting a coat on for the weather it was — the noun first, the verb afterward, no commentary. He gave it the small dry nod.

The drakes had found their thermal. The gondola was steady. He could hear, faintly through the wall, Sable moving in the next cabin — a bag set down, a coat hung up, the small specific noises of a person putting herself into a small specific room. Two doors past hers, the corridor turned, and at the head of the turning the door of cabin six was closed and would stay closed until he walked down to it.

He did not walk to it yet.

He set the leather strap on the writing desk. He set the case book on top of it. He sat with his hands flat on the case book for the length of a slow breath he did not count.

The gondola had found its air and was running south, and he had nothing left to do except sit with the case book until the hour came to stand up with it.

---

# Chapter 34 — The Door of Cabin Six

The cabin was small and well-considered. Two portholes, a writing desk with a worn drawer-pull, a chair, a padded bench under the porthole. The lamp bracket had been angled by someone who had thought about reading in bed. The wardrobe was honest about itself. The hum through the deck was the Wyrm's hum at a lower note, because there were two drakes pulling and not three.

Theron sat on the bench with the case book on the desk and did not look at it.

He thought briefly about the page that was not in the book.

The requirements page was in Davren's case-room. He had agreed to that in the office on Friday and the agreement had been the right shape then and was the right shape now. The page was the page; the page named her at its foot; the page would be read to her, in time, by the magistrate at his table in Sannholt, in the room a magistrate built for that work. It was not for a cabin on a gondola.

What he had on the desk was the abstract, the scry log, the transcripts, the manifest with its forty flat lines and its one unmarked row. The public-facing record. What he had on the desk was what he had said he would carry.

He stood up.

He put on the coat he had hung on the hook by the door. He picked the leather strap off the desk and slid the case book into it and tied the strap, not because the strap needed tying — the cabin was three doors from the cabin he was going to — but because the act of tying it was the act of saying *now*, and he had used the same small ritual every morning of the week.

He left the cabin.

The corridor was empty. The wet-slate green of the doors matched the lacquer of the hull, which was a choice somebody had made about how this gondola was supposed to feel, and the choice was a different choice than the Wyrm's bone-coloured doors had been, and he could not, this morning, say which choice he liked better. He passed cabin three. He passed cabin four. Sable's door opened as he came up to it, which meant she had been at the door waiting, which meant she had set herself to come out when she heard him.

"Ready," she said.

"Ready."

She came out and closed the door behind her without locking it, because the corridor was the corridor of a gondola under a magistral writ and the only people on it were the people who had been named on the order. They walked together. He carried the strap. She carried her notebook closed in her left hand, the pencil through the spine the way she carried it when she had decided she would not be writing.

They came to cabin six.

He knocked.

He knocked once and waited the count he had decided in his cabin he would wait, which was three, and then he knocked again, because the second knock was a courtesy the first had not been. He heard, beyond the door, a small specific movement — a chair, set carefully back from a table — and then the door opened.

Halsa Renn was in her travelling coat with the buttons done up. Her hair was pinned. She was holding the door at the latch with her right hand and she had her left hand in the pocket of her coat. She looked at Theron for the length of a breath, and at Sable for the length of another, and at the strap under Theron's arm for the length of a third. She did not speak first.

"Miss Renn," he said.

"Mr. Mavik."

She had the small steady voice of someone who had been awake since before the morning bell. The voice did not waver and did not perform not-wavering, which were different things.

"I'm here under the magistral order you were given last night."

"I know what you're here under."

"I'd like to lay out what's in the case book to you, if you'll sit."

She looked at him.

She looked at Sable.

She stepped back from the door.

"Come in," she said.

---

Her cabin was the cabin of someone who had unpacked the way a person unpacked who meant to be in a room for one night. The bag was on the floor by the wardrobe with the buckle done up. The coat she was not wearing was on the hook. There was no book on the writing desk. There was a cup on the desk and a small folded sheet beside the cup, sealed, the seal not broken. The wax was Davren's. The sheet was the note from sixth bell yesterday.

She had not opened it.

Theron registered the un-opened note in the part of his head that registered such things and did not, for the moment, do anything with the registration. He set the leather strap on the desk beside the cup. He pulled the chair out from the desk and turned it ninety degrees so that it faced the porthole bench and not the desk. He sat in the chair. He laid the case book on the desk and put one hand on it without opening it.

"Sable will sit by the wall, if that's all right with you."

"That's all right."

Sable went to the wall and took the small chair that was there for taking off boots and sat in it with the notebook on her knee. The notebook stayed closed. Halsa watched her sit. Then Halsa went to the porthole bench and sat in the centre of it with her hands folded in her lap, the left hand still in the pocket and the right one over the wrist of the left, the way a person sat who meant to listen.

He opened the case book.

He did not open it to the abstract first. He had decided in his cabin to open it to the manifest, because the manifest was the public-facing shape of what he had come to lay out, and the abstract could come after. The abstract was a paragraph. The manifest was forty-one rows.

He turned the book around so that the manifest faced her.

"This is the Wyrm manifest," he said. "It's a copy from the magistrate's office at Halverstow. Three columns: cabin, name, place of disembarkation. Forty-one entries. You'll find your row at the head of the third-class block."

She looked at it.

She did not lean forward. She did not need to lean forward; the desk was small and she could read it from the bench. She read it. She found her row. She found the four other rows in the third-class block. She read up through second class and through first class. She read the cabin-one row that was the Mirren factor she had heard about by name in the dining room on the Wyrm and had not, then, given a face to. She read the cabin-eight row that was Esherel. She read the cabin-three row that was Vellaine.

"I'm reading it," she said.

"Take what you need."

She read it.

When she had read it she looked up at him without lifting her chin.

"All right," she said.

"In the case book," he said, "behind the manifest, there's an abstract of the working theory of the method on Joren's killing. I'll walk you through what's in it. I'll stop when you'd like me to stop. If you'd rather read it for yourself I'll turn the page and let you read."

"You walk me through it."

"All right."

He did not turn to the abstract. He had it by heart. He laid his hand flat on the closed face of the case book the way he had laid it flat on the requirements page at Davren's table the morning before, and he began, in the unraised voice he had used at Davren's table, with the booking.

"The booking," he said. "You came onto the Wyrm passenger list through the Kolach channel. The Kolach channel is the booking route the magistrate's office reads as the channel that places travellers who are not placing themselves through the open exchanges. The booking was personally referred eleven years ago by Joren Aldis."

"Yes."

"You worked at the Copper Finch in Sannholt for nine years. Quiet rooms. Cash. No professional reference logged on the Wyrm manifest. Picked up the work; didn't write it down."

"Yes."

"Joren Aldis took you as his apprentice on a coastal placement when you were eighteen. The placement was a Vantrel routing through the Pessel apprentice-line. You worked with him for eleven years."

"Eleven years."

"Pessel's letter dated Tuesday confirms the eleven-year apprenticeship and the working hours. Pessel's letter dated this Thursday confirms a further line about the master side of the work. I have the letters in the book. I'll read you the line, if you'd like."

"Read it."

He turned to the letter. He had set Pessel's two letters in the book in the order they had arrived, with a strip of tied ribbon between them, which was his own small ritual of order and was not in any examination's instruction sheet. He found Thursday's letter. He found the line.

"*The seven years past sufficiency were a deliberate teaching. He kept her because there was a thing he meant to show her that he showed nobody else. He called it the late-stage work, when he called it anything. Quiet method, mostly. He did not write it down.*"

He set the letter back down.

He did not look up. He turned to the next page.

"The method on the cabin," he said. "A sustained scryproof glamour, held through the seating phase. The seating phase is not in any standard text on illusion. It is the point in an apprenticeship where the apprentice's signature becomes durable against the master's. After the seating phase the working between two illusionists is signature-compatible in a way that nobody else is. The signature compatibility is the apprenticeship's working product, not its credential."

"That's the credential nobody writes down," she said.

"Yes."

"Go on."

"The trigger architecture for a sustained scryproof glamour, held to a seating-compatible signature, is a piece of late-stage practical theory that, as far as my year and the year behind me at the Conservatory knew, had been written down in one paper, written down badly, and dismissed at examination as exam fodder. It has no commercial application. It has no defensive application. It has, on the face of it, no application at all. It does have the application of doing exactly what was done in cabin twelve on the Wyrm."

She nodded once.

"In a closed cabin," he said. "Through a sealed bolt and a sealed porthole and a sealed deck hatch. Without the caster needing to be near the target at the moment of activation. With the trigger known to the caster alone."

"Without leaving a scry log."

"Without leaving a scry log."

He turned the page.

"The manifest," he said. "I went through it on Thursday morning with Sable. Cabin by cabin. The requirements on the method are six structural requirements; I wrote them out on a page that is not in this book. The page is in Davren's case-room at Halverstow. The page is going to be read to you in Sannholt, on a table that is not this one. I am not going to read it to you here."

"All right."

"The manifest has forty-one entries. Forty fail at one or another of the six lines on the page that is not in this book. Some fail on the first line. Some fail on the second and the fourth. Three or four pass the first line and fail on the second. One row passes all six lines."

"That row is mine."

"That row is yours."

She did not say *yes*. She did not need to. She had said the sentence first, which was a courtesy of a particular kind, and he heard the courtesy without making anything of it.

He closed the case book.

He did not push it across the desk. He left it where it was, square between them on the small writing desk of cabin six on the southbound morning service of the seventeenth of November, with the manifest closed inside it and the abstract closed inside it and Pessel's two letters tied in their ribbon inside it. He sat with his hands flat on the closed face of the book.

"That's what's in the book," he said.

"That's what's in the book."

"That's what I came in to lay out."

"You've laid it out."

He waited.

He had decided in his cabin that the waiting would not be a tactic. He had decided that he would put the work down on the desk and would not, after he had put it down, ask her a question. He had decided, in the slow count he had done with his hands flat on the case book at sixth bell yesterday, that the work would speak its own sentence and that he would not stand over the work in a way that asked anything of her except what the work itself asked.

He waited.

Halsa was looking at the closed case book.

She had not, in the time he had been speaking, taken her left hand out of the pocket of her coat. She took it out now. She set it on her right wrist where the right hand had been before. She looked at the case book a moment longer. Then she looked up at him.

"He taught me the seating phase in the back room of a working-house in Sannholt that doesn't stand any more," she said. "It was a Tuesday. He had not slept. He had come back from a job he did not name, and he had walked me through the architecture twice on the chalk-wall and a third time on a folded scry-sheet, and at the end of it he had said: *don't write this down*. I asked him why. He said because the only person it was useful against was him."

Theron did not speak.

"I have been alive," she said, "with that sentence in my head, for seven years."

She looked at the case book.

"You have it right," she said. "What's in the book. You have it right."

"I'm not asking you to say it."

"I know you're not asking me to say it."

"I'm laying it out so that you've seen it laid out."

"I've seen it."

She did not look away from the case book.

"I'm not going to dispute the page," she said. "I'm not going to dispute the manifest. I'm not going to dispute the Pessel letter. I'm not going to dispute the Kolach channel. I've had a night to think about what would be a dispute and what would not, and a dispute is not what I have."

"All right."

"All right."

She let the right hand come off the wrist. She set both hands flat on her knees, palms down, the way a person set hands flat on knees who had decided to sit through what was coming and not move.

He registered that posture.

He did not, in the registering, say anything.

> *[Investigator — The page has been laid out. The room has heard it. No contestation logged. Statement under writ: pending.]*

He read the System line through once. He gave it the small dry nod he had given the System every morning of the week. The System had read the room with him. It had not, this morning, used a word he was not willing to use himself.

Sable, at the wall, had not moved.

Halsa looked up from the case book and looked at her for the second time since the door had opened.

"Miss Verrin," she said.

"Miss Renn."

"You're named on the order."

"I am."

"In your own capacity."

"In my own capacity."

"All right."

She looked back at Theron.

"I have something I'd like to ask you," she said. "Before we go on with whatever's after this."

"Ask."

She did not ask.

She held the question for a moment, the way a person held a question that she had been carrying since a Friday evening when a magistrate's clerk had handed her a sealed note at her boarding-house door, and the small folded sheet was still on the desk beside the cup with Davren's wax intact on it, and her left hand had been in her coat pocket the whole of the laying-out, and the case book on the desk was closed.

"Not yet," she said.

"All right."

"I'll ask it when I'm ready to."

"All right."

He sat back in the chair. He let his hands come off the case book and rest on his knees the way her hands had come to rest on hers. The cabin was small and the hum through the deck was the hum of two drakes finding their working thermal, and there was a great deal of the morning left in the air ahead of them and the gondola was a third the size of the Wyrm and the corridor outside the door was empty.

The case book sat closed on the desk between them.

Halsa Renn was not denying it.

She had not denied it.

She had said she would not dispute the page, and she had not disputed the page, and she had said she would not dispute the manifest, and she had not disputed the manifest, and she had said she had a question to ask and would ask it when she was ready, and he was sitting on the other side of the desk and waiting for her to be ready.

He did not ask her to be ready.

He let the cabin be quiet for a slow count he did not count. Sable at the wall was quiet. Halsa on the bench was quiet. The hum through the deck was the hum. The cup on the desk was a cup. The sealed sheet beside the cup was sealed. The case book was closed.

He had laid it out.

The page had been laid out.

The room had heard it.

The room was now Halsa's room to make what shape she would make of it, and he was here to wait until she made it, and he did not, this morning, have anywhere else to be.

---

# Chapter 35 — The Question She Came to Ask

Halsa took a slow breath. Not for the count of it — for the shape.

"All right," she said. "I'll ask it now."

"Ask it."

She did not look at the case book. She looked at the wall above Theron's left shoulder, the way people looked at a wall when they were not borrowing strength from a face. Her hands stayed flat on her knees. The cup on the desk was a cup. The sealed sheet beside it was still sealed.

"On the Wyrm," she said. "After it landed. Or in the days after, in Halverstow, with the magistrate. Or with the captain. Or between the two of you, in a room with a door shut on it. Did anyone use his real name."

Theron waited a count.

"His real name," he said.

"Joren Aldis." She said it without effort. "Not the lord's name. Not the man he was wearing. The name he had when he wasn't working. Did anyone use it. To his face if there had still been a face to use it to, or in a room about him."

He did not answer at once. He set his hands on his knees the way her hands were set on hers. He looked at Sable at the wall.

Sable looked at him.

"Yes," he said. "Someone did."

"Who."

"Sable."

Halsa moved her eyes from the wall to Sable, slowly, the way a person moved their eyes who had been holding a particular question for a long time and did not want to spend it badly.

"Miss Verrin," she said.

"Yes."

"You used it."

"I used it."

"When."

Sable did not look at her notebook. Her notebook was closed on her knee where it had been the whole laying-out, the pencil through the spine, and she did not move to open it. She set it on the small chair beside her instead, the way a person set a thing down who meant her hands free.

"The morning the Wyrm landed at Halverstow," she said. "The fifth of October. In the captain's day-cabin. Theron came in from the aft rail. The harbor was quiet. There was a coal barge working the far slip — I remember the barge because it was the thing the light kept catching on while we were talking."

"Go on."

"I had taken his surname out of the Berric Toll correspondence the night before. Toll uses the full name to sign off the contract particulars. I had read it at the alcove on the third night without saying it; I had wanted Theron to find it himself. He hadn't, by the morning we landed, and I had decided I was not going to wait for him to."

"You said the name first."

"I said it first. He sat down. He took the small cloth bag out of his coat pocket — the bag with the things from the cabin pocket — and put it on the desk between us, and I said the name again, and he said it, and after that he used it."

"And then."

"And then I asked if I could walk through what I had seen." She had not raised her voice. She had not lowered it, either. "He said yes. I walked through the three kindnesses first. The steward thanked by name at boarding. The ginger sweet to the airsick child at the second dinner. The elderly passenger at the gangway step. Then I walked through the accent — the harbor vowel in *harbor* at the first dinner. Then the working-mage callus that Theron had registered at boarding and had not asked himself why a lord would have. Then the sentence about the niece, which he had said at the third dinner."

Halsa's hands did not move.

"The niece," she said.

"He had said one sentence about her at a meal. About a small object she had carved for him at six. He had said she would be unimpressed he hadn't lost it yet."

"That was the wooden fish."

"That was the wooden fish."

"You knew it was the niece's."

"Not on the day," Sable said. "I knew the tone. The tone wasn't a lord's tone for any of the things a lord would have a tone like that for. The day I named the fish was after Pessel."

Halsa nodded once. She did not say *Pessel*; she had heard it through the laying-out, and she was not going to ask anything she did not need to ask now.

"Anything else."

"Two things," Sable said. "After the five details, the System updated the quest. It had been *Solve the Death Aboard the Skybound Wyrm.* It became *Solve the Death of Joren Aldis.* Theron looked at the line. He did not say anything about it. He turned the page on his slate and we kept going."

"That was the first time the System used his name."

"Yes."

"To him."

"To both of us. He read it; I saw him read it."

Halsa took that. She did not nod for it. She gave it the small still space a person gave a piece of information that mattered and was not for showing it mattered.

"You said two things."

"The second thing was a promise." Sable's left hand went, without her looking down, to the inside of her coat where the inside pocket was. She did not take anything out. She rested two fingers against the cloth there and left them. "Two days after the landing, at the foot of the Halverstow gangway, I told Theron that someone had to tell Joren's family properly. Not a magistrate's letter. A person. He heard it. He did not argue."

"You said his family."

"His family."

"You did not say *the deceased's family*. You did not say *the man's family*."

"I did not."

"Are you still going."

"I am still going."

Halsa took that, too. She moved her right hand, the first move of her hands since they had gone flat on her knees, and she put it on the back of her left hand on her left knee. She did not look at Theron. She looked at Sable.

"Where to."

"His sister and his niece," Sable said. "Pessel has the box. I have a contact in Sannholt, through a separate post, with the magistrate's permission. I'm going down after Halverstow as the case allows. I have a thing of his to give back."

She did not name the fish. She did not need to.

Halsa looked at her two fingers against the inside of her coat.

"You have it on you."

"I do."

"You've been carrying it."

"Since the twenty-eighth of October, when Theron handed it to me at the clearing-house yard. It travelled in this pocket to Sannholt with me, and back up the coach road, and it is in the pocket now."

Halsa was quiet for a count.

"All right," she said. "Thank you."

"You're welcome."

She had said *thank you* the way a person said *thank you* who had been asked an unanswerable question for a long time and had been answered. It was not a thanks for kindness. It was a thanks for the absence of the wrong answer, which was a thanks of a different shape.

---

Halsa looked at her hands. Then she looked at the case book. Then she looked at Theron.

"I'm going to give you something," she said. "Not for the page. I am not going to dispute the page. The page is what it is, and we both know it is, and I have already said I will not dispute it, and I am not going back on that. This is not for the page. This is for the question I asked."

"All right."

"I had a brother," she said. "He was seventeen. His name was Tarrin."

Theron did not write the name down. The case book stayed closed under his hands.

"Eight years ago," she said, "Joren took a scare-job in a hill village three days' ride north of Mirren. Magical intimidation. Frighten a debtor into paying. He was not the only mage on the work — there were two of them — and the work was the kind of work that occasionally spilled. He knew it was the kind of work that occasionally spilled. He took it. He had been trying to put aside enough to stop taking that kind of work for two years before that, and he wasn't there yet, and his sister's children were eating, and he took the rest of the fee."

"All right."

"It spilled. The debtor was a man named Hemmel. The debtor's son was eighteen and was at the door of the house when the work went in. My brother was not the debtor's son. He was the boy from the next house. He came over because he had heard a noise and the Hemmel boy was a year older than him and they had been in school together, and the working caught both of them, and the Hemmel boy lived and my brother did not."

"All right."

"Joren was not in the room. He was in the yard. The other mage on the work was the one who set the glamour. Joren took the rest of the fee that night. He took it knowing the work had gone messy, and he did not ask the name of the boy who had died. He did not, in the eight years between that and the Wyrm, ever ask the name. I checked. Pessel did not have the name. The Copper Finch did not have the name. The Sannholt landlady did not have the name. He did not write it anywhere. He did not look it up. He carried the general weight of *a job that had spilled* and he carried it the rest of his working life, and he did the careful thing with the next eight years of money on behalf of his sister and his niece, and what he never did was ask whose blood the eighth year of that money had started in."

She stopped.

She did not look at Theron when she stopped. She looked at the wall above his left shoulder again.

"That is the part of it that is mine to say," she said. "I am not telling you to think anything about it. I am not telling Miss Verrin to think anything about it. I am telling you it because you asked for the page and the page does not say it and I asked for the name and the name was used. The book is even now."

"All right."

"It is not even on his side. It will not ever be even on his side. He is dead. That is the work I did, and the work I did is the work I did, and I am not telling you it was anything but. I am telling you the shape of the grievance because I asked you a question and you answered it and the answer was *yes*, and the answer being *yes* is what makes me able to sit in this cabin and not make this morning any harder than it has to be."

"All right."

"That is all."

"All right."

She looked back down at her hands. Then she looked at the sealed sheet beside the cup.

She picked it up.

She broke the wax with her thumb the way a person broke the wax of a note they had been carrying since Friday evening at a boarding-house door. She unfolded the sheet. She read it once. The reading took the count of a long breath. She folded it again and set it on the desk beside the cup.

"Davren is courteous," she said.

"He is."

"He's set out what the writ does and what the writ doesn't. He has set out that I am not under arrest aboard this gondola. He has set out that I am asked to remain in cabin six for the remainder of the morning service and to disembark with the two of you at Sannholt, where the magistrate's table will be ready."

"That is what he set out, yes."

"I'll do that."

"All right."

She put her right hand into the right pocket of her coat. She brought out a small flat object — a piece of folded paper, folded once, the size of a calling card. She set it on the desk beside the case book. She did not slide it toward him.

"That is the address in Sannholt where the trigger architecture is written down," she said. "It is written down because Joren told me not to write it down, and I did not write it down for six years, and then in the seventh year I wrote it down once, on a single sheet, kept folded, in a stone box, in a back room of a house that does not stand any more. The page exists. Miss Verrin can collect it on her way south, or Davren can send a clerk, or it can stay where it is, and I am telling you it is there because the page being uncollected is the only piece of me that would still be working after the morning service today, and I am not leaving working pieces."

Theron did not pick it up.

"All right."

"You can have the address read to you and you can decide what to do with it."

"All right."

She set both hands flat on her knees again, palms down, the way she had set them at the close of the laying-out. She looked at the case book. She looked at the sealed-and-now-unsealed note. She looked at the folded paper.

"That's everything I had to put down," she said. "I am ready to go where you say I am to go for the rest of the morning."

"You're to stay in this cabin," Theron said. "Sable will be in cabin four. I'll be in cabin two. The corridor will be the corridor. The morning service runs to second bell. The gondola sets down at Sannholt at the third bell. At the third bell I'll knock on this door and we will walk off together to the magistrate's table that Davren has been good enough to arrange."

"All right."

"Is there anything in the cabin you'd like, between now and the third bell."

"No."

"Is there anything you'd like asked of the steward."

"No."

"Is there anything you'd like Miss Verrin or me to bring you from our own cabins."

"No."

"All right."

> *[Investigator — The question has been asked. The question has been answered. The room has set down what it brought.]*

He read the line through once. He gave it the small dry nod he had given the System every morning of the week. He stood.

Sable stood with him.

He took the leather strap off the chair-back where he had set it when he sat down. He picked up the case book and slid it into the strap and tied the strap. He did not pick up the folded paper on the desk. He left it where Halsa had set it.

"At the third bell," he said.

"At the third bell."

Sable did not say anything. She picked up her notebook from the small chair and put it under her arm with the pencil still through the spine. She went to the door. She opened it.

Halsa did not stand. She stayed on the porthole bench with her hands flat on her knees. She looked at Theron once. She did not look at Sable.

"Thank you," she said, again, and this time she was saying it to both of them, and this time the thanks was for the morning that had not been made harder than it had to be, which was a thanks of a different shape again.

"You're welcome," Theron said.

He went out.

Sable followed.

The door of cabin six closed behind them with the small definite sound of a latch falling into its keep, which was the sound the door of a cabin made on a small southbound gondola at the hour past first bell on the seventeenth of November when the hum through the deck was the hum of two drakes finding their working thermal and the corridor was empty and the morning service had four and a half hours to run.

Theron stood in the corridor a count.

He set the case book under his arm.

"Cabin two," he said.

"Cabin four," Sable said.

They walked together to the doors of their own cabins. At his door he stopped. He looked at her over the strap of the case book.

"That was well asked, and well answered," he said. "Both of you."

"It was a question," Sable said. "It needed the answer it needed."

"Yes."

She went into cabin four. He went into cabin two.

The cabin was small. Two portholes, a writing desk, a chair, a padded bench under the porthole. The hum through the deck was the Wyrm's hum at a lower note, because there were two drakes pulling and not three.

He set the case book on the desk.

He sat on the bench.

He did not open the book.

---

# Chapter 36 — The Title at the Foot of the Quest

Second bell sounded through the deck.

Theron heard it the way a person heard a bell on a gondola he had been on the better part of a morning — not as an event but as the deck answering itself. He had been sitting on the bench under the porthole of cabin two with the case book on the desk and his hands flat on his knees in the posture Halsa had used in cabin six. He had not been doing anything in particular. He had not been working the case. There was no case left to work in the morning's working sense; the working had been done at Pessel's table and at his own table at the Arms and at Davren's reading-table and at the small writing desk in cabin six, and what was in front of him now was a procedure, and the procedure did not need rehearsing.

He stood.

He put on the coat he had hung on the hook by the door. He picked up the leather strap. He slid the case book into the strap and tied the strap with the small ritual tying he had used every morning of the week, not because the strap needed tying but because the act of tying was the act of saying *now*. He put his hand on the door latch. He waited a count and did not count it. He went out.

The corridor was empty.

Cabin four's door opened as he came up to it, which meant Sable had been at the door waiting, which meant she had set herself to come out when she heard him. Her notebook was closed in her left hand with the pencil through the spine the way she carried it when she had decided she would not be writing. She had her travelling coat on. She had not buttoned it.

"Ready," she said.

"Ready."

She closed the door behind her without locking it. They walked together. He carried the strap. She came on his left hand, which was the side she had been on at Davren's table and at the door of cabin six and at the foot of the gangway at Halverstow, and the placement had stopped being a placement and become a fact about the corridor.

The porthole in the bend of the corridor looked out on Sannholt harbour.

The harbour was a smaller, southern thing than Halverstow's: two slip-cranes working at the eastern arm, a customs boat at the inner mole with a single oarsman backing it onto a pile, the water flat and the light long across it the way southern afternoon light went long. A gull stood on the pile beside the customs boat. The gondola was descending. The hum through the deck had gone down a note, the way it went down when a drake throttled back for the set-down phase, and Theron registered the note without working it.

"Sannholt," Sable said.

"Sannholt."

They came to cabin six.

He knocked once and waited the count he had decided he would wait, which was three, and then he knocked again, because the second knock was a courtesy the first had not been. He heard, beyond the door, a small specific movement — a chair, set carefully back from a table — and then the door opened.

Halsa Renn was in her travelling coat with the buttons done up. Her hair was pinned. Her bag was on the floor by her left foot, the buckle done up, the strap looped through her hand. She had her right hand free. She looked at Theron and at Sable and at the strap under Theron's arm in the same order she had looked at them yesterday, and she stepped out into the corridor without speaking. She set the bag down beside her foot to close the door of cabin six. She did not lock it. She picked the bag back up.

"Mr. Mavik."

"Miss Renn."

"Miss Verrin."

"Miss Renn."

That was the whole of what was said in the corridor.

They walked together to the disembarkation gangway. The Sannholt steward — a different steward from the Wyrm's Calden, a long-faced quiet man named on the brass plate of his station as Master Pell — was at the head of the gangway with his hands behind his back. He looked at Theron, who was first, and at the strap under Theron's arm, and at the magistral seal that was visible on the cover edge of the strap because Theron had set it visible. He inclined his head a half-inch.

"Mr. Mavik."

"Master Pell."

"Mr. Davren wired the harbour-master at first bell. The receiving room is the second warehouse from the slip. The constables are at the door of it. Master Ottren's at the table inside."

"Thank you."

"Three of you stepping off together."

"Three of us."

Pell inclined his head the second half-inch and stepped aside.

The gondola had set down while they were in the corridor. The gangway was already laid. The Sannholt slip was a working slip, not a passenger pier — paint-marks on the stone for the loading stations, a row of fenders done in a coastal hand, a customs boat tied off forward of the gondola's mooring with the same single oarsman now coiling his line. The gull on the pile had not moved. The light on the slip was the southern afternoon's working light. Theron registered each of these the way Wisp had taught him without teaching him to register a working yard.

They walked down the gangway together. Halsa was between them — Theron on her left, Sable on her right. The order had not been arranged. The order had set itself in the corridor.

The receiving room was the second warehouse from the slip.

It was a working warehouse with a customs office at one end, partitioned off in painted board, with a door in the partition and the door open. Two constables in the Sannholt magistrate's plain blue stood at the door. They did not move when the three of them came up. The constable on the left had a clipboard and the constable on the right had nothing in his hands, which Theron read as the courtesy of the arrangement Davren had set up — the constable on the right was the constable who would walk Halsa through, and his hands were free in the way a man kept his hands free who did not mean to use them.

Inside the partitioned office a small writing table was set against the inner wall.

Behind the table sat a man in his early fifties in a worn grey magistrate's coat, with reading glasses pushed up onto his forehead and a single sheet of paper laid square in front of him. His hands were on the paper. He looked up as they came in and he did not stand, because standing would have been a performance the room had been arranged not to perform.

"Mr. Mavik."

"Master Ottren."

"Mr. Davren is well, I hope."

"He is."

"You're under his deputation for this delivery."

"I am."

"The order."

Theron unbuckled the strap. He took out the magistral travel-order Davren had signed at the close of Friday and laid it on the table in front of Ottren without sliding it. He took out the case book and laid it beside the order, also without sliding it. He stepped back a half-step. Halsa stood where she had been standing. Sable stood where she had been standing.

Ottren read the order. He read it through once. He set his hand on the page in a flat reading hand and he looked up at Halsa.

"Miss Renn."

"Master Ottren."

"You're presented to me at this table under Mr. Davren's order of yesterday. You are not under arrest as of this moment. You are, as of my countersignature, in the custody of the Sannholt magistrate's office for the purpose of giving a statement under writ. The constable on my left will walk you through to the inner room. There is tea in the inner room and there is a chair. The statement will begin when I come through. Do you understand the shape of what I have just said."

"I do."

"Anything to say to Mr. Mavik or to Miss Verrin before we begin."

Halsa looked at Theron. She looked at Sable. She did not look long at either.

"No," she said. "I said it in the cabin. I have nothing to add to it on the dock."

"Thank you."

She did not say *thank you* back. She picked up her bag in her right hand and she walked past the table and past the constable on the right and through the inner door, which the constable held for her without making anything of holding it. The inner door closed behind her with a small definite sound.

Ottren took up his pen.

He countersigned the order. He turned the order and slid it back across the table to Theron, who took it without looking at it and folded it once and put it back in the case book and tied the strap. Ottren laid his hand on the case book itself.

"This is the book."

"This is the book."

"It stays with me."

"It does."

"Thank you for the manner of the delivery, Mr. Mavik."

"It was Mr. Davren's manner. I was carrying it."

"You were carrying it."

Ottren did not stand to see them out. He had not stood to see them in. He inclined his head the half-inch Pell had inclined his head at the gangway, which was the half-inch this part of the country used for the work of magistrates, and he opened the case book and did not look up again.

Theron stepped back.

Sable stepped back.

They turned and went out through the partition door past the two constables, who did not look at them, into the warehouse, and from the warehouse out into the southern afternoon on the slip.

> *Quest closed: Solve the Death of Joren Aldis.*
> *Title at first issue (Wyrm, cabin twelve, second watch, the fifth of October): Solve the Death of Joren Aldis.*
> *Title carried through the run.*

He read the screen.

He read it a second time, slower, the way a man read a sheet of paper that had a thing on it he had thought was on a different sheet of paper.

He had read the quest line in cabin twelve as *Solve the Death Aboard the Skybound Wyrm.* He had carried that title in his head for the better part of a fortnight. He had read in the captain's day-cabin at Halverstow a System line that said *Quest updated: Solve the Death of Joren Aldis* and he had read the word *updated* and not paused on it because the line was already doing a different work in him on that morning. The title had not been updated. The title had been the title. What had been updated was the part of the title the screen had been willing, on that morning, to show him.

He stood with the strap under his arm.

He gave the screen the small dry nod he had given the System every morning of the week.

He gave it a half-count longer than the morning nod.

Sable was beside him. She was looking at the gondola in its mooring at the visitor slip. She did not look at him. She had not seen the screen and she was not going to ask.

"All right," he said.

"All right."

They walked together away from the warehouse, along the working edge of the slip, past the customs boat with the oarsman now sitting on a bollard and eating a piece of bread, past the gull on the pile that had not moved, past the gondola whose two drakes were on their tethered rest with their heads down on their forepaws in the way Wisp had told him drakes rested when a set-down had been clean. The slip-cranes worked at the eastern arm. The light went long across the water.

At the end of the working edge the slip became a wider stone pier with a low rail at the harbour-side. They stopped at the rail. Theron set his free hand on the rail. The rail was warm from the southern afternoon. Sable put her right hand into her coat pocket and rested two fingers against the inside lining of the inside pocket where the carved fish had been since the twenty-eighth of October. She did not take anything out.

"Sannholt," she said.

"Sannholt."

"The room behind us is the room behind us."

"It is."

"There's a thing or two ahead of us that isn't the room behind us."

"There is."

She did not name it. He did not ask her to.

He took the strap off his shoulder and resettled it. The case book inside it was lighter than it had been at the head of the gangway, because Ottren had the part of it that was Ottren's now, and what Theron carried back to the harbour-side inn was what Theron would carry from here on, which was a different weight.

The gull on the pile lifted off and went south and was a working bird on a working afternoon and was none of his business.

"All right," he said.

"All right," Sable said.

They walked off the pier toward the harbour-side inn at the slow working pace of two people who had finished one thing and had not yet begun the next.

---

# Chapter 37 — Seeing the Unseen

The harbour wall ran along the slip for a hundred yards and then bent inward toward a row of buildings that had been put up over a long time and had been mended with whatever the year could spare. The first of them was a chandlery, closed for the afternoon. The second was an oil-merchant's. The third was an inn with a board hung outside it and one word chalked on the board, which was *stew*, and underneath the word, in a smaller hand, *with bread*.

A cat was on the step.

"This one," Sable said.

"This one."
