She did not touch the cat as she went past it; she let it have the step. Theron went past it on the other side, and the cat watched them with the considered unconcern of a working cat that had its own business in the afternoon and had decided they were not part of it.

Inside, the inn was three tables in a front room and a back porch beyond a door at the end of the bar. The man behind the bar had his sleeves rolled and was wiping a glass with a dishcloth that had been white once and was no longer. He looked at Theron and at Sable and at the strap under Theron's arm and at the way Sable was carrying her notebook in her left hand with the pencil through the spine.

"Stew," he said.

"Two," Sable said. "And bread. And whatever you've got that's hot to drink."

"Cider. We've got a cider that's hot."

"That."

"Back porch?"

"Back porch."

He inclined his head a half-inch, which was the half-inch this part of the country used for all work, and they went through.

The porch was a six-board platform on the inn's harbour side with a low rail and three small tables. None of the tables was taken. The light on the porch was the southern afternoon's working light, long and clear, doing nothing in particular to the wood of the tables. The harbour beyond the rail was the harbour they had walked along. The gondola sat at its mooring at the visitor slip; the slip-cranes still worked at the eastern arm; the customs boat was now tied off, the oarsman gone to whatever an oarsman went to when his boat was tied. A gull was on a piling. It was possibly the same gull. Theron did not test it.

They took the table by the rail.

He set the strap down beside his chair. Sable laid her notebook on the table and the pencil beside the notebook with the point inward. They sat. He put his hands on the table and then took them off and put them on his knees and then forgot about them.

"A week," she said.

"A week."

"Counting today."

"Counting today."

That was the whole of what was said before the cider came.

The cider came in a heavy mug that had a chip on the rim on Theron's side; he turned the mug the half-turn that put the chip away from his mouth without making anything of it. Sable's mug had no chip. She wrapped her hands around it and did not drink.

The stew would be a little while. The man behind the bar had said nothing about how long; nobody on a working afternoon in a working harbour-side inn told you how long a stew would be, because a stew was the time a stew was, and you sat with the cider until it came.

Theron put his hands around the mug.

*[Investigator — level six.]*

> *Talent unlocked: Seeing the Unseen.*
> *The suspect your training filtered out has the shape you now know how to see.*
> *Filed at the close of: Solve the Death of Joren Aldis.*

He read the line.

He read it again, slower, the way he had read the title line on the slip an hour ago. The way a man read a sheet that had a thing on it he had thought was on a different sheet. The flavour line was three sentences. It was not the flavour-line shape his Conservatory teachers had warned the class about, which was decorative, congratulatory, ornamental. It was the flavour-line shape of a System that had been watching him work the case and had decided what to put on the page now that the case was closed.

The first sentence was the name of the talent and was a name.

The second sentence was about the shape of the suspect he had been trained to see, and the shape of the suspect he now knew how to see, and the gap between them. The gap was Halsa. The gap was every working illusionist on the Mirren coast he had been trained to file in the verify-not-pursue column because the rivalries his class had been taught to weigh were rivalries between people with houses and seals and entries in the registry. The gap was a woman named on a forged paper, sitting eight years in front of a desk, doing what the man who had not asked her brother's name had not done, which was face the outcome.

The third sentence was the title. The third sentence named the case the System had filed the talent under. The case had a name and the name was Joren's name.

He did not nod.

He did not, this time, give the screen the small dry nod he had given the System every morning of the week. He read the three lines a second time, in the same order, and then he set the mug down on the table because the mug had gone a small distance off the line of his palm and he had not noticed it going.

He put his right hand flat on the table.

The screen sat where it had sat. He let it sit. He did not look out at the harbour. He looked at the surface of the table — the grain of the wood, the small dark line where a knife had once been dragged across it, the place where someone's mug had left a ring that had not come out — and he kept his hand where his hand was.

Sable was sitting across from him.

She had taken her hands off her own mug. The mug was steaming a thin steam up between them. She was looking at him.

She did not look away.

She did not lift her hand to her mug. She did not pick up the pencil. She did not turn her face toward the harbour. She sat with her hands in her lap below the edge of the table and she watched him read the screen a second time, and when his eyes moved across the third line for the second time and his hand went flat on the table, she stayed where she was, and she watched.

She watched him for the count it took him to read the lines twice.

She watched him for the count it took him to set the mug down.

She watched him for the count after that, which was longer, and which was the count in which he was reading the third line, the line with Joren's name on it, for whatever number of times he was reading it, and she let him have the count.

Then she said: "What did it say."

Her voice was the voice she had used at the door of cabin six and at the foot of the gangway and at the rail of the pier — small, working, hers. The count had been long enough. She had been the one to break it.

Theron looked up.

The screen was still there in his vision. He did not have to dismiss it. He let it sit and looked through it.

"It levelled the class," he said.

"Yes."

"It put a name on what it was."

"What was the name."

He looked at her.

He looked at her in a way he had not looked at her at the foot of the table at Ottren's, and had not looked at her at the rail of the pier, and had not looked at her at the bend of the corridor on the gondola. He looked at her the way a man looked at a person who had just asked him for the name of the thing a System had set down in front of him on the porch of an inn in a working harbour in the south, at the end of a week.

"Seeing the unseen," he said.

"Hm."

"The flavour line was three sentences. It named the talent and then it said the suspect my training filtered out has the shape I now know how to see. And then it filed the talent under the case."

"Under Joren's name."

"Under Joren's name."

She let that sit.

She picked up her mug, then. She wrapped both hands around it again. She did not drink. She looked at him over the rim and did not say anything else for the length of a breath, and then she said:

"Good."

That was the whole of it.

Theron took his hand off the table. He moved it back to his mug. The cider had gone from too hot to nearly drinkable, and he drank a small swallow and set the mug down and put his hand around it again.

The screen had dismissed itself. He had not asked it to.

"All right," he said.

"All right."

The stew came.

It came in two wide bowls and was the colour of a stew that had been on a stove since the morning, and it had carrots in it and what looked like mutton and what was definitely a small amount of barley, and the bread came with it on a wooden board with a knife. The man set the bowls down and inclined his half-inch again and went back to his bar.

Sable tore the bread.

She tore it in halves, not pieces — one half put down on Theron's side of the board, one half on hers, the working courtesy of a person who had eaten with people at tables for a long time and knew the shape of dividing a loaf without making anything of it. Theron took his half and tore a smaller piece off it. The bread was warm in the middle. He had not had warm bread since the morning of the day at Halverstow, which had been six days now.

They ate.

The stew was a stew. It was the right stew. It was the stew the chalkboard outside had promised it would be, and it sat warm in him in the way a hot meal sat warm in a person who had not eaten since the dawn loaf on the gondola. Sable ate without speaking, and he ate without speaking, and the gull on the piling lifted off and went down the harbour and was a working bird and was none of his business.

After a while she said: "There's a room here."

"There's a room here."

"They had two."

"Then there are two."

"Mm."

"For tonight."

"For tonight."

She did not say anything about tomorrow. He did not ask. He was tired in the specific way a person was tired who had been thinking about a man's death for a week and had now stopped thinking about it, and the tired was sitting on him the way the southern light was sitting on the table, and he let it sit.

The slip-cranes worked at the eastern arm.

The customs boat sat at its tie. The oarsman had not come back. The gondola at the visitor slip had its two drakes on their tethered rest with their heads on their forepaws, and Petal was the smaller of the two, and Mottled was the one whose harness creak was now a memory rather than a present sound, and the third drake, Gravel, was up at the rigging house, probably, with Wisp, being told off for whatever Gravel had decided to take personally on the descent.

Theron took the last torn piece of his bread and used it to clean the side of his bowl.

Sable did the same with hers.

She put her bread down on the empty board. She set the pencil parallel to the spine of the notebook. She put both hands around the mug, which was nearly empty now.

"All right," she said.

"All right."

The cat from the front step had come around the side of the inn while they were eating and was sitting on the porch rail two tables down, watching the harbour with the considered unconcern it had used on the front step. The light on the rail was the southern afternoon's working light, and the cat had its tail wrapped around its forepaws, and it was a working cat on a working porch and it was not theirs and they were not its.

Theron set his hand on the strap beside his chair.

The case book inside the strap was the lighter book it had been since the warehouse. He did not open it. He left his hand on the leather and let the leather be warm under his palm, and Sable across the table set down her mug and pushed it the half-inch back from the rim of the table that meant the mug was done, and she did not look at him in the way she had looked at him while he read the screen, because that look had been the look it was, and she was, now, looking at the harbour.

The bread board sat empty between them.

"All right," she said again, the second time, which was a different *all right* from the first.

"All right," Theron said.

He left his hand on the strap. The cat did not move from the rail. The chip on Theron's mug was still on Theron's mug, and somewhere up at the visitor slip a drake shifted in its tether and resettled and was quiet again, and the porch held the two of them in the small working pace of two people who had eaten a meal together at the end of a week.

---

# Chapter 38 — The Case Settles

The front room of the inn took its evening occupants the way an inn took its evening occupants in a working harbour, which was without ceremony.

Two men in oil-merchant aprons sat at the table by the door with a jug between them and were not speaking and did not appear to require speech. A woman whose name Theron did not know was at the till with a ledger laid open and a small pair of brass scales, and the scales were the kind that weighed coin rather than goods, and she was running the day's take. The barman had taken off the apron and put on a clean one and was wiping the same glass with a different dishcloth, which was white in the manner of a cloth lately come off a line and not yet put to a working day. The light in the room was the lamp light of an inn that had decided the southern afternoon had gone enough.

Theron and Sable had come in from the back porch through the bar door and had taken a table at the inside wall, not the window, because the porch was the porch and the front room was a different room.

The barman brought bread.

He brought it without being asked, on a wooden board, with a knife laid across it and a small dish of salt to the side of the board, and he set the board between them and inclined his half-inch.

"Stew's done for the day," he said. "There's a pie in the oven."

"Pie," Sable said.

"Two."

"Two."

He inclined his half-inch and went.

Sable did not tear the bread this time. She left it on the board between them and put both hands in her lap. Her notebook was on the table at her left, the pencil through the spine. Theron's strap was on the bench beside him with the personal notebook inside it. The working case book had gone to Ottren at the warehouse. What was in the strap now was the run in his own hand, week by week, which was the lighter book.

The door from the lane opened.

A man came in who was not one of the oil-merchant pair and was not a working local in the manner the oil merchants were working locals. He was a small spare man in a coat that had been good once and was still respectable, with a leather satchel under his left arm of the kind clerks carried, and he stopped just inside the door and looked across the room until he found their table.

"Mr. Mavik. Miss Verrin."

"Mr. Pessel."

"Mr. Pessel."

He did not come to the table. He came to the corner of the table, which was the courtesy a man used who had a thing to say and not a meal to take. He set his hand on the back of the empty chair and did not pull it out.

"The rooms are settled with the house. I spoke to him at the door."

"Thank you."

"It's the two upstairs, the back ones. They look on the lane, not the harbour. The harbour rooms were taken before the gondola was down."

"That'll do."

"It will."

He looked at Sable.

"Miss Verrin."

"Mr. Pessel. The address."

He had been waiting for it. Theron read it in his shoulders before he read it in his face — the small settling of a man who had been carrying a thing in his pocket since the morning and was glad to set it on the table.

"I have it. I have had it since the twenty-eighth. It's in the inside flap of the book on my desk in the back of the chandlery."

"I'll come by in the morning."

"Come early. I open the chandlery at first bell and there's a man from the rigging house who comes at quarter past, and after him there's a woman who has a question about a length of cordage I have not been able to satisfy in four visits, and after her the morning is gone."

"First bell."

"First bell."

He looked at her a moment longer. He did not look at Theron. The look was the look of a man who had been holding a piece of paper for a man and was being asked, now, to give the piece of paper to the woman the man had not lived to give it to himself, and the look did not require any naming.

"Mr. Mavik."

"Mr. Pessel."

"The morning, then."

"The morning."

He inclined his half-inch and went out the way he had come in, and the door closed, and the woman at the till did not look up from her ledger and the oil merchants did not look up from their jug, and the barman set down the white cloth and took up a different one without changing the rhythm of his wiping.

Sable put her hands on the table.

She did not say anything. She picked up the knife from the bread board and she cut the loaf in halves, working courtesy, one half on her side and one half on his, and she set the knife back down with the blade pointing in toward the centre of the board. Theron tore a piece off his half.

The pie came after a while.

It was a pie of the kind a coastal kitchen made at the end of a day with whatever the day had not used — fish, potato, a little of the green that grew at the back of the kitchen garden — and it was hot and the crust was good and they ate without making a thing of it. Sable poured water from the jug on the table into both cups and drank her cup down and poured it again. Theron drank his without noticing he had drunk it.

"Tomorrow," she said.

"Tomorrow."

"After Pessel I'll need a day or two to think on it. I don't want to write the letter and post it and have it arrive ahead of me. I want to be there."

"You said."

"I'm saying it again."

"All right."

She took a piece of bread and used it to clean the side of her plate and put it in her mouth and chewed it and swallowed it.

"You don't have to come to Pessel's with me."

"I know."

"I didn't say I didn't want you to. I said you don't have to."

"I'll walk you over. I won't go in."

"Mm."

That was the whole of it.

---

She went up before he did.

She took her notebook in her left hand with the pencil through the spine and the cup of water in her right and she inclined her half-inch to the barman at the bar, who had stopped wiping glasses at some point and was reading a folded sheet by the lamp, and she went up the inn stairs at the working pace of a person who had eaten and was tired and was not making a performance of either.

Theron sat at the table with the empty plates.

The barman came and took the plates and the board and left the salt and the water jug, and Theron asked for a cup of the cider that had been hot in the afternoon and was now whatever it was, and the barman brought it in the same chipped mug, which Theron read as the barman's small particular courtesy of returning the cup that was his.

He set the strap on the table.

He undid the buckle and took out the personal notebook and laid it square in front of him with the spine at his left. He took the pencil from where it had been tucked in the strap-fold and rolled it once on the table to settle it under his fingers.

The notebook was open to where he had last written, which was at Pessel's table on the twenty-eighth. There were seven working pages between the twenty-eighth and the morning at Ottren's warehouse — interviews, the reading at Davren's table, the long Friday at the Arms, the writing-desk pages in cabin six. He turned through them once, not reading them, the way a man turned the pages of a book he had written to remind his hand where it had been. He came to the first blank.

He wrote the date at the top of the page in the small hand he used for closing entries.

> *Closing — Sannholt, October fifth.*
>
> *H. Renn delivered to Master Ottren, Sannholt magistrate's office, second warehouse from the slip, by Mr. Davren's order of yesterday. Order countersigned. The working case book is with Ottren. The matter is with the Sannholt office. The quest closed at the door of the warehouse.*

He stopped.

He looked at the line he had written and at the white space below it. He had written the procedure. He had not written the case. The procedure was the easy part to write and the case was the part he had been carrying since cabin twelve, and the white space sat under his pencil and asked the question whether he was going to write any of it down.

He did not write it down.

He wrote one more line.

> *J. Aldis. Coastal. The work was not for himself.*

Then he closed the notebook.

He sat for a moment with his hand on the cover. He was aware, in the way a man was aware of a thing he had set on a shelf and had not yet taken back down, of the other thing — the sheet with the small mark on it that had been in Halsa's papers at Davren's table, the device he had not recognised and had not asked her about, because the cabin had been the cabin and the case had been the case and the device was a separate question and Davren had taken the papers. He did not name the thing in the notebook. He did not name it to himself in any sentence longer than the registering of it. He left it where it was, which was on the shelf, which was a place a thing could be while a man finished his cider in a working inn and went up to a room on the second floor.

He drank the cider down.

He buckled the strap.

He inclined his half-inch to the barman without thinking about inclining it, which was a thing he had begun doing in the last two days of the week and had not noticed himself begin doing, and the barman inclined his back and did not look up from the folded sheet by the lamp.

Theron went up.

---

The morning came in at the back window of his room as the southern morning came into a back window on a lane, which was sideways, off the wall of the chandlery opposite, with the colour of an early sky reflected in the whitewash. He had slept in a way he had not slept on the gondola, which was without listening for the deck. He had woken once in the middle of the night and had not known where he was and had then known and had gone back to sleep, and that was the whole of the night's account.

He went down.

Sable was at the front-room table already. She had her travelling coat on and her notebook beside her cup and the pencil through the spine. She had her right hand around the cup and her left flat on the table. She had not buttoned the coat. The barman had brought bread and a small pot of preserve and a pot of tea, and she had poured a cup for the place across from her where Theron had not yet been sitting.

"Morning," she said.

"Morning."

He sat. He picked up the cup. The tea was hot. He drank the first swallow without tasting it and the second more slowly and set the cup down.

"First bell soon," she said.

"Soon."

"I'll go over after this."

"All right."

She put preserve on a piece of bread with the back of the spoon and ate it. She did not eat fast. She drank her tea. He drank his. The oil merchants from the night before were not in the front room this morning; in their place at the door table was an older woman with a basket on her lap and a small dog at her foot, and the dog was watching the barman with the considered attention of a dog that had been given a piece of bread on a previous morning and had not forgotten the precedent.

"Walk with me as far as the door," Sable said.

"As far as the door."

"That's what I'm asking."

"That's what I said."

She finished her tea. She tore a small piece of bread and ate it without preserve. She set her cup down the half-inch back from the rim of the table that meant the cup was done. She put her right hand into her coat pocket, briefly, and rested two fingers against the inside lining of the inside pocket where the carved fish had been since the dock. She took her hand back out. She picked up her notebook.

"Ready," she said.

"Ready."

They went out.

The lane in front of the inn was a working lane at first bell with two carters loading at a yard three doors up and a boy with a bucket sluicing a step further down and the early gulls already at the harbour. The southern morning light was lower and harder than the afternoon's had been, and it laid itself across the cobbles in long stripes broken by the shadows of the buildings opposite, and the smell of the harbour was the smell of a harbour at first bell, which was cold water and rope and a little of yesterday's fish.

The cat from the inn step was not on the step this morning. The step was wet from the boy's bucket and the cat had taken itself elsewhere.

Sable turned left along the lane. Theron walked on her left, the side he had walked on at Davren's table and at the foot of the gangway and at the rail of the pier and at the porch table the afternoon before, and the placement was the placement it had been. Pessel's chandlery was four doors down on the harbour side, and the painted board outside the chandlery's door said *Pessel & Son — Chandlers & Ropework* in a hand that had been done in white over an older hand that had said the same thing in a different white some years ago.

They stopped at the door.

Sable did not put her hand on the latch. She did not look at the board. She looked at the harbour at the end of the lane — the customs boat at its tie, the gull on the pile, the gondola at the visitor slip with its two drakes on their tethered rest — the way a person looked at a view she had decided to take a count of before she did the next thing she had come to do.

"All right," she said.

"All right."

"I'll find you at the inn after."

"You'll find me at the inn after."

She put her hand on the latch.

She lifted it. The door went open under her hand and the small bell at the inside of the lintel rang one note, which was the note a chandler's bell rang at first bell on a working morning in a working harbour, and Pessel was at the back of the shop already with a ledger on the counter and his hand on a pen, and he looked up and saw her and inclined his half-inch.

Sable went in.

The door closed behind her with the small definite sound a door closed with on a working morning, and the bell rang the second note, which was the note it rang on the closing, and the lane was a lane again with the carters at their yard and the boy at his bucket and the early gulls at the harbour.

Theron stood in the lane a moment.

He looked at the board with the white-over-white and at the gull on the pile and at the visitor slip and at the harbour-side inn three doors back, and he turned and walked back at the slow working pace of a person who had walked a friend to a door and had been told to walk back alone, and the southern morning light went sideways across the cobbles ahead of him, and the day had work in it that was not his to do for the next part of the morning.

He inclined his half-inch to the barman as he came back through the inn door, without thinking about inclining it.

The barman inclined his back.

---

# Chapter 39 — The Address on the Page

The front room of the inn did not have very much to do at first bell of a Sunday in October. The basket-and-dog woman had finished her tea and left a small clean place at her table where the dog had set its chin on the wood for the duration. The carters in the lane outside had finished one load and were on the second. The barman had moved from glasses to spoons and was working a thin cloth over the bowl of one with the patience of a man who would have done the same thing at the same hour on any October morning anyone could think of.

Theron sat at the table he and Sable had eaten at and read the room without meaning to read it.

He had told himself, walking back up the lane, that he would not work the morning. The work was Sable's. He had walked her to the chandler's door because she had asked him to walk her to the chandler's door, and the door had closed behind her with the small definite sound a door closed with, and from that sound forward the morning was hers. The barman had brought him a cup of tea without making a thing of him being there alone, and a piece of bread beside it he had not asked for and did not feel he could now decline. The southern light came down the lane through the front window and put two slanted bars across the table.

He put his palm on the wood between the bars.

The wood was warm where the light had been on it for half an hour and cooler at the edges. He took his palm back.

*[Investigator — field interval. The street is quiet. Read the street.]*

He did, for a little while. The carters in the yard up the lane were a father and a son or near enough; the son was loading and the father was counting, the count being the part the father had kept for himself when he no longer wanted to lift the crates. The barman had a wedding ring on the hand that worked the cloth and not on the hand that held the spoon. He stopped himself there. The work was Sable's. He drank his tea.

---

She came back a little before second bell.

He heard the door before he saw her — the lane door, not the bar door this time — and then the small shift of the room's attention as the room registered an arrival and decided it was not for them. She came across the front room in her travelling coat, unbuttoned at the collar in the way she had unbuttoned it last night, with her notebook in her left hand and a folded piece of paper in her right and the second folded piece of paper between the first two fingers of the same right hand. Two papers in one hand, held loose enough that they were two and not one.

She sat down across from him. She set the notebook flat on the table. She put the two papers on the notebook, one on top of the other, without separating them yet.

The barman brought a cup without being asked. She inclined her half-inch to him and put her hand around the cup but did not drink.

"How is he," Theron said.

"He's well. He held the address since the twenty-eighth, like he said. He wanted to give it to me at his counter and not on the doorstep, which is the kind of thing he's particular about."

"All right."

"He gave me the address. And a paper of his own with two lines on it, which is what Joren had given him to hold for the family in case Joren didn't come south himself."

"All right."

She moved the top paper a half-inch to her left with the back of one finger so that the second paper showed at the edge.

"And he gave me this back."

The second paper was familiar to him in the way a piece of paper was familiar to a man who had watched another person read it across a passenger lounge on a long-ago morning in the air — the thick weight of it, the dark border, the particular fold of a property document carried in a coat pocket for more days than was good for the fold. The inheritance papers. Her own.

"He had it?"

"I'd brought it. I'd asked him last week whether his trade extended to a second opinion on a deed I had not yet read carefully, and he had said it did when it was wanted, and the more important thing was the address. I let the deed go to the side. He read it this morning while I waited for him to finish a sale of a length of cordage to a man who knew exactly the length he wanted and would not be hurried about it."

"All right."

She did not open either paper.

She turned the top one — Pessel's address — through ninety degrees with the same finger she had moved it with, and the writing on the outer face came up the right way to her, the inked name and place running in Pessel's small clerkly hand. She did not unfold it. She read the outer face. Theron read it across the table from her, which was the angle his class had been training him in for three years and the angle his eyes had now begun reading at without his asking them to.

The outer face had Joren's family's name at the top and a place beneath it.

The place was three lines: the parish, the road, the marker. The parish was *Tellan's Cross*. The road was *the inner coast road, by the chandler's stile*. The marker was *third house above the white stone*.

Sable did not say anything.

She moved her hand across the table and laid her flat palm on the second paper — her own deed — and looked at it without moving the paper at all. The deed was folded with its outer face down. The outer face would have her own destination on it, the place she had been going to claim since before she met him.

Theron did not turn it over.

He did not need to. Her hand was on it the way a hand was on a thing whose contents the hand already knew, and her stillness was the stillness of a person comparing two pieces of paper she did not need to lay side by side because the comparison was already finished. He had seen that stillness in her once before, three doors down a corridor on the first morning of the run, when a man had thanked a steward by name without performance, and she had stood with her hand on her own door latch and not moved for the time it took a corridor to be a corridor again.

He sat with his cup between his hands and waited.

After a little while she said, "I know that stretch."

"All right."

"The inner coast road runs north of where I'm going. Tellan's Cross is the next parish over. The white stone is at the fork above the chandler's stile, which the road takes left for Tellan's and right for the road my aunt's cottage sits on, two miles further. It's the same coast."

"All right."

"It's the same coast."

Theron did not say anything for a moment.

The morning had a great deal of work in it that was not theirs — the carters at their yard, the barman at his spoons, the basket-woman three doors up the lane. The work in the room they sat in was the quieter work of a piece of paper carried south on a coach and a piece of paper written out in a chandlery this morning, laid one on top of the other on the cover of a notebook.

"I didn't know," she said.

"I know."

"He didn't either. We sat in the back of the front room last week with the deed between us and he asked the parish twice because he wanted to hear it right, and I told him twice, and we talked about the road instead. He didn't put it together at his desk. He put it together when he looked at the deed again this morning with the address he had been holding for Joren beside it. He gave me the deed back with the address on the top, and he said the parish, and that was when he stood up."

"All right."

"He said he had thought about it overnight and would not say it for me. The address was the part that was mine to be told. The rest was the part I should be allowed to read off the page myself."

"He was right about that."

She took her hand off the deed.

She did not turn it over and she did not need to. The two papers sat one on top of the other on the cover of the notebook, and the outer face of the address was the only face of either paper that anyone in the room could read, and Theron could read it from across the table and was not reading it any more, and Sable was looking at her hand on the wood where her hand had been on the paper a moment ago.

The slanted bars of southern light had moved a finger's width along the table since the carters had finished the second load.

*[Investigator — note: the geography you have not been asked to investigate.]*

He read the line once and set it down inside his head where he set lines he did not need to act on, which was a shelf that had begun to fill up in the past week and was, he thought, the kind of shelf that would need its own page in a different book one day.

"Tellan's Cross," he said.

"Tellan's Cross."

"That's where you'll go from the cottage."

"That's where I'll go from the cottage. A day or two with the deed and the cottage first. Then up the inner road. The white stone, the third house. I'll know the house when I'm at it. The work that gets done at a door is done at the door, and I'm not writing it down before I'm there."

"You aren't writing it before you're there."

"I'm not. I won't have the right sentences until I'm in front of her."

"All right."

She picked up the two papers together.

She did not separate them. She opened her notebook to the back, where the cover-fold made a small pocket against the spine, and she slid the two papers into the pocket together, the address on top and the deed below, in the same fold. She closed the notebook. She put the pencil back through the spine. She set her right hand into her coat pocket, the inside pocket, the way she had set it last night before they had gone out into the lane, and her two fingers rested for a half-count against the lining where the carved fish had been since the clearing-house yard in Halverstow.

She took her hand back out.

"All right," she said.

"All right."

She drank her tea then, and it was cold, and she did not remark on it being cold. She set the cup down and inclined her half-inch to the barman without turning her head, and the barman inclined his back from the bar without looking up from the spoon he was working. Theron poured her a fresh cup from the pot, which was still warm at the base if not the rim, and she let him pour it and put her hand around the cup as one puts a hand around a cup that has been poured for one by a person whose right it is to pour it.

"You're going on holiday," she said.

"I'm going on holiday."

"You haven't said where."

"I haven't decided. Two days here. I'll write the report for Davren and put it in Tuesday's coach. After that I am meant to be on a holiday, and I have been told twice by a man who does not say things twice that I am to come back with my head on straight."

"That's a man who knows his work."

"He does."

She drank her tea. She ate a small piece of bread without preserve. The carters' second load went out of the yard up the lane with the slow wooden complaint of a wagon's weight settling on its springs, and the lane was quieter for a moment, and then the boy with the bucket came back to his step and there was the small definite sound of water on stone.

"I'll write you," she said.

"You'll write me."

"From the cottage first. The post-coach from Tellan's Cross goes through here Wednesdays and Saturdays. You'll get the Wednesday one if I post it Tuesday."

"All right."

"I'll tell you what the cottage is like. I won't tell you the rest before I've done it. I'll tell you about it after."

"After."

"After."

That was the whole of it.

She did not move for a moment. Then she stood, and the standing was the working standing of a person whose morning had a next thing in it, and she picked up the notebook with the two papers folded into the back of it and she set it under her arm.

"Two days," she said.

"Two days."

"Then the coach."

"Then the coach."

She inclined her half-inch and went to the stairs. The barman did not look up from the spoon. The basket-woman's dog, which had been at the other table and had now finished considering the barman, had laid its chin back on the wood and was watching the door for whatever the door had next. Theron sat at the table with his cup and the second slanted bar of light running across the wood where Sable's hand had been a minute ago.

He looked at the place where the bar of light fell and read it once, the way his class had taught him to read a thing it had been given a name for now, and then he set the reading down on the shelf with the other reading he had not been asked for, and he drank his tea.

---

# Chapter 40 — The Tuesday Coach

Sable's coach had gone at second bell on the Monday and Theron had walked back from the coach-house yard at the slow working pace of a man who had walked a friend to a coach and had been told to walk back alone, and the barman had a pot of tea on the table where Theron had eaten his breakfast and had not cleared the second cup, which was the courtesy of a man who had been keeping a working inn at the working pace of a working harbour for the years it took to learn that the second cup at a table was a thing one left until the table itself was cleared. Theron sat at his side of the table. He drank his tea. He left the second cup where it was, and he opened his strap, and he laid the personal notebook flat on the wood and he set the case book beside it, and he took out the pencil and he uncapped it, and he began to write the report Warden Davren had asked of him.

The report ran to Monday afternoon and into Monday evening and into Tuesday at first bell.

Davren's stipulated form was the form the Halverstow office had been using since before Theron was at the Conservatory — date and place at the top, party and party, the matter as a single declarative sentence, the procedural chain as numbered paragraphs. He numbered them. He wrote *H. Renn* at the top of paragraph one and *J. Aldis* at the top of paragraph two. He used the names. He wrote *Miss Verrin south on the Monday coach, per her own arrangement* in the place that asked for the disposition of consulting parties, and he did not write anything beside that line.

The barman cleared the second cup at last when he came past with the noon plates on the Monday. He did not say anything about clearing it. He took it and went, and Theron wrote the next paragraph.

He sealed the report at first bell on the Tuesday with the small wax pin he had carried in the strap since Halverstow. He took it across the lane to Pessel's letter-pouch, which hung on a hook by the chandler's door, and he tied the report into the pouch with the cord kept there for the purpose. Pessel was at his counter and inclined his half-inch, and Theron inclined his back.

"The afternoon coach," Pessel said. "It goes at the half of one. The boy takes the pouch over at the quarter."

"All right."

"I'll have a thing for you, if you're at the inn at midmorning."

"All right."

"It's not for the pouch."

"All right."

Theron inclined his half-inch and went back across the lane. The bell at the inside of the lintel rang once on his going out, and once on his going in at the inn door, and the barman did not look up from the keg he was tapping and inclined his half-inch over the tap-arm without looking.

---

Pessel came at the quarter past first bell.

He came himself this time, not the boy. He had the satchel under his left arm and a small leather bundle under his right, and the bundle was tied with a length of the chandler's cord, and he came to the corner of the table where he had stood the night Sable had set the date for the morning meeting, and he set his hand on the back of the empty chair and did not pull it out.

"Mr. Mavik."

"Mr. Pessel."

"This is the rest of what Joren had left with me to hold."

He set the bundle on the table. It was small — the length of a hand, the depth of a clasped fist — and tied as a chandler tied a bundle, which was twice across and once around, the knot in the middle of the upper face.

"For the family," Pessel said. "I gave Miss Verrin the letter on the Sunday and the address on the Sunday, and these were the rest. Miss Verrin is south and the family is north. I am not in the habit of forwarding a man's papers to his sister sight unseen by a person who knows the work the papers were done in. I came to you for the reading. I will take it back if you have it read by midday, and I will put it in the next pouch north myself."

"That'll do."

"It's mostly nothing. A book he had been reading. Two receipts for a coach he had paid for himself for a previous run. The letter I held you have already heard of. There is one slip I did not place, which is the slip I am bringing you for. You will see it. I would not have known the slip from a chandler's receipt if I had not been holding a man's papers for a year, and I have been."

"All right."

Pessel did not sit. He did not appear to want to sit. He set his hand on the back of the chair and he waited the count it took Theron to undo the chandler's cord, and the cord came undone with the small dry sound a chandler's cord came undone with on a working morning, and the bundle opened on the table.

Theron laid the contents in their working order.

The clothbound book first, set at his left. The two coach receipts in a fold beside it. The held letter from Joren to the family — a single folded sheet, the outer face inked in Joren's hand with the family name and the parish — set at the centre. He did not open the letter. The letter was Sable's to give and was not his to read. He set it back into the bundle's wrap with the book and the receipts on either side of it.

And the slip.

It was a single fold, smaller than the receipts and smaller than the letter, the size of a calling card opened once. The paper was a clerk's paper, not a chandler's. The fold was a clerk's fold. The lines on the inside were two — a fee in a number that meant nothing to Theron without the unit at its head, and a delivery clause for a thing that was not named, only signed for with an initial and a date. The date was the third of July of the same year. The Wyrm run had been the last week of September. The initial was not Joren's. It was not Halsa's. It was a single capital letter that Theron read once and did not recognise and read a second time and did not recognise the second time either.

At the upper corner of the slip, stamped not inked, was a small device.

The device was a rondel of three lines folded inward toward a centre, not quite a knot, not quite a wheel. It was the device he had seen on a sheet at Davren's table on the Friday afternoon of the previous week, in among Halsa's papers, in the working pile, and he had registered it without asking after it because the cabin had been the cabin and the case had been the case and the device had been a separate question.

The device on the slip on the table was the same device.

Theron did not move.

He sat at the table with his palm on the wood beside the slip and he read the slip a second time and a third and the device a fourth, and the room around him was the front room of the inn at the quarter past first bell on a working Tuesday in October, and the barman was at the keg, and the older woman with the basket was at the table by the door, and the dog at her foot had the chin on the wood it had had on the Sunday morning, and Pessel was at the back of the chair and was waiting.

"Mr. Pessel."

"Mr. Mavik."

"Did Joren say anything to you about this slip."

"He did not."

"Did he ask you to hold it separately."

"He did not. It was in the bundle. He gave me the bundle in May. He said the bundle was for the family if he did not come south himself by the year's end. He did not separate any of it."

"Did anyone come asking after the bundle since."

"No."

"No one."

"No one."

"All right."

Theron looked at the slip a moment longer.

He did not pick it up. He let it sit on the table beside the wrapped bundle.

*[Investigator — note: the corner of the slip.]*

He read the line once. He did not nod for it. He set it down inside his head where he had set the line on the geography in the front room on the Sunday and the line on the device at Davren's table on the Friday, which was a shelf that had begun to fill up in the past week and was, he thought, the kind of shelf that would need its own page in a different book one day, and he was not going to begin that page on a Tuesday morning at a chandler's letter-pouch with a coach to catch at the half of one.

He took the slip off the table.

He did not put it back into the bundle. He opened the personal notebook to the back cover, where the cover-fold made a small pocket against the spine, and he slid the slip into the pocket. He closed the notebook. He set his hand flat on the cover.

"The rest is for Miss Verrin to take north," he said. "I'd ask you to hold the bundle until she sends word."

"I'll hold it."

"She'll write you from the cottage."

"She said so."

"All right."

"And the slip?"

"The slip stays with me."

Pessel looked at him a count. Then he inclined his half-inch.

"All right."

He took the wrapped bundle off the table — the book and the receipts and the letter, tied again in his hand in the chandler's twice across and once around, the knot at the middle of the upper face — and he set his hand on the back of the chair the half-second a man set his hand on the back of a chair when he had finished what he had come for, and he went.

The bell at the door rang once on the going out.

---

The coach office was across the lane and three doors up from the chandlery, between the oil-merchant's and a small shop that sold bone buttons. The clerk was a thin man at a high desk with a ledger open in front of him and a ruled board of departures hung on the inner wall behind him, the names of the inland stops written in chalk and the times of the coaches written in chalk beside them, and the names of the coastal stops written above. The chalk for Mirren Vale was clean. The time beside it was *one and a half.*

"Mirren Vale," Theron said.

"Inland coach. The half of one."

"That."

"Outside or inside."

"Outside."

The clerk looked at him over the high desk. He was, Theron read, a clerk who had been told *outside* by enough men who had then complained about the wind that he had a clerk's small dry pause for it.

"It's cold on the box at this hour."

"All right."

"Two days to the Vale. There's an overnight at the half-mark at Hessler's Crossing. The Vale's the spring-houses. You'll want the one on the upper terrace. The lower's all coach traffic."

"The upper."

"I'll mark it in the chit."

He marked it in the chit. Theron paid the fare. The clerk took the coin, gave him the chit, and inclined his half-inch over the high desk, and Theron took the chit and inclined his back, and the bell at the coach-office door rang once on his going out.

He went back to the inn.

The barman had bread on the bar and the cold midday cider in the chipped mug he had been using for Theron's afternoon cider since the Sunday, which Theron read as the barman's settled decision about which mug was Theron's mug, and Theron took the mug to the table he had been working the report at and drank the first swallow without tasting it and the second more slowly.

The strap was on the bench beside him.

The personal notebook was in the strap.

The slip with the device on the corner was in the back pocket of the notebook, in the small fold against the spine.

He drank the cider. He ate the bread. The older woman with the basket finished her tea and inclined her half-inch to the barman and went, the dog at her heel. The dog looked back at the barman at the door. The barman did not look up from the lamp. The lamp wick was being trimmed.

At the half-past noon he buckled the strap.

He inclined his half-inch to the barman without thinking about inclining it, and the barman inclined his back without looking up from the wick, and Theron went out into the lane.

---

The coach was at the office door at the quarter past, and the driver counted heads at twenty past, and at the half he looked at the coach clock above the office door and clucked once and shook the reins.

Theron was on the outside bench.

He had his strap across his knees. The strap was warm in the cold of the early afternoon, the leather having taken the warm of his hand at the inn, and the bench under him was the cold of a bench that had stood out in the lane since first bell.

The driver's elbow found the rest on the door rail with the small settled motion of a man whose elbow had found the rest on the door rail at the half of one on a Tuesday for enough Tuesdays that the rest knew the elbow. The coach moved. The lane began to slide back at the slow working pace of a coach starting on a cold cobble. The wheels found their rhythm at the second turn of the lane and held it.

The painted board over the chandlery went past on the right at the slow working pace.

It said *Pessel & Son — Chandlers & Ropework* in white over older white, and the older white was the older white it had been on the Saturday Sable had walked Theron down the lane to Pessel's door, and the new white was the new white it had been then, and the board went past the coach at the working pace of a coach turning at the foot of a working harbour lane and was behind him.

The harbour bell at the customs slip struck once for the hour of the afternoon.

Theron sat with the strap across his knees and the slip in the back fold of the notebook in the strap, and the inland road came up at the bend at the top of the lane, and the driver's elbow stayed on the rest at the door rail, and the coach took the bend at the slow working pace of a coach taking the first bend of an inland run, and the southern light was on the cobbles ahead and on the back of the driver's coat and on the leather of the strap warm under Theron's hand.

He moved his thumb off the strap, and left it there.
